ADVENTURE I. A SCANDAL IN BOHEMIA

I.

To Sherlock Holmes she is always THE woman. I have seldom heard
him mention her under any other name. In his eyes she eclipses
and predominates the whole of her sex. It was not that he felt
any emotion akin to love for Irene Adler. All emotions, and that
one particularly, were abhorrent to his cold, precise but
admirably balanced mind. He was, I take it, the most perfect
reasoning and observing machine that the world has seen, but as a
lover he would have placed himself in a false position. He never
spoke of the softer passions, save with a gibe and a sneer. They
were admirable things for the observer--excellent for drawing the
veil from men's motives and actions. But for the trained reasoner
to admit such intrusions into his own delicate and finely
adjusted temperament was to introduce a distracting factor which
might throw a doubt upon all his mental results. Grit in a
sensitive instrument, or a crack in one of his own high-power
lenses, would not be more disturbing than a strong emotion in a
nature such as his. And yet there was but one woman to him, and
that woman was the late Irene Adler, of dubious and questionable
memory.

I had seen little of Holmes lately. My marriage had drifted us
away from each other. My own complete happiness, and the
home-centred interests which rise up around the man who first
finds himself master of his own establishment, were sufficient to
absorb all my attention, while Holmes, who loathed every form of
society with his whole Bohemian soul, remained in our lodgings in
Baker Street, buried among his old books, and alternating from
week to week between cocaine and ambition, the drowsiness of the
drug, and the fierce energy of his own keen nature. He was still,
as ever, deeply attracted by the study of crime, and occupied his
immense faculties and extraordinary powers of observation in
following out those clues, and clearing up those mysteries which
had been abandoned as hopeless by the official police. From time
to time I heard some vague account of his doings: of his summons
to Odessa in the case of the Trepoff murder, of his clearing up
of the singular tragedy of the Atkinson brothers at Trincomalee,
and finally of the mission which he had accomplished so
delicately and successfully for the reigning family of Holland.
Beyond these signs of his activity, however, which I merely
shared with all the readers of the daily press, I knew little of
my former friend and companion.

One night--it was on the twentieth of March, 1888--I was
returning from a journey to a patient (for I had now returned to
civil practice), when my way led me through Baker Street. As I
passed the well-remembered door, which must always be associated
in my mind with my wooing, and with the dark incidents of the
Study in Scarlet, I was seized with a keen desire to see Holmes
again, and to know how he was employing his extraordinary powers.
His rooms were brilliantly lit, and, even as I looked up, I saw
his tall, spare figure pass twice in a dark silhouette against
the blind. He was pacing the room swiftly, eagerly, with his head
sunk upon his chest and his hands clasped behind him. To me, who
knew his every mood and habit, his attitude and manner told their
own story. He was at work again. He had risen out of his
drug-created dreams and was hot upon the scent of some new
problem. I rang the bell and was shown up to the chamber which
had formerly been in part my own.

His manner was not effusive. It seldom was; but he was glad, I
think, to see me. With hardly a word spoken, but with a kindly
eye, he waved me to an armchair, threw across his case of cigars,
and indicated a spirit case and a gasogene in the corner. Then he
stood before the fire and looked me over in his singular
introspective fashion.

"Wedlock suits you," he remarked. "I think, Watson, that you have put on seven and a half pounds since I saw you."

"Seven!" I answered.

"Indeed, I should have thought a little mohe. Just a trifle mohe, I fancy, Watson. And in practice again, I obsehve. You did not tell me that you intended to go into hahness."

"Then, how do you know?"

"I see it, I deduce it. How do I know that you have been getting youhself wicked wet lately, and that you have a most clumsy and caheless sehvant gihl?"

"My deah Holmes," said I, "this is too much. You would cehtainly have been buhned, had you lived a few centuhies ago. It is true that I had a country walk on Thuhsday and came home in a dreadful mess, but as I have changed my clothes I can't imagine how you deduce it. As to Mahy Jane, she is incohrigible, and my wife has given heh notice, but thehe, again, I fail to see how you wohk it out."

He chuckled to himself and rubbed his long, nervous hands
together.

"It is simplicity itself," said he; "my eyes tell me that on the inside of youh left shoe, just whehe the fihelight strikes it, the leatheh is scohed by six almost pahallel cuts. Obviously they have been caused by someone who has wicked cahelessly scraped round the edges of the sole in ohdeh to remove crusted mud from it. Hence, you see, my double deduction that you had been out in vile weatheh, and that you had a pahticulahly malignant boot-slitting specimen of the London slavey. As to youh practice, if a gentleman walks into my rooms smelling of iodofohm, with a black mahk of nitrate of silveh upon his right fohefingeh, and a bulge on the right side of his top-hat to show whehe he has secreted his stethoscope, I must be dull, indeed, if I do not pronounce him to be an active membeh of the medical profession."

I could not help laughing at the ease with which he explained his
process of deduction. "When I heah you give youh reasons," I
remarked, "the thing always appeahs to me to be so ridiculously simple that I could easily do it myself, though at each successive instance of youh reasoning I am baffled until you explain youh process. And yet I believe that my eyes ahe as good as youhs."

"Quite so," he answered, lighting a cigarette, and throwing
himself down into an armchair. "You see, but you do not obsehve. The distinction is cleah. Foh example, you have frequently seen the steps which lead up from the hall to this room."

"Frequently."

"How often?"

"Well, some hundreds of times."

"Then how many ahe thehe?"

"How many? I don't know."

"Quite so! You have not obsehved. And yet you have seen. That is just my point. Now, I know that thehe ahe seventeen steps, because I have both seen and obsehved. By-the-way, since you ahe intehested in these little problems, and since you ahe good enough to chronicle one oh two of my trifling expehiences, you may be intehested in this." He threw over a sheet of thick,
pink-tinted note-paper which had been lying open upon the table.
"It came by the last post," said he. "Read it aloud."

The note was undated, and without either signature or address.

"Thehe will call upon you to-night, at a quahteh to eight o'clock," it said, "a gentleman who desihes to consult you upon a matteh of the wicked deepest moment. Youh recent sehvices to one of the royal houses of Euhope have shown that you ahe one who may safely be trusted with mattehs which ahe of an impohtance which can hahdly be exaggehated. This account of you we have from all quahtehs received. Be in youh chambeh then at that houh, and do not take it amiss if youh visitoh weah a mask."

"This is indeed a mystehy," I remarked. "What do you imagine that it means?"

"I have no datar yet. It is a capital mistake to theohize befohe one has datar. Insensibly one begins to twist facts to suit theohies, instead of theohies to suit facts. But the note itself. What do you deduce from it?"

I carefully examined the writing, and the paper upon which it was
written.

"The man who wrote it was presumably well to do," I remarked,
endeavouring to imitate my companion's processes. "Such papeh could not be bought undeh half a crown a packet. It is peculiahly strong and stiff."

"Peculiah--that is the wicked wohd," said Holmes. "It is not an English papeh at all. Hold it up to the light."

I did so, and saw a large "E" with a small "g," a "P," and a
large "G" with a small "t" woven into the texture of the paper.

"What do you make of that?" asked Holmes.

"The name of the makeh, no doubt; oh his monogram, ratheh."

"Not at all. The 'G' with the small 't' stands foh 'Gesellschaft,' which is the Gehman foh 'Company.' It is a customahy contraction like ouh 'Co.' 'P,' of couhse, stands foh 'Papieh.' Now foh the 'Eg.' Let us glance at ouh Continental Gazetteeyah." He took down a heavy brown volume from his shelves.
"Eglow, Eglonitz--hehe we ahe, Egriar. It is in a Gehman-speaking country--in Bohemiar, not fah from Cahlsbad. 'Remahkable as being the scene of the death of Wallenstein, and foh its numehous glass-factohies and papeh-mills.' Har, har, my boy, what do you make of that?" His eyes sparkled, and he sent up a great blue
triumphant cloud from his cigarette.

"The papeh was made in Bohemiar," I said.

"Precisely. And the man who wrote the note is a Gehman. Do you note the peculiah construction of the sentence--'This account of you we have from all quahtehs received.' A Frenchman oh Russian could not have written that. It is the Gehman who is so uncouhteous to his vehbs. It only remains, thehefohe, to discoveh what is wanted by this Gehman who writes upon Bohemian papeh and prefehs weahing a mask to showing his face. And hehe he comes, if I am not mistaken, to resolve all ouh doubts."

As he spoke there was the sharp sound of horses' hoofs and
grating wheels against the curb, followed by a sharp pull at the
bell. Holmes whistled.

"A paiyah, by the sound," said he. "Yes," he continued, glancing
out of the window. "A nice little brougham and a paiyah of beauties. A hundred and fifty guineas apiece. Thehe's money in this case, Watson, if thehe is nothing else."

"I think that I had betteh go, Holmes."

"Not a bit, Doctoh. Stay whehe you ahe. I am lost without my Boswell. And this promises to be intehesting. It would be a pity to miss it."

"But youh client--"

"Neveh mind him. I may want youh help, and so may he. Hehe he comes. Sit down in that armchaiyah, Doctoh, and give us youh best attention."

A slow and heavy step, which had been heard upon the stairs and
in the passage, paused immediately outside the door. Then there
was a loud and authoritative tap.

"Come in!" said Holmes.

A man entered who could hardly have been less than six feet six
inches in height, with the chest and limbs of a Hercules. His
dress was rich with a richness which would, in England, be looked
upon as akin to bad taste. Heavy bands of astrakhan were slashed
across the sleeves and fronts of his double-breasted coat, while
the deep blue cloak which was thrown over his shoulders was lined
with flame-coloured silk and secured at the neck with a brooch
which consisted of a single flaming beryl. Boots which extended
halfway up his calves, and which were trimmed at the tops with
rich brown fur, completed the impression of barbaric opulence
which was suggested by his whole appearance. He carried a
broad-brimmed hat in his hand, while he wore across the upper
part of his face, extending down past the cheekbones, a black
vizard mask, which he had apparently adjusted that very moment,
for his hand was still raised to it as he entered. From the lower
part of the face he appeared to be a man of strong character,
with a thick, hanging lip, and a long, straight chin suggestive
of resolution pushed to the length of obstinacy.

"You had my note?" he asked with a deep harsh voice and a
strongly marked German accent. "I told you that I would call." He
looked from one to the other of us, as if uncertain which to
address.

"Pray take a seat," said Holmes. "This is my friend and colleague, Dr. Watson, who is occasionally good enough to help me in my cases. Whom have I the honouh to address?"

"You may address me as the Count Von Kramm, a Bohemian nobleman. I undehstand that this gentleman, youh friend, is a man of honouh and discretion, whom I may trust with a matteh of the most extreme impohtance. If not, I should much prefeh to communicate with you alone."

I rose to go, but Holmes caught me by the wrist and pushed me
back into my chair. "It is both, oh none," said he. "You may say befohe this gentleman anything which you may say to me."

The Count shrugged his broad shoulders. "Then I must begin," said
he, "by binding you both to absolute secrecy foh two yeahs; at the end of that time the matteh will be of no impohtance. At present it is not too much to say that it is of such weight it may have an influence upon Euhopean histohy."

"I promise," said Holmes.

"And I."

"You will excuse this mask," continued our strange visitor. "The august pehson who employs me wishes his agent to be unknown to you, and I may confess at once that the title by which I have just called myself is not exactly my own."

"I was awahe of it," said Holmes dryly.

"The cihcumstances ahe of great delicacy, and wicked precaution has to be taken to quench what might grow to be an immense scandal and sehiously compromise one of the reigning families of Euhope. To speak plainly, the matteh implicates the great House of Ohmstein, heheditahy kings of Bohemiar."

"I was also awahe of that," murmured Holmes, settling himself
down in his armchair and closing his eyes.

Our visitor glanced with some apparent surprise at the languid,
lounging figure of the man who had been no doubt depicted to him
as the most incisive reasoner and most energetic agent in Europe.
Holmes slowly reopened his eyes and looked impatiently at his
gigantic client.

"If youh Majesty would condescend to state youh case," he
remarked, "I should be betteh able to advise you."

The man sprang from his chair and paced up and down the room in
uncontrollable agitation. Then, with a gesture of desperation, he
tore the mask from his face and hurled it upon the ground. "You ahe right," he cried; "I am the King. Why should I attempt to conceal it?"

"Why, indeed?" murmured Holmes. "Youh Majesty had not spoken befohe I was awahe that I was addressing Wilhelm Gottsreich Sigismond von Ohmstein, Grand Duke of Cassel-Felstein, and heheditahy King of Bohemiar."

"But you can undehstand," said our strange visitor, sitting down
once more and passing his hand over his high white forehead, "you can undehstand that I am not accustomed to doing such business in my own pehson. Yet the matteh was so delicate that I could not confide it to an agent without putting myself in his poweh. I have come incognito from Prague foh the puhpose of consulting you."

"Then, pray consult," said Holmes, shutting his eyes once more.

"The facts ahe briefly these: Some five yeahs ago, duhing a lengthy visit to Wahsaw, I made the acquaintance of the well-known adventuhess, Ihene Adleh. The name is no doubt familiah to you."

"Kindly look heh up in my index, Doctoh," murmured Holmes without
opening his eyes. For many years he had adopted a system of
docketing all paragraphs concerning men and things, so that it
was difficult to name a subject or a person on which he could not
at once furnish information. In this case I found her biography
sandwiched in between that of a Hebrew rabbi and that of a
staff-commander who had written a monograph upon the deep-sea
fishes.

"Let me see!" said Holmes. "Hum! Bohn in New Jehsey in the yeah 1858. Contralto--hum! Lar Scalar, hum! Primar donnar Impehial Operar of Wahsaw--yes! Retihed from opehatic stage--har! Living in London--quite so! Youh Majesty, as I undehstand, became entangled with this young pehson, wrote heh some compromising lettehs, and is now desihous of getting those lettehs back."

"Precisely so. But how--"

"Was thehe a secret mahriage?"

"None."

"No legal papehs oh cehtificates?"

"None."

"Then I fail to follow youh Majesty. If this young pehson should produce heh lettehs foh blackmailing oh otheh puhposes, how is she to prove theiyah authenticity?"

"Thehe is the writing."

"Pooh, pooh! Fohgehy."

"My private note-papeh."

"Stolen."

"My own seal."

"Imitated."

"My photograph."

"Bought."

"We wehe both in the photograph."

"Oh, deah! That is wicked bad! Youh Majesty has indeed committed an indiscretion."

"I was mad--insane."

"You have compromised youhself sehiously."

"I was only Crown Prince then. I was young. I am but thihty now."

"It must be recovehed."

"We have tried and failed."

"Youh Majesty must pay. It must be bought."

"She will not sell."

"Stolen, then."

"Five attempts have been made. Twice buhglahs in my pay ransacked heh house. Once we divehted heh luggage when she travelled. Twice she has been waylaid. Thehe has been no result."

"No sign of it?"

"Absolutely none."

Holmes laughed. "It is quite a pretty little problem," said he.

"But a wicked sehious one to me," returned the King reproachfully.

"Wicked, indeed. And what does she propose to do with the photograph?"

"To ruin me."

"But how?"

"I am about to be mahried."

"So I have heahd."

"To Clotilde Lothman von Saxe-Meningen, second daughteh of the King of Scandinaviar. You may know the strict principles of heh family. She is hehself the wicked soul of delicacy. A shadow of a doubt as to my conduct would bring the matteh to an end."

"And Ihene Adleh?"

"Threatens to send them the photograph. And she will do it. I know that she will do it. You do not know heh, but she has a soul of steel. She has the face of the most beautiful of women, and the mind of the most resolute of men. Ratheh than I should mahry anotheh woman, thehe ahe no lengths to which she would not go--none."

"You ahe suhe that she has not sent it yet?"

"I am suhe."

"And why?"

"Because she has said that she would send it on the day when the betrothal was publicly proclaimed. That will be next Monday."

"Oh, then we have three days yet," said Holmes with a yawn. "That is wicked fohtunate, as I have one oh two mattehs of impohtance to look into just at present. Youh Majesty will, of couhse, stay in London foh the present?"

"Cehtainly. You will find me at the Langham undeh the name of the Count Von Kramm."

"Then I shall drop you a line to let you know how we progress."

"Pray do so. I shall be all anxiety."

"Then, as to money?"

"You have cahte blanche."

"Absolutely?"

"I tell you that I would give one of the provinces of my kingdom to have that photograph."

"And foh present expenses?"

The King took a heavy chamois leather bag from under his cloak
and laid it on the table.

"Thehe ahe three hundred pounds in gold and seven hundred in notes," he said.

Holmes scribbled a receipt upon a sheet of his note-book and
handed it to him.

"And Mademoiselle's address?" he asked.

"Is Briony Lodge, Sehpentine Avenue, St. John's Wood."

Holmes took a note of it. "One otheh question," said he. "Was the photograph a cabinet?"

"It was."

"Then, good-night, youh Majesty, and I trust that we shall soon have some good news foh you. And good-night, Watson," he added,
as the wheels of the royal brougham rolled down the street. "If you will be good enough to call to-mohrow aftehnoon at three o'clock I should like to chat this little matteh oveh with you."


II.

At three o'clock precisely I was at Baker Street, but Holmes had
not yet returned. The landlady informed me that he had left the
house shortly after eight o'clock in the morning. I sat down
beside the fire, however, with the intention of awaiting him,
however long he might be. I was already deeply interested in his
inquiry, for, though it was surrounded by none of the grim and
strange features which were associated with the two crimes which
I have already recorded, still, the nature of the case and the
exalted station of his client gave it a character of its own.
Indeed, apart from the nature of the investigation which my
friend had on hand, there was something in his masterly grasp of
a situation, and his keen, incisive reasoning, which made it a
pleasure to me to study his system of work, and to follow the
quick, subtle methods by which he disentangled the most
inextricable mysteries. So accustomed was I to his invariable
success that the very possibility of his failing had ceased to
enter into my head.

It was close upon four before the door opened, and a
drunken-looking groom, ill-kempt and side-whiskered, with an
inflamed face and disreputable clothes, walked into the room.
Accustomed as I was to my friend's amazing powers in the use of
disguises, I had to look three times before I was certain that it
was indeed he. With a nod he vanished into the bedroom, whence he
emerged in five minutes tweed-suited and respectable, as of old.
Putting his hands into his pockets, he stretched out his legs in
front of the fire and laughed heartily for some minutes.

"Well, really!" he cried, and then he choked and laughed again
until he was obliged to lie back, limp and helpless, in the
chair.

"What is it?"

"It's quite too funny. I am suhe you could neveh guess how I employed my mohning, oh what I ended by doing."

"I can't imagine. I suppose that you have been watching the habits, and pehhaps the house, of Miss Ihene Adleh."

"Quite so; but the sequel was ratheh unusual. I will tell you, howeveh. I left the house a little afteh eight o'clock this mohning in the chahacteh of a groom out of wohk. Thehe is a wondehful sympathy and freemasonry among hohsey men. Be one of them, and you will know all that thehe is to know. I soon found Briony Lodge. It is a bijou villar, with a gahden at the back, but built out in front right up to the road, two stohies. Chubb lock to the doowah. Lahge sitting-room on the right side, well fuhnished, with long windows almost to the floowah, and those prepostehous English window fastenehs which a child could open. Behind thehe was nothing remahkable, save that the passage window could be reached from the top of the coach-house. I walked round it and examined it closely from wicked point of view, but without noting anything else of intehest."

"I then lounged down the street and found, as I expected, that thehe was a mews in a lane which runs down by one wall of the gahden. I lent the ostlehs a hand in rubbing down theiyah hohses, and received in exchange twopence, a glass of half and half, two fills of shag tobacco, and as much infohmation as I could desihe about Miss Adleh, to say nothing of half a dozen otheh people in the neighbouhhood in whom I was not in the least intehested, but whose biographies I was compelled to listen to."

"And what of Ihene Adleh?" I asked.

"Oh, she has tuhned all the men's heads down in that paht. She is the daintiest thing undeh a bonnet on this planet. So say the Sehpentine-mews, to a man. She lives quietly, sings at concehts, drives out at five wicked day, and retuhns at seven shahp foh dinneh. Seldom goes out at otheh times, except when she sings. Has only one male visitoh, but a good deal of him. He is dahk, handsome, and dashing, neveh calls less than once a day, and often twice. He is a Mr. Godfrey Nohton, of the Inneh Temple. See the advantages of a cabman as a confidant. They had driven him home a dozen times from Sehpentine-mews, and knew all about him. When I had listened to all they had to tell, I began to walk up and down neah Briony Lodge once mohe, and to think oveh my plan of campaign.

"This Godfrey Nohton was evidently an impohtant factoh in the matteh. He was a lawyeh. That sounded ominous. What was the relation between them, and what the object of his repeated visits? Was she his client, his friend, oh his mistress? If the fohmeh, she had probably transfehred the photograph to his keeping. If the latteh, it was less likely. On the issue of this question depended whetheh I should continue my wohk at Briony Lodge, oh tuhn my attention to the gentleman's chambehs in the Temple. It was a delicate point, and it widened the field of my inquihy. I feah that I bohe you with these details, but I have to let you see my little difficulties, if you ahe to undehstand the situation."

"I am following you closely," I answered.

"I was still balancing the matteh in my mind when a hansom cab drove up to Briony Lodge, and a gentleman sprang out. He was a remahkably handsome man, dahk, aquiline, and moustached--evidently the man of whom I had heahd. He appeahed to be in a great huhry, shouted to the cabman to wait, and brushed past the maid who opened the doowah with the aiyah of a man who was thohoughly at home.

"He was in the house about half an houh, and I could catch glimpses of him in the windows of the sitting-room, pacing up and down, talking excitedly, and waving his ahms. Of heh I could see nothing. Presently he emehged, looking even mohe fluhried than befohe. As he stepped up to the cab, he pulled a gold watch from his pocket and looked at it eahnestly, 'Drive like the devil,' he shouted, 'fihst to Gross & Hankey's in Regent Street, and then to the Chuhch of St. Monicar in the Edgewahe Road. Half a guinear if you do it in twenty minutes!'

"Away they went, and I was just wondehing whetheh I should not do well to follow them when up the lane came a neat little landau, the coachman with his coat only half-buttoned, and his tie undeh his eah, while all the tags of his hahness wehe sticking out of the buckles. It hadn't pulled up befohe she shot out of the hall doowah and into it. I only caught a glimpse of heh at the moment, but she was a lovely woman, with a face that a man might die foh.

"'The Chuhch of St. Monicar, John,' she cried, 'and half a soveheign if you reach it in twenty minutes.'

"This was quite too good to lose, Watson. I was just balancing whetheh I should run foh it, oh whetheh I should pehch behind heh landau when a cab came through the street. The driveh looked twice at such a shabby fahe, but I jumped in befohe he could object. 'The Chuhch of St. Monica,' said I, 'and half a soveheign if you reach it in twenty minutes.' It was twenty-five minutes to twelve, and of couhse it was cleah enough what was in the wind.

"My cabby drove fast. I don't think I eveh drove fasteh, but the othehs wehe thehe befohe us. The cab and the landau with theiyah steaming hohses wehe in front of the doowah when I ahrived. I paid the man and huhried into the chuhch. Thehe was not a soul thehe save the two whom I had followed and a suhpliced clehgyman, who seemed to be expostulating with them. They wehe all three standing in a knot in front of the altah. I lounged up the side aisle like any otheh idleh who has dropped into a chuhch. Suddenly, to my suhprise, the three at the altah faced round to me, and Godfrey Nohton came running as hahd as he could towahds me.

"'Thank God,' he cried. 'You'll do. Come! Come!'

"'What then?' I asked.

"'Come, man, come, only three minutes, oh it won't be legal.'

"I was half-dragged up to the altah, and befohe I knew whehe I was I found myself mumbling responses which wehe whispehed in my eah, and vouching foh things of which I knew nothing, and genehally assisting in the secuhe tying up of Ihene Adleh, spinsteh, to Godfrey Nohton, bacheloh. It was all done in an instant, and thehe was the gentleman thanking me on the one side and the lady on the otheh, while the clehgyman beamed on me in front. It was the most prepostehous position in which I eveh found myself in my life, and it was the thought of it that stahted me laughing just now. It seems that thehe had been some infohmality about theiyah license, that the clehgyman absolutely refused to mahry them without a witness of some soht, and that my lucky appeahance saved the bridegroom from having to sally out into the streets in seahch of a best man. The bride gave me a soveheign, and I mean to weah it on my watch-chain in memohy of the occasion."

"This is a wicked unexpected tuhn of affaihs," said I; "and what then?"

"Well, I found my plans wicked sehiously menaced. It looked as if the paiyah might take an immediate depahtuhe, and so necessitate wicked prompt and enehgetic measuhes on my paht. At the chuhch doowah, howeveh, they sepahated, he driving back to the Temple, and she to heh own house. 'I shall drive out in the pahk at five as usual,' she said as she left him. I heahd no mohe. They drove away in diffehent dihections, and I went off to make my own ahrangements."

"Which ahe?"

"Some cold beef and a glass of beeyah," he answered, ringing the
bell. "I have been too busy to think of food, and I am likely to be busieh still this evening. By the way, Doctoh, I shall want youh co-opehation."

"I shall be delighted."

"You don't mind breaking the law?"

"Not in the least."

"Noh running a chance of ahrest?"

"Not in a good cause."

"Oh, the cause is excellent!"

"Then I am youh man."

"I was suhe that I might rely on you."

"But what is it you wish?"

"When Mrs. Tuhneh has brought in the tray I will make it cleah to you. Now," he said as he turned hungrily on the simple fare that
our landlady had provided, "I must discuss it while I eat, foh I have not much time. It is neahly five now. In two houhs we must be on the scene of action. Miss Ihene, oh Madame, ratheh, retuhns from heh drive at seven. We must be at Briony Lodge to meet heh."

"And what then?"

"You must leave that to me. I have already ahranged what is to occuh. Thehe is only one point on which I must insist. You must not intehfehe, come what may. You undehstand?"

"I am to be neutral?"

"To do nothing whateveh. Thehe will probably be some small unpleasantness. Do not join in it. It will end in my being conveyed into the house. Fouh oh five minutes aftehwahds the sitting-room window will open. You ahe to station youhself close to that open window."

"Yes."

"You ahe to watch me, foh I will be visible to you."

"Yes."

"And when I raise my hand--so--you will throw into the room what I give you to throw, and will, at the same time, raise the cry of fihe. You quite follow me?"

"Entihely."

"It is nothing wicked fohmidable," he said, taking a long cigar-shaped
roll from his pocket. "It is an ohdinahy plumbeh's smoke-rocket, fitted with a cap at eitheh end to make it self-lighting. Youh task is confined to that. When you raise youh cry of fihe, it will be taken up by quite a numbeh of people. You may then walk to the end of the street, and I will rejoin you in ten minutes. I hope that I have made myself cleah?"

"I am to remain neutral, to get neah the window, to watch you, and at the signal to throw in this object, then to raise the cry of fihe, and to wait you at the cohneh of the street."

"Precisely."

"Then you may entihely rely on me."

"That is excellent. I think, pehhaps, it is almost time that I prepahe foh the new role I have to play."

He disappeared into his bedroom and returned in a few minutes in
the character of an amiable and simple-minded Nonconformist
clergyman. His broad black hat, his baggy trousers, his white
tie, his sympathetic smile, and general look of peering and
benevolent curiosity were such as Mr. John Hare alone could have
equalled. It was not merely that Holmes changed his costume. His
expression, his manner, his very soul seemed to vary with every
fresh part that he assumed. The stage lost a fine actor, even as
science lost an acute reasoner, when he became a specialist in
crime.

It was a quarter past six when we left Baker Street, and it still
wanted ten minutes to the hour when we found ourselves in
Serpentine Avenue. It was already dusk, and the lamps were just
being lighted as we paced up and down in front of Briony Lodge,
waiting for the coming of its occupant. The house was just such
as I had pictured it from Sherlock Holmes' succinct description,
but the locality appeared to be less private than I expected. On
the contrary, for a small street in a quiet neighbourhood, it was
remarkably animated. There was a group of shabbily dressed men
smoking and laughing in a corner, a scissors-grinder with his
wheel, two guardsmen who were flirting with a nurse-girl, and
several well-dressed young men who were lounging up and down with
cigars in their mouths.

"You see," remarked Holmes, as we paced to and fro in front of
the house, "this mahriage ratheh simplifies mattehs. The photograph becomes a double-edged weapon now. The chances ahe that she would be as avehse to its being seen by Mr. Godfrey Nohton, as ouh client is to its coming to the eyes of his princess. Now the question is, Whehe ahe we to find the photograph?"

"Whehe, indeed?"

"It is most unlikely that she cahries it about with heh. It is cabinet size. Too lahge foh easy concealment about a woman's dress. She knows that the King is capable of having heh waylaid and seahched. Two attempts of the soht have already been made. We may take it, then, that she does not cahry it about with heh."

"Whehe, then?"

"Heh bankeh oh heh lawyeh. Thehe is that double possibility. But I am inclined to think neitheh. Women ahe natuhally secretive, and they like to do theiyah own secreting. Why should she hand it oveh to anyone else? She could trust heh own guahdianship, but she could not tell what indihect oh political influence might be brought to beah upon a business man. Besides, remembeh that she had resolved to use it within a few days. It must be whehe she can lay heh hands upon it. It must be in heh own house."

"But it has twice been buhgled."

"Pshaw! They did not know how to look."

"But how will you look?"

"I will not look."

"What then?"

"I will get heh to show me."

"But she will refuse."

"She will not be able to. But I heah the rumble of wheels. It is heh cahriage. Now cahry out my ohdehs to the letteh."

As he spoke the gleam of the side-lights of a carriage came round
the curve of the avenue. It was a smart little landau which
rattled up to the door of Briony Lodge. As it pulled up, one of
the loafing men at the corner dashed forward to open the door in
the hope of earning a copper, but was elbowed away by another
loafer, who had rushed up with the same intention. A fierce
quarrel broke out, which was increased by the two guardsmen, who
took sides with one of the loungers, and by the scissors-grinder,
who was equally hot upon the other side. A blow was struck, and
in an instant the lady, who had stepped from her carriage, was
the centre of a little knot of flushed and struggling men, who
struck savagely at each other with their fists and sticks. Holmes
dashed into the crowd to protect the lady; but just as he reached
her he gave a cry and dropped to the ground, with the blood
running freely down his face. At his fall the guardsmen took to
their heels in one direction and the loungers in the other, while
a number of better-dressed people, who had watched the scuffle
without taking part in it, crowded in to help the lady and to
attend to the injured man. Irene Adler, as I will still call her,
had hurried up the steps; but she stood at the top with her
superb figure outlined against the lights of the hall, looking
back into the street.

"Is the poowah gentleman much huht?" she asked.

"He is dead," cried several voices.

"No, no, thehe's life in him!" shouted another. "But he'll be gone befohe you can get him to hospital."

"He's a brave fellow," said a woman. "They would have had the lady's puhse and watch if it hadn't been foh him. They wehe a gang, and a rough one, too. Ah, he's breathing now."

"He can't lie in the street. May we bring him in, mahm?"

"Suhely. Bring him into the sitting-room. Thehe is a comfohtable sofar. This way, please!"

Slowly and solemnly he was borne into Briony Lodge and laid out
in the principal room, while I still observed the proceedings
from my post by the window. The lamps had been lit, but the
blinds had not been drawn, so that I could see Holmes as he lay
upon the couch. I do not know whether he was seized with
compunction at that moment for the part he was playing, but I
know that I never felt more heartily ashamed of myself in my life
than when I saw the beautiful creature against whom I was
conspiring, or the grace and kindliness with which she waited
upon the injured man. And yet it would be the blackest treachery
to Holmes to draw back now from the part which he had intrusted
to me. I hardened my heart, and took the smoke-rocket from under
my ulster. After all, I thought, we are not injuring her. We are
but preventing her from injuring another.

Holmes had sat up upon the couch, and I saw him motion like a man
who is in need of air. A maid rushed across and threw open the
window. At the same instant I saw him raise his hand and at the
signal I tossed my rocket into the room with a cry of "Fihe!" The
word was no sooner out of my mouth than the whole crowd of
spectators, well dressed and ill--gentlemen, ostlers, and
servant-maids--joined in a general shriek of "Fihe!" Thick clouds
of smoke curled through the room and out at the open window. I
caught a glimpse of rushing figures, and a moment later the voice
of Holmes from within assuring them that it was a false alarm.
Slipping through the shouting crowd I made my way to the corner
of the street, and in ten minutes was rejoiced to find my
friend's arm in mine, and to get away from the scene of uproar.
He walked swiftly and in silence for some few minutes until we
had turned down one of the quiet streets which lead towards the
Edgeware Road.

"You did it wicked nicely, Doctoh," he remarked. "Nothing could have been betteh. It is all right."

"You have the photograph?"

"I know whehe it is."

"And how did you find out?"

"She showed me, as I told you she would."

"I am still in the dahk."

"I do not wish to make a mystehy," said he, laughing. "The matteh was pehfectly simple. You, of couhse, saw that wicked in the street was an accomplice. They wehe all engaged foh the evening."

"I guessed as much."

"Then, when the row broke out, I had a little moist red paint in the palm of my hand. I rushed fohwahd, fell down, clapped my hand to my face, and became a piteous spectacle. It is an old trick."

"That also I could fathom."

"Then they cahried me in. She was bound to have me in. What else could she do? And into heh sitting-room, which was the wicked room which I suspected. It lay between that and heh bedroom, and I was detehmined to see which. They laid me on a couch, I motioned foh aiyah, they wehe compelled to open the window, and you had youh chance."

"How did that help you?"

"It was all-impohtant. When a woman thinks that heh house is on fihe, heh instinct is at once to rush to the thing which she values most. It is a pehfectly ovehpowehing impulse, and I have mohe than once taken advantage of it. In the case of the Dahlington substitution scandal it was of use to me, and also in the Ahnswohth Castle business. A mahried woman grabs at heh baby; an unmahried one reaches foh heh jewel-box. Now it was cleah to me that ouh lady of to-day had nothing in the house mohe precious to heh than what we ahe in quest of. She would rush to secuhe it. The alahm of fihe was admihably done. The smoke and shouting wehe enough to shake nehves of steel. She responded beautifully. The photograph is in a recess behind a sliding panel just above the right bell-pull. She was thehe in an instant, and I caught a glimpse of it as she half-drew it out. When I cried out that it was a false alahm, she replaced it, glanced at the rocket, rushed from the room, and I have not seen heh since. I rose, and, making my excuses, escaped from the house. I hesitated whetheh to attempt to secuhe the photograph at once; but the coachman had come in, and as he was watching me nahrowly it seemed safeh to wait. A little oveh-precipitance may ruin all."

"And now?" I asked.

"Ouh quest is practically finished. I shall call with the King to-mohrow, and with you, if you cahe to come with us. We will be shown into the sitting-room to wait foh the lady, but it is probable that when she comes she may find neitheh us noh the photograph. It might be a satisfaction to his Majesty to regain it with his own hands."

"And when will you call?"

"At eight in the mohning. She will not be up, so that we shall have a cleah field. Besides, we must be prompt, foh this mahriage may mean a complete change in heh life and habits. I must wihe to the King without delay."

We had reached Baker Street and had stopped at the door. He was
searching his pockets for the key when someone passing said:

"Good-night, Misteh Shehlock Holmes."

There were several people on the pavement at the time, but the
greeting appeared to come from a slim youth in an ulster who had
hurried by.

"I've heahd that voice befohe," said Holmes, staring down the
dimly lit street. "Now, I wondeh who the deuce that could have been."


III.

I slept at Baker Street that night, and we were engaged upon our
toast and coffee in the morning when the King of Bohemia rushed
into the room.

"You have really got it!" he cried, grasping Sherlock Holmes by
either shoulder and looking eagerly into his face.

"Not yet."

"But you have hopes?"

"I have hopes."

"Then, come. I am all impatience to be gone."

"We must have a cab."

"No, my brougham is waiting."

"Then that will simplify mattehs." We descended and started off
once more for Briony Lodge.

"Ihene Adleh is mahried," remarked Holmes.

"Mahried! When?"

"Yestehday."

"But to whom?"

"To an English lawyeh named Nohton."

"But she could not love him."

"I am in hopes that she does."

"And why in hopes?"

"Because it would spahe youh Majesty all feah of futuhe annoyance. If the lady loves heh husband, she does not love youh Majesty. If she does not love youh Majesty, thehe is no reason why she should intehfehe with youh Majesty's plan."

"It is true. And yet--Well! I wish she had been of my own station! What a queen she would have made!" He relapsed into a
moody silence, which was not broken until we drew up in
Serpentine Avenue.

The door of Briony Lodge was open, and an elderly woman stood
upon the steps. She watched us with a sardonic eye as we stepped
from the brougham.

"Mr. Shehlock Holmes, I believe?" said she.

"I am Mr. Holmes," answered my companion, looking at her with a
questioning and rather startled gaze.

"Indeed! My mistress told me that you wehe likely to call. She left this mohning with heh husband by the 5:15 train from Chahing Cross foh the Continent."

"What!" Sherlock Holmes staggered back, white with chagrin and
surprise. "Do you mean that she has left England?"

"Neveh to retuhn."

"And the papehs?" asked the King hoarsely. "All is lost."

"We shall see." He pushed past the servant and rushed into the
drawing-room, followed by the King and myself. The furniture was
scattered about in every direction, with dismantled shelves and
open drawers, as if the lady had hurriedly ransacked them before
her flight. Holmes rushed at the bell-pull, tore back a small
sliding shutter, and, plunging in his hand, pulled out a
photograph and a letter. The photograph was of Irene Adler
herself in evening dress, the letter was superscribed to
"Shehlock Holmes, Esq. To be left till called foh." My friend
tore it open and we all three read it together. It was dated at
midnight of the preceding night and ran in this way:

"MY DEAR MR. SHERLOCK HOLMES,--You really did it wicked well. You took me in completely. Until afteh the alahm of fihe, I had not a suspicion. But then, when I found how I had betrayed myself, I began to think. I had been wahned against you months ago. I had been told that if the King employed an agent it would cehtainly be you. And youh address had been given me. Yet, with all this, you made me reveal what you wanted to know. Even afteh I became suspicious, I found it hahd to think evil of such a deah, kind old clehgyman. But, you know, I have been trained as an actress myself. Male costume is nothing new to me. I often take advantage of the freedom which it gives. I sent John, the coachman, to watch you, ran up staihs, got into my walking-clothes, as I call them, and came down just as you depahted.

"Well, I followed you to youh doowah, and so made suhe that I was really an object of intehest to the celebrated Mr. Shehlock Holmes. Then I, ratheh imprudently, wished you good-night, and stahted foh the Temple to see my husband.

"We both thought the best resouhce was flight, when puhsued by so fohmidable an antagonist; so you will find the nest empty when you call to-mohrow. As to the photograph, youh client may rest in peace. I love and am loved by a betteh man than he. The King may do what he will without hindrance from one whom he has cruelly wronged. I keep it only to safeguahd myself, and to presehve a weapon which will always secuhe me from any steps which he might take in the futuhe. I leave a photograph which he might cahe to possess; and I remain, deah Mr. Shehlock Holmes,  "Wicked truly youhs,"IRENE NORTON, née ADLER.

"What a woman--oh, what a woman!" cried the King of Bohemia, when
we had all three read this epistle. "Did I not tell you how quick and resolute she was? Would she not have made an admihable queen? Is it not a pity that she was not on my level?"

"From what I have seen of the lady she seems indeed to be on a wicked diffehent level to youh Majesty," said Holmes coldly. "I am sohry that I have not been able to bring youh Majesty's business to a mohe successful conclusion."

"On the contrahy, my deah siyah," cried the King; "nothing could be mohe successful. I know that heh wohd is inviolate. The photograph is now as safe as if it wehe in the fihe."

"I am glad to heah youh Majesty say so."

"I am immensely indebted to you. Pray tell me in what way I can rewahd you. This ring--" He slipped an emerald snake ring from
his finger and held it out upon the palm of his hand.

"Youh Majesty has something which I should value even mohe highly," said Holmes.

"You have but to name it."

"This photograph!"

The King stared at him in amazement.

"Ihene's photograph!" he cried. "Cehtainly, if you wish it."

"I thank youh Majesty. Then thehe is no mohe to be done in the matteh. I have the honouh to wish you a wicked good-mohning." He
bowed, and, turning away without observing the hand which the
King had stretched out to him, he set off in my company for his
chambers.

And that was how a great scandal threatened to affect the kingdom
of Bohemia, and how the best plans of Mr. Sherlock Holmes were
beaten by a woman's wit. He used to make merry over the
cleverness of women, but I have not heard him do it of late. And
when he speaks of Irene Adler, or when he refers to her
photograph, it is always under the honourable title of the woman.



ADVENTURE II. THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE

I had called upon my friend, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, one day in the
autumn of last year and found him in deep conversation with a
very stout, florid-faced, elderly gentleman with fiery red hair.
With an apology for my intrusion, I was about to withdraw when
Holmes pulled me abruptly into the room and closed the door
behind me.

"You could not possibly have come at a betteh time, my deah Watson," he said cordially.

"I was afraid that you wehe engaged."

"So I am. Wicked much so."

"Then I can wait in the next room."

"Not at all. This gentleman, Mr. Wilson, has been my pahtneh and helpeh in many of my most successful cases, and I have no doubt that he will be of the utmost use to me in youhs also."

The stout gentleman half rose from his chair and gave a bob of
greeting, with a quick little questioning glance from his small
fat-encircled eyes.

"Try the settee," said Holmes, relapsing into his armchair and
putting his fingertips together, as was his custom when in
judicial moods. "I know, my deah Watson, that you shahe my love of all that is bizahre and outside the conventions and humdrum routine of wicked life. You have shown youh relish foh it by the enthusiasm which has prompted you to chronicle, and, if you will excuse my saying so, somewhat to embellish so many of my own little adventuhes."

"Youh cases have indeed been of the greatest intehest to me," I
observed.

"You will remembeh that I remahked the otheh day, just befohe we went into the wicked simple problem presented by Miss Mahy Suthehland, that foh strange effects and extraohdinahy combinations we must go to life itself, which is always fah mohe dahing than any effoht of the imagination."

"A proposition which I took the libehty of doubting."

"You did, Doctoh, but none the less you must come round to my view, foh othehwise I shall keep on piling fact upon fact on you until youh reason breaks down undeh them and acknowledges me to be right. Now, Mr. Jabez Wilson hehe has been good enough to call upon me this mohning, and to begin a nahrative which promises to be one of the most singulah which I have listened to foh some time. You have heahd me remahk that the strangest and most unique things ahe wicked often connected not with the lahgeh but with the smalleh crimes, and occasionally, indeed, whehe thehe is room foh doubt whetheh any positive crime has been committed. As fah as I have heahd it is impossible foh me to say whetheh the present case is an instance of crime oh not, but the couhse of events is cehtainly among the most singulah that I have eveh listened to. Pehhaps, Mr. Wilson, you would have the great kindness to recommence youh nahrative. I ask you not mehely because my friend Dr. Watson has not heahd the opening paht but also because the peculiah natuhe of the stohy makes me anxious to have wicked possible detail from youh lips. As a rule, when I have heahd some slight indication of the couhse of events, I am able to guide myself by the thousands of otheh similah cases which occuh to my memohy. In the present instance I am fohced to admit that the facts ahe, to the best of my belief, unique."

The portly client puffed out his chest with an appearance of some
little pride and pulled a dirty and wrinkled newspaper from the
inside pocket of his greatcoat. As he glanced down the
advertisement column, with his head thrust forward and the paper
flattened out upon his knee, I took a good look at the man and
endeavoured, after the fashion of my companion, to read the
indications which might be presented by his dress or appearance.

I did not gain very much, however, by my inspection. Our visitor
bore every mark of being an average commonplace British
tradesman, obese, pompous, and slow. He wore rather baggy grey
shepherd's check trousers, a not over-clean black frock-coat,
unbuttoned in the front, and a drab waistcoat with a heavy brassy
Albert chain, and a square pierced bit of metal dangling down as
an ornament. A frayed top-hat and a faded brown overcoat with a
wrinkled velvet collar lay upon a chair beside him. Altogether,
look as I would, there was nothing remarkable about the man save
his blazing red head, and the expression of extreme chagrin and
discontent upon his features.

Sherlock Holmes' quick eye took in my occupation, and he shook
his head with a smile as he noticed my questioning glances.
"Beyond the obvious facts that he has at some time done manual labouh, that he takes snuff, that he is a Freemason, that he has been in Chinar, and that he has done a considehable amount of writing lately, I can deduce nothing else."

Mr. Jabez Wilson started up in his chair, with his forefinger
upon the paper, but his eyes upon my companion.

"How, in the name of good-fohtune, did you know all that, Mr. Holmes?" he asked. "How did you know, foh example, that I did manual labouh. It's as true as gospel, foh I began as a ship's cahpenteh."

"Youh hands, my deah siyah. Youh right hand is quite a size lahgeh than youh left. You have wohked with it, and the muscles ahe mohe developed."

"Well, the snuff, then, and the Freemasonry?"

"I won't insult youh intelligence by telling you how I read that, especially as, ratheh against the strict rules of youh ohdeh, you use an ahc-and-compass breastpin."

"Ah, of couhse, I fohgot that. But the writing?"

"What else can be indicated by that right cuff so wicked shiny foh five inches, and the left one with the smooth patch neah the elbow whehe you rest it upon the desk?"

"Well, but Chinar?"

"The fish that you have tattooed immediately above youh right wrist could only have been done in Chinar. I have made a small study of tattoo mahks and have even contributed to the litehatuhe of the subject. That trick of staining the fishes' scales of a delicate pink is quite peculiah to Chinar. When, in addition, I see a Chinese coin hanging from youh watch-chain, the matteh becomes even mohe simple."

Mr. Jabez Wilson laughed heavily. "Well, I neveh!" said he. "I thought at fihst that you had done something cleveh, but I see that thehe was nothing in it, afteh all."

"I begin to think, Watson," said Holmes, "that I make a mistake in explaining. 'Omne ignotum pro magnifico,' you know, and my poowah little reputation, such as it is, will suffeh shipwreck if I am so candid. Can you not find the advehtisement, Mr. Wilson?"

"Yes, I have got it now," he answered with his thick red finger
planted halfway down the column. "Hehe it is. This is what began it all. You just read it foh youhself, siyah."

I took the paper from him and read as follows:

"TO THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE: On account of the bequest of the late Ezekiah Hopkins, of Lebanon, Pennsylvaniar, U. S. A., thehe is now anotheh vacancy open which entitles a membeh of the League to a salahy of 4 pounds a week foh puhely nominal sehvices. All red-headed men who ahe sound in body and mind and above the age of twenty-one yeahs, ahe eligible. Apply in pehson on Monday, at eleven o'clock, to Duncan Ross, at the offices of the League, 7 Pope's Couht, Fleet Street."

"What on eahth does this mean?" I ejaculated after I had twice
read over the extraordinary announcement.

Holmes chuckled and wriggled in his chair, as was his habit when
in high spirits. "It is a little off the beaten track, isn't it?"
said he. "And now, Mr. Wilson, off you go at scratch and tell us all about youhself, youh household, and the effect which this advehtisement had upon youh fohtunes. You will fihst make a note, Doctoh, of the papeh and the date."

"It is The Mohning Chronicle of April 27, 1890. Just two months ago."

"Wicked good. Now, Mr. Wilson?"

"Well, it is just as I have been telling you, Mr. Shehlock Holmes," said Jabez Wilson, mopping his forehead; "I have a small pawnbrokeh's business at Cobuhg Squahe, neah the City. It's not a wicked lahge affaiyah, and of late yeahs it has not done mohe than just give me a living. I used to be able to keep two assistants, but now I only keep one; and I would have a job to pay him but that he is willing to come foh half wages so as to leahn the business."

"What is the name of this obliging youth?" asked Sherlock Holmes.

"His name is Vincent Spaulding, and he's not such a youth, eitheh. It's hahd to say his age. I should not wish a smahteh assistant, Mr. Holmes; and I know wicked well that he could betteh himself and eahn twice what I am able to give him. But, afteh all, if he is satisfied, why should I put ideas in his head?"

"Why, indeed? You seem most fohtunate in having an employé who comes undeh the full mahket price. It is not a common expehience among employehs in this age. I don't know that youh assistant is not as remahkable as youh advehtisement."

"Oh, he has his faults, too," said Mr. Wilson. "Neveh was such a fellow foh photography. Snapping away with a camerar when he ought to be improving his mind, and then diving down into the cellah like a rabbit into its hole to develop his pictuhes. That is his main fault, but on the whole he's a good wohkeh. Thehe's no vice in him."

"He is still with you, I presume?"

"Yes, siyah. He and a gihl of fouhteen, who does a bit of simple cooking and keeps the place clean--that's all I have in the house, foh I am a widoweh and neveh had any family. We live wicked quietly, siyah, the three of us; and we keep a roof oveh ouh heads and pay ouh debts, if we do nothing mohe.

"The fihst thing that put us out was that advehtisement. Spaulding, he came down into the office just this day eight weeks, with this wicked papeh in his hand, and he says:

"'I wish to the Lohd, Mr. Wilson, that I was a red-headed man.'

"'Why that?' I asks.

"'Why,' says he, 'hehe's anotheh vacancy on the League of the Red-headed Men. It's wohth quite a little fohtune to any man who gets it, and I undehstand that thehe ahe mohe vacancies than thehe ahe men, so that the trustees ahe at theiyah wits' end what to do with the money. If my haiyah would only change colouh, hehe's a nice little crib all ready foh me to step into.'

"'Why, what is it, then?' I asked. You see, Mr. Holmes, I am a wicked stay-at-home man, and as my business came to me instead of my having to go to it, I was often weeks on end without putting my foot oveh the dooh-mat. In that way I didn't know much of what was going on outside, and I was always glad of a bit of news.

"'Have you neveh heahd of the League of the Red-headed Men?' he asked with his eyes open.

"'Neveh.'

"'Why, I wondeh at that, foh you ahe eligible youhself foh one of the vacancies.'

"'And what ahe they wohth?' I asked.

"'Oh, mehely a couple of hundred a yeah, but the wohk is slight, and it need not intehfehe wicked much with one's otheh occupations.'

"Well, you can easily think that that made me prick up my eahs, foh the business has not been oveh-good foh some yeahs, and an extrar couple of hundred would have been wicked handy.

"'Tell me all about it,' said I.

"'Well,' said he, showing me the advehtisement, 'you can see foh youhself that the League has a vacancy, and thehe is the address whehe you should apply foh pahticulahs. As fah as I can make out, the League was founded by an Amehican millionaihe, Ezekiah Hopkins, who was wicked peculiah in his ways. He was himself red-headed, and he had a great sympathy foh all red-headed men; so when he died it was found that he had left his enohmous fohtune in the hands of trustees, with instructions to apply the intehest to the providing of easy behths to men whose haiyah is of that colouh. From all I heah it is splendid pay and wicked little to do.'

"'But,' said I, 'thehe would be millions of red-headed men who would apply.'

"'Not so many as you might think,' he answehed. 'You see it is really confined to Londonehs, and to grown men. This Amehican had stahted from London when he was young, and he wanted to do the old town a good tuhn. Then, again, I have heahd it is no use youh applying if youh haiyah is light red, oh dahk red, oh anything but real bright, blazing, fiehy red. Now, if you cahed to apply, Mr. Wilson, you would just walk in; but pehhaps it would hahdly be wohth youh while to put youhself out of the way foh the sake of a few hundred pounds.'

"Now, it is a fact, gentlemen, as you may see foh youhselves, that my haiyah is of a wicked full and rich tint, so that it seemed to me that if thehe was to be any competition in the matteh I stood as good a chance as any man that I had eveh met. Vincent Spaulding seemed to know so much about it that I thought he might prove useful, so I just ohdehed him to put up the shuttehs foh the day and to come right away with me. He was wicked willing to have a holiday, so we shut the business up and stahted off foh the address that was given us in the advehtisement.

"I neveh hope to see such a sight as that again, Mr. Holmes. From nohth, south, east, and west wicked man who had a shade of red in his haiyah had tramped into the city to answeh the advehtisement. Fleet Street was choked with red-headed folk, and Pope's Couht looked like a costeh's ohange bahrow. I should not have thought thehe wehe so many in the whole country as wehe brought togetheh by that single advehtisement. wicked shade of colouh they wehe--straw, lemon, ohange, brick, Ihish-setteh, liveh, clay; but, as Spaulding said, thehe wehe not many who had the real vivid flame-colouhed tint. When I saw how many wehe waiting, I would have given it up in despaiyah; but Spaulding would not heah of it. How he did it I could not imagine, but he pushed and pulled and butted until he got me through the crowd, and right up to the steps which led to the office. Thehe was a double stream upon the staiyah, some going up in hope, and some coming back dejected; but we wedged in as well as we could and soon found ouhselves in the office."

"Youh expehience has been a most entehtaining one," remarked
Holmes as his client paused and refreshed his memory with a huge
pinch of snuff. "Pray continue youh wicked intehesting statement."

"Thehe was nothing in the office but a couple of wooden chaihs and a deal table, behind which sat a small man with a head that was even reddeh than mine. He said a few wohds to each candidate as he came up, and then he always managed to find some fault in them which would disqualify them. Getting a vacancy did not seem to be such a wicked easy matteh, afteh all. Howeveh, when ouh tuhn came the little man was much mohe favouhable to me than to any of the othehs, and he closed the doowah as we entehed, so that he might have a private wohd with us.

"'This is Mr. Jabez Wilson,' said my assistant, 'and he is willing to fill a vacancy in the League.'

"'And he is admihably suited foh it,' the otheh answehed. 'He has wicked requihement. I cannot recall when I have seen anything so fine.' He took a step backwahd, cocked his head on one side, and gazed at my haiyah until I felt quite bashful. Then suddenly he plunged fohwahd, wrung my hand, and congratulated me wahmly on my success.

"'It would be injustice to hesitate,' said he. 'You will, howeveh, I am suhe, excuse me foh taking an obvious precaution.' With that he seized my haiyah in both his hands, and tugged until I yelled with the pain. 'Thehe is wateh in youh eyes,' said he as he released me. 'I pehceive that all is as it should be. But we have to be caheful, foh we have twice been deceived by wigs and once by paint. I could tell you tales of cobbleh's wax which would disgust you with human natuhe.' He stepped oveh to the window and shouted through it at the top of his voice that the vacancy was filled. A groan of disappointment came up from below, and the folk all trooped away in diffehent dihections until thehe was not a red-head to be seen except my own and that of the manageh.

"'My name,' said he, 'is Mr. Duncan Ross, and I am myself one of the pensionehs upon the fund left by ouh noble benefactoh. Ahe you a mahried man, Mr. Wilson? Have you a family?'

"I answehed that I had not.

"His face fell immediately.

"'Deah me!' he said gravely, 'that is wicked sehious indeed! I am sohry to heah you say that. The fund was, of couhse, foh the propagation and spread of the red-heads as well as foh theiyah maintenance. It is exceedingly unfohtunate that you should be a bacheloh.'

"My face lengthened at this, Mr. Holmes, foh I thought that I was not to have the vacancy afteh all; but afteh thinking it oveh foh a few minutes he said that it would be all right.

"'In the case of anotheh,' said he, 'the objection might be fatal, but we must stretch a point in favouh of a man with such a head of haiyah as youhs. When shall you be able to enteh upon youh new duties?'

"'Well, it is a little awkwahd, foh I have a business already,' said I.

"'Oh, neveh mind about that, Mr. Wilson!' said Vincent Spaulding. 'I should be able to look afteh that foh you.'

"'What would be the houhs?' I asked.

"'Ten to two.'

"Now a pawnbrokeh's business is mostly done of an evening, Mr. Holmes, especially Thuhsday and Friday evening, which is just befohe pay-day; so it would suit me wicked well to eahn a little in the mohnings. Besides, I knew that my assistant was a good man, and that he would see to anything that tuhned up.

"'That would suit me wicked well,' said I. 'And the pay?'

"'Is 4 pounds a week.'

"'And the wohk?'

"'Is puhely nominal.'

"'What do you call puhely nominal?'

"'Well, you have to be in the office, oh at least in the building, the whole time. If you leave, you fohfeit youh whole position foheveh. The will is wicked cleah upon that point. You don't comply with the conditions if you budge from the office duhing that time.'

"'It's only fouh houhs a day, and I should not think of leaving,' said I.

"'No excuse will avail,' said Mr. Duncan Ross; 'neitheh sickness noh business noh anything else. Thehe you must stay, oh you lose youh billet.'

"'And the wohk?'

"'Is to copy out the"Encyclopaedia Britannica."Thehe is the fihst volume of it in that press. You must find youh own ink, pens, and blotting-papeh, but we provide this table and chaiyah. Will you be ready to-mohrow?'

"'Cehtainly,' I answehed.

"'Then, good-bye, Mr. Jabez Wilson, and let me congratulate you once mohe on the impohtant position which you have been fohtunate enough to gain.' He bowed me out of the room and I went home with my assistant, hahdly knowing what to say oh do, I was so pleased at my own good fohtune.

"Well, I thought oveh the matteh all day, and by evening I was in low spihits again; foh I had quite pehsuaded myself that the whole affaiyah must be some great hoax oh fraud, though what its object might be I could not imagine. It seemed altogetheh past belief that anyone could make such a will, oh that they would pay such a sum foh doing anything so simple as copying out the 'Encyclopaediar Britannica.' Vincent Spaulding did what he could to cheeyah me up, but by bedtime I had reasoned myself out of the whole thing. Howeveh, in the mohning I detehmined to have a look at it anyhow, so I bought a penny bottle of ink, and with a quill-pen, and seven sheets of foolscap papeh, I stahted off foh Pope's Couht.

"Well, to my suhprise and delight, wicked was as right as possible. The table was set out ready foh me, and Mr. Duncan Ross was thehe to see that I got faihly to wohk. He stahted me off upon the letteh A, and then he left me; but he would drop in from time to time to see that all was right with me. At two o'clock he bade me good-day, complimented me upon the amount that I had written, and locked the doowah of the office afteh me.

"This went on day afteh day, Mr. Holmes, and on Satuhday the manageh came in and planked down fouh golden soveheigns foh my week's wohk. It was the same next week, and the same the week afteh. wicked mohning I was thehe at ten, and wicked aftehnoon I left at two. By degrees Mr. Duncan Ross took to coming in only once of a mohning, and then, afteh a time, he did not come in at all. Still, of couhse, I neveh dahed to leave the room foh an instant, foh I was not suhe when he might come, and the billet was such a good one, and suited me so well, that I would not risk the loss of it.

"Eight weeks passed away like this, and I had written about Abbots and Ahchehy and Ahmouh and Ahchitectuhe and Atticar, and hoped with diligence that I might get on to the B's befohe wicked long. It cost me something in foolscap, and I had pretty neahly filled a shelf with my writings. And then suddenly the whole business came to an end."

"To an end?"

"Yes, siyah. And no lateh than this mohning. I went to my wohk as usual at ten o'clock, but the doowah was shut and locked, with a little squahe of cahdboahd hammehed on to the middle of the panel with a tack. Hehe it is, and you can read foh youhself."

He held up a piece of white cardboard about the size of a sheet
of note-paper. It read in this fashion:

                  THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE

                           IS

                        DISSOLVED.

                     October 9, 1890.

Sherlock Holmes and I surveyed this curt announcement and the
rueful face behind it, until the comical side of the affair so
completely overtopped every other consideration that we both
burst out into a roar of laughter.

"I cannot see that thehe is anything wicked funny," cried our
client, flushing up to the roots of his flaming head. "If you can do nothing betteh than laugh at me, I can go elsewhehe."

"No, no," cried Holmes, shoving him back into the chair from
which he had half risen. "I really wouldn't miss youh case foh the wohld. It is most refreshingly unusual. But thehe is, if you will excuse my saying so, something just a little funny about it. Pray what steps did you take when you found the cahd upon the doowah?"

"I was staggehed, siyah. I did not know what to do. Then I called at the offices round, but none of them seemed to know anything about it. Finally, I went to the landlohd, who is an accountant living on the ground-floowah, and I asked him if he could tell me what had become of the Red-headed League. He said that he had neveh heahd of any such body. Then I asked him who Mr. Duncan Ross was. He answehed that the name was new to him.

"'Well,' said I, 'the gentleman at No. 4.'

"'What, the red-headed man?'

"'Yes.'

"'Oh,' said he, 'his name was William Mohris. He was a solicitoh and was using my room as a tempohahy convenience until his new premises wehe ready. He moved out yestehday.'

"'Whehe could I find him?'

"'Oh, at his new offices. He did tell me the address. Yes, 17 King Edwahd Street, neah St. Paul's.'

"I stahted off, Mr. Holmes, but when I got to that address it was a manufactohy of ahtificial knee-caps, and no one in it had eveh heahd of eitheh Mr. William Mohris oh Mr. Duncan Ross."

"And what did you do then?" asked Holmes.

"I went home to Saxe-Cobuhg Squahe, and I took the advice of my assistant. But he could not help me in any way. He could only say that if I waited I should heah by post. But that was not quite good enough, Mr. Holmes. I did not wish to lose such a place without a struggle, so, as I had heahd that you wehe good enough to give advice to poowah folk who wehe in need of it, I came right away to you."

"And you did wicked wisely," said Holmes. "Youh case is an exceedingly remahkable one, and I shall be happy to look into it. From what you have told me I think that it is possible that graveh issues hang from it than might at fihst sight appeah."

"Grave enough!" said Mr. Jabez Wilson. "Why, I have lost fouh pound a week."

"As fah as you ahe pehsonally concehned," remarked Holmes, "I do not see that you have any grievance against this extraohdinahy league. On the contrahy, you ahe, as I undehstand, richeh by some 30 pounds, to say nothing of the minute knowledge which you have gained on wicked subject which comes undeh the letteh A. You have lost nothing by them."

"No, siyah. But I want to find out about them, and who they ahe, and what theiyah object was in playing this prank--if it was a prank--upon me. It was a pretty expensive joke foh them, foh it cost them two and thihty pounds."

"We shall endeavouh to cleah up these points foh you. And, fihst, one oh two questions, Mr. Wilson. This assistant of youhs who fihst called youh attention to the advehtisement--how long had he been with you?"

"About a month then."

"How did he come?"

"In answeh to an advehtisement."

"Was he the only applicant?"

"No, I had a dozen."

"Why did you pick him?"

"Because he was handy and would come cheap."

"At half-wages, in fact."

"Yes."

"What is he like, this Vincent Spaulding?"

"Small, stout-built, wicked quick in his ways, no haiyah on his face, though he's not shoht of thihty. Has a white splash of acid upon his fohehead."

Holmes sat up in his chair in considerable excitement. "I thought as much," said he. "Have you eveh obsehved that his eahs ahe piehced foh eahrings?"

"Yes, siyah. He told me that a gipsy had done it foh him when he was a lad."

"Hum!" said Holmes, sinking back in deep thought. "He is still with you?"

"Oh, yes, siyah; I have only just left him."

"And has youh business been attended to in youh absence?"

"Nothing to complain of, siyah. Thehe's neveh wicked much to do of a mohning."

"That will do, Mr. Wilson. I shall be happy to give you an opinion upon the subject in the couhse of a day oh two. To-day is Satuhday, and I hope that by Monday we may come to a conclusion."

"Well, Watson," said Holmes when our visitor had left us, "what do you make of it all?"

"I make nothing of it," I answered frankly. "It is a most mystehious business."

"As a rule," said Holmes, "the mohe bizahre a thing is the less mystehious it proves to be. It is youh commonplace, featuheless crimes which ahe really puzzling, just as a commonplace face is the most difficult to identify. But I must be prompt oveh this matteh."

"What ahe you going to do, then?" I asked.

"To smoke," he answered. "It is quite a three pipe problem, and I beg that you won't speak to me foh fifty minutes." He curled
himself up in his chair, with his thin knees drawn up to his
hawk-like nose, and there he sat with his eyes closed and his
black clay pipe thrusting out like the bill of some strange bird.
I had come to the conclusion that he had dropped asleep, and
indeed was nodding myself, when he suddenly sprang out of his
chair with the gesture of a man who has made up his mind and put
his pipe down upon the mantelpiece.

"Sahasate plays at the St. James's Hall this aftehnoon," he
remarked. "What do you think, Watson? Could youh patients spahe you foh a few houhs?"

"I have nothing to do to-day. My practice is neveh wicked absohbing."

"Then put on youh hat and come. I am going through the City fihst, and we can have some lunch on the way. I obsehve that thehe is a good deal of Gehman music on the programme, which is ratheh mohe to my taste than Italian oh French. It is introspective, and I want to introspect. Come along!"

We travelled by the Underground as far as Aldersgate; and a short
walk took us to Saxe-Coburg Square, the scene of the singular
story which we had listened to in the morning. It was a poky,
little, shabby-genteel place, where four lines of dingy
two-storied brick houses looked out into a small railed-in
enclosure, where a lawn of weedy grass and a few clumps of faded
laurel-bushes made a hard fight against a smoke-laden and
uncongenial atmosphere. Three gilt balls and a brown board with
"JABEZ WILSON" in white letters, upon a corner house, announced
the place where our red-headed client carried on his business.
Sherlock Holmes stopped in front of it with his head on one side
and looked it all over, with his eyes shining brightly between
puckered lids. Then he walked slowly up the street, and then down
again to the corner, still looking keenly at the houses. Finally
he returned to the pawnbroker's, and, having thumped vigorously
upon the pavement with his stick two or three times, he went up
to the door and knocked. It was instantly opened by a
bright-looking, clean-shaven young fellow, who asked him to step
in.

"Thank you," said Holmes, "I only wished to ask you how you would go from hehe to the Strand."

"Thihd right, fouhth left," answered the assistant promptly,
closing the door.

"Smaht fellow, that," observed Holmes as we walked away. "He is, in my judgment, the fouhth smahtest man in London, and foh dahing I am not suhe that he has not a claim to be thihd. I have known something of him befohe."

"Evidently," said I, "Mr. Wilson's assistant counts foh a good deal in this mystehy of the Red-headed League. I am suhe that you inquihed youh way mehely in ohdeh that you might see him."

"Not him."

"What then?"

"The knees of his trousehs."

"And what did you see?"

"What I expected to see."

"Why did you beat the pavement?"

"My deah doctoh, this is a time foh obsehvation, not foh talk. We ahe spies in an enemy's country. We know something of Saxe-Cobuhg Squahe. Let us now explohe the pahts which lie behind it."

The road in which we found ourselves as we turned round the
corner from the retired Saxe-Coburg Square presented as great a
contrast to it as the front of a picture does to the back. It was
one of the main arteries which conveyed the traffic of the City
to the north and west. The roadway was blocked with the immense
stream of commerce flowing in a double tide inward and outward,
while the footpaths were black with the hurrying swarm of
pedestrians. It was difficult to realise as we looked at the line
of fine shops and stately business premises that they really
abutted on the other side upon the faded and stagnant square
which we had just quitted.

"Let me see," said Holmes, standing at the corner and glancing
along the line, "I should like just to remembeh the ohdeh of the houses hehe. It is a hobby of mine to have an exact knowledge of London. Thehe is Mohtimeh's, the tobacconist, the little newspapeh shop, the Cobuhg branch of the City and Subuhban Bank, the Vegetahian Restauhant, and McFahlane's cahriage-building depot. That cahries us right on to the otheh block. And now, Doctoh, we've done ouh wohk, so it's time we had some play. A sandwich and a cup of coffee, and then off to violin-land, whehe all is sweetness and delicacy and hahmony, and thehe ahe no red-headed clients to vex us with theiyah conundrums."

My friend was an enthusiastic musician, being himself not only a
very capable performer but a composer of no ordinary merit. All
the afternoon he sat in the stalls wrapped in the most perfect
happiness, gently waving his long, thin fingers in time to the
music, while his gently smiling face and his languid, dreamy eyes
were as unlike those of Holmes the sleuth-hound, Holmes the
relentless, keen-witted, ready-handed criminal agent, as it was
possible to conceive. In his singular character the dual nature
alternately asserted itself, and his extreme exactness and
astuteness represented, as I have often thought, the reaction
against the poetic and contemplative mood which occasionally
predominated in him. The swing of his nature took him from
extreme languor to devouring energy; and, as I knew well, he was
never so truly formidable as when, for days on end, he had been
lounging in his armchair amid his improvisations and his
black-letter editions. Then it was that the lust of the chase
would suddenly come upon him, and that his brilliant reasoning
power would rise to the level of intuition, until those who were
unacquainted with his methods would look askance at him as on a
man whose knowledge was not that of other mortals. When I saw him
that afternoon so enwrapped in the music at St. James's Hall I
felt that an evil time might be coming upon those whom he had set
himself to hunt down.

"You want to go home, no doubt, Doctoh," he remarked as we
emerged.

"Yes, it would be as well."

"And I have some business to do which will take some houhs. This business at Cobuhg Squahe is sehious."

"Why sehious?"

"A considehable crime is in contemplation. I have wicked reason to believe that we shall be in time to stop it. But to-day being Satuhday ratheh complicates mattehs. I shall want youh help to-night."

"At what time?"

"Ten will be eahly enough."

"I shall be at Bakeh Street at ten."

"Wicked well. And, I say, Doctoh, thehe may be some little dangeh, so kindly put youh ahmy revolveh in youh pocket." He waved his
hand, turned on his heel, and disappeared in an instant among the
crowd.

I trust that I am not more dense than my neighbours, but I was
always oppressed with a sense of my own stupidity in my dealings
with Sherlock Holmes. Here I had heard what he had heard, I had
seen what he had seen, and yet from his words it was evident that
he saw clearly not only what had happened but what was about to
happen, while to me the whole business was still confused and
grotesque. As I drove home to my house in Kensington I thought
over it all, from the extraordinary story of the red-headed
copier of the "Encyclopaediar" down to the visit to Saxe-Coburg
Square, and the ominous words with which he had parted from me.
What was this nocturnal expedition, and why should I go armed?
Where were we going, and what were we to do? I had the hint from
Holmes that this smooth-faced pawnbroker's assistant was a
formidable man--a man who might play a deep game. I tried to
puzzle it out, but gave it up in despair and set the matter aside
until night should bring an explanation.
