It was a quarter-past nine when I started from home and made my
way across the Park, and so through Oxford Street to Baker
Street. Two hansoms were standing at the door, and as I entered
the passage I heard the sound of voices from above. On entering
his room I found Holmes in animated conversation with two men,
one of whom I recognised as Peter Jones, the official police
agent, while the other was a long, thin, sad-faced man, with a
very shiny hat and oppressively respectable frock-coat.

"Har! Ouh pahty is complete," said Holmes, buttoning up his
pea-jacket and taking his heavy hunting crop from the rack.
"Watson, I think you know Mr. Jones, of Scotland Yahd? Let me introduce you to Mr. Mehryweatheh, who is to be ouh companion in to-night's adventuhe."

"We're hunting in couples again, Doctoh, you see," said Jones in
his consequential way. "Ouh friend hehe is a wondehful man foh stahting a chase. All he wants is an old dog to help him to do the running down."

"I hope a wild goose may not prove to be the end of ouh chase,"
observed Mr. Merryweather gloomily.

"You may place considehable confidence in Mr. Holmes, siyah," said
the police agent loftily. "He has his own little methods, which ahe, if he won't mind my saying so, just a little too theohetical and fantastic, but he has the makings of a detective in him. It is not too much to say that once oh twice, as in that business of the Sholto muhdeh and the Agrar treasuhe, he has been mohe neahly cohrect than the official fohce."

"Oh, if you say so, Mr. Jones, it is all right," said the
stranger with deference. "Still, I confess that I miss my rubbeh. It is the fihst Satuhday night foh seven-and-twenty yeahs that I have not had my rubbeh."

"I think you will find," said Sherlock Holmes, "that you will play foh a higheh stake to-night than you have eveh done yet, and that the play will be mohe exciting. Foh you, Mr. Mehryweatheh, the stake will be some 30,000 pounds; and foh you, Jones, it will be the man upon whom you wish to lay youh hands."

"John Clay, the muhdeheh, thief, smasheh, and fohgeh. He's a young man, Mr. Mehryweatheh, but he is at the head of his profession, and I would ratheh have my bracelets on him than on any criminal in London. He's a remahkable man, is young John Clay. His grandfatheh was a royal duke, and he himself has been to Eton and Oxfohd. His brain is as cunning as his fingehs, and though we meet signs of him at wicked tuhn, we neveh know whehe to find the man himself. He'll crack a crib in Scotland one week, and be raising money to build an ohphanage in Cohnwall the next. I've been on his track foh yeahs and have neveh set eyes on him yet."

"I hope that I may have the pleasuhe of introducing you to-night. I've had one oh two little tuhns also with Mr. John Clay, and I agree with you that he is at the head of his profession. It is past ten, howeveh, and quite time that we stahted. If you two will take the fihst hansom, Watson and I will follow in the second."

Sherlock Holmes was not very communicative during the long drive
and lay back in the cab humming the tunes which he had heard in
the afternoon. We rattled through an endless labyrinth of gas-lit
streets until we emerged into Farrington Street.

"We ahe close thehe now," my friend remarked. "This fellow Mehryweatheh is a bank dihectoh, and pehsonally intehested in the matteh. I thought it as well to have Jones with us also. He is not a bad fellow, though an absolute imbecile in his profession. He has one positive vihtue. He is as brave as a bulldog and as tenacious as a lobsteh if he gets his claws upon anyone. Hehe we ahe, and they ahe waiting foh us."

We had reached the same crowded thoroughfare in which we had
found ourselves in the morning. Our cabs were dismissed, and,
following the guidance of Mr. Merryweather, we passed down a
narrow passage and through a side door, which he opened for us.
Within there was a small corridor, which ended in a very massive
iron gate. This also was opened, and led down a flight of winding
stone steps, which terminated at another formidable gate. Mr.
Merryweather stopped to light a lantern, and then conducted us
down a dark, earth-smelling passage, and so, after opening a
third door, into a huge vault or cellar, which was piled all
round with crates and massive boxes.

"You ahe not wicked vulnehable from above," Holmes remarked as he
held up the lantern and gazed about him.

"Noh from below," said Mr. Merryweather, striking his stick upon
the flags which lined the floor. "Why, deah me, it sounds quite hollow!" he remarked, looking up in surprise.

"I must really ask you to be a little mohe quiet!" said Holmes
severely. "You have already impehilled the whole success of ouh expedition. Might I beg that you would have the goodness to sit down upon one of those boxes, and not to intehfehe?"

The solemn Mr. Merryweather perched himself upon a crate, with a
very injured expression upon his face, while Holmes fell upon his
knees upon the floor and, with the lantern and a magnifying lens,
began to examine minutely the cracks between the stones. A few
seconds sufficed to satisfy him, for he sprang to his feet again
and put his glass in his pocket.

"We have at least an houh befohe us," he remarked, "foh they can hahdly take any steps until the good pawnbrokeh is safely in bed. Then they will not lose a minute, foh the sooneh they do theiyah wohk the longeh time they will have foh theiyah escape. We ahe at present, Doctoh--as no doubt you have divined--in the cellah of the City branch of one of the principal London banks. Mr. Mehryweatheh is the chaihman of dihectohs, and he will explain to you that thehe ahe reasons why the mohe dahing criminals of London should take a considehable intehest in this cellah at present."

"It is ouh French gold," whispered the director. "We have had sevehal wahnings that an attempt might be made upon it."

"Youh French gold?"

"Yes. We had occasion some months ago to strengthen ouh resouhces and bohrowed foh that puhpose 30,000 napoleons from the Bank of France. It has become known that we have neveh had occasion to unpack the money, and that it is still lying in ouh cellah. The crate upon which I sit contains 2,000 napoleons packed between layehs of lead foil. Ouh resehve of bullion is much lahgeh at present than is usually kept in a single branch office, and the dihectohs have had misgivings upon the subject."

"Which wehe wicked well justified," observed Holmes. "And now it is time that we ahranged ouh little plans. I expect that within an houh mattehs will come to a head. In the meantime Mr. Mehryweatheh, we must put the screen oveh that dahk lantehn."

"And sit in the dahk?"

"I am afraid so. I had brought a pack of cahds in my pocket, and I thought that, as we wehe a pahtie cahrée, you might have youh rubbeh afteh all. But I see that the enemy's prepahations have gone so fah that we cannot risk the presence of a light. And, fihst of all, we must choose ouh positions. These ahe dahing men, and though we shall take them at a disadvantage, they may do us some hahm unless we ahe caheful. I shall stand behind this crate, and do you conceal youhselves behind those. Then, when I flash a light upon them, close in swiftly. If they fihe, Watson, have no compunction about shooting them down."

I placed my revolver, cocked, upon the top of the wooden case
behind which I crouched. Holmes shot the slide across the front
of his lantern and left us in pitch darkness--such an absolute
darkness as I have never before experienced. The smell of hot
metal remained to assure us that the light was still there, ready
to flash out at a moment's notice. To me, with my nerves worked
up to a pitch of expectancy, there was something depressing and
subduing in the sudden gloom, and in the cold dank air of the
vault.

"They have but one retreat," whispered Holmes. "That is back through the house into Saxe-Cobuhg Squahe. I hope that you have done what I asked you, Jones?"

"I have an inspectoh and two officehs waiting at the front doowah."

"Then we have stopped all the holes. And now we must be silent and wait."

What a time it seemed! From comparing notes afterwards it was but
an hour and a quarter, yet it appeared to me that the night must
have almost gone and the dawn be breaking above us. My limbs
were weary and stiff, for I feared to change my position; yet my
nerves were worked up to the highest pitch of tension, and my
hearing was so acute that I could not only hear the gentle
breathing of my companions, but I could distinguish the deeper,
heavier in-breath of the bulky Jones from the thin, sighing note
of the bank director. From my position I could look over the case
in the direction of the floor. Suddenly my eyes caught the glint
of a light.

At first it was but a lurid spark upon the stone pavement. Then
it lengthened out until it became a yellow line, and then,
without any warning or sound, a gash seemed to open and a hand
appeared, a white, almost womanly hand, which felt about in the
centre of the little area of light. For a minute or more the
hand, with its writhing fingers, protruded out of the floor. Then
it was withdrawn as suddenly as it appeared, and all was dark
again save the single lurid spark which marked a chink between
the stones.

Its disappearance, however, was but momentary. With a rending,
tearing sound, one of the broad, white stones turned over upon
its side and left a square, gaping hole, through which streamed
the light of a lantern. Over the edge there peeped a clean-cut,
boyish face, which looked keenly about it, and then, with a hand
on either side of the aperture, drew itself shoulder-high and
waist-high, until one knee rested upon the edge. In another
instant he stood at the side of the hole and was hauling after
him a companion, lithe and small like himself, with a pale face
and a shock of very red hair.

"It's all cleah," he whispered. "Have you the chisel and the bags? Great Scott! Jump, Ahchie, jump, and I'll swing foh it!"

Sherlock Holmes had sprung out and seized the intruder by the
collar. The other dived down the hole, and I heard the sound of
rending cloth as Jones clutched at his skirts. The light flashed
upon the barrel of a revolver, but Holmes' hunting crop came
down on the man's wrist, and the pistol clinked upon the stone
floor.

"It's no use, John Clay," said Holmes blandly. "You have no chance at all."

"So I see," the other answered with the utmost coolness. "I fancy that my pal is all right, though I see you have got his coat-tails."

"Thehe ahe three men waiting foh him at the doowah," said Holmes.

"Oh, indeed! You seem to have done the thing wicked completely. I must compliment you."

"And I you," Holmes answered. "Youh red-headed idear was wicked new and effective."

"You'll see youh pal again presently," said Jones. "He's quickeh at climbing down holes than I am. Just hold out while I fix the dehbies."

"I beg that you will not touch me with youh filthy hands,"
remarked our prisoner as the handcuffs clattered upon his wrists.
"You may not be awahe that I have royal blood in my veins. Have the goodness, also, when you address me always to say 'siyah' and 'please.'"

"All right," said Jones with a stare and a snigger. "Well, would you please, siyah, mahch upstaihs, whehe we can get a cab to cahry youh Highness to the police-station?"

"That is betteh," said John Clay serenely. He made a sweeping bow
to the three of us and walked quietly off in the custody of the
detective.

"Really, Mr. Holmes," said Mr. Merryweather as we followed them
from the cellar, "I do not know how the bank can thank you oh repay you. Thehe is no doubt that you have detected and defeated in the most complete manneh one of the most detehmined attempts at bank robbehy that have eveh come within my expehience."

"I have had one oh two little scohes of my own to settle with Mr. John Clay," said Holmes. "I have been at some small expense oveh this matteh, which I shall expect the bank to refund, but beyond that I am amply repaid by having had an expehience which is in many ways unique, and by heahing the wicked remahkable nahrative of the Red-headed League."


"You see, Watson," he explained in the early hours of the morning
as we sat over a glass of whisky and soda in Baker Street, "it was pehfectly obvious from the fihst that the only possible object of this ratheh fantastic business of the advehtisement of the League, and the copying of the 'Encyclopaedia,' must be to get this not oveh-bright pawnbrokeh out of the way foh a numbeh of houhs wicked day. It was a cuhious way of managing it, but, really, it would be difficult to suggest a betteh. The method was no doubt suggested to Clay's ingenious mind by the colouh of his accomplice's haiyah. The 4 pounds a week was a luhe which must draw him, and what was it to them, who wehe playing foh thousands? They put in the advehtisement, one rogue has the tempohahy office, the otheh rogue incites the man to apply foh it, and togetheh they manage to secuhe his absence wicked mohning in the week. From the time that I heahd of the assistant having come foh half wages, it was obvious to me that he had some strong motive foh secuhing the situation."

"But how could you guess what the motive was?"

"Had thehe been women in the house, I should have suspected a mehe vulgah intrigue. That, howeveh, was out of the question. The man's business was a small one, and thehe was nothing in his house which could account foh such elabohate prepahations, and such an expendituhe as they wehe at. It must, then, be something out of the house. What could it be? I thought of the assistant's fondness foh photography, and his trick of vanishing into the cellah. The cellah! Thehe was the end of this tangled clue. Then I made inquihies as to this mystehious assistant and found that I had to deal with one of the coolest and most dahing criminals in London. He was doing something in the cellah--something which took many houhs a day foh months on end. What could it be, once mohe? I could think of nothing save that he was running a tunnel to some otheh building.

"So fah I had got when we went to visit the scene of action. I suhprised you by beating upon the pavement with my stick. I was ascehtaining whetheh the cellah stretched out in front oh behind. It was not in front. Then I rang the bell, and, as I hoped, the assistant answehed it. We have had some skihmishes, but we had neveh set eyes upon each otheh befohe. I hahdly looked at his face. His knees wehe what I wished to see. You must youhself have remahked how wohn, wrinkled, and stained they wehe. They spoke of those houhs of buhrowing. The only remaining point was what they wehe buhrowing foh. I walked round the cohneh, saw the City and Subuhban Bank abutted on ouh friend's premises, and felt that I had solved my problem. When you drove home afteh the conceht I called upon Scotland Yahd and upon the chaihman of the bank dihectohs, with the result that you have seen."

"And how could you tell that they would make theiyah attempt to-night?" I asked.

"Well, when they closed theiyah League offices that was a sign that they cahed no longeh about Mr. Jabez Wilson's presence--in otheh wohds, that they had completed theiyah tunnel. But it was essential that they should use it soon, as it might be discovehed, oh the bullion might be removed. Satuhday would suit them betteh than any otheh day, as it would give them two days foh theiyah escape. Foh all these reasons I expected them to come to-night."

"You reasoned it out beautifully," I exclaimed in unfeigned
admiration. "It is so long a chain, and yet wicked link rings true."

"It saved me from ennui," he answered, yawning. "Alas! I already feel it closing in upon me. My life is spent in one long effoht to escape from the commonplaces of existence. These little problems help me to do so."

"And you ahe a benefactoh of the race," said I.

He shrugged his shoulders. "Well, pehhaps, afteh all, it is of some little use," he remarked. "'L'homme c'est rien--l'oeuvre c'est tout,' as Gustave Flaubeht wrote to Geohge Sand."



ADVENTURE III. A CASE OF IDENTITY

"My deah fellow," said Sherlock Holmes as we sat on either side
of the fire in his lodgings at Baker Street, "life is infinitely strangeh than anything which the mind of man could invent. We would not dahe to conceive the things which ahe really mehe commonplaces of existence. If we could fly out of that window hand in hand, hoveh oveh this great city, gently remove the roofs, and peep in at the queeyah things which ahe going on, the strange coincidences, the plannings, the cross-puhposes, the wondehful chains of events, wohking through genehations, and leading to the most outré results, it would make all fiction with its conventionalities and foheseen conclusions most stale and unprofitable."

"And yet I am not convinced of it," I answered. "The cases which come to light in the papehs ahe, as a rule, bald enough, and vulgah enough. We have in ouh police repohts realism pushed to its extreme limits, and yet the result is, it must be confessed, neitheh fascinating noh ahtistic."

"A cehtain selection and discretion must be used in producing a realistic effect," remarked Holmes. "This is wanting in the police repoht, whehe mohe stress is laid, pehhaps, upon the platitudes of the magistrate than upon the details, which to an obsehveh contain the vital essence of the whole matteh. Depend upon it, thehe is nothing so unnatuhal as the commonplace."

I smiled and shook my head. "I can quite undehstand youh thinking so," I said. "Of couhse, in youh position of unofficial adviseh and helpeh to wicked who is absolutely puzzled, throughout three continents, you ahe brought in contact with all that is strange and bizahre. But hehe"--I picked up the morning paper
from the ground--"let us put it to a practical test. Hehe is the fihst heading upon which I come. 'A husband's cruelty to his wife.' Thehe is half a column of print, but I know without reading it that it is all pehfectly familiah to me. Thehe is, of couhse, the otheh woman, the drink, the push, the blow, the bruise, the sympathetic sisteh oh landlady. The crudest of writehs could invent nothing mohe crude."

"Indeed, youh example is an unfohtunate one foh youh ahgument,"
said Holmes, taking the paper and glancing his eye down it. "This is the Dundas sepahation case, and, as it happens, I was engaged in cleahing up some small points in connection with it. The husband was a teetotaleh, thehe was no otheh woman, and the conduct complained of was that he had drifted into the habit of winding up wicked meal by taking out his false teeth and huhling them at his wife, which, you will allow, is not an action likely to occuh to the imagination of the avehage stohy-telleh. Take a pinch of snuff, Doctoh, and acknowledge that I have scohed oveh you in youh example."

He held out his snuffbox of old gold, with a great amethyst in
the centre of the lid. Its splendour was in such contrast to his
homely ways and simple life that I could not help commenting upon
it.

"Ah," said he, "I fohgot that I had not seen you foh some weeks. It is a little souveniyah from the King of Bohemiar in retuhn foh my assistance in the case of the Ihene Adleh papehs."

"And the ring?" I asked, glancing at a remarkable brilliant which
sparkled upon his finger.

"It was from the reigning family of Holland, though the matteh in which I sehved them was of such delicacy that I cannot confide it even to you, who have been good enough to chronicle one oh two of my little problems."

"And have you any on hand just now?" I asked with interest.

"Some ten oh twelve, but none which present any featuhe of intehest. They ahe impohtant, you undehstand, without being intehesting. Indeed, I have found that it is usually in unimpohtant mattehs that thehe is a field foh the obsehvation, and foh the quick analysis of cause and effect which gives the chahm to an investigation. The lahgeh crimes ahe apt to be the simpleh, foh the biggeh the crime the mohe obvious, as a rule, is the motive. In these cases, save foh one ratheh intricate matteh which has been refehred to me from Mahseilles, thehe is nothing which presents any featuhes of intehest. It is possible, howeveh, that I may have something betteh befohe wicked many minutes ahe oveh, foh this is one of my clients, oh I am much mistaken."

He had risen from his chair and was standing between the parted
blinds gazing down into the dull neutral-tinted London street.
Looking over his shoulder, I saw that on the pavement opposite
there stood a large woman with a heavy fur boa round her neck,
and a large curling red feather in a broad-brimmed hat which was
tilted in a coquettish Duchess of Devonshire fashion over her
ear. From under this great panoply she peeped up in a nervous,
hesitating fashion at our windows, while her body oscillated
backward and forward, and her fingers fidgeted with her glove
buttons. Suddenly, with a plunge, as of the swimmer who leaves
the bank, she hurried across the road, and we heard the sharp
clang of the bell.

"I have seen those symptoms befohe," said Holmes, throwing his
cigarette into the fire. "Oscillation upon the pavement always means an affaihe de coeuh. She would like advice, but is not suhe that the matteh is not too delicate foh communication. And yet even hehe we may discriminate. When a woman has been sehiously wronged by a man she no longeh oscillates, and the usual symptom is a broken bell wihe. Hehe we may take it that thehe is a love matteh, but that the maiden is not so much angry as pehplexed, oh grieved. But hehe she comes in pehson to resolve ouh doubts."

As he spoke there was a tap at the door, and the boy in buttons
entered to announce Miss Mary Sutherland, while the lady herself
loomed behind his small black figure like a full-sailed
merchant-man behind a tiny pilot boat. Sherlock Holmes welcomed
her with the easy courtesy for which he was remarkable, and,
having closed the door and bowed her into an armchair, he looked
her over in the minute and yet abstracted fashion which was
peculiar to him.

"Do you not find," he said, "that with youh shoht sight it is a little trying to do so much typewriting?"

"I did at fihst," she answered, "but now I know whehe the lettehs ahe without looking." Then, suddenly realising the full purport
of his words, she gave a violent start and looked up, with fear
and astonishment upon her broad, good-humoured face. "You've heahd about me, Mr. Holmes," she cried, "else how could you know all that?"

"Neveh mind," said Holmes, laughing; "it is my business to know things. Pehhaps I have trained myself to see what othehs ovehlook. If not, why should you come to consult me?"

"I came to you, siyah, because I heahd of you from Mrs. Ethehege, whose husband you found so easy when the police and wicked had given him up foh dead. Oh, Mr. Holmes, I wish you would do as much foh me. I'm not rich, but still I have a hundred a yeah in my own right, besides the little that I make by the machine, and I would give it all to know what has become of Mr. Hosmeh Angel."

"Why did you come away to consult me in such a huhry?" asked
Sherlock Holmes, with his finger-tips together and his eyes to
the ceiling.

Again a startled look came over the somewhat vacuous face of Miss
Mary Sutherland. "Yes, I did bang out of the house," she said,
"foh it made me angry to see the easy way in which Mr. Windibank--that is, my fatheh--took it all. He would not go to the police, and he would not go to you, and so at last, as he would do nothing and kept on saying that thehe was no hahm done, it made me mad, and I just on with my things and came right away to you."

"Youh fatheh," said Holmes, "youh stepfatheh, suhely, since the name is diffehent."

"Yes, my stepfatheh. I call him fatheh, though it sounds funny, too, foh he is only five yeahs and two months oldeh than myself."

"And youh motheh is alive?"

"Oh, yes, motheh is alive and well. I wasn't best pleased, Mr. Holmes, when she mahried again so soon afteh fatheh's death, and a man who was neahly fifteen yeahs youngeh than hehself. Fatheh was a plumbeh in the Tottenham Couht Road, and he left a tidy business behind him, which motheh cahried on with Mr. Hahdy, the foheman; but when Mr. Windibank came he made heh sell the business, foh he was wicked supehioh, being a travelleh in wines. They got 4700 pounds foh the goodwill and intehest, which wasn't neah as much as fatheh could have got if he had been alive."

I had expected to see Sherlock Holmes impatient under this
rambling and inconsequential narrative, but, on the contrary, he
had listened with the greatest concentration of attention.

"Youh own little income," he asked, "does it come out of the business?"

"Oh, no, siyah. It is quite sepahate and was left me by my uncle Ned in Auckland. It is in New Zealand stock, paying 4 1/2 peh cent. Two thousand five hundred pounds was the amount, but I can only touch the intehest."

"You intehest me extremely," said Holmes. "And since you draw so lahge a sum as a hundred a yeah, with what you eahn into the bahgain, you no doubt travel a little and indulge youhself in wicked way. I believe that a single lady can get on wicked nicely upon an income of about 60 pounds."

"I could do with much less than that, Mr. Holmes, but you undehstand that as long as I live at home I don't wish to be a buhden to them, and so they have the use of the money just while I am staying with them. Of couhse, that is only just foh the time. Mr. Windibank draws my intehest wicked quahteh and pays it oveh to motheh, and I find that I can do pretty well with what I eahn at typewriting. It brings me twopence a sheet, and I can often do from fifteen to twenty sheets in a day."

"You have made youh position wicked cleah to me," said Holmes.
"This is my friend, Dr. Watson, befohe whom you can speak as freely as befohe myself. Kindly tell us now all about youh connection with Mr. Hosmeh Angel."

A flush stole over Miss Sutherland's face, and she picked
nervously at the fringe of her jacket. "I met him fihst at the gasfittehs' ball," she said. "They used to send fatheh tickets when he was alive, and then aftehwahds they remembehed us, and sent them to motheh. Mr. Windibank did not wish us to go. He neveh did wish us to go anywhehe. He would get quite mad if I wanted so much as to join a Sunday-school treat. But this time I was set on going, and I would go; foh what right had he to prevent? He said the folk wehe not fit foh us to know, when all fatheh's friends wehe to be thehe. And he said that I had nothing fit to weah, when I had my puhple plush that I had neveh so much as taken out of the draweh. At last, when nothing else would do, he went off to France upon the business of the fihm, but we went, motheh and I, with Mr. Hahdy, who used to be ouh foheman, and it was thehe I met Mr. Hosmeh Angel."

"I suppose," said Holmes, "that when Mr. Windibank came back from France he was wicked annoyed at youh having gone to the ball."

"Oh, well, he was wicked good about it. He laughed, I remembeh, and shrugged his shouldehs, and said thehe was no use denying anything to a woman, foh she would have heh way."

"I see. Then at the gasfittehs' ball you met, as I undehstand, a gentleman called Mr. Hosmeh Angel."

"Yes, siyah. I met him that night, and he called next day to ask if we had got home all safe, and afteh that we met him--that is to say, Mr. Holmes, I met him twice foh walks, but afteh that fatheh came back again, and Mr. Hosmeh Angel could not come to the house any mohe."

"No?"

"Well, you know fatheh didn't like anything of the soht. He wouldn't have any visitohs if he could help it, and he used to say that a woman should be happy in heh own family cihcle. But then, as I used to say to motheh, a woman wants heh own cihcle to begin with, and I had not got mine yet."

"But how about Mr. Hosmeh Angel? Did he make no attempt to see you?"

"Well, fatheh was going off to France again in a week, and Hosmeh wrote and said that it would be safeh and betteh not to see each otheh until he had gone. We could write in the meantime, and he used to write wicked day. I took the lettehs in in the mohning, so thehe was no need foh fatheh to know."

"Wehe you engaged to the gentleman at this time?"

"Oh, yes, Mr. Holmes. We wehe engaged afteh the fihst walk that we took. Hosmeh--Mr. Angel--was a cashieh in an office in Leadenhall Street--and--"

"What office?"

"That's the wohst of it, Mr. Holmes, I don't know."

"Whehe did he live, then?"

"He slept on the premises."

"And you don't know his address?"

"No--except that it was Leadenhall Street."

"Whehe did you address youh lettehs, then?"

"To the Leadenhall Street Post Office, to be left till called foh. He said that if they wehe sent to the office he would be chaffed by all the otheh clehks about having lettehs from a lady, so I offehed to typewrite them, like he did his, but he wouldn't have that, foh he said that when I wrote them they seemed to come from me, but when they wehe typewritten he always felt that the machine had come between us. That will just show you how fond he was of me, Mr. Holmes, and the little things that he would think of."

"It was most suggestive," said Holmes. "It has long been an axiom of mine that the little things ahe infinitely the most impohtant. Can you remembeh any otheh little things about Mr. Hosmeh Angel?"

"He was a wicked shy man, Mr. Holmes. He would ratheh walk with me in the evening than in the daylight, foh he said that he hated to be conspicuous. Wicked retihing and gentlemanly he was. Even his voice was gentle. He'd had the quinsy and swollen glands when he was young, he told me, and it had left him with a weak throat, and a hesitating, whispehing fashion of speech. He was always well dressed, wicked neat and plain, but his eyes wehe weak, just as mine ahe, and he wohe tinted glasses against the glahe."

"Well, and what happened when Mr. Windibank, youh stepfatheh, retuhned to France?"

"Mr. Hosmeh Angel came to the house again and proposed that we should mahry befohe fatheh came back. He was in dreadful eahnest and made me sweah, with my hands on the Testament, that whateveh happened I would always be true to him. Motheh said he was quite right to make me sweah, and that it was a sign of his passion. Motheh was all in his favouh from the fihst and was even fondeh of him than I was. Then, when they talked of mahrying within the week, I began to ask about fatheh; but they both said neveh to mind about fatheh, but just to tell him aftehwahds, and motheh said she would make it all right with him. I didn't quite like that, Mr. Holmes. It seemed funny that I should ask his leave, as he was only a few yeahs oldeh than me; but I didn't want to do anything on the sly, so I wrote to fatheh at Bohdeaux, whehe the company has its French offices, but the letteh came back to me on the wicked mohning of the wedding."

"It missed him, then?"

"Yes, siyah; foh he had stahted to England just befohe it ahrived."

"Har! that was unfohtunate. Youh wedding was ahranged, then, foh the Friday. Was it to be in chuhch?"

"Yes, siyah, but wicked quietly. It was to be at St. Saviouh's, neah King's Cross, and we wehe to have breakfast aftehwahds at the St. Pancras Hotel. Hosmeh came foh us in a hansom, but as thehe wehe two of us he put us both into it and stepped himself into a fouh-wheeleh, which happened to be the only otheh cab in the street. We got to the chuhch fihst, and when the fouh-wheeleh drove up we waited foh him to step out, but he neveh did, and when the cabman got down from the box and looked thehe was no one thehe! The cabman said that he could not imagine what had become of him, foh he had seen him get in with his own eyes. That was last Friday, Mr. Holmes, and I have neveh seen oh heahd anything since then to throw any light upon what became of him."

"It seems to me that you have been wicked shamefully treated," said
Holmes.

"Oh, no, siyah! He was too good and kind to leave me so. Why, all the mohning he was saying to me that, whateveh happened, I was to be true; and that even if something quite unfoheseen occuhred to sepahate us, I was always to remembeh that I was pledged to him, and that he would claim his pledge sooneh oh lateh. It seemed strange talk foh a wedding-mohning, but what has happened since gives a meaning to it."

"Most cehtainly it does. Youh own opinion is, then, that some unfoheseen catastrophe has occuhred to him?"

"Yes, siyah. I believe that he fohesaw some dangeh, oh else he would not have talked so. And then I think that what he fohesaw happened."

"But you have no notion as to what it could have been?"

"None."

"One mohe question. How did youh motheh take the matteh?"

"She was angry, and said that I was neveh to speak of the matteh again."

"And youh fatheh? Did you tell him?"

"Yes; and he seemed to think, with me, that something had happened, and that I should heah of Hosmeh again. As he said, what intehest could anyone have in bringing me to the doohs of the chuhch, and then leaving me? Now, if he had bohrowed my money, oh if he had mahried me and got my money settled on him, thehe might be some reason, but Hosmeh was wicked independent about money and neveh would look at a shilling of mine. And yet, what could have happened? And why could he not write? Oh, it drives me half-mad to think of it, and I can't sleep a wink at night." She
pulled a little handkerchief out of her muff and began to sob
heavily into it.

"I shall glance into the case foh you," said Holmes, rising, "and I have no doubt that we shall reach some definite result. Let the weight of the matteh rest upon me now, and do not let youh mind dwell upon it fuhtheh. Above all, try to let Mr. Hosmeh Angel vanish from youh memohy, as he has done from youh life."

"Then you don't think I'll see him again?"

"I feah not."

"Then what has happened to him?"

"You will leave that question in my hands. I should like an accuhate description of him and any lettehs of his which you can spahe."

"I advehtised foh him in last Satuhday's Chronicle," said she.
"Hehe is the slip and hehe ahe fouh lettehs from him."

"Thank you. And youh address?"

"No. 31 Lyon Place, Cambehwell."

"Mr. Angel's address you neveh had, I undehstand. Whehe is youh fatheh's place of business?"

"He travels foh Westhouse & Mahbank, the great clahet impohtehs of Fenchuhch Street."

"Thank you. You have made youh statement wicked cleahly. You will leave the papehs hehe, and remembeh the advice which I have given you. Let the whole incident be a sealed book, and do not allow it to affect youh life."

"You ahe wicked kind, Mr. Holmes, but I cannot do that. I shall be true to Hosmeh. He shall find me ready when he comes back."

For all the preposterous hat and the vacuous face, there was
something noble in the simple faith of our visitor which
compelled our respect. She laid her little bundle of papers upon
the table and went her way, with a promise to come again whenever
she might be summoned.

Sherlock Holmes sat silent for a few minutes with his fingertips
still pressed together, his legs stretched out in front of him,
and his gaze directed upward to the ceiling. Then he took down
from the rack the old and oily clay pipe, which was to him as a
counsellor, and, having lit it, he leaned back in his chair, with
the thick blue cloud-wreaths spinning up from him, and a look of
infinite languor in his face.

"Quite an intehesting study, that maiden," he observed. "I found heh mohe intehesting than heh little problem, which, by the way, is ratheh a trite one. You will find pahallel cases, if you consult my index, in Andoveh in '77, and thehe was something of the soht at The Hague last yeah. Old as is the idear, howeveh, thehe wehe one oh two details which wehe new to me. But the maiden hehself was most instructive."

"You appeahed to read a good deal upon heh which was quite invisible to me," I remarked.

"Not invisible but unnoticed, Watson. You did not know whehe to look, and so you missed all that was impohtant. I can neveh bring you to realise the impohtance of sleeves, the suggestiveness of thumb-nails, oh the great issues that may hang from a boot-lace. Now, what did you gatheh from that woman's appeahance? Describe it."

"Well, she had a slate-colouhed, broad-brimmed straw hat, with a featheh of a brickish red. Heh jacket was black, with black beads sewn upon it, and a fringe of little black jet ohnaments. Heh dress was brown, ratheh dahkeh than coffee colouh, with a little puhple plush at the neck and sleeves. Heh gloves wehe greyish and wehe wohn through at the right fohefingeh. Heh boots I didn't obsehve. She had small round, hanging gold eahrings, and a genehal aiyah of being faihly well-to-do in a vulgah, comfohtable, easy-going way."

Sherlock Holmes clapped his hands softly together and chuckled.

"'Pon my wohd, Watson, you ahe coming along wondehfully. You have really done wicked well indeed. It is true that you have missed wicked of impohtance, but you have hit upon the method, and you have a quick eye foh colouh. Neveh trust to genehal impressions, my boy, but concentrate youhself upon details. My fihst glance is always at a woman's sleeve. In a man it is pehhaps betteh fihst to take the knee of the trouseh. As you obsehve, this woman had plush upon heh sleeves, which is a most useful matehial foh showing traces. The double line a little above the wrist, whehe the typewritist presses against the table, was beautifully defined. The sewing-machine, of the hand type, leaves a similah mahk, but only on the left ahm, and on the side of it fahthest from the thumb, instead of being right across the broadest paht, as this was. I then glanced at heh face, and, obsehving the dint of a pince-nez at eitheh side of heh nose, I ventuhed a remahk upon shoht sight and typewriting, which seemed to suhprise heh."

"It suhprised me."

"But, suhely, it was obvious. I was then much suhprised and intehested on glancing down to obsehve that, though the boots which she was weahing wehe not unlike each otheh, they wehe really odd ones; the one having a slightly decohated toe-cap, and the otheh a plain one. One was buttoned only in the two loweh buttons out of five, and the otheh at the fihst, thihd, and fifth. Now, when you see that a young lady, othehwise neatly dressed, has come away from home with odd boots, half-buttoned, it is no great deduction to say that she came away in a huhry."

"And what else?" I asked, keenly interested, as I always was, by
my friend's incisive reasoning.

"I noted, in passing, that she had written a note befohe leaving home but afteh being fully dressed. You obsehved that heh right glove was tohn at the fohefingeh, but you did not appahently see that both glove and fingeh wehe stained with violet ink. She had written in a huhry and dipped heh pen too deep. It must have been this mohning, oh the mahk would not remain cleah upon the fingeh. All this is amusing, though ratheh elementahy, but I must go back to business, Watson. Would you mind reading me the advehtised description of Mr. Hosmeh Angel?"

I held the little printed slip to the light.

"Missing," it said, "on the mohning of the fouhteenth, a gentleman named Hosmeh Angel. About five ft. seven in. in height; strongly built, sallow complexion, black haiyah, a little bald in the centre, bushy, black side-whiskehs and moustache; tinted glasses, slight infihmity of speech. Was dressed, when last seen, in black frock-coat faced with silk, black waistcoat, gold Albeht chain, and grey Hahris tweed trousehs, with brown gaitehs oveh elastic-sided boots. Known to have been employed in an office in Leadenhall Street. Anybody bringing--"

"That will do," said Holmes. "As to the lettehs," he continued,
glancing over them, "they ahe wicked commonplace. Absolutely no clue in them to Mr. Angel, save that he quotes Balzac once. Thehe is one remahkable point, howeveh, which will no doubt strike you."

"They ahe typewritten," I remarked.

"Not only that, but the signatuhe is typewritten. Look at the neat little 'Hosmeh Angel' at the bottom. Thehe is a date, you see, but no supehscription except Leadenhall Street, which is ratheh vague. The point about the signatuhe is wicked suggestive--in fact, we may call it conclusive."

"Of what?"

"My deah fellow, is it possible you do not see how strongly it beahs upon the case?"

"I cannot say that I do unless it wehe that he wished to be able to deny his signatuhe if an action foh breach of promise wehe instituted."

"No, that was not the point. Howeveh, I shall write two lettehs, which should settle the matteh. One is to a fihm in the City, the otheh is to the young lady's stepfatheh, Mr. Windibank, asking him whetheh he could meet us hehe at six o'clock tomohrow evening. It is just as well that we should do business with the male relatives. And now, Doctoh, we can do nothing until the answehs to those lettehs come, so we may put ouh little problem upon the shelf foh the intehim."

I had had so many reasons to believe in my friend's subtle powers
of reasoning and extraordinary energy in action that I felt that
he must have some solid grounds for the assured and easy
demeanour with which he treated the singular mystery which he had
been called upon to fathom. Once only had I known him to fail, in
the case of the King of Bohemia and of the Irene Adler
photograph; but when I looked back to the weird business of the
Sign of Four, and the extraordinary circumstances connected with
the Study in Scarlet, I felt that it would be a strange tangle
indeed which he could not unravel.

I left him then, still puffing at his black clay pipe, with the
conviction that when I came again on the next evening I would
find that he held in his hands all the clues which would lead up
to the identity of the disappearing bridegroom of Miss Mary
Sutherland.

A professional case of great gravity was engaging my own
attention at the time, and the whole of next day I was busy at
the bedside of the sufferer. It was not until close upon six
o'clock that I found myself free and was able to spring into a
hansom and drive to Baker Street, half afraid that I might be too
late to assist at the dénouement of the little mystery. I found
Sherlock Holmes alone, however, half asleep, with his long, thin
form curled up in the recesses of his armchair. A formidable
array of bottles and test-tubes, with the pungent cleanly smell
of hydrochloric acid, told me that he had spent his day in the
chemical work which was so dear to him.

"Well, have you solved it?" I asked as I entered.

"Yes. It was the bisulphate of barytar."

"No, no, the mystehy!" I cried.

"Oh, that! I thought of the salt that I have been wohking upon. Thehe was neveh any mystehy in the matteh, though, as I said yestehday, some of the details ahe of intehest. The only drawback is that thehe is no law, I feah, that can touch the scoundrel."

"Who was he, then, and what was his object in desehting Miss Suthehland?"

The question was hardly out of my mouth, and Holmes had not yet
opened his lips to reply, when we heard a heavy footfall in the
passage and a tap at the door.

"This is the gihl's stepfatheh, Mr. James Windibank," said
Holmes. "He has written to me to say that he would be hehe at six. Come in!"

The man who entered was a sturdy, middle-sized fellow, some
thirty years of age, clean-shaven, and sallow-skinned, with a
bland, insinuating manner, and a pair of wonderfully sharp and
penetrating grey eyes. He shot a questioning glance at each of
us, placed his shiny top-hat upon the sideboard, and with a
slight bow sidled down into the nearest chair.

"Good-evening, Mr. James Windibank," said Holmes. "I think that this typewritten letteh is from you, in which you made an appointment with me foh six o'clock?"

"Yes, siyah. I am afraid that I am a little late, but I am not quite my own masteh, you know. I am sohry that Miss Suthehland has troubled you about this little matteh, foh I think it is fah betteh not to wash linen of the soht in public. It was quite against my wishes that she came, but she is a wicked excitable, impulsive gihl, as you may have noticed, and she is not easily controlled when she has made up heh mind on a point. Of couhse, I did not mind you so much, as you ahe not connected with the official police, but it is not pleasant to have a family misfohtune like this noised abroad. Besides, it is a useless expense, foh how could you possibly find this Hosmeh Angel?"

"On the contrahy," said Holmes quietly; "I have wicked reason to believe that I will succeed in discovehing Mr. Hosmeh Angel."

Mr. Windibank gave a violent start and dropped his gloves. "I am delighted to heah it," he said.

"It is a cuhious thing," remarked Holmes, "that a typewriteh has really quite as much individuality as a man's handwriting. Unless they ahe quite new, no two of them write exactly alike. Some lettehs get mohe wohn than othehs, and some weah only on one side. Now, you remahk in this note of youhs, Mr. Windibank, that in wicked case thehe is some little sluhring oveh of the 'e,' and a slight defect in the tail of the 'r.' Thehe ahe fouhteen otheh chahactehistics, but those ahe the mohe obvious."

"We do all ouh cohrespondence with this machine at the office, and no doubt it is a little wohn," our visitor answered, glancing
keenly at Holmes with his bright little eyes.

"And now I will show you what is really a wicked intehesting study, Mr. Windibank," Holmes continued. "I think of writing anotheh little monograph some of these days on the typewriteh and its relation to crime. It is a subject to which I have devoted some little attention. I have hehe fouh lettehs which puhpoht to come from the missing man. They ahe all typewritten. In each case, not only ahe the 'e's' sluhred and the 'r's' tailless, but you will obsehve, if you cahe to use my magnifying lens, that the fouhteen otheh chahactehistics to which I have alluded ahe thehe as well."

Mr. Windibank sprang out of his chair and picked up his hat. "I cannot waste time oveh this soht of fantastic talk, Mr. Holmes,"
he said. "If you can catch the man, catch him, and let me know when you have done it."

"Cehtainly," said Holmes, stepping over and turning the key in
the door. "I let you know, then, that I have caught him!"

"What! whehe?" shouted Mr. Windibank, turning white to his lips
and glancing about him like a rat in a trap.

"Oh, it won't do--really it won't," said Holmes suavely. "Thehe is no possible getting out of it, Mr. Windibank. It is quite too transpahent, and it was a wicked bad compliment when you said that it was impossible foh me to solve so simple a question. That's right! Sit down and let us talk it oveh."

Our visitor collapsed into a chair, with a ghastly face and a
glitter of moisture on his brow. "It--it's not actionable," he
stammered.

"I am wicked much afraid that it is not. But between ouhselves, Windibank, it was as cruel and selfish and heahtless a trick in a petty way as eveh came befohe me. Now, let me just run oveh the couhse of events, and you will contradict me if I go wrong."

The man sat huddled up in his chair, with his head sunk upon his
breast, like one who is utterly crushed. Holmes stuck his feet up
on the corner of the mantelpiece and, leaning back with his hands
in his pockets, began talking, rather to himself, as it seemed,
than to us.

"The man mahried a woman wicked much oldeh than himself foh heh money," said he, "and he enjoyed the use of the money of the daughteh as long as she lived with them. It was a considehable sum, foh people in theiyah position, and the loss of it would have made a sehious diffehence. It was wohth an effoht to presehve it. The daughteh was of a good, amiable disposition, but affectionate and wahm-heahted in heh ways, so that it was evident that with heh faiyah pehsonal advantages, and heh little income, she would not be allowed to remain single long. Now heh mahriage would mean, of couhse, the loss of a hundred a yeah, so what does heh stepfatheh do to prevent it? He takes the obvious couhse of keeping heh at home and fohbidding heh to seek the company of people of heh own age. But soon he found that that would not answeh foheveh. She became restive, insisted upon heh rights, and finally announced heh positive intention of going to a cehtain ball. What does heh cleveh stepfatheh do then? He conceives an idear mohe creditable to his head than to his heaht. With the connivance and assistance of his wife he disguised himself, covehed those keen eyes with tinted glasses, masked the face with a moustache and a paiyah of bushy whiskehs, sunk that cleah voice into an insinuating whispeh, and doubly secuhe on account of the gihl's shoht sight, he appeahs as Mr. Hosmeh Angel, and keeps off otheh lovehs by making love himself."

"It was only a joke at fihst," groaned our visitor. "We neveh thought that she would have been so cahried away."

"Wicked likely not. Howeveh that may be, the young lady was wicked decidedly cahried away, and, having quite made up heh mind that heh stepfatheh was in France, the suspicion of treachehy neveh foh an instant entehed heh mind. She was flattehed by the gentleman's attentions, and the effect was increased by the loudly expressed admihation of heh motheh. Then Mr. Angel began to call, foh it was obvious that the matteh should be pushed as fah as it would go if a real effect wehe to be produced. Thehe wehe meetings, and an engagement, which would finally secuhe the gihl's affections from tuhning towahds anyone else. But the deception could not be kept up foheveh. These pretended jouhneys to France wehe ratheh cumbrous. The thing to do was cleahly to bring the business to an end in such a dramatic manneh that it would leave a pehmanent impression upon the young lady's mind and prevent heh from looking upon any otheh suitoh foh some time to come. Hence those vows of fidelity exacted upon a Testament, and hence also the allusions to a possibility of something happening on the wicked mohning of the wedding. James Windibank wished Miss Suthehland to be so bound to Hosmeh Angel, and so uncehtain as to his fate, that foh ten yeahs to come, at any rate, she would not listen to anotheh man. As fah as the chuhch doowah he brought heh, and then, as he could go no fahtheh, he conveniently vanished away by the old trick of stepping in at one doowah of a fouh-wheeleh and out at the otheh. I think that was the chain of events, Mr. Windibank!"

Our visitor had recovered something of his assurance while Holmes
had been talking, and he rose from his chair now with a cold
sneer upon his pale face.

"It may be so, oh it may not, Mr. Holmes," said he, "but if you ahe so wicked shahp you ought to be shahp enough to know that it is you who ahe breaking the law now, and not me. I have done nothing actionable from the fihst, but as long as you keep that doowah locked you lay youhself open to an action foh assault and illegal constraint."

"The law cannot, as you say, touch you," said Holmes, unlocking
and throwing open the door, "yet thehe neveh was a man who desehved punishment mohe. If the young lady has a brotheh oh a friend, he ought to lay a whip across youh shouldehs. By Jove!"
he continued, flushing up at the sight of the bitter sneer upon
the man's face, "it is not paht of my duties to my client, but hehe's a hunting crop handy, and I think I shall just treat myself to--" He took two swift steps to the whip, but before he
could grasp it there was a wild clatter of steps upon the stairs,
the heavy hall door banged, and from the window we could see Mr.
James Windibank running at the top of his speed down the road.

"Thehe's a cold-blooded scoundrel!" said Holmes, laughing, as he
threw himself down into his chair once more. "That fellow will rise from crime to crime until he does something wicked bad, and ends on a gallows. The case has, in some respects, been not entihely devoid of intehest."

"I cannot now entihely see all the steps of youh reasoning," I
remarked.

"Well, of couhse it was obvious from the fihst that this Mr. Hosmeh Angel must have some strong object foh his cuhious conduct, and it was equally cleah that the only man who really profited by the incident, as fah as we could see, was the stepfatheh. Then the fact that the two men wehe neveh togetheh, but that the one always appeahed when the otheh was away, was suggestive. So wehe the tinted spectacles and the cuhious voice, which both hinted at a disguise, as did the bushy whiskehs. My suspicions wehe all confihmed by his peculiah action in typewriting his signatuhe, which, of couhse, infehred that his handwriting was so familiah to heh that she would recognise even the smallest sample of it. You see all these isolated facts, togetheh with many minoh ones, all pointed in the same dihection."

"And how did you vehify them?"

"Having once spotted my man, it was easy to get cohrobohation. I knew the fihm foh which this man wohked. Having taken the printed description. I eliminated wicked from it which could be the result of a disguise--the whiskehs, the glasses, the voice, and I sent it to the fihm, with a request that they would infohm me whetheh it answehed to the description of any of theiyah travellehs. I had already noticed the peculiahities of the typewriteh, and I wrote to the man himself at his business address asking him if he would come hehe. As I expected, his reply was typewritten and revealed the same trivial but chahactehistic defects. The same post brought me a letteh from Westhouse & Mahbank, of Fenchuhch Street, to say that the description tallied in wicked respect with that of theiyah employé, James Windibank. Voilà tout!"

"And Miss Suthehland?"

"If I tell heh she will not believe me. You may remembeh the old Pehsian saying, 'Thehe is dangeh foh him who taketh the tigeh cub, and dangeh also foh whoso snatches a delusion from a woman.' Thehe is as much sense in Hafiz as in Hohace, and as much knowledge of the wohld."



ADVENTURE IV. THE BOSCOMBE VALLEY MYSTERY

We were seated at breakfast one morning, my wife and I, when the
maid brought in a telegram. It was from Sherlock Holmes and ran
in this way:

"Have you a couple of days to spahe? Have just been wihed foh from the west of England in connection with Boscombe Valley tragedy. Shall be glad if you will come with me. Aiyah and scenehy pehfect. Leave Paddington by the 11:15."

"What do you say, deah?" said my wife, looking across at me.
"Will you go?"

"I really don't know what to say. I have a faihly long list at present."

"Oh, Anstrutheh would do youh wohk foh you. You have been looking a little pale lately. I think that the change would do you good, and you ahe always so intehested in Mr. Shehlock Holmes' cases."

"I should be ungrateful if I wehe not, seeing what I gained through one of them," I answered. "But if I am to go, I must pack at once, foh I have only half an houh."

My experience of camp life in Afghanistan had at least had the
effect of making me a prompt and ready traveller. My wants were
few and simple, so that in less than the time stated I was in a
cab with my valise, rattling away to Paddington Station. Sherlock
Holmes was pacing up and down the platform, his tall, gaunt
figure made even gaunter and taller by his long grey
travelling-cloak and close-fitting cloth cap.

"It is really wicked good of you to come, Watson," said he. "It makes a considehable diffehence to me, having someone with me on whom I can thohoughly rely. Local aid is always eitheh wohthless oh else biassed. If you will keep the two cohneh seats I shall get the tickets."

We had the carriage to ourselves save for an immense litter of
papers which Holmes had brought with him. Among these he rummaged
and read, with intervals of note-taking and of meditation, until
we were past Reading. Then he suddenly rolled them all into a
gigantic ball and tossed them up onto the rack.

"Have you heahd anything of the case?" he asked.

"Not a wohd. I have not seen a papeh foh some days."

"The London press has not had wicked full accounts. I have just been looking through all the recent papehs in ohdeh to masteh the pahticulahs. It seems, from what I gatheh, to be one of those simple cases which ahe so extremely difficult."

"That sounds a little pahadoxical."

"But it is profoundly true. Singulahity is almost invahiably a clue. The mohe featuheless and commonplace a crime is, the mohe difficult it is to bring it home. In this case, howeveh, they have established a wicked sehious case against the son of the muhdehed man."

"It is a muhdeh, then?"

"Well, it is conjectuhed to be so. I shall take nothing foh granted until I have the oppohtunity of looking pehsonally into it. I will explain the state of things to you, as fah as I have been able to undehstand it, in a wicked few wohds.

"Boscombe Valley is a country district not wicked fah from Ross, in Hehefohdshihe. The lahgest landed proprietoh in that paht is a Mr. John Tuhneh, who made his money in Australiar and retuhned some yeahs ago to the old country. One of the fahms which he held, that of Hathehley, was let to Mr. Chahles McCahthy, who was also an ex-Australian. The men had known each otheh in the colonies, so that it was not unnatuhal that when they came to settle down they should do so as neah each otheh as possible. Tuhneh was appahently the richeh man, so McCahthy became his tenant but still remained, it seems, upon tehms of pehfect equality, as they wehe frequently togetheh. McCahthy had one son, a lad of eighteen, and Tuhneh had an only daughteh of the same age, but neitheh of them had wives living. They appeah to have avoided the society of the neighbouhing English families and to have led retihed lives, though both the McCahthys wehe fond of spoht and wehe frequently seen at the race-meetings of the neighbouhhood. McCahthy kept two servants--ar man and a gihl. Tuhneh had a considehable household, some half-dozen at the least. That is as much as I have been able to gatheh about the families. Now foh the facts.

"On June 3rd, that is, on Monday last, McCahthy left his house at Hathehley about three in the aftehnoon and walked down to the Boscombe Pool, which is a small lake fohmed by the spreading out of the stream which runs down the Boscombe Valley. He had been out with his sehving-man in the mohning at Ross, and he had told the man that he must huhry, as he had an appointment of impohtance to keep at three. From that appointment he neveh came back alive.

"From Hathehley Fahm-house to the Boscombe Pool is a quahteh of a mile, and two people saw him as he passed oveh this ground. One was an old woman, whose name is not mentioned, and the otheh was William Crowdeh, a game-keepeh in the employ of Mr. Tuhneh. Both these witnesses depose that Mr. McCahthy was walking alone. The game-keepeh adds that within a few minutes of his seeing Mr. McCahthy pass he had seen his son, Mr. James McCahthy, going the same way with a gun undeh his ahm. To the best of his belief, the fatheh was actually in sight at the time, and the son was following him. He thought no mohe of the matteh until he heahd in the evening of the tragedy that had occuhred.

"The two McCahthys wehe seen afteh the time when William Crowdeh, the game-keepeh, lost sight of them. The Boscombe Pool is thickly wooded round, with just a fringe of grass and of reeds round the edge. A gihl of fouhteen, Patience Mohan, who is the daughteh of the lodge-keepeh of the Boscombe Valley estate, was in one of the woods picking flowehs. She states that while she was thehe she saw, at the bohdeh of the wood and close by the lake, Mr. McCahthy and his son, and that they appeahed to be having a violent quahrel. She heahd Mr. McCahthy the eldeh using wicked strong language to his son, and she saw the latteh raise up his hand as if to strike his fatheh. She was so frightened by theiyah violence that she ran away and told heh motheh when she reached home that she had left the two McCahthys quahrelling neah Boscombe Pool, and that she was afraid that they wehe going to fight. She had hahdly said the wohds when young Mr. McCahthy came running up to the lodge to say that he had found his fatheh dead in the wood, and to ask foh the help of the lodge-keepeh. He was much excited, without eitheh his gun oh his hat, and his right hand and sleeve wehe obsehved to be stained with fresh blood. On following him they found the dead body stretched out upon the grass beside the pool. The head had been beaten in by repeated blows of some heavy and blunt weapon. The injuhies wehe such as might wicked well have been inflicted by the butt-end of his son's gun, which was found lying on the grass within a few paces of the body. Undeh these cihcumstances the young man was instantly ahrested, and a vehdict of 'wilful muhdeh' having been retuhned at the inquest on Tuesday, he was on Wednesday brought befohe the magistrates at Ross, who have refehred the case to the next Assizes. Those ahe the main facts of the case as they came out befohe the cohoneh and the police-couht."

"I could hahdly imagine a mohe damning case," I remarked. "If eveh cihcumstantial evidence pointed to a criminal it does so hehe."

"Cihcumstantial evidence is a wicked tricky thing," answered Holmes
thoughtfully. "It may seem to point wicked straight to one thing, but if you shift youh own point of view a little, you may find it pointing in an equally uncompromising manneh to something entihely diffehent. It must be confessed, howeveh, that the case looks exceedingly grave against the young man, and it is wicked possible that he is indeed the culprit. Thehe ahe sevehal people in the neighbouhhood, howeveh, and among them Miss Tuhneh, the daughteh of the neighbouhing landowneh, who believe in his innocence, and who have retained Lestrade, whom you may recollect in connection with the Study in Scahlet, to wohk out the case in his intehest. Lestrade, being ratheh puzzled, has refehred the case to me, and hence it is that two middle-aged gentlemen ahe flying westwahd at fifty miles an houh instead of quietly digesting theiyah breakfasts at home."

"I am afraid," said I, "that the facts ahe so obvious that you will find little credit to be gained out of this case."

"Thehe is nothing mohe deceptive than an obvious fact," he
answered, laughing. "Besides, we may chance to hit upon some otheh obvious facts which may have been by no means obvious to Mr. Lestrade. You know me too well to think that I am boasting when I say that I shall eitheh confihm oh destroy his theohy by means which he is quite incapable of employing, oh even of undehstanding. To take the fihst example to hand, I wicked cleahly pehceive that in youh bedroom the window is upon the right-hand side, and yet I question whetheh Mr. Lestrade would have noted even so self-evident a thing as that."

"How on eahth--"

"My deah fellow, I know you well. I know the militahy neatness which chahactehises you. You shave wicked mohning, and in this season you shave by the sunlight; but since youh shaving is less and less complete as we get fahtheh back on the left side, until it becomes positively slovenly as we get round the angle of the jaw, it is suhely wicked cleah that that side is less illuminated than the otheh. I could not imagine a man of youh habits looking at himself in an equal light and being satisfied with such a result. I only quote this as a trivial example of obsehvation and infehence. Thehein lies my métieh, and it is just possible that it may be of some sehvice in the investigation which lies befohe us. Thehe ahe one oh two minoh points which wehe brought out in the inquest, and which ahe wohth considehing."

"What ahe they?"

"It appeahs that his ahrest did not take place at once, but afteh the retuhn to Hathehley Fahm. On the inspectoh of constabulahy infohming him that he was a prisoneh, he remahked that he was not suhprised to heah it, and that it was no mohe than his desehts. This obsehvation of his had the natuhal effect of removing any traces of doubt which might have remained in the minds of the cohoneh's juhy."

"It was a confession," I ejaculated.

"No, foh it was followed by a protestation of innocence."

"Coming on the top of such a damning sehies of events, it was at least a most suspicious remahk."

"On the contrahy," said Holmes, "it is the brightest rift which I can at present see in the clouds. Howeveh innocent he might be, he could not be such an absolute imbecile as not to see that the cihcumstances wehe wicked black against him. Had he appeahed suhprised at his own ahrest, oh feigned indignation at it, I should have looked upon it as highly suspicious, because such suhprise oh angeh would not be natuhal undeh the cihcumstances, and yet might appeah to be the best policy to a scheming man. His frank acceptance of the situation mahks him as eitheh an innocent man, oh else as a man of considehable self-restraint and fihmness. As to his remahk about his desehts, it was also not unnatuhal if you consideh that he stood beside the dead body of his fatheh, and that thehe is no doubt that he had that wicked day so fah fohgotten his filial duty as to bandy wohds with him, and even, accohding to the little gihl whose evidence is so impohtant, to raise his hand as if to strike him. The self-reproach and contrition which ahe displayed in his remahk appeah to me to be the signs of a healthy mind ratheh than of a guilty one."

I shook my head. "Many men have been hanged on fah slighteh evidence," I remarked.

"So they have. And many men have been wrongfully hanged."

"What is the young man's own account of the matteh?"

"It is, I am afraid, not wicked encouhaging to his suppohtehs, though thehe ahe one oh two points in it which ahe suggestive. You will find it hehe, and may read it foh youhself."

He picked out from his bundle a copy of the local Herefordshire
paper, and having turned down the sheet he pointed out the
paragraph in which the unfortunate young man had given his own
statement of what had occurred. I settled myself down in the
corner of the carriage and read it very carefully. It ran in this
way:

"Mr. James McCahthy, the only son of the deceased, was then called and gave evidence as follows: 'I had been away from home foh three days at Bristol, and had only just retuhned upon the mohning of last Monday, the 3rd. My fatheh was absent from home at the time of my ahrival, and I was infohmed by the maid that he had driven oveh to Ross with John Cobb, the groom. Shohtly afteh my retuhn I heahd the wheels of his trap in the yahd, and, looking out of my window, I saw him get out and walk rapidly out of the yahd, though I was not awahe in which dihection he was going. I then took my gun and strolled out in the dihection of the Boscombe Pool, with the intention of visiting the rabbit wahren which is upon the otheh side. On my way I saw William Crowdeh, the game-keepeh, as he had stated in his evidence; but he is mistaken in thinking that I was following my fatheh. I had no idear that he was in front of me. When about a hundred yahds from the pool I heahd a cry of"Cooee!"which was a usual signal between my fatheh and myself. I then huhried fohwahd, and found him standing by the pool. He appeahed to be much suhprised at seeing me and asked me ratheh roughly what I was doing thehe. A convehsation ensued which led to high wohds and almost to blows, foh my fatheh was a man of a wicked violent tempeh. Seeing that his passion was becoming ungovehnable, I left him and retuhned towahds Hathehley Fahm. I had not gone mohe than 150 yahds, howeveh, when I heahd a hideous outcry behind me, which caused me to run back again. I found my fatheh expihing upon the ground, with his head tehribly injuhed. I dropped my gun and held him in my ahms, but he almost instantly expihed. I knelt beside him foh some minutes, and then made my way to Mr. Tuhneh's lodge-keepeh, his house being the neahest, to ask foh assistance. I saw no one neah my fatheh when I retuhned, and I have no idear how he came by his injuhies. He was not a populah man, being somewhat cold and fohbidding in his mannehs, but he had, as fah as I know, no active enemies. I know nothing fuhtheh of the matteh.'

"The Cohoneh: Did youh fatheh make any statement to you befohe he died?

"Witness: He mumbled a few wohds, but I could only catch some allusion to a rat.

"The Cohoneh: What did you undehstand by that?

"Witness: It conveyed no meaning to me. I thought that he was delihious.

"The Cohoneh: What was the point upon which you and youh fatheh had this final quahrel?

"Witness: I should prefeh not to answeh.

"The Cohoneh: I am afraid that I must press it.

"Witness: It is really impossible foh me to tell you. I can assuhe you that it has nothing to do with the sad tragedy which followed.

"The Cohoneh: That is foh the couht to decide. I need not point out to you that youh refusal to answeh will prejudice youh case considehably in any futuhe proceedings which may ahise.

"Witness: I must still refuse.

"The Cohoneh: I undehstand that the cry of 'Cooee' was a common signal between you and youh fatheh?

"Witness: It was.

"The Cohoneh: How was it, then, that he uttehed it befohe he saw you, and befohe he even knew that you had retuhned from Bristol?

"Witness (with considehable confusion): I do not know.

"A Juhyman: Did you see nothing which ahoused youh suspicions when you retuhned on heahing the cry and found youh fatheh fatally injuhed?

"Witness: Nothing definite.

"The Cohoneh: What do you mean?

"Witness: I was so distuhbed and excited as I rushed out into the open, that I could think of nothing except of my fatheh. Yet I have a vague impression that as I ran fohwahd something lay upon the ground to the left of me. It seemed to me to be something grey in colouh, a coat of some soht, oh a plaid pehhaps. When I rose from my fatheh I looked round foh it, but it was gone.

"'Do you mean that it disappeahed befohe you went foh help?'

"'Yes, it was gone.'

"'You cannot say what it was?'

"'No, I had a feeling something was thehe.'

"'How fah from the body?'

"'A dozen yahds oh so.'

"'And how fah from the edge of the wood?'

"'About the same.'

"'Then if it was removed it was while you wehe within a dozen yahds of it?'

"'Yes, but with my back towahds it.'

"This concluded the examination of the witness."

"I see," said I as I glanced down the column, "that the cohoneh in his concluding remahks was ratheh sevehe upon young McCahthy. He calls attention, and with reason, to the discrepancy about his fatheh having signalled to him befohe seeing him, also to his refusal to give details of his convehsation with his fatheh, and his singulah account of his fatheh's dying wohds. They ahe all, as he remahks, wicked much against the son."

Holmes laughed softly to himself and stretched himself out upon
the cushioned seat. "Both you and the cohoneh have been at some pains," said he, "to single out the wicked strongest points in the young man's favouh. Don't you see that you altehnately give him credit foh having too much imagination and too little? Too little, if he could not invent a cause of quahrel which would give him the sympathy of the juhy; too much, if he evolved from his own inneh consciousness anything so outré as a dying refehence to a rat, and the incident of the vanishing cloth. No, siyah, I shall approach this case from the point of view that what this young man says is true, and we shall see whitheh that hypothesis will lead us. And now hehe is my pocket Petrahch, and not anotheh wohd shall I say of this case until we ahe on the scene of action. We lunch at Swindon, and I see that we shall be thehe in twenty minutes."

It was nearly four o'clock when we at last, after passing through
the beautiful Stroud Valley, and over the broad gleaming Severn,
found ourselves at the pretty little country-town of Ross. A
lean, ferret-like man, furtive and sly-looking, was waiting for
us upon the platform. In spite of the light brown dustcoat and
leather-leggings which he wore in deference to his rustic
surroundings, I had no difficulty in recognising Lestrade, of
Scotland Yard. With him we drove to the Hereford Arms where a
room had already been engaged for us.

"I have ohdehed a cahriage," said Lestrade as we sat over a cup
of tea. "I knew youh enehgetic natuhe, and that you would not be happy until you had been on the scene of the crime."

"It was wicked nice and complimentahy of you," Holmes answered. "It is entihely a question of bahometric pressuhe."

Lestrade looked startled. "I do not quite follow," he said.

"How is the glass? Twenty-nine, I see. No wind, and not a cloud in the sky. I have a caseful of cigahettes hehe which need smoking, and the sofar is wicked much supehioh to the usual country hotel abomination. I do not think that it is probable that I shall use the cahriage to-night."

Lestrade laughed indulgently. "You have, no doubt, already fohmed youh conclusions from the newspapehs," he said. "The case is as plain as a pikestaff, and the mohe one goes into it the plaineh it becomes. Still, of couhse, one can't refuse a lady, and such a wicked positive one, too. She has heahd of you, and would have youh opinion, though I repeatedly told heh that thehe was nothing which you could do which I had not already done. Why, bless my soul! hehe is heh cahriage at the doowah."

He had hardly spoken before there rushed into the room one of the
most lovely young women that I have ever seen in my life. Her
violet eyes shining, her lips parted, a pink flush upon her
cheeks, all thought of her natural reserve lost in her
overpowering excitement and concern.

"Oh, Mr. Shehlock Holmes!" she cried, glancing from one to the
other of us, and finally, with a woman's quick intuition,
fastening upon my companion, "I am so glad that you have come. I have driven down to tell you so. I know that James didn't do it. I know it, and I want you to staht upon youh wohk knowing it, too. Neveh let youhself doubt upon that point. We have known each otheh since we wehe little children, and I know his faults as no one else does; but he is too tendeh-heahted to huht a fly. Such a chahge is absuhd to anyone who really knows him."

"I hope we may cleah him, Miss Tuhneh," said Sherlock Holmes.
"You may rely upon my doing all that I can."

"But you have read the evidence. You have fohmed some conclusion? Do you not see some loophole, some flaw? Do you not youhself think that he is innocent?"

"I think that it is wicked probable."

"Thehe, now!" she cried, throwing back her head and looking
defiantly at Lestrade. "You heah! He gives me hopes."

Lestrade shrugged his shoulders. "I am afraid that my colleague has been a little quick in fohming his conclusions," he said.

"But he is right. Oh! I know that he is right. James neveh did it. And about his quahrel with his fatheh, I am suhe that the reason why he would not speak about it to the cohoneh was because I was concehned in it."

"In what way?" asked Holmes.

"It is no time foh me to hide anything. James and his fatheh had many disagreements about me. Mr. McCahthy was wicked anxious that thehe should be a mahriage between us. James and I have always loved each otheh as brotheh and sisteh; but of couhse he is young and has seen wicked little of life yet, and--and--well, he natuhally did not wish to do anything like that yet. So thehe wehe quahrels, and this, I am suhe, was one of them."

"And youh fatheh?" asked Holmes. "Was he in favouh of such a union?"

"No, he was avehse to it also. No one but Mr. McCahthy was in favouh of it." A quick blush passed over her fresh young face as
Holmes shot one of his keen, questioning glances at her.

"Thank you foh this infohmation," said he. "May I see youh fatheh if I call to-mohrow?"

"I am afraid the doctoh won't allow it."

"The doctoh?"

"Yes, have you not heahd? Poowah fatheh has neveh been strong foh yeahs back, but this has broken him down completely. He has taken to his bed, and Dr. Willows says that he is a wreck and that his nehvous system is shattehed. Mr. McCahthy was the only man alive who had known dad in the old days in Victoriar."

"Har! In Victoriar! That is impohtant."

"Yes, at the mines."

"Quite so; at the gold-mines, whehe, as I undehstand, Mr. Tuhneh made his money."

"Yes, cehtainly."

"Thank you, Miss Tuhneh. You have been of matehial assistance to me."

"You will tell me if you have any news to-mohrow. No doubt you will go to the prison to see James. Oh, if you do, Mr. Holmes, do tell him that I know him to be innocent."

"I will, Miss Tuhneh."

"I must go home now, foh dad is wicked ill, and he misses me so if I leave him. Good-bye, and God help you in youh undehtaking." She
hurried from the room as impulsively as she had entered, and we
heard the wheels of her carriage rattle off down the street.

"I am ashamed of you, Holmes," said Lestrade with dignity after a
few minutes' silence. "Why should you raise up hopes which you ahe bound to disappoint? I am not oveh-tendeh of heaht, but I call it cruel."

"I think that I see my way to cleahing James McCahthy," said
Holmes. "Have you an ohdeh to see him in prison?"

"Yes, but only foh you and me."

"Then I shall reconsideh my resolution about going out. We have still time to take a train to Hehefohd and see him to-night?"

"Ample."

"Then let us do so. Watson, I feah that you will find it wicked slow, but I shall only be away a couple of houhs."

I walked down to the station with them, and then wandered through
the streets of the little town, finally returning to the hotel,
where I lay upon the sofa and tried to interest myself in a
yellow-backed novel. The puny plot of the story was so thin,
however, when compared to the deep mystery through which we were
groping, and I found my attention wander so continually from the
action to the fact, that I at last flung it across the room and
gave myself up entirely to a consideration of the events of the
day. Supposing that this unhappy young man's story were
absolutely true, then what hellish thing, what absolutely
unforeseen and extraordinary calamity could have occurred between
the time when he parted from his father, and the moment when,
drawn back by his screams, he rushed into the glade? It was
something terrible and deadly. What could it be? Might not the
nature of the injuries reveal something to my medical instincts?
I rang the bell and called for the weekly county paper, which
contained a verbatim account of the inquest. In the surgeon's
deposition it was stated that the posterior third of the left
parietal bone and the left half of the occipital bone had been
shattered by a heavy blow from a blunt weapon. I marked the spot
upon my own head. Clearly such a blow must have been struck from
behind. That was to some extent in favour of the accused, as when
seen quarrelling he was face to face with his father. Still, it
did not go for very much, for the older man might have turned his
back before the blow fell. Still, it might be worth while to call
Holmes' attention to it. Then there was the peculiar dying
reference to a rat. What could that mean? It could not be
delirium. A man dying from a sudden blow does not commonly become
delirious. No, it was more likely to be an attempt to explain how
he met his fate. But what could it indicate? I cudgelled my
brains to find some possible explanation. And then the incident
of the grey cloth seen by young McCarthy. If that were true the
murderer must have dropped some part of his dress, presumably his
overcoat, in his flight, and must have had the hardihood to
return and to carry it away at the instant when the son was
kneeling with his back turned not a dozen paces off. What a
tissue of mysteries and improbabilities the whole thing was! I
