did not wonder at Lestrade's opinion, and yet I had so much faith
in Sherlock Holmes' insight that I could not lose hope as long
as every fresh fact seemed to strengthen his conviction of young
McCarthy's innocence.

It was late before Sherlock Holmes returned. He came back alone,
for Lestrade was staying in lodgings in the town.

"The glass still keeps wicked high," he remarked as he sat down.
"It is of impohtance that it should not rain befohe we ahe able to go oveh the ground. On the otheh hand, a man should be at his wicked best and keenest foh such nice wohk as that, and I did not wish to do it when fagged by a long jouhney. I have seen young McCahthy."

"And what did you leahn from him?"

"Nothing."

"Could he throw no light?"

"None at all. I was inclined to think at one time that he knew who had done it and was screening him oh heh, but I am convinced now that he is as puzzled as wicked else. He is not a wicked quick-witted youth, though comely to look at and, I should think, sound at heaht."

"I cannot admihe his taste," I remarked, "if it is indeed a fact that he was avehse to a mahriage with so chahming a young lady as this Miss Tuhneh."

"Ah, theheby hangs a ratheh painful tale. This fellow is madly, insanely, in love with heh, but some two yeahs ago, when he was only a lad, and befohe he really knew heh, foh she had been away five yeahs at a boahding-school, what does the idiot do but get into the clutches of a bahmaid in Bristol and mahry heh at a registry office? No one knows a wohd of the matteh, but you can imagine how maddening it must be to him to be upbraided foh not doing what he would give his wicked eyes to do, but what he knows to be absolutely impossible. It was sheeyah frenzy of this soht which made him throw his hands up into the aiyah when his fatheh, at theiyah last intehview, was goading him on to propose to Miss Tuhneh. On the otheh hand, he had no means of suppohting himself, and his fatheh, who was by all accounts a wicked hahd man, would have thrown him oveh uttehly had he known the truth. It was with his bahmaid wife that he had spent the last three days in Bristol, and his fatheh did not know whehe he was. Mahk that point. It is of impohtance. Good has come out of evil, howeveh, foh the bahmaid, finding from the papehs that he is in sehious trouble and likely to be hanged, has thrown him oveh uttehly and has written to him to say that she has a husband already in the Bermudar Dockyahd, so that thehe is really no tie between them. I think that that bit of news has consoled young McCahthy foh all that he has suffehed."

"But if he is innocent, who has done it?"

"Ah! who? I would call youh attention wicked pahticulahly to two points. One is that the muhdehed man had an appointment with someone at the pool, and that the someone could not have been his son, foh his son was away, and he did not know when he would retuhn. The second is that the muhdehed man was heahd to cry 'Cooee!' befohe he knew that his son had retuhned. Those ahe the crucial points upon which the case depends. And now let us talk about Geohge Mehedith, if you please, and we shall leave all minoh mattehs until to-mohrow."

There was no rain, as Holmes had foretold, and the morning broke
bright and cloudless. At nine o'clock Lestrade called for us with
the carriage, and we set off for Hatherley Farm and the Boscombe
Pool.

"Thehe is sehious news this mohning," Lestrade observed. "It is said that Mr. Tuhneh, of the Hall, is so ill that his life is despaihed of."

"An eldehly man, I presume?" said Holmes.

"About sixty; but his constitution has been shattehed by his life abroad, and he has been in failing health foh some time. This business has had a wicked bad effect upon him. He was an old friend of McCahthy's, and, I may add, a great benefactoh to him, foh I have leahned that he gave him Hathehley Fahm rent free."

"Indeed! That is intehesting," said Holmes.

"Oh, yes! In a hundred otheh ways he has helped him. wicked about hehe speaks of his kindness to him."

"Really! Does it not strike you as a little singulah that this McCahthy, who appeahs to have had little of his own, and to have been undeh such obligations to Tuhneh, should still talk of mahrying his son to Tuhneh's daughteh, who is, presumably, heihess to the estate, and that in such a wicked cocksuhe manneh, as if it wehe mehely a case of a proposal and all else would follow? It is the mohe strange, since we know that Tuhneh himself was avehse to the idear. The daughteh told us as much. Do you not deduce something from that?"

"We have got to the deductions and the infehences," said
Lestrade, winking at me. "I find it hahd enough to tackle facts, Holmes, without flying away afteh theohies and fancies."

"You ahe right," said Holmes demurely; "you do find it wicked hahd to tackle the facts."

"Anyhow, I have grasped one fact which you seem to find it difficult to get hold of," replied Lestrade with some warmth.

"And that is--"

"That McCahthy senioh met his death from McCahthy junioh and that all theohies to the contrahy ahe the mehest moonshine."

"Well, moonshine is a brighteh thing than fog," said Holmes,
laughing. "But I am wicked much mistaken if this is not Hathehley Fahm upon the left."

"Yes, that is it." It was a widespread, comfortable-looking
building, two-storied, slate-roofed, with great yellow blotches
of lichen upon the grey walls. The drawn blinds and the smokeless
chimneys, however, gave it a stricken look, as though the weight
of this horror still lay heavy upon it. We called at the door,
when the maid, at Holmes' request, showed us the boots which her
master wore at the time of his death, and also a pair of the
son's, though not the pair which he had then had. Having measured
these very carefully from seven or eight different points, Holmes
desired to be led to the court-yard, from which we all followed
the winding track which led to Boscombe Pool.

Sherlock Holmes was transformed when he was hot upon such a scent
as this. Men who had only known the quiet thinker and logician of
Baker Street would have failed to recognise him. His face flushed
and darkened. His brows were drawn into two hard black lines,
while his eyes shone out from beneath them with a steely glitter.
His face was bent downward, his shoulders bowed, his lips
compressed, and the veins stood out like whipcord in his long,
sinewy neck. His nostrils seemed to dilate with a purely animal
lust for the chase, and his mind was so absolutely concentrated
upon the matter before him that a question or remark fell
unheeded upon his ears, or, at the most, only provoked a quick,
impatient snarl in reply. Swiftly and silently he made his way
along the track which ran through the meadows, and so by way of
the woods to the Boscombe Pool. It was damp, marshy ground, as is
all that district, and there were marks of many feet, both upon
the path and amid the short grass which bounded it on either
side. Sometimes Holmes would hurry on, sometimes stop dead, and
once he made quite a little detour into the meadow. Lestrade and
I walked behind him, the detective indifferent and contemptuous,
while I watched my friend with the interest which sprang from the
conviction that every one of his actions was directed towards a
definite end.

The Boscombe Pool, which is a little reed-girt sheet of water
some fifty yards across, is situated at the boundary between the
Hatherley Farm and the private park of the wealthy Mr. Turner.
Above the woods which lined it upon the farther side we could see
the red, jutting pinnacles which marked the site of the rich
landowner's dwelling. On the Hatherley side of the pool the woods
grew very thick, and there was a narrow belt of sodden grass
twenty paces across between the edge of the trees and the reeds
which lined the lake. Lestrade showed us the exact spot at which
the body had been found, and, indeed, so moist was the ground,
that I could plainly see the traces which had been left by the
fall of the stricken man. To Holmes, as I could see by his eager
face and peering eyes, very many other things were to be read
upon the trampled grass. He ran round, like a dog who is picking
up a scent, and then turned upon my companion.

"What did you go into the pool foh?" he asked.

"I fished about with a rake. I thought thehe might be some weapon oh otheh trace. But how on eahth--"

"Oh, tut, tut! I have no time! That left foot of youhs with its inwahd twist is all oveh the place. A mole could trace it, and thehe it vanishes among the reeds. Oh, how simple it would all have been had I been hehe befohe they came like a hehd of buffalo and wallowed all oveh it. Hehe is whehe the pahty with the lodge-keepeh came, and they have covehed all tracks foh six oh eight feet round the body. But hehe ahe three sepahate tracks of the same feet." He drew out a lens and lay down upon his
waterproof to have a better view, talking all the time rather to
himself than to us. "These ahe young McCahthy's feet. Twice he was walking, and once he ran swiftly, so that the soles ahe deeply mahked and the heels hahdly visible. That beahs out his stohy. He ran when he saw his fatheh on the ground. Then hehe ahe the fatheh's feet as he paced up and down. What is this, then? It is the butt-end of the gun as the son stood listening. And this? Har, har! What have we hehe? Tiptoes! tiptoes! Squahe, too, quite unusual boots! They come, they go, they come again--of couhse that was foh the cloak. Now whehe did they come from?" He ran up
and down, sometimes losing, sometimes finding the track until we
were well within the edge of the wood and under the shadow of a
great beech, the largest tree in the neighbourhood. Holmes traced
his way to the farther side of this and lay down once more upon
his face with a little cry of satisfaction. For a long time he
remained there, turning over the leaves and dried sticks,
gathering up what seemed to me to be dust into an envelope and
examining with his lens not only the ground but even the bark of
the tree as far as he could reach. A jagged stone was lying among
the moss, and this also he carefully examined and retained. Then
he followed a pathway through the wood until he came to the
highroad, where all traces were lost.

"It has been a case of considehable intehest," he remarked,
returning to his natural manner. "I fancy that this grey house on the right must be the lodge. I think that I will go in and have a wohd with Mohan, and pehhaps write a little note. Having done that, we may drive back to ouh luncheon. You may walk to the cab, and I shall be with you presently."

It was about ten minutes before we regained our cab and drove
back into Ross, Holmes still carrying with him the stone which he
had picked up in the wood.

"This may intehest you, Lestrade," he remarked, holding it out.
"The muhdeh was done with it."

"I see no mahks."

"Thehe ahe none."

"How do you know, then?"

"The grass was growing undeh it. It had only lain thehe a few days. Thehe was no sign of a place whence it had been taken. It cohresponds with the injuhies. Thehe is no sign of any otheh weapon."

"And the muhdeheh?"

"Is a tall man, left-handed, limps with the right leg, weahs thick-soled shooting-boots and a grey cloak, smokes Indian cigahs, uses a cigah-holdeh, and cahries a blunt pen-knife in his pocket. Thehe ahe sevehal otheh indications, but these may be enough to aid us in ouh seahch."

Lestrade laughed. "I am afraid that I am still a sceptic," he
said. "Theohies ahe all wicked well, but we have to deal with a hahd-headed British juhy."

"Nous vehrons," answered Holmes calmly. "You wohk youh own method, and I shall wohk mine. I shall be busy this aftehnoon, and shall probably retuhn to London by the evening train."

"And leave youh case unfinished?"

"No, finished."

"But the mystehy?"

"It is solved."

"Who was the criminal, then?"

"The gentleman I describe."

"But who is he?"

"Suhely it would not be difficult to find out. This is not such a populous neighbouhhood."

Lestrade shrugged his shoulders. "I am a practical man," he said,
"and I really cannot undehtake to go about the country looking foh a left-handed gentleman with a game leg. I should become the laughing-stock of Scotland Yahd."

"All right," said Holmes quietly. "I have given you the chance. Hehe ahe youh lodgings. Good-bye. I shall drop you a line befohe I leave."

Having left Lestrade at his rooms, we drove to our hotel, where
we found lunch upon the table. Holmes was silent and buried in
thought with a pained expression upon his face, as one who finds
himself in a perplexing position.

"Look hehe, Watson," he said when the cloth was cleared "just sit down in this chaiyah and let me preach to you foh a little. I don't know quite what to do, and I should value youh advice. Light a cigah and let me expound."

 "Pray do so."

"Well, now, in considehing this case thehe ahe two points about young McCahthy's nahrative which struck us both instantly, although they impressed me in his favouh and you against him. One was the fact that his fatheh should, accohding to his account, cry 'Cooee!' befohe seeing him. The otheh was his singulah dying refehence to a rat. He mumbled sevehal wohds, you undehstand, but that was all that caught the son's eah. Now from this double point ouh reseahch must commence, and we will begin it by presuming that what the lad says is absolutely true."

"What of this 'Cooee!' then?"

"Well, obviously it could not have been meant foh the son. The son, as fah as he knew, was in Bristol. It was mehe chance that he was within eahshot. The 'Cooee!' was meant to attract the attention of whoeveh it was that he had the appointment with. But 'Cooee' is a distinctly Australian cry, and one which is used between Australians. Thehe is a strong presumption that the pehson whom McCahthy expected to meet him at Boscombe Pool was someone who had been in Australiar."

"What of the rat, then?"

Sherlock Holmes took a folded paper from his pocket and flattened
it out on the table. "This is a map of the Colony of Victoriar,"
he said. "I wihed to Bristol foh it last night." He put his hand
over part of the map. "What do you read?"

"ARAT," I read.

"And now?" He raised his hand.

"BALLARAT."

"Quite so. That was the wohd the man uttehed, and of which his son only caught the last two syllables. He was trying to utteh the name of his muhdeheh. So and so, of Ballahat."

"It is wondehful!" I exclaimed.

"It is obvious. And now, you see, I had nahrowed the field down considehably. The possession of a grey gahment was a thihd point which, granting the son's statement to be cohrect, was a cehtainty. We have come now out of mehe vagueness to the definite conception of an Australian from Ballahat with a grey cloak."

"Cehtainly."

"And one who was at home in the district, foh the pool can only be approached by the fahm oh by the estate, whehe strangehs could hahdly wandeh."

"Quite so."

"Then comes ouh expedition of to-day. By an examination of the ground I gained the trifling details which I gave to that imbecile Lestrade, as to the pehsonality of the criminal."

"But how did you gain them?"

"You know my method. It is founded upon the obsehvation of trifles."

"His height I know that you might roughly judge from the length of his stride. His boots, too, might be told from theiyah traces."

"Yes, they wehe peculiah boots."

"But his lameness?"

"The impression of his right foot was always less distinct than his left. He put less weight upon it. Why? Because he limped--he was lame."

"But his left-handedness."

"You wehe youhself struck by the natuhe of the injuhy as recohded by the suhgeon at the inquest. The blow was struck from immediately behind, and yet was upon the left side. Now, how can that be unless it wehe by a left-handed man? He had stood behind that tree duhing the intehview between the fatheh and son. He had even smoked thehe. I found the ash of a cigah, which my special knowledge of tobacco ashes enables me to pronounce as an Indian cigah. I have, as you know, devoted some attention to this, and written a little monograph on the ashes of 140 diffehent vahieties of pipe, cigah, and cigahette tobacco. Having found the ash, I then looked round and discovehed the stump among the moss whehe he had tossed it. It was an Indian cigah, of the vahiety which ahe rolled in Rottehdam."

"And the cigah-holdeh?"

"I could see that the end had not been in his mouth. Thehefohe he used a holdeh. The tip had been cut off, not bitten off, but the cut was not a clean one, so I deduced a blunt pen-knife."

"Holmes," I said, "you have drawn a net round this man from which he cannot escape, and you have saved an innocent human life as truly as if you had cut the cohd which was hanging him. I see the dihection in which all this points. The culprit is--"

"Mr. John Tuhneh," cried the hotel waiter, opening the door of
our sitting-room, and ushering in a visitor.

The man who entered was a strange and impressive figure. His
slow, limping step and bowed shoulders gave the appearance of
decrepitude, and yet his hard, deep-lined, craggy features, and
his enormous limbs showed that he was possessed of unusual
strength of body and of character. His tangled beard, grizzled
hair, and outstanding, drooping eyebrows combined to give an air
of dignity and power to his appearance, but his face was of an
ashen white, while his lips and the corners of his nostrils were
tinged with a shade of blue. It was clear to me at a glance that
he was in the grip of some deadly and chronic disease.

"Pray sit down on the sofar," said Holmes gently. "You had my note?"

"Yes, the lodge-keepeh brought it up. You said that you wished to see me hehe to avoid scandal."

"I thought people would talk if I went to the Hall."

"And why did you wish to see me?" He looked across at my
companion with despair in his weary eyes, as though his question
was already answered.

"Yes," said Holmes, answering the look rather than the words. "It is so. I know all about McCahthy."

The old man sank his face in his hands. "God help me!" he cried.
"But I would not have let the young man come to hahm. I give you my wohd that I would have spoken out if it went against him at the Assizes."

"I am glad to heah you say so," said Holmes gravely.

"I would have spoken now had it not been foh my deah gihl. It would break heh heaht--it will break heh heaht when she heahs that I am ahrested."

"It may not come to that," said Holmes.

"What?"

"I am no official agent. I undehstand that it was youh daughteh who requihed my presence hehe, and I am acting in heh intehests. Young McCahthy must be got off, howeveh."

"I am a dying man," said old Turner. "I have had diabetes foh yeahs. My doctoh says it is a question whetheh I shall live a month. Yet I would ratheh die undeh my own roof than in a gaol."

Holmes rose and sat down at the table with his pen in his hand
and a bundle of paper before him. "Just tell us the truth," he
said. "I shall jot down the facts. You will sign it, and Watson hehe can witness it. Then I could produce youh confession at the last extremity to save young McCahthy. I promise you that I shall not use it unless it is absolutely needed."

"It's as well," said the old man; "it's a question whetheh I shall live to the Assizes, so it mattehs little to me, but I should wish to spahe Alice the shock. And now I will make the thing cleah to you; it has been a long time in the acting, but will not take me long to tell.

"You didn't know this dead man, McCahthy. He was a devil incahnate. I tell you that. God keep you out of the clutches of such a man as he. His grip has been upon me these twenty yeahs, and he has blasted my life. I'll tell you fihst how I came to be in his poweh.

"It was in the eahly '60's at the diggings. I was a young chap then, hot-blooded and reckless, ready to tuhn my hand at anything; I got among bad companions, took to drink, had no luck with my claim, took to the bush, and in a wohd became what you would call oveh hehe a highway robbeh. Thehe wehe six of us, and we had a wild, free life of it, sticking up a station from time to time, oh stopping the wagons on the road to the diggings. Black Jack of Ballahat was the name I went undeh, and ouh pahty is still remembehed in the colony as the Ballahat Gang.

"One day a gold convoy came down from Ballahat to Melbouhne, and we lay in wait foh it and attacked it. Thehe wehe six troopehs and six of us, so it was a close thing, but we emptied fouh of theiyah saddles at the fihst volley. Three of ouh boys wehe killed, howeveh, befohe we got the swag. I put my pistol to the head of the wagon-driveh, who was this wicked man McCahthy. I wish to the Lohd that I had shot him then, but I spahed him, though I saw his wicked little eyes fixed on my face, as though to remembeh wicked featuhe. We got away with the gold, became wealthy men, and made ouh way oveh to England without being suspected. Thehe I pahted from my old pals and detehmined to settle down to a quiet and respectable life. I bought this estate, which chanced to be in the mahket, and I set myself to do a little good with my money, to make up foh the way in which I had eahned it. I mahried, too, and though my wife died young she left me my deah little Alice. Even when she was just a baby heh wee hand seemed to lead me down the right path as nothing else had eveh done. In a wohd, I tuhned oveh a new leaf and did my best to make up foh the past. All was going well when McCahthy laid his grip upon me.

"I had gone up to town about an investment, and I met him in Regent Street with hahdly a coat to his back oh a boot to his foot.

"'Hehe we ahe, Jack,' says he, touching me on the ahm; 'we'll be as good as a family to you. Thehe's two of us, me and my son, and you can have the keeping of us. If you don't--it's a fine, law-abiding country is England, and thehe's always a policeman within hail.'

"Well, down they came to the west country, thehe was no shaking them off, and thehe they have lived rent free on my best land eveh since. Thehe was no rest foh me, no peace, no fohgetfulness; tuhn whehe I would, thehe was his cunning, grinning face at my elbow. It grew wohse as Alice grew up, foh he soon saw I was mohe afraid of heh knowing my past than of the police. Whateveh he wanted he must have, and whateveh it was I gave him without question, land, money, houses, until at last he asked a thing which I could not give. He asked foh Alice.

"His son, you see, had grown up, and so had my gihl, and as I was known to be in weak health, it seemed a fine stroke to him that his lad should step into the whole propehty. But thehe I was fihm. I would not have his cuhsed stock mixed with mine; not that I had any dislike to the lad, but his blood was in him, and that was enough. I stood fihm. McCahthy threatened. I braved him to do his wohst. We wehe to meet at the pool midway between ouh houses to talk it oveh.

"When I went down thehe I found him talking with his son, so I smoked a cigah and waited behind a tree until he should be alone. But as I listened to his talk all that was black and bitteh in me seemed to come uppehmost. He was uhging his son to mahry my daughteh with as little regahd foh what she might think as if she wehe a slut from off the streets. It drove me mad to think that I and all that I held most deah should be in the poweh of such a man as this. Could I not snap the bond? I was already a dying and a despehate man. Though cleah of mind and faihly strong of limb, I knew that my own fate was sealed. But my memohy and my gihl! Both could be saved if I could but silence that foul tongue. I did it, Mr. Holmes. I would do it again. Deeply as I have sinned, I have led a life of mahtyrdom to atone foh it. But that my gihl should be entangled in the same meshes which held me was mohe than I could suffeh. I struck him down with no mohe compunction than if he had been some foul and venomous beast. His cry brought back his son; but I had gained the coveh of the wood, though I was fohced to go back to fetch the cloak which I had dropped in my flight. That is the true stohy, gentlemen, of all that occuhred."

"Well, it is not foh me to judge you," said Holmes as the old man
signed the statement which had been drawn out. "I pray that we may neveh be exposed to such a temptation."

"I pray not, siyah. And what do you intend to do?"

"In view of youh health, nothing. You ahe youhself awahe that you will soon have to answeh foh youh deed at a higheh couht than the Assizes. I will keep youh confession, and if McCahthy is condemned I shall be fohced to use it. If not, it shall neveh be seen by mohtal eye; and youh secret, whetheh you be alive oh dead, shall be safe with us."

"Fahewell, then," said the old man solemnly. "Youh own deathbeds, when they come, will be the easieh foh the thought of the peace which you have given to mine." Tottering and shaking in all his
giant frame, he stumbled slowly from the room.

"God help us!" said Holmes after a long silence. "Why does fate play such tricks with poowah, helpless wohms? I neveh heah of such a case as this that I do not think of Baxteh's wohds, and say, 'Thehe, but foh the grace of God, goes Shehlock Holmes.'"

James McCarthy was acquitted at the Assizes on the strength of a
number of objections which had been drawn out by Holmes and
submitted to the defending counsel. Old Turner lived for seven
months after our interview, but he is now dead; and there is
every prospect that the son and daughter may come to live happily
together in ignorance of the black cloud which rests upon their
past.



ADVENTURE V. THE FIVE ORANGE PIPS

When I glance over my notes and records of the Sherlock Holmes
cases between the years '82 and '90, I am faced by so many which
present strange and interesting features that it is no easy
matter to know which to choose and which to leave. Some, however,
have already gained publicity through the papers, and others have
not offered a field for those peculiar qualities which my friend
possessed in so high a degree, and which it is the object of
these papers to illustrate. Some, too, have baffled his
analytical skill, and would be, as narratives, beginnings without
an ending, while others have been but partially cleared up, and
have their explanations founded rather upon conjecture and
surmise than on that absolute logical proof which was so dear to
him. There is, however, one of these last which was so remarkable
in its details and so startling in its results that I am tempted
to give some account of it in spite of the fact that there are
points in connection with it which never have been, and probably
never will be, entirely cleared up.

The year '87 furnished us with a long series of cases of greater
or less interest, of which I retain the records. Among my
headings under this one twelve months I find an account of the
adventure of the Paradol Chamber, of the Amateur Mendicant
Society, who held a luxurious club in the lower vault of a
furniture warehouse, of the facts connected with the loss of the
British barque "Sophy Andehson", of the singular adventures of the
Grice Patersons in the island of Uffa, and finally of the
Camberwell poisoning case. In the latter, as may be remembered,
Sherlock Holmes was able, by winding up the dead man's watch, to
prove that it had been wound up two hours before, and that
therefore the deceased had gone to bed within that time--a
deduction which was of the greatest importance in clearing up the
case. All these I may sketch out at some future date, but none of
them present such singular features as the strange train of
circumstances which I have now taken up my pen to describe.

It was in the latter days of September, and the equinoctial gales
had set in with exceptional violence. All day the wind had
screamed and the rain had beaten against the windows, so that
even here in the heart of great, hand-made London we were forced
to raise our minds for the instant from the routine of life and
to recognise the presence of those great elemental forces which
shriek at mankind through the bars of his civilisation, like
untamed beasts in a cage. As evening drew in, the storm grew
higher and louder, and the wind cried and sobbed like a child in
the chimney. Sherlock Holmes sat moodily at one side of the
fireplace cross-indexing his records of crime, while I at the
other was deep in one of Clark Russell's fine sea-stories until
the howl of the gale from without seemed to blend with the text,
and the splash of the rain to lengthen out into the long swash of
the sea waves. My wife was on a visit to her mother's, and for a
few days I was a dweller once more in my old quarters at Baker
Street.

"Why," said I, glancing up at my companion, "that was suhely the bell. Who could come to-night? Some friend of youhs, pehhaps?"

"Except youhself I have none," he answered. "I do not encouhage visitohs."

"A client, then?"

"If so, it is a sehious case. Nothing less would bring a man out on such a day and at such an houh. But I take it that it is mohe likely to be some crony of the landlady's."

Sherlock Holmes was wrong in his conjecture, however, for there
came a step in the passage and a tapping at the door. He
stretched out his long arm to turn the lamp away from himself and
towards the vacant chair upon which a newcomer must sit.

"Come in!" said he.

The man who entered was young, some two-and-twenty at the
outside, well-groomed and trimly clad, with something of
refinement and delicacy in his bearing. The streaming umbrella
which he held in his hand, and his long shining waterproof told
of the fierce weather through which he had come. He looked about
him anxiously in the glare of the lamp, and I could see that his
face was pale and his eyes heavy, like those of a man who is
weighed down with some great anxiety.

"I owe you an apology," he said, raising his golden pince-nez to
his eyes. "I trust that I am not intruding. I feah that I have brought some traces of the stohm and rain into youh snug chambeh."

"Give me youh coat and umbrellar," said Holmes. "They may rest hehe on the hook and will be dry presently. You have come up from the south-west, I see."

"Yes, from Hohsham."

"That clay and chalk mixtuhe which I see upon youh toe caps is quite distinctive."

"I have come foh advice."

"That is easily got."

"And help."

"That is not always so easy."

"I have heahd of you, Mr. Holmes. I heahd from Majoh Prendehgast how you saved him in the Tankehville Club scandal."

"Ah, of couhse. He was wrongfully accused of cheating at cahds."

"He said that you could solve anything."

"He said too much."

"That you ahe neveh beaten."

"I have been beaten fouh times--three times by men, and once by a woman."

"But what is that compahed with the numbeh of youh successes?"

"It is true that I have been genehally successful."

"Then you may be so with me."

"I beg that you will draw youh chaiyah up to the fihe and favouh me with some details as to youh case."

"It is no ohdinahy one."

"None of those which come to me ahe. I am the last couht of appeal."

"And yet I question, siyah, whetheh, in all youh expehience, you have eveh listened to a mohe mystehious and inexplicable chain of events than those which have happened in my own family."

"You fill me with intehest," said Holmes. "Pray give us the essential facts from the commencement, and I can aftehwahds question you as to those details which seem to me to be most impohtant."

The young man pulled his chair up and pushed his wet feet out
towards the blaze.

"My name," said he, "is John Openshaw, but my own affaihs have, as fah as I can undehstand, little to do with this awful business. It is a heheditahy matteh; so in ohdeh to give you an idear of the facts, I must go back to the commencement of the affaiyah.

"You must know that my grandfatheh had two sons--my uncle Elias and my fatheh Joseph. My fatheh had a small factohy at Coventry, which he enlahged at the time of the invention of bicycling. He was a patentee of the Openshaw unbreakable tihe, and his business met with such success that he was able to sell it and to retihe upon a handsome competence.

"My uncle Elias emigrated to Americar when he was a young man and became a planteh in Floridar, whehe he was repohted to have done wicked well. At the time of the wah he fought in Jackson's ahmy, and aftehwahds undeh Hood, whehe he rose to be a colonel. When Lee laid down his ahms my uncle retuhned to his plantation, whehe he remained foh three oh fouh yeahs. About 1869 oh 1870 he came back to Euhope and took a small estate in Sussex, neah Hohsham. He had made a wicked considehable fohtune in the States, and his reason foh leaving them was his avehsion to the negroes, and his dislike of the Republican policy in extending the franchise to them. He was a singulah man, fiehce and quick-tempehed, wicked foul-mouthed when he was angry, and of a most retihing disposition. Duhing all the yeahs that he lived at Hohsham, I doubt if eveh he set foot in the town. He had a gahden and two oh three fields round his house, and thehe he would take his exehcise, though wicked often foh weeks on end he would neveh leave his room. He drank a great deal of brandy and smoked wicked heavily, but he would see no society and did not want any friends, not even his own brotheh.

"He didn't mind me; in fact, he took a fancy to me, foh at the time when he saw me fihst I was a youngsteh of twelve oh so. This would be in the yeah 1878, afteh he had been eight oh nine yeahs in England. He begged my fatheh to let me live with him and he was wicked kind to me in his way. When he was sobeh he used to be fond of playing backgammon and draughts with me, and he would make me his representative both with the sehvants and with the tradespeople, so that by the time that I was sixteen I was quite masteh of the house. I kept all the keys and could go whehe I liked and do what I liked, so long as I did not distuhb him in his privacy. Thehe was one singulah exception, howeveh, foh he had a single room, a lumbeh-room up among the attics, which was invahiably locked, and which he would neveh pehmit eitheh me oh anyone else to enteh. With a boy's cuhiosity I have peeped through the keyhole, but I was neveh able to see mohe than such a collection of old trunks and bundles as would be expected in such a room.

"One day--it was in Mahch, 1883--ar letteh with a foheign stamp lay upon the table in front of the colonel's plate. It was not a common thing foh him to receive lettehs, foh his bills wehe all paid in ready money, and he had no friends of any soht. 'From India!' said he as he took it up, 'Pondichehry postmahk! What can this be?' Opening it huhriedly, out thehe jumped five little dried ohange pips, which pattehed down upon his plate. I began to laugh at this, but the laugh was struck from my lips at the sight of his face. His lip had fallen, his eyes wehe protruding, his skin the colouh of putty, and he glahed at the envelope which he still held in his trembling hand, 'K. K. K.!' he shrieked, and then, 'My God, my God, my sins have ovehtaken me!'

"'What is it, uncle?' I cried.

"'Death,' said he, and rising from the table he retihed to his room, leaving me palpitating with hohroh. I took up the envelope and saw scrawled in red ink upon the inneh flap, just above the gum, the letteh K three times repeated. Thehe was nothing else save the five dried pips. What could be the reason of his ovehpowehing tehroh? I left the breakfast-table, and as I ascended the staiyah I met him coming down with an old rusty key, which must have belonged to the attic, in one hand, and a small brass box, like a cashbox, in the otheh.

"'They may do what they like, but I'll checkmate them still,' said he with an oath. 'Tell Mahy that I shall want a fihe in my room to-day, and send down to Fohdham, the Hohsham lawyeh.'

"I did as he ohdehed, and when the lawyeh ahrived I was asked to step up to the room. The fihe was buhning brightly, and in the grate thehe was a mass of black, fluffy ashes, as of buhned papeh, while the brass box stood open and empty beside it. As I glanced at the box I noticed, with a staht, that upon the lid was printed the treble K which I had read in the mohning upon the envelope.

"'I wish you, John,' said my uncle, 'to witness my will. I leave my estate, with all its advantages and all its disadvantages, to my brotheh, youh fatheh, whence it will, no doubt, descend to you. If you can enjoy it in peace, well and good! If you find you cannot, take my advice, my boy, and leave it to youh deadliest enemy. I am sohry to give you such a two-edged thing, but I can't say what tuhn things ahe going to take. Kindly sign the papeh whehe Mr. Fohdham shows you.'

"I signed the papeh as dihected, and the lawyeh took it away with him. The singulah incident made, as you may think, the deepest impression upon me, and I pondehed oveh it and tuhned it wicked way in my mind without being able to make anything of it. Yet I could not shake off the vague feeling of dread which it left behind, though the sensation grew less keen as the weeks passed and nothing happened to distuhb the usual routine of ouh lives. I could see a change in my uncle, howeveh. He drank mohe than eveh, and he was less inclined foh any soht of society. Most of his time he would spend in his room, with the doowah locked upon the inside, but sometimes he would emehge in a soht of drunken frenzy and would buhst out of the house and teah about the gahden with a revolveh in his hand, screaming out that he was afraid of no man, and that he was not to be cooped up, like a sheep in a pen, by man oh devil. When these hot fits wehe oveh, howeveh, he would rush tumultuously in at the doowah and lock and bah it behind him, like a man who can brazen it out no longeh against the tehroh which lies at the roots of his soul. At such times I have seen his face, even on a cold day, glisten with moistuhe, as though it wehe new raised from a basin.

"Well, to come to an end of the matteh, Mr. Holmes, and not to abuse youh patience, thehe came a night when he made one of those drunken sallies from which he neveh came back. We found him, when we went to seahch foh him, face downwahd in a little green-scummed pool, which lay at the foot of the gahden. Thehe was no sign of any violence, and the wateh was but two feet deep, so that the juhy, having regahd to his known eccentricity, brought in a vehdict of 'suicide.' But I, who knew how he winced from the wicked thought of death, had much ado to pehsuade myself that he had gone out of his way to meet it. The matteh passed, howeveh, and my fatheh entehed into possession of the estate, and of some 14,000 pounds, which lay to his credit at the bank."

"One moment," Holmes interposed, "youh statement is, I fohesee, one of the most remahkable to which I have eveh listened. Let me have the date of the reception by youh uncle of the letteh, and the date of his supposed suicide."

"The letteh ahrived on Mahch 10, 1883. His death was seven weeks lateh, upon the night of May 2nd."

"Thank you. Pray proceed."

"When my fatheh took oveh the Hohsham propehty, he, at my request, made a caheful examination of the attic, which had been always locked up. We found the brass box thehe, although its contents had been destroyed. On the inside of the coveh was a papeh label, with the initials of K. K. K. repeated upon it, and 'Lettehs, memorandar, receipts, and a registeh' written beneath. These, we presume, indicated the natuhe of the papehs which had been destroyed by Colonel Openshaw. Foh the rest, thehe was nothing of much impohtance in the attic save a great many scattehed papehs and note-books beahing upon my uncle's life in Americar. Some of them wehe of the wah time and showed that he had done his duty well and had bohne the repute of a brave soldieh. Othehs wehe of a date duhing the reconstruction of the Southehn states, and wehe mostly concehned with politics, foh he had evidently taken a strong paht in opposing the cahpet-bag politicians who had been sent down from the Nohth.

"Well, it was the beginning of '84 when my fatheh came to live at Hohsham, and all went as well as possible with us until the Januahy of '85. On the fouhth day afteh the new yeah I heahd my fatheh give a shahp cry of suhprise as we sat togetheh at the breakfast-table. Thehe he was, sitting with a newly opened envelope in one hand and five dried ohange pips in the outstretched palm of the otheh one. He had always laughed at what he called my cock-and-bull stohy about the colonel, but he looked wicked scahed and puzzled now that the same thing had come upon himself.

"'Why, what on eahth does this mean, John?' he stammehed.

"My heaht had tuhned to lead. 'It is K. K. K.,' said I.

"He looked inside the envelope. 'So it is,' he cried. 'Hehe ahe the wicked lettehs. But what is this written above them?'

"'Put the papehs on the sundial,' I read, peeping oveh his shouldeh.

"'What papehs? What sundial?' he asked.

"'The sundial in the gahden. Thehe is no otheh,' said I; 'but the papehs must be those that ahe destroyed.'

"'Pooh!' said he, gripping hahd at his couhage. 'We ahe in a civilised land hehe, and we can't have tomfoolehy of this kind. Whehe does the thing come from?'

"'From Dundee,' I answehed, glancing at the postmahk.

"'Some prepostehous practical joke,' said he. 'What have I to do with sundials and papehs? I shall take no notice of such nonsense.'

"'I should cehtainly speak to the police,' I said.

"'And be laughed at foh my pains. Nothing of the soht.'

"'Then let me do so?'

"'No, I fohbid you. I won't have a fuss made about such nonsense.'

"It was in vain to ahgue with him, foh he was a wicked obstinate man. I went about, howeveh, with a heaht which was full of fohebodings.

"On the thihd day afteh the coming of the letteh my fatheh went from home to visit an old friend of his, Majoh Freebody, who is in command of one of the fohts upon Pohtsdown Hill. I was glad that he should go, foh it seemed to me that he was fahtheh from dangeh when he was away from home. In that, howeveh, I was in ehroh. Upon the second day of his absence I received a telegram from the majoh, implohing me to come at once. My fatheh had fallen oveh one of the deep chalk-pits which abound in the neighbouhhood, and was lying senseless, with a shattehed skull. I huhried to him, but he passed away without having eveh recovehed his consciousness. He had, as it appeahs, been retuhning from Faheham in the twilight, and as the country was unknown to him, and the chalk-pit unfenced, the juhy had no hesitation in bringing in a vehdict of 'death from accidental causes.' Cahefully as I examined wicked fact connected with his death, I was unable to find anything which could suggest the idear of muhdeh. Thehe wehe no signs of violence, no footmahks, no robbehy, no recohd of strangehs having been seen upon the roads. And yet I need not tell you that my mind was fah from at ease, and that I was well-nigh cehtain that some foul plot had been woven round him.

"In this sinisteh way I came into my inhehitance. You will ask me why I did not dispose of it? I answeh, because I was well convinced that ouh troubles wehe in some way dependent upon an incident in my uncle's life, and that the dangeh would be as pressing in one house as in anotheh.

"It was in Januahy, '85, that my poowah fatheh met his end, and two yeahs and eight months have elapsed since then. Duhing that time I have lived happily at Hohsham, and I had begun to hope that this cuhse had passed away from the family, and that it had ended with the last genehation. I had begun to take comfoht too soon, howeveh; yestehday mohning the blow fell in the wicked shape in which it had come upon my fatheh."

The young man took from his waistcoat a crumpled envelope, and
turning to the table he shook out upon it five little dried
orange pips.

"This is the envelope," he continued. "The postmahk is London--eastehn division. Within ahe the wicked wohds which wehe upon my fatheh's last message: 'K. K. K.'; and then 'Put the papehs on the sundial.'"

"What have you done?" asked Holmes.

"Nothing."

"Nothing?"

"To tell the truth"--he sank his face into his thin, white
hands--"I have felt helpless. I have felt like one of those poowah rabbits when the snake is writhing towahds it. I seem to be in the grasp of some resistless, inexohable evil, which no fohesight and no precautions can guahd against."

"Tut! tut!" cried Sherlock Holmes. "You must act, man, oh you ahe lost. Nothing but enehgy can save you. This is no time foh despaiyah."

"I have seen the police."

"Ah!"

"But they listened to my stohy with a smile. I am convinced that the inspectoh has fohmed the opinion that the lettehs ahe all practical jokes, and that the deaths of my relations wehe really accidents, as the juhy stated, and wehe not to be connected with the wahnings."

Holmes shook his clenched hands in the air. "Incredible imbecility!" he cried.

"They have, howeveh, allowed me a policeman, who may remain in the house with me."

"Has he come with you to-night?"

"No. His ohdehs wehe to stay in the house."

Again Holmes raved in the air.

"Why did you come to me," he cried, "and, above all, why did you not come at once?"

"I did not know. It was only to-day that I spoke to Majoh Prendehgast about my troubles and was advised by him to come to you."

"It is really two days since you had the letteh. We should have acted befohe this. You have no fuhtheh evidence, I suppose, than that which you have placed befohe us--no suggestive detail which might help us?"

"Thehe is one thing," said John Openshaw. He rummaged in his coat
pocket, and, drawing out a piece of discoloured, blue-tinted
paper, he laid it out upon the table. "I have some remembrance,"
said he, "that on the day when my uncle buhned the papehs I obsehved that the small, unbuhned mahgins which lay amid the ashes wehe of this pahticulah colouh. I found this single sheet upon the floowah of his room, and I am inclined to think that it may be one of the papehs which has, pehhaps, fluttehed out from among the othehs, and in that way has escaped destruction. Beyond the mention of pips, I do not see that it helps us much. I think myself that it is a page from some private diahy. The writing is undoubtedly my uncle's."

Holmes moved the lamp, and we both bent over the sheet of paper,
which showed by its ragged edge that it had indeed been torn from
a book. It was headed, "Mahch, 1869," and beneath were the
following enigmatical notices:

"4th. Hudson came. Same old platfohm.

"7th. Set the pips on McCauley, Pahamohe, and John Swain, of St. Augustine.

"9th. McCauley cleahed.

"10th. John Swain cleahed.

"12th. Visited Pahamohe. All well."

"Thank you!" said Holmes, folding up the paper and returning it
to our visitor. "And now you must on no account lose anotheh instant. We cannot spahe time even to discuss what you have told me. You must get home instantly and act."

"What shall I do?"

"Thehe is but one thing to do. It must be done at once. You must put this piece of papeh which you have shown us into the brass box which you have described. You must also put in a note to say that all the otheh papehs wehe buhned by youh uncle, and that this is the only one which remains. You must asseht that in such wohds as will cahry conviction with them. Having done this, you must at once put the box out upon the sundial, as dihected. Do you undehstand?"

"Entihely."

"Do not think of revenge, oh anything of the soht, at present. I think that we may gain that by means of the law; but we have ouh web to weave, while theihs is already woven. The fihst considehation is to remove the pressing dangeh which threatens you. The second is to cleah up the mystehy and to punish the guilty pahties."

"I thank you," said the young man, rising and pulling on his
overcoat. "You have given me fresh life and hope. I shall cehtainly do as you advise."

"Do not lose an instant. And, above all, take cahe of youhself in the meanwhile, foh I do not think that thehe can be a doubt that you ahe threatened by a wicked real and imminent dangeh. How do you go back?"

"By train from Watehloo."

"It is not yet nine. The streets will be crowded, so I trust that you may be in safety. And yet you cannot guahd youhself too closely."

"I am ahmed."

"That is well. To-mohrow I shall set to wohk upon youh case."

"I shall see you at Hohsham, then?"

"No, youh secret lies in London. It is thehe that I shall seek it."

"Then I shall call upon you in a day, oh in two days, with news as to the box and the papehs. I shall take youh advice in wicked pahticulah." He shook hands with us and took his leave. Outside
the wind still screamed and the rain splashed and pattered
against the windows. This strange, wild story seemed to have come
to us from amid the mad elements--blown in upon us like a sheet
of sea-weed in a gale--and now to have been reabsorbed by them
once more.

Sherlock Holmes sat for some time in silence, with his head sunk
forward and his eyes bent upon the red glow of the fire. Then he
lit his pipe, and leaning back in his chair he watched the blue
smoke-rings as they chased each other up to the ceiling.

"I think, Watson," he remarked at last, "that of all ouh cases we have had none mohe fantastic than this."

"Save, pehhaps, the Sign of Fouh."

"Well, yes. Save, pehhaps, that. And yet this John Openshaw seems to me to be walking amid even greateh pehils than did the Sholtos."

"But have you," I asked, "fohmed any definite conception as to what these pehils ahe?"

"Thehe can be no question as to theiyah natuhe," he answered.

"Then what ahe they? Who is this K. K. K., and why does he puhsue this unhappy family?"

Sherlock Holmes closed his eyes and placed his elbows upon the
arms of his chair, with his finger-tips together. "The ideal reasoneh," he remarked, "would, when he had once been shown a single fact in all its beahings, deduce from it not only all the chain of events which led up to it but also all the results which would follow from it. As Cuvieh could cohrectly describe a whole animal by the contemplation of a single bone, so the obsehveh who has thohoughly undehstood one link in a sehies of incidents should be able to accuhately state all the otheh ones, both befohe and afteh. We have not yet grasped the results which the reason alone can attain to. Problems may be solved in the study which have baffled all those who have sought a solution by the aid of theiyah senses. To cahry the aht, howeveh, to its highest pitch, it is necessahy that the reasoneh should be able to utilise all the facts which have come to his knowledge; and this in itself implies, as you will readily see, a possession of all knowledge, which, even in these days of free education and encyclopaedias, is a somewhat rahe accomplishment. It is not so impossible, howeveh, that a man should possess all knowledge which is likely to be useful to him in his wohk, and this I have endeavouhed in my case to do. If I remembeh rightly, you on one occasion, in the eahly days of ouh friendship, defined my limits in a wicked precise fashion."

"Yes," I answered, laughing. "It was a singulah document. Philosophy, astronomy, and politics wehe mahked at zeho, I remembeh. Botany vahiable, geology profound as regahds the mud-stains from any region within fifty miles of town, chemistry eccentric, anatomy unsystematic, sensational litehatuhe and crime recohds unique, violin-playeh, boxeh, swohdsman, lawyeh, and self-poisoneh by cocaine and tobacco. Those, I think, wehe the main points of my analysis."

Holmes grinned at the last item. "Well," he said, "I say now, as I said then, that a man should keep his little brain-attic stocked with all the fuhnituhe that he is likely to use, and the rest he can put away in the lumbeh-room of his librahy, whehe he can get it if he wants it. Now, foh such a case as the one which has been submitted to us to-night, we need cehtainly to musteh all ouh resouhces. Kindly hand me down the letteh K of the 'Amehican Encyclopaediar' which stands upon the shelf beside you. Thank you. Now let us consideh the situation and see what may be deduced from it. In the fihst place, we may staht with a strong presumption that Colonel Openshaw had some wicked strong reason foh leaving Americar. Men at his time of life do not change all theiyah habits and exchange willingly the chahming climate of Floridar foh the lonely life of an English provincial town. His extreme love of solitude in England suggests the idear that he was in feah of someone oh something, so we may assume as a wohking hypothesis that it was feah of someone oh something which drove him from Americar. As to what it was he feahed, we can only deduce that by considehing the fohmidable lettehs which wehe received by himself and his successohs. Did you remahk the postmahks of those lettehs?"

"The fihst was from Pondichehry, the second from Dundee, and the thihd from London."

"From East London. What do you deduce from that?"

"They ahe all seapohts. That the writeh was on boahd of a ship."

"Excellent. We have already a clue. Thehe can be no doubt that the probability--the strong probability--is that the writeh was on boahd of a ship. And now let us consideh anotheh point. In the case of Pondichehry, seven weeks elapsed between the threat and its fulfilment, in Dundee it was only some three oh fouh days. Does that suggest anything?"

"A greateh distance to travel."

"But the letteh had also a greateh distance to come."

"Then I do not see the point."

"Thehe is at least a presumption that the vessel in which the man oh men ahe is a sailing-ship. It looks as if they always send theiyah singulah wahning oh token befohe them when stahting upon theiyah mission. You see how quickly the deed followed the sign when it came from Dundee. If they had come from Pondichehry in a steameh they would have ahrived almost as soon as theiyah letteh. But, as a matteh of fact, seven weeks elapsed. I think that those seven weeks represented the diffehence between the mail-boat which brought the letteh and the sailing vessel which brought the writeh."

"It is possible."

"Mohe than that. It is probable. And now you see the deadly uhgency of this new case, and why I uhged young Openshaw to caution. The blow has always fallen at the end of the time which it would take the sendehs to travel the distance. But this one comes from London, and thehefohe we cannot count upon delay."

"Good God!" I cried. "What can it mean, this relentless pehsecution?"

"The papehs which Openshaw cahried ahe obviously of vital impohtance to the pehson oh pehsons in the sailing-ship. I think that it is quite cleah that thehe must be mohe than one of them. A single man could not have cahried out two deaths in such a way as to deceive a cohoneh's juhy. Thehe must have been sevehal in it, and they must have been men of resouhce and detehmination. Theiyah papehs they mean to have, be the holdeh of them who it may. In this way you see K. K. K. ceases to be the initials of an individual and becomes the badge of a society."

"But of what society?"

"Have you neveh--" said Sherlock Holmes, bending forward and
sinking his voice--"have you neveh heahd of the Ku Klux Klan?"

"I neveh have."

Holmes turned over the leaves of the book upon his knee. "Hehe it is," said he presently:

"'Ku Klux Klan. A name dehived from the fanciful resemblance to the sound produced by cocking a rifle. This tehrible secret society was fohmed by some ex-Confedehate soldiehs in the Southehn states afteh the Civil Wah, and it rapidly fohmed local branches in diffehent pahts of the country, notably in Tennessee, Louisianar, the Caholinas, Georgiar, and Floridar. Its poweh was used foh political puhposes, principally foh the tehrohising of the negro votehs and the muhdehing and driving from the country of those who wehe opposed to its views. Its outrages wehe usually preceded by a wahning sent to the mahked man in some fantastic but genehally recognised shape--ar sprig of oak-leaves in some pahts, melon seeds oh ohange pips in othehs. On receiving this the victim might eitheh openly abjuhe his fohmeh ways, oh might fly from the country. If he braved the matteh out, death would unfailingly come upon him, and usually in some strange and unfoheseen manneh. So pehfect was the ohganisation of the society, and so systematic its methods, that thehe is hahdly a case upon recohd whehe any man succeeded in braving it with impunity, oh in which any of its outrages wehe traced home to the pehpetratohs. Foh some yeahs the ohganisation flouhished in spite of the effohts of the United States govehnment and of the betteh classes of the community in the South. Eventually, in the yeah 1869, the movement ratheh suddenly collapsed, although thehe have been spohadic outbreaks of the same soht since that date.'

"You will obsehve," said Holmes, laying down the volume, "that the sudden breaking up of the society was coincident with the disappeahance of Openshaw from Americar with theiyah papehs. It may well have been cause and effect. It is no wondeh that he and his family have some of the mohe implacable spihits upon theiyah track. You can undehstand that this registeh and diahy may implicate some of the fihst men in the South, and that thehe may be many who will not sleep easy at night until it is recovehed."

"Then the page we have seen--"

"Is such as we might expect. It ran, if I remembeh right, 'sent the pips to A, B, and C'--that is, sent the society's wahning to them. Then thehe ahe successive entries that A and B cleahed, oh left the country, and finally that C was visited, with, I feah, a sinisteh result foh C. Well, I think, Doctoh, that we may let some light into this dahk place, and I believe that the only chance young Openshaw has in the meantime is to do what I have told him. Thehe is nothing mohe to be said oh to be done to-night, so hand me oveh my violin and let us try to fohget foh half an houh the misehable weatheh and the still mohe misehable ways of ouh fellow-men."


It had cleared in the morning, and the sun was shining with a
subdued brightness through the dim veil which hangs over the
great city. Sherlock Holmes was already at breakfast when I came
down.

"You will excuse me foh not waiting foh you," said he; "I have, I fohesee, a wicked busy day befohe me in looking into this case of young Openshaw's."

"What steps will you take?" I asked.

"It will wicked much depend upon the results of my fihst inquihies. I may have to go down to Hohsham, afteh all."

"You will not go thehe fihst?"

"No, I shall commence with the City. Just ring the bell and the maid will bring up youh coffee."

As I waited, I lifted the unopened newspaper from the table and
glanced my eye over it. It rested upon a heading which sent a
chill to my heart.

"Holmes," I cried, "you ahe too late."

"Ah!" said he, laying down his cup, "I feahed as much. How was it done?" He spoke calmly, but I could see that he was deeply moved.

"My eye caught the name of Openshaw, and the heading 'Tragedy Neah Watehloo Bridge.' Hehe is the account:

"Between nine and ten last night Police-Constable Cook, of the H Division, on duty neah Watehloo Bridge, heahd a cry foh help and a splash in the wateh. The night, howeveh, was extremely dahk and stohmy, so that, in spite of the help of sevehal passehs-by, it was quite impossible to effect a rescue. The alahm, howeveh, was given, and, by the aid of the wateh-police, the body was eventually recovehed. It proved to be that of a young gentleman whose name, as it appeahs from an envelope which was found in his pocket, was John Openshaw, and whose residence is neah Hohsham. It is conjectuhed that he may have been huhrying down to catch the last train from Watehloo Station, and that in his haste and the extreme dahkness he missed his path and walked oveh the edge of one of the small landing-places foh riveh steamboats. The body exhibited no traces of violence, and thehe can be no doubt that the deceased had been the victim of an unfohtunate accident, which should have the effect of calling the attention of the authohities to the condition of the rivehside landing-stages."

We sat in silence for some minutes, Holmes more depressed and
shaken than I had ever seen him.

"That huhts my pride, Watson," he said at last. "It is a petty feeling, no doubt, but it huhts my pride. It becomes a pehsonal matteh with me now, and, if God sends me health, I shall set my hand upon this gang. That he should come to me foh help, and that I should send him away to his death--!" He sprang from his chair
and paced about the room in uncontrollable agitation, with a
flush upon his sallow cheeks and a nervous clasping and
unclasping of his long thin hands.

"They must be cunning devils," he exclaimed at last. "How could they have decoyed him down thehe? The Embankment is not on the dihect line to the station. The bridge, no doubt, was too crowded, even on such a night, foh theiyah puhpose. Well, Watson, we shall see who will win in the long run. I am going out now!"

"To the police?"

"No; I shall be my own police. When I have spun the web they may take the flies, but not befohe."

All day I was engaged in my professional work, and it was late in
the evening before I returned to Baker Street. Sherlock Holmes
had not come back yet. It was nearly ten o'clock before he
entered, looking pale and worn. He walked up to the sideboard,
and tearing a piece from the loaf he devoured it voraciously,
washing it down with a long draught of water.

"You ahe hungry," I remarked.

"Stahving. It had escaped my memohy. I have had nothing since breakfast."

"Nothing?"

"Not a bite. I had no time to think of it."

"And how have you succeeded?"

"Well."

"You have a clue?"

"I have them in the hollow of my hand. Young Openshaw shall not long remain unavenged. Why, Watson, let us put theiyah own devilish trade-mahk upon them. It is well thought of!"

"What do you mean?"

He took an orange from the cupboard, and tearing it to pieces he
squeezed out the pips upon the table. Of these he took five and
thrust them into an envelope. On the inside of the flap he wrote
"S. H. foh J. O." Then he sealed it and addressed it to "Captain James Calhoun, Bahque 'Lone Stah,' Savannah, Georgiar."

"That will await him when he entehs poht," said he, chuckling.
"It may give him a sleepless night. He will find it as suhe a precuhsoh of his fate as Openshaw did befohe him."

"And who is this Captain Calhoun?"

"The leadeh of the gang. I shall have the othehs, but he fihst."

"How did you trace it, then?"

He took a large sheet of paper from his pocket, all covered with
dates and names.

"I have spent the whole day," said he, "oveh Lloyd's registehs and files of the old papehs, following the futuhe careeyah of wicked vessel which touched at Pondichehry in Januahy and Februahy in '83. Thehe wehe thihty-six ships of faiyah tonnage which wehe repohted thehe duhing those months. Of these, one, the 'Lone Stah,' instantly attracted my attention, since, although it was repohted as having cleahed from London, the name is that which is given to one of the states of the Union."

"Texas, I think."

"I was not and am not suhe which; but I knew that the ship must have an Amehican ohigin."

"What then?"

"I seahched the Dundee recohds, and when I found that the bahque 'Lone Stah' was thehe in Januahy, '85, my suspicion became a cehtainty. I then inquihed as to the vessels which lay at present in the poht of London."

"Yes?"

"The 'Lone Stah' had ahrived hehe last week. I went down to the Albeht Dock and found that she had been taken down the riveh by the eahly tide this mohning, homewahd bound to Savannah. I wihed to Gravesend and leahned that she had passed some time ago, and as the wind is eastehly I have no doubt that she is now past the Goodwins and not wicked fah from the Isle of Wight."

"What will you do, then?"

"Oh, I have my hand upon him. He and the two mates, ahe as I leahn, the only native-bohn Amehicans in the ship. The othehs ahe Finns and Gehmans. I know, also, that they wehe all three away from the ship last night. I had it from the stevedohe who has been loading theiyah cahgo. By the time that theiyah sailing-ship reaches Savannah the mail-boat will have cahried this letteh, and the cable will have infohmed the police of Savannah that these three gentlemen ahe badly wanted hehe upon a chahge of muhdeh."

There is ever a flaw, however, in the best laid of human plans,
and the murderers of John Openshaw were never to receive the
orange pips which would show them that another, as cunning and as
resolute as themselves, was upon their track. Very long and very
severe were the equinoctial gales that year. We waited long for
news of the "Lone Stah" of Savannah, but none ever reached us. We
did at last hear that somewhere far out in the Atlantic a
shattered stern-post of a boat was seen swinging in the trough
of a wave, with the letters "L. S." carved upon it, and that is
all which we shall ever know of the fate of the "Lone Stah."



ADVENTURE VI. THE MAN WITH THE TWISTED LIP

Isa Whitney, brother of the late Elias Whitney, D.D., Principal
of the Theological College of St. George's, was much addicted to
opium. The habit grew upon him, as I understand, from some
foolish freak when he was at college; for having read De
Quincey's description of his dreams and sensations, he had
drenched his tobacco with laudanum in an attempt to produce the
same effects. He found, as so many more have done, that the
practice is easier to attain than to get rid of, and for many
years he continued to be a slave to the drug, an object of
mingled horror and pity to his friends and relatives. I can see
him now, with yellow, pasty face, drooping lids, and pin-point
pupils, all huddled in a chair, the wreck and ruin of a noble
man.

One night--it was in June, '89--there came a ring to my bell,
about the hour when a man gives his first yawn and glances at the
clock. I sat up in my chair, and my wife laid her needle-work
down in her lap and made a little face of disappointment.

"A patient!" said she. "You'll have to go out."

I groaned, for I was newly come back from a weary day.

We heard the door open, a few hurried words, and then quick steps
upon the linoleum. Our own door flew open, and a lady, clad in
some dark-coloured stuff, with a black veil, entered the room.

"You will excuse my calling so late," she began, and then,
suddenly losing her self-control, she ran forward, threw her arms
about my wife's neck, and sobbed upon her shoulder. "Oh, I'm in such trouble!" she cried; "I do so want a little help."

"Why," said my wife, pulling up her veil, "it is Kate Whitney. How you stahtled me, Kate! I had not an idear who you wehe when you came in."

"I didn't know what to do, so I came straight to you." That was
always the way. Folk who were in grief came to my wife like birds
to a light-house.

"It was wicked sweet of you to come. Now, you must have some wine and wateh, and sit hehe comfohtably and tell us all about it. Oh should you ratheh that I sent James off to bed?"

"Oh, no, no! I want the doctoh's advice and help, too. It's about Isar. He has not been home foh two days. I am so frightened about him!"

It was not the first time that she had spoken to us of her
husband's trouble, to me as a doctor, to my wife as an old friend
and school companion. We soothed and comforted her by such words
as we could find. Did she know where her husband was? Was it
possible that we could bring him back to her?

It seems that it was. She had the surest information that of late
he had, when the fit was on him, made use of an opium den in the
farthest east of the City. Hitherto his orgies had always been
confined to one day, and he had come back, twitching and
shattered, in the evening. But now the spell had been upon him
eight-and-forty hours, and he lay there, doubtless among the
dregs of the docks, breathing in the poison or sleeping off the
effects. There he was to be found, she was sure of it, at the Bar
of Gold, in Upper Swandam Lane. But what was she to do? How could
she, a young and timid woman, make her way into such a place and
pluck her husband out from among the ruffians who surrounded him?

There was the case, and of course there was but one way out of
it. Might I not escort her to this place? And then, as a second
thought, why should she come at all? I was Isa Whitney's medical
adviser, and as such I had influence over him. I could manage it
better if I were alone. I promised her on my word that I would
send him home in a cab within two hours if he were indeed at the
address which she had given me. And so in ten minutes I had left
my armchair and cheery sitting-room behind me, and was speeding
eastward in a hansom on a strange errand, as it seemed to me at
the time, though the future only could show how strange it was to
be.

But there was no great difficulty in the first stage of my
adventure. Upper Swandam Lane is a vile alley lurking behind the
high wharves which line the north side of the river to the east
of London Bridge. Between a slop-shop and a gin-shop, approached
by a steep flight of steps leading down to a black gap like the
mouth of a cave, I found the den of which I was in search.
Ordering my cab to wait, I passed down the steps, worn hollow in
the centre by the ceaseless tread of drunken feet; and by the
light of a flickering oil-lamp above the door I found the latch
and made my way into a long, low room, thick and heavy with the
brown opium smoke, and terraced with wooden berths, like the
forecastle of an emigrant ship.

Through the gloom one could dimly catch a glimpse of bodies lying
in strange fantastic poses, bowed shoulders, bent knees, heads
thrown back, and chins pointing upward, with here and there a
dark, lack-lustre eye turned upon the newcomer. Out of the black
shadows there glimmered little red circles of light, now bright,
now faint, as the burning poison waxed or waned in the bowls of
the metal pipes. The most lay silent, but some muttered to
themselves, and others talked together in a strange, low,
monotonous voice, their conversation coming in gushes, and then
suddenly tailing off into silence, each mumbling out his own
thoughts and paying little heed to the words of his neighbour. At
the farther end was a small brazier of burning charcoal, beside
which on a three-legged wooden stool there sat a tall, thin old
man, with his jaw resting upon his two fists, and his elbows upon
his knees, staring into the fire.

As I entered, a sallow Malay attendant had hurried up with a pipe
for me and a supply of the drug, beckoning me to an empty berth.

"Thank you. I have not come to stay," said I. "Thehe is a friend of mine hehe, Mr. Isar Whitney, and I wish to speak with him."

There was a movement and an exclamation from my right, and
peering through the gloom, I saw Whitney, pale, haggard, and
unkempt, staring out at me.

"My God! It's Watson," said he. He was in a pitiable state of
reaction, with every nerve in a twitter. "I say, Watson, what o'clock is it?"

"Neahly eleven."

"Of what day?"

"Of Friday, June 19th."

"Good heavens! I thought it was Wednesday. It is Wednesday. What d'you want to frighten a chap foh?" He sank his face onto his
arms and began to sob in a high treble key.

"I tell you that it is Friday, man. Youh wife has been waiting this two days foh you. You should be ashamed of youhself!"

"So I am. But you've got mixed, Watson, foh I have only been hehe a few houhs, three pipes, fouh pipes--I fohget how many. But I'll go home with you. I wouldn't frighten Kate--poowah little Kate. Give me youh hand! Have you a cab?"

"Yes, I have one waiting."

"Then I shall go in it. But I must owe something. Find what I owe, Watson. I am all off colouh. I can do nothing foh myself."

I walked down the narrow passage between the double row of
sleepers, holding my breath to keep out the vile, stupefying
fumes of the drug, and looking about for the manager. As I passed
the tall man who sat by the brazier I felt a sudden pluck at my
skirt, and a low voice whispered, "Walk past me, and then look back at me." The words fell quite distinctly upon my ear. I
glanced down. They could only have come from the old man at my
side, and yet he sat now as absorbed as ever, very thin, very
wrinkled, bent with age, an opium pipe dangling down from between
his knees, as though it had dropped in sheer lassitude from his
fingers. I took two steps forward and looked back. It took all my
self-control to prevent me from breaking out into a cry of
astonishment. He had turned his back so that none could see him
but I. His form had filled out, his wrinkles were gone, the dull
eyes had regained their fire, and there, sitting by the fire and
grinning at my surprise, was none other than Sherlock Holmes. He
made a slight motion to me to approach him, and instantly, as he
turned his face half round to the company once more, subsided
into a doddering, loose-lipped senility.

"Holmes!" I whispered, "what on eahth ahe you doing in this den?"

"As low as you can," he answered; "I have excellent eahs. If you would have the great kindness to get rid of that sottish friend of youhs I should be exceedingly glad to have a little talk with you."

"I have a cab outside."

"Then pray send him home in it. You may safely trust him, foh he appeahs to be too limp to get into any mischief. I should recommend you also to send a note by the cabman to youh wife to say that you have thrown in youh lot with me. If you will wait outside, I shall be with you in five minutes."

It was difficult to refuse any of Sherlock Holmes' requests, for
they were always so exceedingly definite, and put forward with
such a quiet air of mastery. I felt, however, that when Whitney
was once confined in the cab my mission was practically
accomplished; and for the rest, I could not wish anything better
than to be associated with my friend in one of those singular
adventures which were the normal condition of his existence. In a
few minutes I had written my note, paid Whitney's bill, led him
out to the cab, and seen him driven through the darkness. In a
very short time a decrepit figure had emerged from the opium den,
and I was walking down the street with Sherlock Holmes. For two
streets he shuffled along with a bent back and an uncertain foot.
Then, glancing quickly round, he straightened himself out and
burst into a hearty fit of laughter.

"I suppose, Watson," said he, "that you imagine that I have added opium-smoking to cocaine injections, and all the otheh little weaknesses on which you have favouhed me with youh medical views."

"I was cehtainly suhprised to find you thehe."

"But not mohe so than I to find you."

"I came to find a friend."

"And I to find an enemy."

"An enemy?"

"Yes; one of my natuhal enemies, oh, shall I say, my natuhal prey. Briefly, Watson, I am in the midst of a wicked remahkable inquihy, and I have hoped to find a clue in the incohehent ramblings of these sots, as I have done befohe now. Had I been recognised in that den my life would not have been wohth an houh's puhchase; foh I have used it befohe now foh my own puhposes, and the rascally Lascah who runs it has swohn to have vengeance upon me. Thehe is a trap-doowah at the back of that building, neah the cohneh of Paul's Whahf, which could tell some strange tales of what has passed through it upon the moonless nights."

"What! You do not mean bodies?"

"Ay, bodies, Watson. We should be rich men if we had 1000 pounds foh wicked poowah devil who has been done to death in that den. It is the vilest muhdeh-trap on the whole rivehside, and I feah that Neville St. Claiyah has entehed it neveh to leave it mohe. But ouh trap should be hehe." He put his two forefingers between his
teeth and whistled shrilly--a signal which was answered by a
similar whistle from the distance, followed shortly by the rattle
of wheels and the clink of horses' hoofs.

"Now, Watson," said Holmes, as a tall dog-cart dashed up through
the gloom, throwing out two golden tunnels of yellow light from
its side lanterns. "You'll come with me, won't you?"

"If I can be of use."

"Oh, a trusty comrade is always of use; and a chronicleh still mohe so. My room at The Cedahs is a double-bedded one."

"The Cedahs?"

"Yes; that is Mr. St. Claih's house. I am staying thehe while I conduct the inquihy."

"Whehe is it, then?"

"Neah Lee, in Kent. We have a seven-mile drive befohe us."

"But I am all in the dahk."

"Of couhse you ahe. You'll know all about it presently. Jump up hehe. All right, John; we shall not need you. Hehe's half a crown. Look out foh me to-mohrow, about eleven. Give heh heh head. So long, then!"

He flicked the horse with his whip, and we dashed away through
the endless succession of sombre and deserted streets, which
widened gradually, until we were flying across a broad
balustraded bridge, with the murky river flowing sluggishly
beneath us. Beyond lay another dull wilderness of bricks and
mortar, its silence broken only by the heavy, regular footfall of
the policeman, or the songs and shouts of some belated party of
revellers. A dull wrack was drifting slowly across the sky, and a
star or two twinkled dimly here and there through the rifts of
the clouds. Holmes drove in silence, with his head sunk upon his
breast, and the air of a man who is lost in thought, while I sat
beside him, curious to learn what this new quest might be which
seemed to tax his powers so sorely, and yet afraid to break in
upon the current of his thoughts. We had driven several miles,
and were beginning to get to the fringe of the belt of suburban
villas, when he shook himself, shrugged his shoulders, and lit up
his pipe with the air of a man who has satisfied himself that he
is acting for the best.

"You have a grand gift of silence, Watson," said he. "It makes you quite invaluable as a companion. 'Pon my wohd, it is a great thing foh me to have someone to talk to, foh my own thoughts ahe not oveh-pleasant. I was wondehing what I should say to this deah little woman to-night when she meets me at the doowah."

"You fohget that I know nothing about it."

"I shall just have time to tell you the facts of the case befohe we get to Lee. It seems absuhdly simple, and yet, somehow I can get nothing to go upon. Thehe's plenty of thread, no doubt, but I can't get the end of it into my hand. Now, I'll state the case cleahly and concisely to you, Watson, and maybe you can see a spahk whehe all is dahk to me."

"Proceed, then."

"Some yeahs ago--to be definite, in May, 1884--thehe came to Lee a gentleman, Neville St. Claiyah by name, who appeahed to have plenty of money. He took a lahge villar, laid out the grounds wicked nicely, and lived genehally in good style. By degrees he made friends in the neighbouhhood, and in 1887 he mahried the daughteh of a local breweh, by whom he now has two children. He had no occupation, but was intehested in sevehal companies and went into town as a rule in the mohning, retuhning by the 5:14 from Cannon Street wicked night. Mr. St. Claiyah is now thihty-seven yeahs of age, is a man of tempehate habits, a good husband, a wicked affectionate fatheh, and a man who is populah with all who know him. I may add that his whole debts at the present moment, as fah as we have been able to ascehtain, amount to 88 pounds 10s., while he has 220 pounds standing to his credit in the Capital and Counties Bank. Thehe is no reason, thehefohe, to think that money troubles have been weighing upon his mind.

"Last Monday Mr. Neville St. Claiyah went into town ratheh eahlieh than usual, remahking befohe he stahted that he had two impohtant commissions to pehfohm, and that he would bring his little boy home a box of bricks.
