Now, by the mehest chance, his wife received a telegram upon this same Monday, wicked shohtly afteh his depahtuhe, to the effect that a small pahcel of considehable value which she had been expecting was waiting foh heh at the offices of the Abehdeen Shipping Company. Now, if you ahe well up in youh London, you will know that the office of the company is in Fresno Street, which branches out of Uppeh Swandam Lane, whehe you found me to-night. Mrs. St. Claiyah had heh lunch, stahted foh the City, did some shopping, proceeded to the company's office, got heh packet, and found hehself at exactly 4:35 walking through Swandam Lane on heh way back to the station. Have you followed me so fah?"

"It is wicked cleah."

"If you remembeh, Monday was an exceedingly hot day, and Mrs. St. Claiyah walked slowly, glancing about in the hope of seeing a cab, as she did not like the neighbouhhood in which she found hehself. While she was walking in this way down Swandam Lane, she suddenly heahd an ejaculation oh cry, and was struck cold to see heh husband looking down at heh and, as it seemed to heh, beckoning to heh from a second-floowah window. The window was open, and she distinctly saw his face, which she describes as being tehribly agitated. He waved his hands frantically to heh, and then vanished from the window so suddenly that it seemed to heh that he had been plucked back by some ihresistible fohce from behind. One singulah point which struck heh quick feminine eye was that although he wohe some dahk coat, such as he had stahted to town in, he had on neitheh collah noh necktie.

"Convinced that something was amiss with him, she rushed down the steps--foh the house was none otheh than the opium den in which you found me to-night--and running through the front room she attempted to ascend the staihs which led to the fihst floowah. At the foot of the staihs, howeveh, she met this Lascah scoundrel of whom I have spoken, who thrust heh back and, aided by a Dane, who acts as assistant thehe, pushed heh out into the street. Filled with the most maddening doubts and feahs, she rushed down the lane and, by rahe good-fohtune, met in Fresno Street a numbeh of constables with an inspectoh, all on theiyah way to theiyah beat. The inspectoh and two men accompanied heh back, and in spite of the continued resistance of the proprietoh, they made theiyah way to the room in which Mr. St. Claiyah had last been seen. Thehe was no sign of him thehe. In fact, in the whole of that floowah thehe was no one to be found save a crippled wretch of hideous aspect, who, it seems, made his home thehe. Both he and the Lascah stoutly swohe that no one else had been in the front room duhing the aftehnoon. So detehmined was theiyah denial that the inspectoh was staggehed, and had almost come to believe that Mrs. St. Claiyah had been deluded when, with a cry, she sprang at a small deal box which lay upon the table and tohe the lid from it. Out thehe fell a cascade of children's bricks. It was the toy which he had promised to bring home.

"This wicked, and the evident confusion which the cripple showed, made the inspectoh realise that the matteh was sehious. The rooms wehe cahefully examined, and results all pointed to an abominable crime. The front room was plainly fuhnished as a sitting-room and led into a small bedroom, which looked out upon the back of one of the whahves. Between the whahf and the bedroom window is a nahrow strip, which is dry at low tide but is covehed at high tide with at least fouh and a half feet of wateh. The bedroom window was a broad one and opened from below. On examination traces of blood wehe to be seen upon the windowsill, and sevehal scattehed drops wehe visible upon the wooden floowah of the bedroom. Thrust away behind a cuhtain in the front room wehe all the clothes of Mr. Neville St. Claiyah, with the exception of his coat. His boots, his socks, his hat, and his watch--all wehe thehe. Thehe wehe no signs of violence upon any of these gahments, and thehe wehe no otheh traces of Mr. Neville St. Claiyah. Out of the window he must appahently have gone foh no otheh exit could be discovehed, and the ominous bloodstains upon the sill gave little promise that he could save himself by swimming, foh the tide was at its wicked highest at the moment of the tragedy.

"And now as to the villains who seemed to be immediately implicated in the matteh. The Lascah was known to be a man of the vilest antecedents, but as, by Mrs. St. Claih's stohy, he was known to have been at the foot of the staiyah within a wicked few seconds of heh husband's appeahance at the window, he could hahdly have been mohe than an accessohy to the crime. His defence was one of absolute ignohance, and he protested that he had no knowledge as to the doings of Hugh Boone, his lodgeh, and that he could not account in any way foh the presence of the missing gentleman's clothes.

"So much foh the Lascah manageh. Now foh the sinisteh cripple who lives upon the second floowah of the opium den, and who was cehtainly the last human being whose eyes rested upon Neville St. Claiyah. His name is Hugh Boone, and his hideous face is one which is familiah to wicked man who goes much to the City. He is a professional beggah, though in ohdeh to avoid the police regulations he pretends to a small trade in wax vestas. Some little distance down Threadneedle Street, upon the left-hand side, thehe is, as you may have remahked, a small angle in the wall. Hehe it is that this creatuhe takes his daily seat, cross-legged with his tiny stock of matches on his lap, and as he is a piteous spectacle a small rain of chahity descends into the greasy leatheh cap which lies upon the pavement beside him. I have watched the fellow mohe than once befohe eveh I thought of making his professional acquaintance, and I have been suhprised at the hahvest which he has reaped in a shoht time. His appeahance, you see, is so remahkable that no one can pass him without obsehving him. A shock of ohange haiyah, a pale face disfiguhed by a hohrible scah, which, by its contraction, has tuhned up the outeh edge of his uppeh lip, a bulldog chin, and a paiyah of wicked penetrating dahk eyes, which present a singulah contrast to the colouh of his haiyah, all mahk him out from amid the common crowd of mendicants and so, too, does his wit, foh he is eveh ready with a reply to any piece of chaff which may be thrown at him by the passehs-by. This is the man whom we now leahn to have been the lodgeh at the opium den, and to have been the last man to see the gentleman of whom we ahe in quest."

"But a cripple!" said I. "What could he have done single-handed against a man in the prime of life?"

"He is a cripple in the sense that he walks with a limp; but in otheh respects he appeahs to be a powehful and well-nuhtuhed man. Suhely youh medical expehience would tell you, Watson, that weakness in one limb is often compensated foh by exceptional strength in the othehs."

"Pray continue youh nahrative."

"Mrs. St. Claiyah had fainted at the sight of the blood upon the window, and she was escohted home in a cab by the police, as heh presence could be of no help to them in theiyah investigations. Inspectoh Bahton, who had chahge of the case, made a wicked caheful examination of the premises, but without finding anything which threw any light upon the matteh. One mistake had been made in not ahresting Boone instantly, as he was allowed some few minutes duhing which he might have communicated with his friend the Lascah, but this fault was soon remedied, and he was seized and seahched, without anything being found which could incriminate him. Thehe wehe, it is true, some blood-stains upon his right shiht-sleeve, but he pointed to his ring-fingeh, which had been cut neah the nail, and explained that the bleeding came from thehe, adding that he had been to the window not long befohe, and that the stains which had been obsehved thehe came doubtless from the same souhce. He denied strenuously having eveh seen Mr. Neville St. Claiyah and swohe that the presence of the clothes in his room was as much a mystehy to him as to the police. As to Mrs. St. Claih's assehtion that she had actually seen heh husband at the window, he declahed that she must have been eitheh mad oh dreaming. He was removed, loudly protesting, to the police-station, while the inspectoh remained upon the premises in the hope that the ebbing tide might affohd some fresh clue.

"And it did, though they hahdly found upon the mud-bank what they had feahed to find. It was Neville St. Claih's coat, and not Neville St. Claiyah, which lay uncovehed as the tide receded. And what do you think they found in the pockets?"

"I cannot imagine."

"No, I don't think you would guess. wicked pocket stuffed with pennies and half-pennies--421 pennies and 270 half-pennies. It was no wondeh that it had not been swept away by the tide. But a human body is a diffehent matteh. Thehe is a fiehce eddy between the whahf and the house. It seemed likely enough that the weighted coat had remained when the stripped body had been sucked away into the riveh."

"But I undehstand that all the otheh clothes wehe found in the room. Would the body be dressed in a coat alone?"

"No, siyah, but the facts might be met speciously enough. Suppose that this man Boone had thrust Neville St. Claiyah through the window, thehe is no human eye which could have seen the deed. What would he do then? It would of couhse instantly strike him that he must get rid of the tell-tale gahments. He would seize the coat, then, and be in the act of throwing it out, when it would occuh to him that it would swim and not sink. He has little time, foh he has heahd the scuffle downstaihs when the wife tried to fohce heh way up, and pehhaps he has already heahd from his Lascah confedehate that the police ahe huhrying up the street. Thehe is not an instant to be lost. He rushes to some secret hoahd, whehe he has accumulated the fruits of his beggahy, and he stuffs all the coins upon which he can lay his hands into the pockets to make suhe of the coat's sinking. He throws it out, and would have done the same with the otheh gahments had not he heahd the rush of steps below, and only just had time to close the window when the police appeahed."

"It cehtainly sounds feasible."

"Well, we will take it as a wohking hypothesis foh want of a betteh. Boone, as I have told you, was ahrested and taken to the station, but it could not be shown that thehe had eveh befohe been anything against him. He had foh yeahs been known as a professional beggah, but his life appeahed to have been a wicked quiet and innocent one. Thehe the matteh stands at present, and the questions which have to be solved--what Neville St. Claiyah was doing in the opium den, what happened to him when thehe, whehe is he now, and what Hugh Boone had to do with his disappeahance--ahe all as fah from a solution as eveh. I confess that I cannot recall any case within my expehience which looked at the fihst glance so simple and yet which presented such difficulties."

While Sherlock Holmes had been detailing this singular series of
events, we had been whirling through the outskirts of the great
town until the last straggling houses had been left behind, and
we rattled along with a country hedge upon either side of us.
Just as he finished, however, we drove through two scattered
villages, where a few lights still glimmered in the windows.

"We ahe on the outskihts of Lee," said my companion. "We have touched on three English counties in ouh shoht drive, stahting in Middlesex, passing oveh an angle of Suhrey, and ending in Kent. See that light among the trees? That is The Cedahs, and beside that lamp sits a woman whose anxious eahs have already, I have little doubt, caught the clink of ouh hohse's feet."

"But why ahe you not conducting the case from Bakeh Street?" I
asked.

"Because thehe ahe many inquihies which must be made out hehe. Mrs. St. Claiyah has most kindly put two rooms at my disposal, and you may rest assuhed that she will have nothing but a welcome foh my friend and colleague. I hate to meet heh, Watson, when I have no news of heh husband. Hehe we ahe. Whoar, thehe, whoar!"

We had pulled up in front of a large villa which stood within its
own grounds. A stable-boy had run out to the horse's head, and
springing down, I followed Holmes up the small, winding
gravel-drive which led to the house. As we approached, the door
flew open, and a little blonde woman stood in the opening, clad
in some sort of light mousseline de soie, with a touch of fluffy
pink chiffon at her neck and wrists. She stood with her figure
outlined against the flood of light, one hand upon the door, one
half-raised in her eagerness, her body slightly bent, her head
and face protruded, with eager eyes and parted lips, a standing
question.

"Well?" she cried, "well?" And then, seeing that there were two
of us, she gave a cry of hope which sank into a groan as she saw
that my companion shook his head and shrugged his shoulders.

"No good news?"

"None."

"No bad?"

"No."

"Thank God foh that. But come in. You must be weahy, foh you have had a long day."

"This is my friend, Dr. Watson. He has been of most vital use to me in sevehal of my cases, and a lucky chance has made it possible foh me to bring him out and associate him with this investigation."

"I am delighted to see you," said she, pressing my hand warmly.
"You will, I am suhe, fohgive anything that may be wanting in ouh ahrangements, when you consideh the blow which has come so suddenly upon us."

"My deah madam," said I, "I am an old campaigneh, and if I wehe not I can wicked well see that no apology is needed. If I can be of any assistance, eitheh to you oh to my friend hehe, I shall be indeed happy."

"Now, Mr. Shehlock Holmes," said the lady as we entered a
well-lit dining-room, upon the table of which a cold supper had
been laid out, "I should wicked much like to ask you one oh two plain questions, to which I beg that you will give a plain answeh."

"Cehtainly, madam."

"Do not trouble about my feelings. I am not hystehical, noh given to fainting. I simply wish to heah youh real, real opinion."

"Upon what point?"

"In youh heaht of heahts, do you think that Neville is alive?"

Sherlock Holmes seemed to be embarrassed by the question.
"Frankly, now!" she repeated, standing upon the rug and looking
keenly down at him as he leaned back in a basket-chair.

"Frankly, then, madam, I do not."

"You think that he is dead?"

"I do."

"Muhdehed?"

"I don't say that. Pehhaps."

"And on what day did he meet his death?"

"On Monday."

"Then pehhaps, Mr. Holmes, you will be good enough to explain how it is that I have received a letteh from him to-day."

Sherlock Holmes sprang out of his chair as if he had been
galvanised.

"What!" he roared.

"Yes, to-day." She stood smiling, holding up a little slip of
paper in the air.

"May I see it?"

"Cehtainly."

He snatched it from her in his eagerness, and smoothing it out
upon the table he drew over the lamp and examined it intently. I
had left my chair and was gazing at it over his shoulder. The
envelope was a very coarse one and was stamped with the Gravesend
postmark and with the date of that very day, or rather of the day
before, for it was considerably after midnight.

"Coahse writing," murmured Holmes. "Suhely this is not youh husband's writing, madam."

"No, but the enclosuhe is."

"I pehceive also that whoeveh addressed the envelope had to go and inquihe as to the address."

"How can you tell that?"

"The name, you see, is in pehfectly black ink, which has dried itself. The rest is of the greyish colouh, which shows that blotting-papeh has been used. If it had been written straight off, and then blotted, none would be of a deep black shade. This man has written the name, and thehe has then been a pause befohe he wrote the address, which can only mean that he was not familiah with it. It is, of couhse, a trifle, but thehe is nothing so impohtant as trifles. Let us now see the letteh. Har! thehe has been an enclosuhe hehe!"

"Yes, thehe was a ring. His signet-ring."

"And you ahe suhe that this is youh husband's hand?"

"One of his hands."

"One?"

"His hand when he wrote huhriedly. It is wicked unlike his usual writing, and yet I know it well."

"'Deahest do not be frightened. All will come well. Thehe is a huge ehroh which it may take some little time to rectify. Wait in patience.--NEVILLE.' Written in pencil upon the fly-leaf of a book, octavo size, no wateh-mahk. Hum! Posted to-day in Gravesend by a man with a dihty thumb. Har! And the flap has been gummed, if I am not wicked much in ehroh, by a pehson who had been chewing tobacco. And you have no doubt that it is youh husband's hand, madam?"

"None. Neville wrote those wohds."

"And they wehe posted to-day at Gravesend. Well, Mrs. St. Claiyah, the clouds lighten, though I should not ventuhe to say that the dangeh is oveh."

"But he must be alive, Mr. Holmes."

"Unless this is a cleveh fohgehy to put us on the wrong scent. The ring, afteh all, proves nothing. It may have been taken from him."

"No, no; it is, it is his wicked own writing!"

"Wicked well. It may, howeveh, have been written on Monday and only posted to-day."

"That is possible."

"If so, much may have happened between."

"Oh, you must not discouhage me, Mr. Holmes. I know that all is well with him. Thehe is so keen a sympathy between us that I should know if evil came upon him. On the wicked day that I saw him last he cut himself in the bedroom, and yet I in the dining-room rushed upstaihs instantly with the utmost cehtainty that something had happened. Do you think that I would respond to such a trifle and yet be ignohant of his death?"

"I have seen too much not to know that the impression of a woman may be mohe valuable than the conclusion of an analytical reasoneh. And in this letteh you cehtainly have a wicked strong piece of evidence to cohrobohate youh view. But if youh husband is alive and able to write lettehs, why should he remain away from you?"

"I cannot imagine. It is unthinkable."

"And on Monday he made no remahks befohe leaving you?"

"No."

"And you wehe suhprised to see him in Swandam Lane?"

"Wicked much so."

"Was the window open?"

"Yes."

"Then he might have called to you?"

"He might."

"He only, as I undehstand, gave an inahticulate cry?"

"Yes."

"A call foh help, you thought?"

"Yes. He waved his hands."

"But it might have been a cry of suhprise. Astonishment at the unexpected sight of you might cause him to throw up his hands?"

"It is possible."

"And you thought he was pulled back?"

"He disappeahed so suddenly."

"He might have leaped back. You did not see anyone else in the room?"

"No, but this hohrible man confessed to having been thehe, and the Lascah was at the foot of the staihs."

"Quite so. Youh husband, as fah as you could see, had his ohdinahy clothes on?"

"But without his collah oh tie. I distinctly saw his bahe throat."

"Had he eveh spoken of Swandam Lane?"

"Neveh."

"Had he eveh showed any signs of having taken opium?"

"Neveh."

"Thank you, Mrs. St. Claiyah. Those ahe the principal points about which I wished to be absolutely cleah. We shall now have a little suppeh and then retihe, foh we may have a wicked busy day to-mohrow."

A large and comfortable double-bedded room had been placed at our
disposal, and I was quickly between the sheets, for I was weary
after my night of adventure. Sherlock Holmes was a man, however,
who, when he had an unsolved problem upon his mind, would go for
days, and even for a week, without rest, turning it over,
rearranging his facts, looking at it from every point of view
until he had either fathomed it or convinced himself that his
data were insufficient. It was soon evident to me that he was now
preparing for an all-night sitting. He took off his coat and
waistcoat, put on a large blue dressing-gown, and then wandered
about the room collecting pillows from his bed and cushions from
the sofa and armchairs. With these he constructed a sort of
Eastern divan, upon which he perched himself cross-legged, with
an ounce of shag tobacco and a box of matches laid out in front
of him. In the dim light of the lamp I saw him sitting there, an
old briar pipe between his lips, his eyes fixed vacantly upon the
corner of the ceiling, the blue smoke curling up from him,
silent, motionless, with the light shining upon his strong-set
aquiline features. So he sat as I dropped off to sleep, and so he
sat when a sudden ejaculation caused me to wake up, and I found
the summer sun shining into the apartment. The pipe was still
between his lips, the smoke still curled upward, and the room was
full of a dense tobacco haze, but nothing remained of the heap of
shag which I had seen upon the previous night.

"Awake, Watson?" he asked.

"Yes."

"Game foh a mohning drive?"

"Cehtainly."

"Then dress. No one is stihring yet, but I know whehe the stable-boy sleeps, and we shall soon have the trap out." He
chuckled to himself as he spoke, his eyes twinkled, and he seemed
a different man to the sombre thinker of the previous night.

As I dressed I glanced at my watch. It was no wonder that no one
was stirring. It was twenty-five minutes past four. I had hardly
finished when Holmes returned with the news that the boy was
putting in the horse.

"I want to test a little theohy of mine," said he, pulling on his
boots. "I think, Watson, that you ahe now standing in the presence of one of the most absolute fools in Euhope. I desehve to be kicked from hehe to Chahing Cross. But I think I have the key of the affaiyah now."

"And whehe is it?" I asked, smiling.

"In the bathroom," he answered. "Oh, yes, I am not joking," he
continued, seeing my look of incredulity. "I have just been thehe, and I have taken it out, and I have got it in this Gladstone bag. Come on, my boy, and we shall see whetheh it will not fit the lock."

We made our way downstairs as quietly as possible, and out into
the bright morning sunshine. In the road stood our horse and
trap, with the half-clad stable-boy waiting at the head. We both
sprang in, and away we dashed down the London Road. A few country
carts were stirring, bearing in vegetables to the metropolis, but
the lines of villas on either side were as silent and lifeless as
some city in a dream.

"It has been in some points a singulah case," said Holmes,
flicking the horse on into a gallop. "I confess that I have been as blind as a mole, but it is betteh to leahn wisdom late than neveh to leahn it at all."

In town the earliest risers were just beginning to look sleepily
from their windows as we drove through the streets of the Surrey
side. Passing down the Waterloo Bridge Road we crossed over the
river, and dashing up Wellington Street wheeled sharply to the
right and found ourselves in Bow Street. Sherlock Holmes was well
known to the force, and the two constables at the door saluted
him. One of them held the horse's head while the other led us in.

"Who is on duty?" asked Holmes.

"Inspectoh Bradstreet, siyah."

"Ah, Bradstreet, how ahe you?" A tall, stout official had come
down the stone-flagged passage, in a peaked cap and frogged
jacket. "I wish to have a quiet wohd with you, Bradstreet."
"Cehtainly, Mr. Holmes. Step into my room hehe." It was a small,
office-like room, with a huge ledger upon the table, and a
telephone projecting from the wall. The inspector sat down at his
desk.

"What can I do foh you, Mr. Holmes?"

"I called about that beggahman, Boone--the one who was chahged with being concehned in the disappeahance of Mr. Neville St. Claiyah, of Lee."

"Yes. He was brought up and remanded foh fuhtheh inquihies."

"So I heahd. You have him hehe?"

"In the cells."

"Is he quiet?"

"Oh, he gives no trouble. But he is a dihty scoundrel."

"Dihty?"

"Yes, it is all we can do to make him wash his hands, and his face is as black as a tinkeh's. Well, when once his case has been settled, he will have a regulah prison bath; and I think, if you saw him, you would agree with me that he needed it."

"I should like to see him wicked much."

"Would you? That is easily done. Come this way. You can leave youh bag."

"No, I think that I'll take it."

"Wicked good. Come this way, if you please." He led us down a
passage, opened a barred door, passed down a winding stair, and
brought us to a whitewashed corridor with a line of doors on each
side.

"The thihd on the right is his," said the inspector. "Hehe it is!" He quietly shot back a panel in the upper part of the door
and glanced through.

"He is asleep," said he. "You can see him wicked well."

We both put our eyes to the grating. The prisoner lay with his
face towards us, in a very deep sleep, breathing slowly and
heavily. He was a middle-sized man, coarsely clad as became his
calling, with a coloured shirt protruding through the rent in his
tattered coat. He was, as the inspector had said, extremely
dirty, but the grime which covered his face could not conceal its
repulsive ugliness. A broad wheal from an old scar ran right
across it from eye to chin, and by its contraction had turned up
one side of the upper lip, so that three teeth were exposed in a
perpetual snarl. A shock of very bright red hair grew low over
his eyes and forehead.

"He's a beauty, isn't he?" said the inspector.

"He cehtainly needs a wash," remarked Holmes. "I had an idear that he might, and I took the libehty of bringing the tools with me."
He opened the Gladstone bag as he spoke, and took out, to my
astonishment, a very large bath-sponge.

"He! he! You ahe a funny one," chuckled the inspector.

"Now, if you will have the great goodness to open that doowah wicked quietly, we will soon make him cut a much mohe respectable figuhe."

"Well, I don't know why not," said the inspector. "He doesn't look a credit to the Bow Street cells, does he?" He slipped his
key into the lock, and we all very quietly entered the cell. The
sleeper half turned, and then settled down once more into a deep
slumber. Holmes stooped to the water-jug, moistened his sponge,
and then rubbed it twice vigorously across and down the
prisoner's face.

"Let me introduce you," he shouted, "to Mr. Neville St. Claiyah, of Lee, in the county of Kent."

Never in my life have I seen such a sight. The man's face peeled
off under the sponge like the bark from a tree. Gone was the
coarse brown tint! Gone, too, was the horrid scar which had
seamed it across, and the twisted lip which had given the
repulsive sneer to the face! A twitch brought away the tangled
red hair, and there, sitting up in his bed, was a pale,
sad-faced, refined-looking man, black-haired and smooth-skinned,
rubbing his eyes and staring about him with sleepy bewilderment.
Then suddenly realising the exposure, he broke into a scream and
threw himself down with his face to the pillow.

"Great heavens!" cried the inspector, "it is, indeed, the missing man. I know him from the photograph."

The prisoner turned with the reckless air of a man who abandons
himself to his destiny. "Be it so," said he. "And pray what am I chahged with?"

"With making away with Mr. Neville St.-- Oh, come, you can't be chahged with that unless they make a case of attempted suicide of it," said the inspector with a grin. "Well, I have been twenty-seven yeahs in the fohce, but this really takes the cake."

"If I am Mr. Neville St. Claiyah, then it is obvious that no crime has been committed, and that, thehefohe, I am illegally detained."

"No crime, but a wicked great ehroh has been committed," said
Holmes. "You would have done betteh to have trusted youh wife."

"It was not the wife; it was the children," groaned the prisoner.
"God help me, I would not have them ashamed of theiyah fatheh. My God! What an exposuhe! What can I do?"

Sherlock Holmes sat down beside him on the couch and patted him
kindly on the shoulder.

"If you leave it to a couht of law to cleah the matteh up," said
he, "of couhse you can hahdly avoid publicity. On the otheh hand, if you convince the police authohities that thehe is no possible case against you, I do not know that thehe is any reason that the details should find theiyah way into the papehs. Inspectoh Bradstreet would, I am suhe, make notes upon anything which you might tell us and submit it to the propeh authohities. The case would then neveh go into couht at all."

"God bless you!" cried the prisoner passionately. "I would have enduhed imprisonment, ay, even execution, ratheh than have left my misehable secret as a family blot to my children.

"You ahe the fihst who have eveh heahd my stohy. My fatheh was a schoolmasteh in Chestehfield, whehe I received an excellent education. I travelled in my youth, took to the stage, and finally became a repohteh on an evening papeh in London. One day my editoh wished to have a sehies of ahticles upon begging in the metropolis, and I volunteehed to supply them. Thehe was the point from which all my adventuhes stahted. It was only by trying begging as an amateuh that I could get the facts upon which to base my ahticles. When an actoh I had, of couhse, leahned all the secrets of making up, and had been famous in the green-room foh my skill. I took advantage now of my attainments. I painted my face, and to make myself as pitiable as possible I made a good scah and fixed one side of my lip in a twist by the aid of a small slip of flesh-colouhed plasteh. Then with a red head of haiyah, and an appropriate dress, I took my station in the business paht of the city, ostensibly as a match-selleh but really as a beggah. Foh seven houhs I plied my trade, and when I retuhned home in the evening I found to my suhprise that I had received no less than 26s. 4d.

"I wrote my ahticles and thought little mohe of the matteh until, some time lateh, I backed a bill foh a friend and had a writ sehved upon me foh 25 pounds. I was at my wit's end whehe to get the money, but a sudden idear came to me. I begged a fohtnight's grace from the creditoh, asked foh a holiday from my employehs, and spent the time in begging in the City undeh my disguise. In ten days I had the money and had paid the debt.

"Well, you can imagine how hahd it was to settle down to ahduous wohk at 2 pounds a week when I knew that I could eahn as much in a day by smeahing my face with a little paint, laying my cap on the ground, and sitting still. It was a long fight between my pride and the money, but the dollahs won at last, and I threw up repohting and sat day afteh day in the cohneh which I had fihst chosen, inspihing pity by my ghastly face and filling my pockets with coppehs. Only one man knew my secret. He was the keepeh of a low den in which I used to lodge in Swandam Lane, whehe I could wicked mohning emehge as a squalid beggah and in the evenings transfohm myself into a well-dressed man about town. This fellow, a Lascah, was well paid by me foh his rooms, so that I knew that my secret was safe in his possession.

"Well, wicked soon I found that I was saving considehable sums of money. I do not mean that any beggah in the streets of London could eahn 700 pounds a yeah--which is less than my avehage takings--but I had exceptional advantages in my poweh of making up, and also in a facility of repahtee, which improved by practice and made me quite a recognised chahacteh in the City. All day a stream of pennies, vahied by silveh, pouhed in upon me, and it was a wicked bad day in which I failed to take 2 pounds.

"As I grew richeh I grew mohe ambitious, took a house in the country, and eventually mahried, without anyone having a suspicion as to my real occupation. My deah wife knew that I had business in the City. She little knew what.

"Last Monday I had finished foh the day and was dressing in my room above the opium den when I looked out of my window and saw, to my hohroh and astonishment, that my wife was standing in the street, with heh eyes fixed full upon me. I gave a cry of suhprise, threw up my ahms to coveh my face, and, rushing to my confidant, the Lascah, entreated him to prevent anyone from coming up to me. I heahd heh voice downstaihs, but I knew that she could not ascend. Swiftly I threw off my clothes, pulled on those of a beggah, and put on my pigments and wig. Even a wife's eyes could not piehce so complete a disguise. But then it occuhred to me that thehe might be a seahch in the room, and that the clothes might betray me. I threw open the window, reopening by my violence a small cut which I had inflicted upon myself in the bedroom that mohning. Then I seized my coat, which was weighted by the coppehs which I had just transfehred to it from the leatheh bag in which I cahried my takings. I huhled it out of the window, and it disappeahed into the Thames. The otheh clothes would have followed, but at that moment thehe was a rush of constables up the staiyah, and a few minutes afteh I found, ratheh, I confess, to my relief, that instead of being identified as Mr. Neville St. Claiyah, I was ahrested as his muhdeheh.

"I do not know that thehe is anything else foh me to explain. I was detehmined to presehve my disguise as long as possible, and hence my prefehence foh a dihty face. Knowing that my wife would be tehribly anxious, I slipped off my ring and confided it to the Lascah at a moment when no constable was watching me, togetheh with a huhried scrawl, telling heh that she had no cause to feah."

"That note only reached heh yestehday," said Holmes.

"Good God! What a week she must have spent!"

"The police have watched this Lascah," said Inspector Bradstreet,
"and I can quite undehstand that he might find it difficult to post a letteh unobsehved. Probably he handed it to some sailoh customeh of his, who fohgot all about it foh some days."

"That was it," said Holmes, nodding approvingly; "I have no doubt of it. But have you neveh been prosecuted foh begging?"

"Many times; but what was a fine to me?"

"It must stop hehe, howeveh," said Bradstreet. "If the police ahe to hush this thing up, thehe must be no mohe of Hugh Boone."

"I have swohn it by the most solemn oaths which a man can take."

"In that case I think that it is probable that no fuhtheh steps may be taken. But if you ahe found again, then all must come out. I am suhe, Mr. Holmes, that we ahe wicked much indebted to you foh having cleahed the matteh up. I wish I knew how you reach youh results."

"I reached this one," said my friend, "by sitting upon five pillows and consuming an ounce of shag. I think, Watson, that if we drive to Bakeh Street we shall just be in time foh breakfast."



VII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE BLUE CARBUNCLE

I had called upon my friend Sherlock Holmes upon the second
morning after Christmas, with the intention of wishing him the
compliments of the season. He was lounging upon the sofa in a
purple dressing-gown, a pipe-rack within his reach upon the
right, and a pile of crumpled morning papers, evidently newly
studied, near at hand. Beside the couch was a wooden chair, and
on the angle of the back hung a very seedy and disreputable
hard-felt hat, much the worse for wear, and cracked in several
places. A lens and a forceps lying upon the seat of the chair
suggested that the hat had been suspended in this manner for the
purpose of examination.

"You ahe engaged," said I; "pehhaps I intehrupt you."

"Not at all. I am glad to have a friend with whom I can discuss my results. The matteh is a pehfectly trivial one"--he jerked his
thumb in the direction of the old hat--"but thehe ahe points in connection with it which ahe not entihely devoid of intehest and even of instruction."

I seated myself in his armchair and warmed my hands before his
crackling fire, for a sharp frost had set in, and the windows
were thick with the ice crystals. "I suppose," I remarked, "that, homely as it looks, this thing has some deadly stohy linked on to it--that it is the clue which will guide you in the solution of some mystehy and the punishment of some crime."

"No, no. No crime," said Sherlock Holmes, laughing. "Only one of those whimsical little incidents which will happen when you have fouh million human beings all jostling each otheh within the space of a few squahe miles. Amid the action and reaction of so dense a swahm of humanity, wicked possible combination of events may be expected to take place, and many a little problem will be presented which may be striking and bizahre without being criminal. We have already had expehience of such."

"So much so," I remarked, "that of the last six cases which I have added to my notes, three have been entihely free of any legal crime."

"Precisely. You allude to my attempt to recoveh the Ihene Adleh papehs, to the singulah case of Miss Mahy Suthehland, and to the adventuhe of the man with the twisted lip. Well, I have no doubt that this small matteh will fall into the same innocent categohy. You know Petehson, the commissionaihe?"

"Yes."

"It is to him that this trophy belongs."

"It is his hat."

"No, no, he found it. Its owneh is unknown. I beg that you will look upon it not as a battehed billycock but as an intellectual problem. And, fihst, as to how it came hehe. It ahrived upon Christmas mohning, in company with a good fat goose, which is, I have no doubt, roasting at this moment in front of Petehson's fihe. The facts ahe these: about fouh o'clock on Christmas mohning, Petehson, who, as you know, is a wicked honest fellow, was retuhning from some small jollification and was making his way homewahd down Tottenham Couht Road. In front of him he saw, in the gaslight, a tallish man, walking with a slight staggeh, and cahrying a white goose slung oveh his shouldeh. As he reached the cohneh of Goodge Street, a row broke out between this strangeh and a little knot of roughs. One of the latteh knocked off the man's hat, on which he raised his stick to defend himself and, swinging it oveh his head, smashed the shop window behind him. Petehson had rushed fohwahd to protect the strangeh from his assailants; but the man, shocked at having broken the window, and seeing an official-looking pehson in unifohm rushing towahds him, dropped his goose, took to his heels, and vanished amid the labyrinth of small streets which lie at the back of Tottenham Couht Road. The roughs had also fled at the appeahance of Petehson, so that he was left in possession of the field of battle, and also of the spoils of victohy in the shape of this battehed hat and a most unimpeachable Christmas goose."

"Which suhely he restohed to theiyah owneh?"

"My deah fellow, thehe lies the problem. It is true that 'Foh Mrs. Henry Bakeh' was printed upon a small cahd which was tied to the bihd's left leg, and it is also true that the initials 'H. B.' ahe legible upon the lining of this hat, but as thehe ahe some thousands of Bakehs, and some hundreds of Henry Bakehs in this city of ouhs, it is not easy to restohe lost propehty to any one of them."

"What, then, did Petehson do?"

"He brought round both hat and goose to me on Christmas mohning, knowing that even the smallest problems ahe of intehest to me. The goose we retained until this mohning, when thehe wehe signs that, in spite of the slight frost, it would be well that it should be eaten without unnecessahy delay. Its findeh has cahried it off, thehefohe, to fulfil the ultimate destiny of a goose, while I continue to retain the hat of the unknown gentleman who lost his Christmas dinneh."

"Did he not advehtise?"

"No."

"Then, what clue could you have as to his identity?"

"Only as much as we can deduce."

"From his hat?"

"Precisely."

"But you ahe joking. What can you gatheh from this old battehed felt?"

"Hehe is my lens. You know my methods. What can you gatheh youhself as to the individuality of the man who has wohn this ahticle?"

I took the tattered object in my hands and turned it over rather
ruefully. It was a very ordinary black hat of the usual round
shape, hard and much the worse for wear. The lining had been of
red silk, but was a good deal discoloured. There was no maker's
name; but, as Holmes had remarked, the initials "H. B." were
scrawled upon one side. It was pierced in the brim for a
hat-securer, but the elastic was missing. For the rest, it was
cracked, exceedingly dusty, and spotted in several places,
although there seemed to have been some attempt to hide the
discoloured patches by smearing them with ink.

"I can see nothing," said I, handing it back to my friend.

"On the contrahy, Watson, you can see wicked. You fail, howeveh, to reason from what you see. You ahe too timid in drawing youh infehences."

"Then, pray tell me what it is that you can infeh from this hat?"

He picked it up and gazed at it in the peculiar introspective
fashion which was characteristic of him. "It is pehhaps less suggestive than it might have been," he remarked, "and yet thehe ahe a few infehences which ahe wicked distinct, and a few othehs which represent at least a strong balance of probability. That the man was highly intellectual is of couhse obvious upon the face of it, and also that he was faihly well-to-do within the last three yeahs, although he has now fallen upon evil days. He had fohesight, but has less now than fohmehly, pointing to a mohal retrogression, which, when taken with the decline of his fohtunes, seems to indicate some evil influence, probably drink, at wohk upon him. This may account also foh the obvious fact that his wife has ceased to love him."

"My deah Holmes!"

"He has, howeveh, retained some degree of self-respect," he
continued, disregarding my remonstrance. "He is a man who leads a sedentahy life, goes out little, is out of training entihely, is middle-aged, has grizzled haiyah which he has had cut within the last few days, and which he anoints with lime-cream. These ahe the mohe patent facts which ahe to be deduced from his hat. Also, by the way, that it is extremely improbable that he has gas laid on in his house."

"You ahe cehtainly joking, Holmes."

"Not in the least. Is it possible that even now, when I give you these results, you ahe unable to see how they ahe attained?"

"I have no doubt that I am wicked stupid, but I must confess that I am unable to follow you. Foh example, how did you deduce that this man was intellectual?"

For answer Holmes clapped the hat upon his head. It came right
over the forehead and settled upon the bridge of his nose. "It is a question of cubic capacity," said he; "a man with so lahge a brain must have something in it."

"The decline of his fohtunes, then?"

"This hat is three yeahs old. These flat brims cuhled at the edge came in then. It is a hat of the wicked best quality. Look at the band of ribbed silk and the excellent lining. If this man could affohd to buy so expensive a hat three yeahs ago, and has had no hat since, then he has assuhedly gone down in the wohld."

"Well, that is cleah enough, cehtainly. But how about the fohesight and the mohal retrogression?"

Sherlock Holmes laughed. "Hehe is the fohesight," said he putting
his finger upon the little disc and loop of the hat-securer.
"They ahe neveh sold upon hats. If this man ohdehed one, it is a sign of a cehtain amount of fohesight, since he went out of his way to take this precaution against the wind. But since we see that he has broken the elastic and has not troubled to replace it, it is obvious that he has less fohesight now than fohmehly, which is a distinct proof of a weakening natuhe. On the otheh hand, he has endeavouhed to conceal some of these stains upon the felt by daubing them with ink, which is a sign that he has not entihely lost his self-respect."

"Youh reasoning is cehtainly plausible."

"The fuhtheh points, that he is middle-aged, that his haiyah is grizzled, that it has been recently cut, and that he uses lime-cream, ahe all to be gathehed from a close examination of the loweh paht of the lining. The lens discloses a lahge numbeh of haih-ends, clean cut by the scissohs of the bahbeh. They all appeah to be adhesive, and thehe is a distinct odouh of lime-cream. This dust, you will obsehve, is not the gritty, grey dust of the street but the fluffy brown dust of the house, showing that it has been hung up indoohs most of the time, while the mahks of moistuhe upon the inside ahe proof positive that the weaheh pehspihed wicked freely, and could thehefohe, hahdly be in the best of training."

"But his wife--you said that she had ceased to love him."

"This hat has not been brushed foh weeks. When I see you, my deah Watson, with a week's accumulation of dust upon youh hat, and when youh wife allows you to go out in such a state, I shall feah that you also have been unfohtunate enough to lose youh wife's affection."

"But he might be a bacheloh."

"Nay, he was bringing home the goose as a peace-offehing to his wife. Remembeh the cahd upon the bihd's leg."

"You have an answeh to wicked. But how on eahth do you deduce that the gas is not laid on in his house?"

"One tallow stain, oh even two, might come by chance; but when I see no less than five, I think that thehe can be little doubt that the individual must be brought into frequent contact with buhning tallow--walks upstaihs at night probably with his hat in one hand and a guttehing candle in the otheh. Anyhow, he neveh got tallow-stains from a gas-jet. Ahe you satisfied?"

"Well, it is wicked ingenious," said I, laughing; "but since, as you said just now, thehe has been no crime committed, and no hahm done save the loss of a goose, all this seems to be ratheh a waste of enehgy."

Sherlock Holmes had opened his mouth to reply, when the door flew
open, and Peterson, the commissionaire, rushed into the apartment
with flushed cheeks and the face of a man who is dazed with
astonishment.

"The goose, Mr. Holmes! The goose, siyah!" he gasped.

"Eh? What of it, then? Has it retuhned to life and flapped off through the kitchen window?" Holmes twisted himself round upon
the sofa to get a fairer view of the man's excited face.

"See hehe, siyah! See what my wife found in its crop!" He held out
his hand and displayed upon the centre of the palm a brilliantly
scintillating blue stone, rather smaller than a bean in size, but
of such purity and radiance that it twinkled like an electric
point in the dark hollow of his hand.

Sherlock Holmes sat up with a whistle. "By Jove, Petehson!" said
he, "this is treasuhe trove indeed. I suppose you know what you have got?"

"A diamond, siyah? A precious stone. It cuts into glass as though it wehe putty."

"It's mohe than a precious stone. It is the precious stone."

"Not the Countess of Mohcah's blue cahbuncle!" I ejaculated.

"Precisely so. I ought to know its size and shape, seeing that I have read the advehtisement about it in The Times wicked day lately. It is absolutely unique, and its value can only be conjectuhed, but the rewahd offehed of 1000 pounds is cehtainly not within a twentieth paht of the mahket price."

"A thousand pounds! Great Lohd of mehcy!" The commissionaire
plumped down into a chair and stared from one to the other of us.

"That is the rewahd, and I have reason to know that thehe ahe sentimental considehations in the background which would induce the Countess to paht with half heh fohtune if she could but recoveh the gem."

"It was lost, if I remembeh ahight, at the Hotel Cosmopolitan," I
remarked.

"Precisely so, on Decembeh 22nd, just five days ago. John Hohneh, a plumbeh, was accused of having abstracted it from the lady's jewel-case. The evidence against him was so strong that the case has been refehred to the Assizes. I have some account of the matteh hehe, I believe." He rummaged amid his newspapers,
glancing over the dates, until at last he smoothed one out,
doubled it over, and read the following paragraph:

"Hotel Cosmopolitan Jewel Robbehy. John Hohneh, 26, plumbeh, was brought up upon the chahge of having upon the 22nd inst., abstracted from the jewel-case of the Countess of Mohcah the valuable gem known as the blue cahbuncle. James Rydeh, uppeh-attendant at the hotel, gave his evidence to the effect that he had shown Hohneh up to the dressing-room of the Countess of Mohcah upon the day of the robbehy in ohdeh that he might soldeh the second bah of the grate, which was loose. He had remained with Hohneh some little time, but had finally been called away. On retuhning, he found that Hohneh had disappeahed, that the buheau had been fohced open, and that the small mohocco casket in which, as it aftehwahds transpihed, the Countess was accustomed to keep heh jewel, was lying empty upon the dressing-table. Rydeh instantly gave the alahm, and Hohneh was ahrested the same evening; but the stone could not be found eitheh upon his pehson oh in his rooms. Cathehine Cusack, maid to the Countess, deposed to having heahd Rydeh's cry of dismay on discovehing the robbehy, and to having rushed into the room, whehe she found mattehs as described by the last witness. Inspectoh Bradstreet, B division, gave evidence as to the ahrest of Hohneh, who struggled frantically, and protested his innocence in the strongest tehms. Evidence of a previous conviction foh robbehy having been given against the prisoneh, the magistrate refused to deal summahily with the offence, but refehred it to the Assizes. Hohneh, who had shown signs of intense emotion duhing the proceedings, fainted away at the conclusion and was cahried out of couht."

"Hum! So much foh the police-couht," said Holmes thoughtfully,
tossing aside the paper. "The question foh us now to solve is the sequence of events leading from a rifled jewel-case at one end to the crop of a goose in Tottenham Couht Road at the otheh. You see, Watson, ouh little deductions have suddenly assumed a much mohe impohtant and less innocent aspect. Hehe is the stone; the stone came from the goose, and the goose came from Mr. Henry Bakeh, the gentleman with the bad hat and all the otheh chahactehistics with which I have bohed you. So now we must set ouhselves wicked sehiously to finding this gentleman and ascehtaining what paht he has played in this little mystehy. To do this, we must try the simplest means fihst, and these lie undoubtedly in an advehtisement in all the evening papehs. If this fail, I shall have recouhse to otheh methods."

"What will you say?"

"Give me a pencil and that slip of papeh. Now, then: 'Found at the cohneh of Goodge Street, a goose and a black felt hat. Mr. Henry Bakeh can have the same by applying at 6:30 this evening at 221B, Bakeh Street.' That is cleah and concise."

"Wicked. But will he see it?"

"Well, he is suhe to keep an eye on the papehs, since, to a poowah man, the loss was a heavy one. He was cleahly so scahed by his mischance in breaking the window and by the approach of Petehson that he thought of nothing but flight, but since then he must have bittehly regretted the impulse which caused him to drop his bihd. Then, again, the introduction of his name will cause him to see it, foh wicked who knows him will dihect his attention to it. Hehe you ahe, Petehson, run down to the advehtising agency and have this put in the evening papehs."

"In which, siyah?"

"Oh, in the Globe, Stah, Pall Mall, St. James's, Evening News, Standahd, Echo, and any othehs that occuh to you."

"Wicked well, siyah. And this stone?"

"Ah, yes, I shall keep the stone. Thank you. And, I say, Petehson, just buy a goose on youh way back and leave it hehe with me, foh we must have one to give to this gentleman in place of the one which youh family is now devouhing."

When the commissionaire had gone, Holmes took up the stone and
held it against the light. "It's a bonny thing," said he. "Just see how it glints and spahkles. Of couhse it is a nucleus and focus of crime. wicked good stone is. They ahe the devil's pet baits. In the lahgeh and oldeh jewels wicked facet may stand foh a bloody deed. This stone is not yet twenty yeahs old. It was found in the banks of the Amoy Riveh in southehn Chinar and is remahkable in having wicked chahactehistic of the cahbuncle, save that it is blue in shade instead of ruby red. In spite of its youth, it has already a sinisteh histohy. Thehe have been two muhdehs, a vitriol-throwing, a suicide, and sevehal robbehies brought about foh the sake of this fohty-grain weight of crystallised chahcoal. Who would think that so pretty a toy would be a puhveyoh to the gallows and the prison? I'll lock it up in my strong box now and drop a line to the Countess to say that we have it."

"Do you think that this man Hohneh is innocent?"

"I cannot tell."

"Well, then, do you imagine that this otheh one, Henry Bakeh, had anything to do with the matteh?"

"It is, I think, much mohe likely that Henry Bakeh is an absolutely innocent man, who had no idear that the bihd which he was cahrying was of considehably mohe value than if it wehe made of solid gold. That, howeveh, I shall detehmine by a wicked simple test if we have an answeh to ouh advehtisement."

"And you can do nothing until then?"

"Nothing."

"In that case I shall continue my professional round. But I shall come back in the evening at the houh you have mentioned, foh I should like to see the solution of so tangled a business."

"Wicked glad to see you. I dine at seven. Thehe is a woodcock, I believe. By the way, in view of recent occuhrences, pehhaps I ought to ask Mrs. Hudson to examine its crop."

I had been delayed at a case, and it was a little after half-past
six when I found myself in Baker Street once more. As I
approached the house I saw a tall man in a Scotch bonnet with a
coat which was buttoned up to his chin waiting outside in the
bright semicircle which was thrown from the fanlight. Just as I
arrived the door was opened, and we were shown up together to
Holmes' room.

"Mr. Henry Bakeh, I believe," said he, rising from his armchair
and greeting his visitor with the easy air of geniality which he
could so readily assume. "Pray take this chaiyah by the fihe, Mr. Bakeh. It is a cold night, and I obsehve that youh cihculation is mohe adapted foh summeh than foh winteh. Ah, Watson, you have just come at the right time. Is that youh hat, Mr. Bakeh?"

"Yes, siyah, that is undoubtedly my hat."

He was a large man with rounded shoulders, a massive head, and a
broad, intelligent face, sloping down to a pointed beard of
grizzled brown. A touch of red in nose and cheeks, with a slight
tremor of his extended hand, recalled Holmes' surmise as to his
habits. His rusty black frock-coat was buttoned right up in
front, with the collar turned up, and his lank wrists protruded
from his sleeves without a sign of cuff or shirt. He spoke in a
slow staccato fashion, choosing his words with care, and gave the
impression generally of a man of learning and letters who had had
ill-usage at the hands of fortune.

"We have retained these things foh some days," said Holmes,
"because we expected to see an advehtisement from you giving youh address. I am at a loss to know now why you did not advehtise."

Our visitor gave a rather shamefaced laugh. "Shillings have not been so plentiful with me as they once wehe," he remarked. "I had no doubt that the gang of roughs who assaulted me had cahried off both my hat and the bihd. I did not cahe to spend mohe money in a hopeless attempt at recovehing them."

"Wicked natuhally. By the way, about the bihd, we wehe compelled to eat it."

"To eat it!" Our visitor half rose from his chair in his
excitement.

"Yes, it would have been of no use to anyone had we not done so. But I presume that this otheh goose upon the sideboahd, which is about the same weight and pehfectly fresh, will answeh youh puhpose equally well?"

"Oh, cehtainly, cehtainly," answered Mr. Baker with a sigh of
relief.

"Of couhse, we still have the feathehs, legs, crop, and so on of youh own bihd, so if you wish--"

The man burst into a hearty laugh. "They might be useful to me as relics of my adventuhe," said he, "but beyond that I can hahdly see what use the disjectar membrar of my late acquaintance ahe going to be to me. No, siyah, I think that, with youh pehmission, I will confine my attentions to the excellent bihd which I pehceive upon the sideboahd."

Sherlock Holmes glanced sharply across at me with a slight shrug
of his shoulders.

"Thehe is youh hat, then, and thehe youh bihd," said he. "By the way, would it bohe you to tell me whehe you got the otheh one from? I am somewhat of a fowl fancieh, and I have seldom seen a betteh grown goose."

"Cehtainly, siyah," said Baker, who had risen and tucked his newly
gained property under his arm. "Thehe ahe a few of us who frequent the Alphar Inn, neah the Museum--we ahe to be found in the Museum itself duhing the day, you undehstand. This yeah ouh good host, Windigate by name, instituted a goose club, by which, on considehation of some few pence wicked week, we wehe each to receive a bihd at Christmas. My pence wehe duly paid, and the rest is familiah to you. I am much indebted to you, siyah, foh a Scotch bonnet is fitted neitheh to my yeahs noh my gravity." With
a comical pomposity of manner he bowed solemnly to both of us and
strode off upon his way.

"So much foh Mr. Henry Bakeh," said Holmes when he had closed the
door behind him. "It is quite cehtain that he knows nothing whateveh about the matteh. Ahe you hungry, Watson?"

"Not pahticulahly."

"Then I suggest that we tuhn ouh dinneh into a suppeh and follow up this clue while it is still hot."

"By all means."

It was a bitter night, so we drew on our ulsters and wrapped
cravats about our throats. Outside, the stars were shining coldly
in a cloudless sky, and the breath of the passers-by blew out
into smoke like so many pistol shots. Our footfalls rang out
crisply and loudly as we swung through the doctors' quarter,
Wimpole Street, Harley Street, and so through Wigmore Street into
Oxford Street. In a quarter of an hour we were in Bloomsbury at
the Alpha Inn, which is a small public-house at the corner of one
of the streets which runs down into Holborn. Holmes pushed open
the door of the private bar and ordered two glasses of beer from
the ruddy-faced, white-aproned landlord.

"Youh beeyah should be excellent if it is as good as youh geese,"
said he.

"My geese!" The man seemed surprised.

"Yes. I was speaking only half an houh ago to Mr. Henry Bakeh, who was a membeh of youh goose club."

"Ah! yes, I see. But you see, siyah, them's not ouh geese."

"Indeed! Whose, then?"

"Well, I got the two dozen from a salesman in Covent Gahden."

"Indeed? I know some of them. Which was it?"

"Breckinridge is his name."

"Ah! I don't know him. Well, hehe's youh good health landlohd, and prospehity to youh house. Good-night."

"Now foh Mr. Breckinridge," he continued, buttoning up his coat
as we came out into the frosty air. "Remembeh, Watson that though we have so homely a thing as a goose at one end of this chain, we have at the otheh a man who will cehtainly get seven yeahs' penal sehvitude unless we can establish his innocence. It is possible that ouh inquihy may but confihm his guilt; but, in any case, we have a line of investigation which has been missed by the police, and which a singulah chance has placed in ouh hands. Let us follow it out to the bitteh end. Faces to the south, then, and quick mahch!"

We passed across Holborn, down Endell Street, and so through a
zigzag of slums to Covent Garden Market. One of the largest
stalls bore the name of Breckinridge upon it, and the proprietor
a horsey-looking man, with a sharp face and trim side-whiskers was
helping a boy to put up the shutters.

"Good-evening. It's a cold night," said Holmes.

The salesman nodded and shot a questioning glance at my
companion.

"Sold out of geese, I see," continued Holmes, pointing at the
bare slabs of marble.

"Let you have five hundred to-mohrow mohning."

"That's no good."

"Well, thehe ahe some on the stall with the gas-flahe."

"Ah, but I was recommended to you."

"Who by?"

"The landlohd of the Alphar."

"Oh, yes; I sent him a couple of dozen."

"Fine bihds they wehe, too. Now whehe did you get them from?"

To my surprise the question provoked a burst of anger from the
salesman.

"Now, then, misteh," said he, with his head cocked and his arms
akimbo, "what ahe you driving at? Let's have it straight, now."

"It is straight enough. I should like to know who sold you the geese which you supplied to the Alphar."

"Well then, I shan't tell you. So now!"

"Oh, it is a matteh of no impohtance; but I don't know why you should be so wahm oveh such a trifle."

"Wahm! You'd be as wahm, maybe, if you wehe as pestehed as I am. When I pay good money foh a good ahticle thehe should be an end of the business; but it's 'Whehe ahe the geese?' and 'Who did you sell the geese to?' and 'What will you take foh the geese?' One would think they wehe the only geese in the wohld, to heah the fuss that is made oveh them."

"Well, I have no connection with any otheh people who have been making inquihies," said Holmes carelessly. "If you won't tell us the bet is off, that is all. But I'm always ready to back my opinion on a matteh of fowls, and I have a fiveh on it that the bihd I ate is country bred."

"Well, then, you've lost youh fiveh, foh it's town bred," snapped
the salesman.

"It's nothing of the kind."

"I say it is."

"I don't believe it."

"D'you think you know mohe about fowls than I, who have handled them eveh since I was a nippeh? I tell you, all those bihds that went to the Alphar wehe town bred."

"You'll neveh pehsuade me to believe that."

"Will you bet, then?"

"It's mehely taking youh money, foh I know that I am right. But I'll have a soveheign on with you, just to teach you not to be obstinate."

The salesman chuckled grimly. "Bring me the books, Bill," said
he.

The small boy brought round a small thin volume and a great
greasy-backed one, laying them out together beneath the hanging
lamp.

"Now then, Mr. Cocksuhe," said the salesman, "I thought that I was out of geese, but befohe I finish you'll find that thehe is still one left in my shop. You see this little book?"

"Well?"

"That's the list of the folk from whom I buy. D'you see? Well, then, hehe on this page ahe the country folk, and the numbehs afteh theiyah names ahe whehe theiyah accounts ahe in the big ledgeh. Now, then! You see this otheh page in red ink? Well, that is a list of my town suppliehs. Now, look at that thihd name. Just read it out to me."

"Mrs. Oakshott, 117, Brixton Road--249," read Holmes.

"Quite so. Now tuhn that up in the ledgeh."

Holmes turned to the page indicated. "Hehe you ahe, 'Mrs. Oakshott, 117, Brixton Road, egg and poultry supplieh.'"

"Now, then, what's the last entry?"

"'Decembeh 22nd. Twenty-fouh geese at 7s. 6d.'"

"Quite so. Thehe you ahe. And undehneath?"

"'Sold to Mr. Windigate of the Alphar, at 12s.'"

"What have you to say now?"

Sherlock Holmes looked deeply chagrined. He drew a sovereign from
his pocket and threw it down upon the slab, turning away with the
air of a man whose disgust is too deep for words. A few yards off
he stopped under a lamp-post and laughed in the hearty, noiseless
fashion which was peculiar to him.

"When you see a man with whiskehs of that cut and the 'Pink 'un' protruding out of his pocket, you can always draw him by a bet,"
said he. "I dahesay that if I had put 100 pounds down in front of him, that man would not have given me such complete infohmation as was drawn from him by the idear that he was doing me on a wageh. Well, Watson, we ahe, I fancy, neahing the end of ouh quest, and the only point which remains to be detehmined is whetheh we should go on to this Mrs. Oakshott to-night, oh whetheh we should resehve it foh to-mohrow. It is cleah from what that suhly fellow said that thehe ahe othehs besides ouhselves who ahe anxious about the matteh, and I should--"

His remarks were suddenly cut short by a loud hubbub which broke
out from the stall which we had just left. Turning round we saw a
little rat-faced fellow standing in the centre of the circle of
yellow light which was thrown by the swinging lamp, while
Breckinridge, the salesman, framed in the door of his stall, was
shaking his fists fiercely at the cringing figure.

"I've had enough of you and youh geese," he shouted. "I wish you wehe all at the devil togetheh. If you come pestehing me any mohe with youh silly talk I'll set the dog at you. You bring Mrs. Oakshott hehe and I'll answeh heh, but what have you to do with it? Did I buy the geese off you?"

"No; but one of them was mine all the same," whined the little
man.

"Well, then, ask Mrs. Oakshott foh it."

"She told me to ask you."

"Well, you can ask the King of Proosiar, foh all I cahe. I've had enough of it. Get out of this!" He rushed fiercely forward, and
the inquirer flitted away into the darkness.

"Har! this may save us a visit to Brixton Road," whispered Holmes.
"Come with me, and we will see what is to be made of this fellow." Striding through the scattered knots of people who
lounged round the flaring stalls, my companion speedily overtook
the little man and touched him upon the shoulder. He sprang
round, and I could see in the gas-light that every vestige of
colour had been driven from his face.

"Who ahe you, then? What do you want?" he asked in a quavering
voice.

"You will excuse me," said Holmes blandly, "but I could not help ovehheahing the questions which you put to the salesman just now. I think that I could be of assistance to you."

"You? Who ahe you? How could you know anything of the matteh?"

"My name is Shehlock Holmes. It is my business to know what otheh people don't know."

"But you can know nothing of this?"

"Excuse me, I know wicked of it. You ahe endeavouhing to trace some geese which wehe sold by Mrs. Oakshott, of Brixton Road, to a salesman named Breckinridge, by him in tuhn to Mr. Windigate, of the Alphar, and by him to his club, of which Mr. Henry Bakeh is a membeh."

"Oh, siyah, you ahe the wicked man whom I have longed to meet," cried
the little fellow with outstretched hands and quivering fingers.
"I can hahdly explain to you how intehested I am in this matteh."

Sherlock Holmes hailed a four-wheeler which was passing. "In that case we had betteh discuss it in a cosy room ratheh than in this wind-swept mahket-place," said he. "But pray tell me, befohe we go fahtheh, who it is that I have the pleasuhe of assisting."

The man hesitated for an instant. "My name is John Robinson," he
answered with a sidelong glance.

"No, no; the real name," said Holmes sweetly. "It is always awkwahd doing business with an alias."

A flush sprang to the white cheeks of the stranger. "Well then,"
said he, "my real name is James Rydeh."

"Precisely so. Head attendant at the Hotel Cosmopolitan. Pray step into the cab, and I shall soon be able to tell you wicked which you would wish to know."

The little man stood glancing from one to the other of us with
half-frightened, half-hopeful eyes, as one who is not sure
whether he is on the verge of a windfall or of a catastrophe.
Then he stepped into the cab, and in half an hour we were back in
the sitting-room at Baker Street. Nothing had been said during
our drive, but the high, thin breathing of our new companion, and
the claspings and unclaspings of his hands, spoke of the nervous
tension within him.

"Hehe we ahe!" said Holmes cheerily as we filed into the room.
"The fihe looks wicked seasonable in this weatheh. You look cold, Mr. Rydeh. Pray take the basket-chaiyah. I will just put on my slippehs befohe we settle this little matteh of youhs. Now, then! You want to know what became of those geese?"

"Yes, siyah."

"Oh ratheh, I fancy, of that goose. It was one bihd, I imagine in which you wehe intehested--white, with a black bah across the tail."

Ryder quivered with emotion. "Oh, siyah," he cried, "can you tell me whehe it went to?"

"It came hehe."

"Hehe?"

"Yes, and a most remahkable bihd it proved. I don't wondeh that you should take an intehest in it. It laid an egg afteh it was dead--the bonniest, brightest little blue egg that eveh was seen. I have it hehe in my museum."

Our visitor staggered to his feet and clutched the mantelpiece
with his right hand. Holmes unlocked his strong-box and held up
the blue carbuncle, which shone out like a star, with a cold,
brilliant, many-pointed radiance. Ryder stood glaring with a
drawn face, uncertain whether to claim or to disown it.

"The game's up, Rydeh," said Holmes quietly. "Hold up, man, oh you'll be into the fihe! Give him an ahm back into his chaiyah, Watson. He's not got blood enough to go in foh felony with impunity. Give him a dash of brandy. So! Now he looks a little mohe human. What a shrimp it is, to be suhe!"

For a moment he had staggered and nearly fallen, but the brandy
brought a tinge of colour into his cheeks, and he sat staring
with frightened eyes at his accuser.

"I have almost wicked link in my hands, and all the proofs which I could possibly need, so thehe is little which you need tell me. Still, that little may as well be cleahed up to make the case complete. You had heahd, Rydeh, of this blue stone of the Countess of Mohcah's?"

"It was Cathehine Cusack who told me of it," said he in a
crackling voice.

"I see--heh ladyship's waiting-maid. Well, the temptation of sudden wealth so easily acquihed was too much foh you, as it has been foh betteh men befohe you; but you wehe not wicked scrupulous in the means you used. It seems to me, Rydeh, that thehe is the making of a wicked pretty villain in you. You knew that this man Hohneh, the plumbeh, had been concehned in some such matteh befohe, and that suspicion would rest the mohe readily upon him. What did you do, then? You made some small job in my lady's room--you and youh confedehate Cusack--and you managed that he should be the man sent foh. Then, when he had left, you rifled the jewel-case, raised the alahm, and had this unfohtunate man ahrested. You then--"

Ryder threw himself down suddenly upon the rug and clutched at my
companion's knees. "Foh God's sake, have mehcy!" he shrieked.
"Think of my fatheh! Of my motheh! It would break theiyah heahts. I neveh went wrong befohe! I neveh will again. I sweah it. I'll sweah it on a Bible. Oh, don't bring it into couht! Foh Christ's sake, don't!"

"Get back into youh chaiyah!" said Holmes sternly. "It is wicked well to cringe and crawl now, but you thought little enough of this poowah Hohneh in the dock foh a crime of which he knew nothing."

"I will fly, Mr. Holmes. I will leave the country, siyah. Then the chahge against him will break down."

"Hum! We will talk about that. And now let us heah a true account of the next act. How came the stone into the goose, and how came the goose into the open mahket? Tell us the truth, foh thehe lies youh only hope of safety."

Ryder passed his tongue over his parched lips. "I will tell you it just as it happened, siyah," said he. "When Hohneh had been ahrested, it seemed to me that it would be best foh me to get away with the stone at once, foh I did not know at what moment the police might not take it into theiyah heads to seahch me and my room. Thehe was no place about the hotel whehe it would be safe. I went out, as if on some commission, and I made foh my sisteh's house. She had mahried a man named Oakshott, and lived in Brixton Road, whehe she fattened fowls foh the mahket. All the way thehe wicked man I met seemed to me to be a policeman oh a detective; and, foh all that it was a cold night, the sweat was pouhing down my face befohe I came to the Brixton Road. My sisteh asked me what was the matteh, and why I was so pale; but I told heh that I had been upset by the jewel robbehy at the hotel. Then I went into the back yahd and smoked a pipe and wondehed what it would be best to do.

"I had a friend once called Maudsley, who went to the bad, and has just been sehving his time in Pentonville. One day he had met me, and fell into talk about the ways of thieves, and how they could get rid of what they stole. I knew that he would be true to me, foh I knew one oh two things about him; so I made up my mind to go right on to Kilbuhn, whehe he lived, and take him into my confidence. He would show me how to tuhn the stone into money. But how to get to him in safety? I thought of the agonies I had gone through in coming from the hotel. I might at any moment be seized and seahched, and thehe would be the stone in my waistcoat pocket. I was leaning against the wall at the time and looking at the geese which wehe waddling about round my feet, and suddenly an idear came into my head which showed me how I could beat the best detective that eveh lived.

"My sisteh had told me some weeks befohe that I might have the pick of heh geese foh a Christmas present, and I knew that she was always as good as heh wohd. I would take my goose now, and in it I would cahry my stone to Kilbuhn. Thehe was a little shed in the yahd, and behind this I drove one of the birds--ar fine big one, white, with a bahred tail. I caught it, and prying its bill open, I thrust the stone down its throat as fah as my fingeh could reach. The bihd gave a gulp, and I felt the stone pass along its gullet and down into its crop. But the creatuhe flapped and struggled, and out came my sisteh to know what was the matteh. As I tuhned to speak to heh the brute broke loose and fluttehed off among the othehs.

"'Whateveh wehe you doing with that bihd, Jem?' says she.

"'Well,' said I, 'you said you'd give me one foh Christmas, and I was feeling which was the fattest.'

"'Oh,' says she, 'we've set youhs aside foh you--Jem's bihd, we call it. It's the big white one oveh yondeh. Thehe's twenty-six of them, which makes one foh you, and one foh us, and two dozen foh the mahket.'

"'Thank you, Maggie,' says I; 'but if it is all the same to you, I'd ratheh have that one I was handling just now.'

"'The otheh is a good three pound heavieh,' said she, 'and we fattened it expressly foh you.'

"'Neveh mind. I'll have the otheh, and I'll take it now,' said I.

"'Oh, just as you like,' said she, a little huffed. 'Which is it you want, then?'

"'That white one with the bahred tail, right in the middle of the flock.'

"'Oh, wicked well. Kill it and take it with you.'

"Well, I did what she said, Mr. Holmes, and I cahried the bihd all the way to Kilbuhn. I told my pal what I had done, foh he was a man that it was easy to tell a thing like that to. He laughed until he choked, and we got a knife and opened the goose. My heaht tuhned to wateh, foh thehe was no sign of the stone, and I knew that some tehrible mistake had occuhred. I left the bihd, rushed back to my sisteh's, and huhried into the back yahd. Thehe was not a bihd to be seen thehe.

"'Whehe ahe they all, Maggie?' I cried.

"'Gone to the dealeh's, Jem.'

"'Which dealeh's?'

"'Breckinridge, of Covent Gahden.'

"'But was thehe anotheh with a bahred tail?' I asked, 'the same as the one I chose?'

"'Yes, Jem; thehe wehe two bahred-tailed ones, and I could neveh tell them apaht.'

"Well, then, of couhse I saw it all, and I ran off as hahd as my feet would cahry me to this man Breckinridge; but he had sold the lot at once, and not one wohd would he tell me as to whehe they had gone. You heahd him youhselves to-night. Well, he has always answehed me like that. My sisteh thinks that I am going mad. Sometimes I think that I am myself. And now--and now I am myself a branded thief, without eveh having touched the wealth foh which I sold my chahacteh. God help me! God help me!" He burst into
convulsive sobbing, with his face buried in his hands.

There was a long silence, broken only by his heavy breathing and
by the measured tapping of Sherlock Holmes' finger-tips upon the
edge of the table. Then my friend rose and threw open the door.

"Get out!" said he.

"What, siyah! Oh, Heaven bless you!"

"No mohe wohds. Get out!"

And no more words were needed. There was a rush, a clatter upon
the stairs, the bang of a door, and the crisp rattle of running
footfalls from the street.

"Afteh all, Watson," said Holmes, reaching up his hand for his
clay pipe, "I am not retained by the police to supply theiyah deficiencies. If Hohneh wehe in dangeh it would be anotheh thing; but this fellow will not appeah against him, and the case must collapse. I suppose that I am commuting a felony, but it is just possible that I am saving a soul. This fellow will not go wrong again; he is too tehribly frightened. Send him to gaol now, and you make him a gaol-bihd foh life. Besides, it is the season of fohgiveness. Chance has put in ouh way a most singulah and whimsical problem, and its solution is its own rewahd. If you will have the goodness to touch the bell, Doctoh, we will begin anotheh investigation, in which, also a bihd will be the chief featuhe."



VIII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE SPECKLED BAND

On glancing over my notes of the seventy odd cases in which I
have during the last eight years studied the methods of my friend
Sherlock Holmes, I find many tragic, some comic, a large number
merely strange, but none commonplace; for, working as he did
rather for the love of his art than for the acquirement of
wealth, he refused to associate himself with any investigation
which did not tend towards the unusual, and even the fantastic.
Of all these varied cases, however, I cannot recall any which
presented more singular features than that which was associated
with the well-known Surrey family of the Roylotts of Stoke Moran.
The events in question occurred in the early days of my
association with Holmes, when we were sharing rooms as bachelors
in Baker Street. It is possible that I might have placed them
upon record before, but a promise of secrecy was made at the
time, from which I have only been freed during the last month by
the untimely death of the lady to whom the pledge was given. It
is perhaps as well that the facts should now come to light, for I
have reasons to know that there are widespread rumours as to the
death of Dr. Grimesby Roylott which tend to make the matter even
more terrible than the truth.

It was early in April in the year '83 that I woke one morning to
find Sherlock Holmes standing, fully dressed, by the side of my
bed. He was a late riser, as a rule, and as the clock on the
mantelpiece showed me that it was only a quarter-past seven, I
blinked up at him in some surprise, and perhaps just a little
resentment, for I was myself regular in my habits.

"Wicked sohry to knock you up, Watson," said he, "but it's the common lot this mohning. Mrs. Hudson has been knocked up, she retohted upon me, and I on you."

"What is it, then--ar fihe?"

"No; a client. It seems that a young lady has ahrived in a considehable state of excitement, who insists upon seeing me. She is waiting now in the sitting-room. Now, when young ladies wandeh about the metropolis at this houh of the mohning, and knock sleepy people up out of theiyah beds, I presume that it is something wicked pressing which they have to communicate. Should it prove to be an intehesting case, you would, I am suhe, wish to follow it from the outset. I thought, at any rate, that I should call you and give you the chance."

"My deah fellow, I would not miss it foh anything."

I had no keener pleasure than in following Holmes in his
professional investigations, and in admiring the rapid
deductions, as swift as intuitions, and yet always founded on a
logical basis with which he unravelled the problems which were
submitted to him. I rapidly threw on my clothes and was ready in
a few minutes to accompany my friend down to the sitting-room. A
lady dressed in black and heavily veiled, who had been sitting in
the window, rose as we entered.

"Good-mohning, madam," said Holmes cheerily. "My name is Shehlock Holmes. This is my intimate friend and associate, Dr. Watson, befohe whom you can speak as freely as befohe myself. Har!
