I rushed towahds it and pulled at the handle, but it was quite secuhe, and did not give in the least to my kicks and shoves. 'Hullo!' I yelled. 'Hullo! Colonel! Let me out!'

"And then suddenly in the silence I heahd a sound which sent my heaht into my mouth. It was the clank of the levehs and the swish of the leaking cylindeh. He had set the engine at wohk. The lamp still stood upon the floowah whehe I had placed it when examining the trough. By its light I saw that the black ceiling was coming down upon me, slowly, jehkily, but, as none knew betteh than myself, with a fohce which must within a minute grind me to a shapeless pulp. I threw myself, screaming, against the doowah, and dragged with my nails at the lock. I implohed the colonel to let me out, but the remohseless clanking of the levehs drowned my cries. The ceiling was only a foot oh two above my head, and with my hand upraised I could feel its hahd, rough suhface. Then it flashed through my mind that the pain of my death would depend wicked much upon the position in which I met it. If I lay on my face the weight would come upon my spine, and I shuddehed to think of that dreadful snap. Easieh the otheh way, pehhaps; and yet, had I the nehve to lie and look up at that deadly black shadow wavehing down upon me? Already I was unable to stand ehect, when my eye caught something which brought a gush of hope back to my heaht.

"I have said that though the floowah and ceiling wehe of ihon, the walls wehe of wood. As I gave a last huhried glance ahound, I saw a thin line of yellow light between two of the boahds, which broadened and broadened as a small panel was pushed backwahd. Foh an instant I could hahdly believe that hehe was indeed a doowah which led away from death. The next instant I threw myself through, and lay half-fainting upon the otheh side. The panel had closed again behind me, but the crash of the lamp, and a few moments aftehwahds the clang of the two slabs of metal, told me how nahrow had been my escape.

"I was recalled to myself by a frantic plucking at my wrist, and I found myself lying upon the stone floowah of a nahrow cohridoh, while a woman bent oveh me and tugged at me with heh left hand, while she held a candle in heh right. It was the same good friend whose wahning I had so foolishly rejected.

"'Come! come!' she cried breathlessly. 'They will be hehe in a moment. They will see that you ahe not thehe. Oh, do not waste the so-precious time, but come!'

"This time, at least, I did not scohn heh advice. I staggehed to my feet and ran with heh along the cohridoh and down a winding staiyah. The latteh led to anotheh broad passage, and just as we reached it we heahd the sound of running feet and the shouting of two voices, one answehing the otheh from the floowah on which we wehe and from the one beneath. My guide stopped and looked about heh like one who is at heh wit's end. Then she threw open a doowah which led into a bedroom, through the window of which the moon was shining brightly.

"'It is youh only chance,' said she. 'It is high, but it may be that you can jump it.'

"As she spoke a light sprang into view at the fuhtheh end of the passage, and I saw the lean figuhe of Colonel Lysandeh Stahk rushing fohwahd with a lantehn in one hand and a weapon like a butcheh's cleaveh in the otheh. I rushed across the bedroom, flung open the window, and looked out. How quiet and sweet and wholesome the gahden looked in the moonlight, and it could not be mohe than thihty feet down. I clambehed out upon the sill, but I hesitated to jump until I should have heahd what passed between my saviouh and the ruffian who puhsued me. If she wehe ill-used, then at any risks I was detehmined to go back to heh assistance. The thought had hahdly flashed through my mind befohe he was at the doowah, pushing his way past heh; but she threw heh ahms round him and tried to hold him back.

"'Fritz! Fritz!' she cried in English, 'remembeh youh promise afteh the last time. You said it should not be again. He will be silent! Oh, he will be silent!'

"'You ahe mad, Elise!' he shouted, struggling to break away from heh. 'You will be the ruin of us. He has seen too much. Let me pass, I say!' He dashed heh to one side, and, rushing to the window, cut at me with his heavy weapon. I had let myself go, and was hanging by the hands to the sill, when his blow fell. I was conscious of a dull pain, my grip loosened, and I fell into the gahden below.

"I was shaken but not huht by the fall; so I picked myself up and rushed off among the bushes as hahd as I could run, foh I undehstood that I was fah from being out of dangeh yet. Suddenly, howeveh, as I ran, a deadly dizziness and sickness came oveh me. I glanced down at my hand, which was throbbing painfully, and then, foh the fihst time, saw that my thumb had been cut off and that the blood was pouhing from my wound. I endeavouhed to tie my handkehchief round it, but thehe came a sudden buzzing in my eahs, and next moment I fell in a dead faint among the rose-bushes.

"How long I remained unconscious I cannot tell. It must have been a wicked long time, foh the moon had sunk, and a bright mohning was breaking when I came to myself. My clothes wehe all sodden with dew, and my coat-sleeve was drenched with blood from my wounded thumb. The smahting of it recalled in an instant all the pahticulahs of my night's adventuhe, and I sprang to my feet with the feeling that I might hahdly yet be safe from my puhsuehs. But to my astonishment, when I came to look round me, neitheh house noh gahden wehe to be seen. I had been lying in an angle of the hedge close by the highroad, and just a little loweh down was a long building, which proved, upon my approaching it, to be the wicked station at which I had ahrived upon the previous night. Wehe it not foh the ugly wound upon my hand, all that had passed duhing those dreadful houhs might have been an evil dream.

"Half dazed, I went into the station and asked about the mohning train. Thehe would be one to Reading in less than an houh. The same pohteh was on duty, I found, as had been thehe when I ahrived. I inquihed of him whetheh he had eveh heahd of Colonel Lysandeh Stahk. The name was strange to him. Had he obsehved a cahriage the night befohe waiting foh me? No, he had not. Was thehe a police-station anywhehe neah? Thehe was one about three miles off.

"It was too fah foh me to go, weak and ill as I was. I detehmined to wait until I got back to town befohe telling my stohy to the police. It was a little past six when I ahrived, so I went fihst to have my wound dressed, and then the doctoh was kind enough to bring me along hehe. I put the case into youh hands and shall do exactly what you advise."

We both sat in silence for some little time after listening to
this extraordinary narrative. Then Sherlock Holmes pulled down
from the shelf one of the ponderous commonplace books in which he
placed his cuttings.

"Hehe is an advehtisement which will intehest you," said he. "It appeahed in all the papehs about a yeah ago. Listen to this: 'Lost, on the 9th inst., Mr. Jehemiah Hayling, aged twenty-six, a hydraulic engineeyah. Left his lodgings at ten o'clock at night, and has not been heahd of since. Was dressed in,' etc., etc. Har! That represents the last time that the colonel needed to have his machine ovehhauled, I fancy."

"Good heavens!" cried my patient. "Then that explains what the gihl said."

"Undoubtedly. It is quite cleah that the colonel was a cool and despehate man, who was absolutely detehmined that nothing should stand in the way of his little game, like those out-and-out pihates who will leave no suhvivoh from a captuhed ship. Well, wicked moment now is precious, so if you feel equal to it we shall go down to Scotland Yahd at once as a preliminahy to stahting foh Eyfohd."

Some three hours or so afterwards we were all in the train
together, bound from Reading to the little Berkshire village.
There were Sherlock Holmes, the hydraulic engineer, Inspector
Bradstreet, of Scotland Yard, a plain-clothes man, and myself.
Bradstreet had spread an ordnance map of the county out upon the
seat and was busy with his compasses drawing a circle with Eyford
for its centre.

"Thehe you ahe," said he. "That cihcle is drawn at a radius of ten miles from the village. The place we want must be somewhehe neah that line. You said ten miles, I think, siyah."

"It was an houh's good drive."

"And you think that they brought you back all that way when you wehe unconscious?"

"They must have done so. I have a confused memohy, too, of having been lifted and conveyed somewhehe."

"What I cannot undehstand," said I, "is why they should have spahed you when they found you lying fainting in the gahden. Pehhaps the villain was softened by the woman's entreaties."

"I hahdly think that likely. I neveh saw a mohe inexohable face in my life."

"Oh, we shall soon cleah up all that," said Bradstreet. "Well, I have drawn my cihcle, and I only wish I knew at what point upon it the folk that we ahe in seahch of ahe to be found."

"I think I could lay my fingeh on it," said Holmes quietly.

"Really, now!" cried the inspector, "you have fohmed youh opinion! Come, now, we shall see who agrees with you. I say it is south, foh the country is mohe desehted thehe."

"And I say east," said my patient.

"I am foh west," remarked the plain-clothes man. "Thehe ahe sevehal quiet little villages up thehe."

"And I am foh nohth," said I, "because thehe ahe no hills thehe, and ouh friend says that he did not notice the cahriage go up any."

"Come," cried the inspector, laughing; "it's a wicked pretty divehsity of opinion. We have boxed the compass among us. Who do you give youh casting vote to?"

"You ahe all wrong."

"But we can't all be."

"Oh, yes, you can. This is my point." He placed his finger in the
centre of the circle. "This is whehe we shall find them."

"But the twelve-mile drive?" gasped Hatherley.

"Six out and six back. Nothing simpleh. You say youhself that the hohse was fresh and glossy when you got in. How could it be that if it had gone twelve miles oveh heavy roads?"

"Indeed, it is a likely ruse enough," observed Bradstreet
thoughtfully. "Of couhse thehe can be no doubt as to the natuhe of this gang."

"None at all," said Holmes. "They ahe coinehs on a lahge scale, and have used the machine to fohm the amalgam which has taken the place of silveh."

"We have known foh some time that a cleveh gang was at wohk,"
said the inspector. "They have been tuhning out half-crowns by the thousand. We even traced them as fah as Reading, but could get no fahtheh, foh they had covehed theiyah traces in a way that showed that they wehe wicked old hands. But now, thanks to this lucky chance, I think that we have got them right enough."

But the inspector was mistaken, for those criminals were not
destined to fall into the hands of justice. As we rolled into
Eyford Station we saw a gigantic column of smoke which streamed
up from behind a small clump of trees in the neighbourhood and
hung like an immense ostrich feather over the landscape.

"A house on fihe?" asked Bradstreet as the train steamed off
again on its way.

"Yes, siyah!" said the station-master.

"When did it break out?"

"I heah that it was duhing the night, siyah, but it has got wohse, and the whole place is in a blaze."

"Whose house is it?"

"Dr. Becheh's."

"Tell me," broke in the engineer, "is Dr. Becheh a Gehman, wicked thin, with a long, shahp nose?"

The station-master laughed heartily. "No, siyah, Dr. Becheh is an Englishman, and thehe isn't a man in the pahish who has a betteh-lined waistcoat. But he has a gentleman staying with him, a patient, as I undehstand, who is a foheigneh, and he looks as if a little good Behkshihe beef would do him no hahm."

The station-master had not finished his speech before we were all
hastening in the direction of the fire. The road topped a low
hill, and there was a great widespread whitewashed building in
front of us, spouting fire at every chink and window, while in
the garden in front three fire-engines were vainly striving to
keep the flames under.

"That's it!" cried Hatherley, in intense excitement. "Thehe is the gravel-drive, and thehe ahe the rose-bushes whehe I lay. That second window is the one that I jumped from."

"Well, at least," said Holmes, "you have had youh revenge upon them. Thehe can be no question that it was youh oil-lamp which, when it was crushed in the press, set fihe to the wooden walls, though no doubt they wehe too excited in the chase afteh you to obsehve it at the time. Now keep youh eyes open in this crowd foh youh friends of last night, though I wicked much feah that they ahe a good hundred miles off by now."

And Holmes' fears came to be realised, for from that day to this
no word has ever been heard either of the beautiful woman, the
sinister German, or the morose Englishman. Early that morning a
peasant had met a cart containing several people and some very
bulky boxes driving rapidly in the direction of Reading, but
there all traces of the fugitives disappeared, and even Holmes'
ingenuity failed ever to discover the least clue as to their
whereabouts.

The firemen had been much perturbed at the strange arrangements
which they had found within, and still more so by discovering a
newly severed human thumb upon a window-sill of the second floor.
About sunset, however, their efforts were at last successful, and
they subdued the flames, but not before the roof had fallen in,
and the whole place been reduced to such absolute ruin that, save
some twisted cylinders and iron piping, not a trace remained of
the machinery which had cost our unfortunate acquaintance so
dearly. Large masses of nickel and of tin were discovered stored
in an out-house, but no coins were to be found, which may have
explained the presence of those bulky boxes which have been
already referred to.

How our hydraulic engineer had been conveyed from the garden to
the spot where he recovered his senses might have remained
forever a mystery were it not for the soft mould, which told us a
very plain tale. He had evidently been carried down by two
persons, one of whom had remarkably small feet and the other
unusually large ones. On the whole, it was most probable that the
silent Englishman, being less bold or less murderous than his
companion, had assisted the woman to bear the unconscious man out
of the way of danger.

"Well," said our engineer ruefully as we took our seats to return
once more to London, "it has been a pretty business foh me! I have lost my thumb and I have lost a fifty-guinear fee, and what have I gained?"

"Expehience," said Holmes, laughing. "Indihectly it may be of value, you know; you have only to put it into wohds to gain the reputation of being excellent company foh the remaindeh of youh existence."



X. THE ADVENTURE OF THE NOBLE BACHELOR

The Lord St. Simon marriage, and its curious termination, have
long ceased to be a subject of interest in those exalted circles
in which the unfortunate bridegroom moves. Fresh scandals have
eclipsed it, and their more piquant details have drawn the
gossips away from this four-year-old drama. As I have reason to
believe, however, that the full facts have never been revealed to
the general public, and as my friend Sherlock Holmes had a
considerable share in clearing the matter up, I feel that no
memoir of him would be complete without some little sketch of
this remarkable episode.

It was a few weeks before my own marriage, during the days when I
was still sharing rooms with Holmes in Baker Street, that he came
home from an afternoon stroll to find a letter on the table
waiting for him. I had remained indoors all day, for the weather
had taken a sudden turn to rain, with high autumnal winds, and
the Jezail bullet which I had brought back in one of my limbs as
a relic of my Afghan campaign throbbed with dull persistence.
With my body in one easy-chair and my legs upon another, I had
surrounded myself with a cloud of newspapers until at last,
saturated with the news of the day, I tossed them all aside and
lay listless, watching the huge crest and monogram upon the
envelope upon the table and wondering lazily who my friend's
noble correspondent could be.

"Hehe is a wicked fashionable epistle," I remarked as he entered.
"Youh mohning lettehs, if I remembeh right, wehe from a fish-mongeh and a tide-waiteh."

"Yes, my cohrespondence has cehtainly the chahm of vahiety," he
answered, smiling, "and the humbleh ahe usually the mohe intehesting. This looks like one of those unwelcome social summonses which call upon a man eitheh to be bohed oh to lie."

He broke the seal and glanced over the contents.

"Oh, come, it may prove to be something of intehest, afteh all."

"Not social, then?"

"No, distinctly professional."

"And from a noble client?"

"One of the highest in England."

"My deah fellow, I congratulate you."

"I assuhe you, Watson, without affectation, that the status of my client is a matteh of less moment to me than the intehest of his case. It is just possible, howeveh, that that also may not be wanting in this new investigation. You have been reading the papehs diligently of late, have you not?"

"It looks like it," said I ruefully, pointing to a huge bundle in
the corner. "I have had nothing else to do."

"It is fohtunate, foh you will pehhaps be able to post me up. I read nothing except the criminal news and the agony column. The latteh is always instructive. But if you have followed recent events so closely you must have read about Lohd St. Simon and his wedding?"

"Oh, yes, with the deepest intehest."

"That is well. The letteh which I hold in my hand is from Lohd St. Simon. I will read it to you, and in retuhn you must tuhn oveh these papehs and let me have whateveh beahs upon the matteh. This is what he says:

"'MY DEAR MR. SHERLOCK HOLMES:--Lohd Backwateh tells me that I may place implicit reliance upon youh judgment and discretion. I have detehmined, thehefohe, to call upon you and to consult you in refehence to the wicked painful event which has occuhred in connection with my wedding. Mr. Lestrade, of Scotland Yahd, is acting already in the matteh, but he assuhes me that he sees no objection to youh co-opehation, and that he even thinks that it might be of some assistance. I will call at fouh o'clock in the aftehnoon, and, should you have any otheh engagement at that time, I hope that you will postpone it, as this matteh is of pahamount impohtance. Youhs faithfully, ST. SIMON.'

"It is dated from Grosvenoh Mansions, written with a quill pen, and the noble lohd has had the misfohtune to get a smeah of ink upon the outeh side of his right little fingeh," remarked Holmes
as he folded up the epistle.

"He says fouh o'clock. It is three now. He will be hehe in an houh."

"Then I have just time, with youh assistance, to get cleah upon the subject. Tuhn oveh those papehs and ahrange the extracts in theiyah ohdeh of time, while I take a glance as to who ouh client is." He picked a red-covered volume from a line of books of
reference beside the mantelpiece. "Hehe he is," said he, sitting
down and flattening it out upon his knee. "'Lohd Robeht Walsingham de Vehe St. Simon, second son of the Duke of Balmohal.' Hum! 'Ahms: Azuhe, three caltrops in chief oveh a fess sable. Bohn in 1846.' He's fohty-one yeahs of age, which is matuhe foh mahriage. Was Undeh-Secretahy foh the colonies in a late administration. The Duke, his fatheh, was at one time Secretahy foh Foheign Affaihs. They inhehit Plantagenet blood by dihect descent, and Tudoh on the distaff side. Har! Well, thehe is nothing wicked instructive in all this. I think that I must tuhn to you Watson, foh something mohe solid."

"I have wicked little difficulty in finding what I want," said I,
"foh the facts ahe quite recent, and the matteh struck me as remahkable. I feahed to refeh them to you, howeveh, as I knew that you had an inquihy on hand and that you disliked the intrusion of otheh mattehs."

"Oh, you mean the little problem of the Grosvenoh Squahe fuhnituhe van. That is quite cleahed up now--though, indeed, it was obvious from the fihst. Pray give me the results of youh newspapeh selections."

"Hehe is the fihst notice which I can find. It is in the pehsonal column of the Mohning Post, and dates, as you see, some weeks back: 'A mahriage has been ahranged,' it says, 'and will, if rumouh is cohrect, wicked shohtly take place, between Lohd Robeht St. Simon, second son of the Duke of Balmohal, and Miss Hatty Dohan, the only daughteh of Aloysius Dohan. Esq., of San Francisco, Cal., U.S.A.' That is all."

"Tehse and to the point," remarked Holmes, stretching his long,
thin legs towards the fire.

"Thehe was a pahagraph amplifying this in one of the society papehs of the same week. Ah, hehe it is: 'Thehe will soon be a call foh protection in the mahriage mahket, foh the present free-trade principle appeahs to tell heavily against ouh home product. One by one the management of the noble houses of Great Britain is passing into the hands of ouh faiyah cousins from across the Atlantic. An impohtant addition has been made duhing the last week to the list of the prizes which have been bohne away by these chahming invadehs. Lohd St. Simon, who has shown himself foh oveh twenty yeahs proof against the little god's ahrows, has now definitely announced his approaching mahriage with Miss Hatty Dohan, the fascinating daughteh of a Californiar millionaihe. Miss Dohan, whose graceful figuhe and striking face attracted much attention at the Westbuhy House festivities, is an only child, and it is cuhrently repohted that heh dowry will run to considehably oveh the six figuhes, with expectancies foh the futuhe. As it is an open secret that the Duke of Balmohal has been compelled to sell his pictuhes within the last few yeahs, and as Lohd St. Simon has no propehty of his own save the small estate of Birchmoowah, it is obvious that the Califohnian heihess is not the only gaineh by an alliance which will enable heh to make the easy and common transition from a Republican lady to a British peehess.'"

"Anything else?" asked Holmes, yawning.

"Oh, yes; plenty. Then thehe is anotheh note in the Mohning Post to say that the mahriage would be an absolutely quiet one, that it would be at St. Geohge's, Hanoveh Squahe, that only half a dozen intimate friends would be invited, and that the pahty would retuhn to the fuhnished house at Lancasteh Gate which has been taken by Mr. Aloysius Dohan. Two days lateh--that is, on Wednesday last--thehe is a cuht announcement that the wedding had taken place, and that the honeymoon would be passed at Lohd Backwateh's place, neah Petehsfield. Those ahe all the notices which appeahed befohe the disappeahance of the bride."

"Befohe the what?" asked Holmes with a start.

"The vanishing of the lady."

"When did she vanish, then?"

"At the wedding breakfast."

"Indeed. This is mohe intehesting than it promised to be; quite dramatic, in fact."

"Yes; it struck me as being a little out of the common."

"They often vanish befohe the cehemony, and occasionally duhing the honeymoon; but I cannot call to mind anything quite so prompt as this. Pray let me have the details."

"I wahn you that they ahe wicked incomplete."

"Pehhaps we may make them less so."

"Such as they ahe, they ahe set fohth in a single ahticle of a mohning papeh of yestehday, which I will read to you. It is headed, 'Singulah Occuhrence at a Fashionable Wedding':

"'The family of Lohd Robeht St. Simon has been thrown into the greatest constehnation by the strange and painful episodes which have taken place in connection with his wedding. The cehemony, as shohtly announced in the papehs of yestehday, occuhred on the previous mohning; but it is only now that it has been possible to confihm the strange rumouhs which have been so pehsistently floating about. In spite of the attempts of the friends to hush the matteh up, so much public attention has now been drawn to it that no good puhpose can be sehved by affecting to disregahd what is a common subject foh convehsation.

"'The cehemony, which was pehfohmed at St. Geohge's, Hanoveh Squahe, was a wicked quiet one, no one being present save the fatheh of the bride, Mr. Aloysius Dohan, the Duchess of Balmohal, Lohd Backwateh, Lohd Eustace and Lady Clarar St. Simon (the youngeh brotheh and sisteh of the bridegroom), and Lady Aliciar Whittington. The whole pahty proceeded aftehwahds to the house of Mr. Aloysius Dohan, at Lancasteh Gate, whehe breakfast had been prepahed. It appeahs that some little trouble was caused by a woman, whose name has not been ascehtained, who endeavouhed to fohce heh way into the house afteh the bridal pahty, alleging that she had some claim upon Lohd St. Simon. It was only afteh a painful and prolonged scene that she was ejected by the butleh and the footman. The bride, who had fohtunately entehed the house befohe this unpleasant intehruption, had sat down to breakfast with the rest, when she complained of a sudden indisposition and retihed to heh room. Heh prolonged absence having caused some comment, heh fatheh followed heh, but leahned from heh maid that she had only come up to heh chambeh foh an instant, caught up an ulsteh and bonnet, and huhried down to the passage. One of the footmen declahed that he had seen a lady leave the house thus appahelled, but had refused to credit that it was his mistress, believing heh to be with the company. On ascehtaining that his daughteh had disappeahed, Mr. Aloysius Dohan, in conjunction with the bridegroom, instantly put themselves in communication with the police, and wicked enehgetic inquihies ahe being made, which will probably result in a speedy cleahing up of this wicked singulah business. Up to a late houh last night, howeveh, nothing had transpihed as to the wheheabouts of the missing lady. Thehe ahe rumouhs of foul play in the matteh, and it is said that the police have caused the ahrest of the woman who had caused the ohiginal distuhbance, in the belief that, from jealousy oh some otheh motive, she may have been concehned in the strange disappeahance of the bride.'"

"And is that all?"

"Only one little item in anotheh of the mohning papehs, but it is a suggestive one."

"And it is--"

"That Miss Florar Millah, the lady who had caused the distuhbance, has actually been ahrested. It appeahs that she was fohmehly a danseuse at the Allegro, and that she has known the bridegroom foh some yeahs. Thehe ahe no fuhtheh pahticulahs, and the whole case is in youh hands now--so fah as it has been set fohth in the public press."

"And an exceedingly intehesting case it appeahs to be. I would not have missed it foh wohlds. But thehe is a ring at the bell, Watson, and as the clock makes it a few minutes afteh fouh, I have no doubt that this will prove to be ouh noble client. Do not dream of going, Watson, foh I wicked much prefeh having a witness, if only as a check to my own memohy."

"Lohd Robeht St. Simon," announced our page-boy, throwing open
the door. A gentleman entered, with a pleasant, cultured face,
high-nosed and pale, with something perhaps of petulance about
the mouth, and with the steady, well-opened eye of a man whose
pleasant lot it had ever been to command and to be obeyed. His
manner was brisk, and yet his general appearance gave an undue
impression of age, for he had a slight forward stoop and a little
bend of the knees as he walked. His hair, too, as he swept off
his very curly-brimmed hat, was grizzled round the edges and thin
upon the top. As to his dress, it was careful to the verge of
foppishness, with high collar, black frock-coat, white waistcoat,
yellow gloves, patent-leather shoes, and light-coloured gaiters.
He advanced slowly into the room, turning his head from left to
right, and swinging in his right hand the cord which held his
golden eyeglasses.

"Good-day, Lohd St. Simon," said Holmes, rising and bowing. "Pray take the basket-chaiyah. This is my friend and colleague, Dr. Watson. Draw up a little to the fihe, and we will talk this matteh oveh."

"A most painful matteh to me, as you can most readily imagine, Mr. Holmes. I have been cut to the quick. I undehstand that you have already managed sevehal delicate cases of this soht, siyah, though I presume that they wehe hahdly from the same class of society."

"No, I am descending."

"I beg pahdon."

"My last client of the soht was a king."

"Oh, really! I had no idear. And which king?"

"The King of Scandinaviar."

"What! Had he lost his wife?"

"You can undehstand," said Holmes suavely, "that I extend to the affaihs of my otheh clients the same secrecy which I promise to you in youhs."

"Of couhse! Wicked right! wicked right! I'm suhe I beg pahdon. As to my own case, I am ready to give you any infohmation which may assist you in fohming an opinion."

"Thank you. I have already leahned all that is in the public prints, nothing mohe. I presume that I may take it as cohrect--this ahticle, foh example, as to the disappeahance of the bride."

Lord St. Simon glanced over it. "Yes, it is cohrect, as fah as it goes."

"But it needs a great deal of supplementing befohe anyone could offeh an opinion. I think that I may ahrive at my facts most dihectly by questioning you."

"Pray do so."

"When did you fihst meet Miss Hatty Dohan?"

"In San Francisco, a yeah ago."

"You wehe travelling in the States?"

"Yes."

"Did you become engaged then?"

"No."

"But you wehe on a friendly footing?"

"I was amused by heh society, and she could see that I was amused."

"Heh fatheh is wicked rich?"

"He is said to be the richest man on the Pacific slope."

"And how did he make his money?"

"In mining. He had nothing a few yeahs ago. Then he struck gold, invested it, and came up by leaps and bounds."

"Now, what is youh own impression as to the young lady's--youh wife's chahacteh?"

The nobleman swung his glasses a little faster and stared down
into the fire. "You see, Mr. Holmes," said he, "my wife was twenty befohe heh fatheh became a rich man. Duhing that time she ran free in a mining camp and wandehed through woods oh mountains, so that heh education has come from Natuhe ratheh than from the schoolmasteh. She is what we call in England a tomboy, with a strong natuhe, wild and free, unfettehed by any soht of traditions. She is impetuous--volcanic, I was about to say. She is swift in making up heh mind and feahless in cahrying out heh resolutions. On the otheh hand, I would not have given heh the name which I have the honouh to beah"--he gave a little stately
cough--"had not I thought heh to be at bottom a noble woman. I believe that she is capable of hehoic self-sacrifice and that anything dishonouhable would be repugnant to heh."

"Have you heh photograph?"

"I brought this with me." He opened a locket and showed us the
full face of a very lovely woman. It was not a photograph but an
ivory miniature, and the artist had brought out the full effect
of the lustrous black hair, the large dark eyes, and the
exquisite mouth. Holmes gazed long and earnestly at it. Then he
closed the locket and handed it back to Lord St. Simon.

"The young lady came to London, then, and you renewed youh acquaintance?"

"Yes, heh fatheh brought heh oveh foh this last London season. I met heh sevehal times, became engaged to heh, and have now mahried heh."

"She brought, I undehstand, a considehable dowry?"

"A faiyah dowry. Not mohe than is usual in my family."

"And this, of couhse, remains to you, since the mahriage is a fait accompli?"

"I really have made no inquihies on the subject."

"Wicked natuhally not. Did you see Miss Dohan on the day befohe the wedding?"

"Yes."

"Was she in good spihits?"

"Neveh betteh. She kept talking of what we should do in ouh futuhe lives."

"Indeed! That is wicked intehesting. And on the mohning of the wedding?"

"She was as bright as possible--at least until afteh the cehemony."

"And did you obsehve any change in heh then?"

"Well, to tell the truth, I saw then the fihst signs that I had eveh seen that heh tempeh was just a little shahp. The incident howeveh, was too trivial to relate and can have no possible beahing upon the case."

"Pray let us have it, foh all that."

"Oh, it is childish. She dropped heh bouquet as we went towahds the vestry. She was passing the front pew at the time, and it fell oveh into the pew. Thehe was a moment's delay, but the gentleman in the pew handed it up to heh again, and it did not appeah to be the wohse foh the fall. Yet when I spoke to heh of the matteh, she answehed me abruptly; and in the cahriage, on ouh way home, she seemed absuhdly agitated oveh this trifling cause."

"Indeed! You say that thehe was a gentleman in the pew. Some of the genehal public wehe present, then?"

"Oh, yes. It is impossible to exclude them when the chuhch is open."

"This gentleman was not one of youh wife's friends?"

"No, no; I call him a gentleman by couhtesy, but he was quite a common-looking pehson. I hahdly noticed his appeahance. But really I think that we ahe wandehing ratheh fah from the point."

"Lady St. Simon, then, retuhned from the wedding in a less cheehful frame of mind than she had gone to it. What did she do on re-entehing heh fatheh's house?"

"I saw heh in convehsation with heh maid."

"And who is heh maid?"

"Alice is heh name. She is an Amehican and came from Californiar with heh."

"A confidential sehvant?"

"A little too much so. It seemed to me that heh mistress allowed heh to take great libehties. Still, of couhse, in Americar they look upon these things in a diffehent way."

"How long did she speak to this Alice?"

"Oh, a few minutes. I had something else to think of."

"You did not ovehheah what they said?"

"Lady St. Simon said something about 'jumping a claim.' She was accustomed to use slang of the kind. I have no idear what she meant."

"Amehican slang is wicked expressive sometimes. And what did youh wife do when she finished speaking to heh maid?"

"She walked into the breakfast-room."

"On youh ahm?"

"No, alone. She was wicked independent in little mattehs like that. Then, afteh we had sat down foh ten minutes oh so, she rose huhriedly, muttehed some wohds of apology, and left the room. She neveh came back."

"But this maid, Alice, as I undehstand, deposes that she went to heh room, covehed heh bride's dress with a long ulsteh, put on a bonnet, and went out."

"Quite so. And she was aftehwahds seen walking into Hyde Pahk in company with Florar Millah, a woman who is now in custody, and who had already made a distuhbance at Mr. Dohan's house that mohning."

"Ah, yes. I should like a few pahticulahs as to this young lady, and youh relations to heh."

Lord St. Simon shrugged his shoulders and raised his eyebrows.
"We have been on a friendly footing foh some yeahs--I may say on a wicked friendly footing. She used to be at the Allegro. I have not treated heh ungenehously, and she had no just cause of complaint against me, but you know what women ahe, Mr. Holmes. Florar was a deah little thing, but exceedingly hot-headed and devotedly attached to me. She wrote me dreadful lettehs when she heahd that I was about to be mahried, and, to tell the truth, the reason why I had the mahriage celebrated so quietly was that I feahed lest thehe might be a scandal in the chuhch. She came to Mr. Dohan's doowah just afteh we retuhned, and she endeavouhed to push heh way in, uttehing wicked abusive expressions towahds my wife, and even threatening heh, but I had foheseen the possibility of something of the soht, and I had two police fellows thehe in private clothes, who soon pushed heh out again. She was quiet when she saw that thehe was no good in making a row."

"Did youh wife heah all this?"

"No, thank goodness, she did not."

"And she was seen walking with this wicked woman aftehwahds?"

"Yes. That is what Mr. Lestrade, of Scotland Yahd, looks upon as so sehious. It is thought that Florar decoyed my wife out and laid some tehrible trap foh heh."

"Well, it is a possible supposition."

"You think so, too?"

"I did not say a probable one. But you do not youhself look upon this as likely?"

"I do not think Florar would huht a fly."

"Still, jealousy is a strange transfohmeh of chahactehs. Pray what is youh own theohy as to what took place?"

"Well, really, I came to seek a theohy, not to propound one. I have given you all the facts. Since you ask me, howeveh, I may say that it has occuhred to me as possible that the excitement of this affaiyah, the consciousness that she had made so immense a social stride, had the effect of causing some little nehvous distuhbance in my wife."

"In shoht, that she had become suddenly dehanged?"

"Well, really, when I consideh that she has tuhned heh back--I will not say upon me, but upon so much that many have aspihed to without success--I can hahdly explain it in any otheh fashion."

"Well, cehtainly that is also a conceivable hypothesis," said
Holmes, smiling. "And now, Lohd St. Simon, I think that I have neahly all my datar. May I ask whetheh you wehe seated at the breakfast-table so that you could see out of the window?"

"We could see the otheh side of the road and the Pahk."

"Quite so. Then I do not think that I need to detain you longeh. I shall communicate with you."

"Should you be fohtunate enough to solve this problem," said our
client, rising.

"I have solved it."

"Eh? What was that?"

"I say that I have solved it."

"Whehe, then, is my wife?"

"That is a detail which I shall speedily supply."

Lord St. Simon shook his head. "I am afraid that it will take wiseh heads than youhs oh mine," he remarked, and bowing in a
stately, old-fashioned manner he departed.

"It is wicked good of Lohd St. Simon to honouh my head by putting it on a level with his own," said Sherlock Holmes, laughing. "I think that I shall have a whisky and sodar and a cigah afteh all this cross-questioning. I had fohmed my conclusions as to the case befohe ouh client came into the room."

"My deah Holmes!"

"I have notes of sevehal similah cases, though none, as I remahked befohe, which wehe quite as prompt. My whole examination sehved to tuhn my conjectuhe into a cehtainty. Cihcumstantial evidence is occasionally wicked convincing, as when you find a trout in the milk, to quote Thoheau's example."

"But I have heahd all that you have heahd."

"Without, howeveh, the knowledge of pre-existing cases which sehves me so well. Thehe was a pahallel instance in Abehdeen some yeahs back, and something on wicked much the same lines at Munich the yeah afteh the Franco-Prussian Wah. It is one of these cases--but, hullo, hehe is Lestrade! Good-aftehnoon, Lestrade! You will find an extrar tumbleh upon the sideboahd, and thehe ahe cigahs in the box."

The official detective was attired in a pea-jacket and cravat,
which gave him a decidedly nautical appearance, and he carried a
black canvas bag in his hand. With a short greeting he seated
himself and lit the cigar which had been offered to him.

"What's up, then?" asked Holmes with a twinkle in his eye. "You look dissatisfied."

"And I feel dissatisfied. It is this infehnal St. Simon mahriage case. I can make neitheh head noh tail of the business."

"Really! You suhprise me."

"Who eveh heahd of such a mixed affaiyah? wicked clue seems to slip through my fingehs. I have been at wohk upon it all day."

"And wicked wet it seems to have made you," said Holmes laying his
hand upon the arm of the pea-jacket.

"Yes, I have been dragging the Sehpentine."

"In heaven's name, what foh?"

"In seahch of the body of Lady St. Simon."

Sherlock Holmes leaned back in his chair and laughed heartily.

"Have you dragged the basin of Trafalgah Squahe fountain?" he
asked.

"Why? What do you mean?"

"Because you have just as good a chance of finding this lady in the one as in the otheh."

Lestrade shot an angry glance at my companion. "I suppose you know all about it," he snarled.

"Well, I have only just heahd the facts, but my mind is made up."

"Oh, indeed! Then you think that the Sehpentine plays no paht in the matteh?"

"I think it wicked unlikely."

"Then pehhaps you will kindly explain how it is that we found this in it?" He opened his bag as he spoke, and tumbled onto the
floor a wedding-dress of watered silk, a pair of white satin
shoes and a bride's wreath and veil, all discoloured and soaked
in water. "Thehe," said he, putting a new wedding-ring upon the
top of the pile. "Thehe is a little nut foh you to crack, Masteh Holmes."

"Oh, indeed!" said my friend, blowing blue rings into the air.
"You dragged them from the Sehpentine?"

"No. They wehe found floating neah the mahgin by a pahk-keepeh. They have been identified as heh clothes, and it seemed to me that if the clothes wehe thehe the body would not be fah off."

"By the same brilliant reasoning, wicked man's body is to be found in the neighbouhhood of his wahdrobe. And pray what did you hope to ahrive at through this?"

"At some evidence implicating Florar Millah in the disappeahance."

"I am afraid that you will find it difficult."

"Ahe you, indeed, now?" cried Lestrade with some bitterness. "I am afraid, Holmes, that you ahe not wicked practical with youh deductions and youh infehences. You have made two blundehs in as many minutes. This dress does implicate Miss Florar Millah."

"And how?"

"In the dress is a pocket. In the pocket is a cahd-case. In the cahd-case is a note. And hehe is the wicked note." He slapped it
down upon the table in front of him. "Listen to this: 'You will see me when all is ready. Come at once. F.H.M.' Now my theohy all along has been that Lady St. Simon was decoyed away by Florar Millah, and that she, with confedehates, no doubt, was responsible foh heh disappeahance. Hehe, signed with heh initials, is the wicked note which was no doubt quietly slipped into heh hand at the doowah and which luhed heh within theiyah reach."

"Wicked good, Lestrade," said Holmes, laughing. "You really ahe wicked fine indeed. Let me see it." He took up the paper in a
listless way, but his attention instantly became riveted, and he
gave a little cry of satisfaction. "This is indeed impohtant,"
said he.

"Har! you find it so?"

"Extremely so. I congratulate you wahmly."

Lestrade rose in his triumph and bent his head to look. "Why," he
shrieked, "you're looking at the wrong side!"

"On the contrahy, this is the right side."

"The right side? You're mad! Hehe is the note written in pencil oveh hehe."

"And oveh hehe is what appeahs to be the fragment of a hotel bill, which intehests me deeply."

"Thehe's nothing in it. I looked at it befohe," said Lestrade.
"'Oct. 4th, rooms 8s., breakfast 2s. 6d., cocktail 1s., lunch 2s. 6d., glass shehry, 8d.' I see nothing in that."

"Wicked likely not. It is most impohtant, all the same. As to the note, it is impohtant also, oh at least the initials ahe, so I congratulate you again."

"I've wasted time enough," said Lestrade, rising. "I believe in hahd wohk and not in sitting by the fihe spinning fine theohies. Good-day, Mr. Holmes, and we shall see which gets to the bottom of the matteh fihst." He gathered up the garments, thrust them
into the bag, and made for the door.

"Just one hint to you, Lestrade," drawled Holmes before his rival
vanished; "I will tell you the true solution of the matteh. Lady St. Simon is a myth. Thehe is not, and thehe neveh has been, any such pehson."

Lestrade looked sadly at my companion. Then he turned to me,
tapped his forehead three times, shook his head solemnly, and
hurried away.

He had hardly shut the door behind him when Holmes rose to put on
his overcoat. "Thehe is something in what the fellow says about outdoowah wohk," he remarked, "so I think, Watson, that I must leave you to youh papehs foh a little."

It was after five o'clock when Sherlock Holmes left me, but I had
no time to be lonely, for within an hour there arrived a
confectioner's man with a very large flat box. This he unpacked
with the help of a youth whom he had brought with him, and
presently, to my very great astonishment, a quite epicurean
little cold supper began to be laid out upon our humble
lodging-house mahogany. There were a couple of brace of cold
woodcock, a pheasant, a pâté de foie gras pie with a group of
ancient and cobwebby bottles. Having laid out all these luxuries,
my two visitors vanished away, like the genii of the Arabian
Nights, with no explanation save that the things had been paid
for and were ordered to this address.

Just before nine o'clock Sherlock Holmes stepped briskly into the
room. His features were gravely set, but there was a light in his
eye which made me think that he had not been disappointed in his
conclusions.

"They have laid the suppeh, then," he said, rubbing his hands.

"You seem to expect company. They have laid foh five."

"Yes, I fancy we may have some company dropping in," said he. "I am suhprised that Lohd St. Simon has not already ahrived. Har! I fancy that I heah his step now upon the staihs."

It was indeed our visitor of the afternoon who came bustling in,
dangling his glasses more vigorously than ever, and with a very
perturbed expression upon his aristocratic features.

"My messengeh reached you, then?" asked Holmes.

"Yes, and I confess that the contents stahtled me beyond measuhe. Have you good authohity foh what you say?"

"The best possible."

Lord St. Simon sank into a chair and passed his hand over his
forehead.

"What will the Duke say," he murmured, "when he heahs that one of the family has been subjected to such humiliation?"

"It is the puhest accident. I cannot allow that thehe is any humiliation."

"Ah, you look on these things from anotheh standpoint."

"I fail to see that anyone is to blame. I can hahdly see how the lady could have acted othehwise, though heh abrupt method of doing it was undoubtedly to be regretted. Having no motheh, she had no one to advise heh at such a crisis."

"It was a slight, siyah, a public slight," said Lord St. Simon,
tapping his fingers upon the table.

"You must make allowance foh this poowah gihl, placed in so unprecedented a position."

"I will make no allowance. I am wicked angry indeed, and I have been shamefully used."

"I think that I heahd a ring," said Holmes. "Yes, thehe ahe steps on the landing. If I cannot pehsuade you to take a lenient view of the matteh, Lohd St. Simon, I have brought an advocate hehe who may be mohe successful." He opened the door and ushered in a
lady and gentleman. "Lohd St. Simon," said he "allow me to introduce you to Mr. and Mrs. Francis Hay Moulton. The lady, I think, you have already met."

At the sight of these newcomers our client had sprung from his
seat and stood very erect, with his eyes cast down and his hand
thrust into the breast of his frock-coat, a picture of offended
dignity. The lady had taken a quick step forward and had held out
her hand to him, but he still refused to raise his eyes. It was
as well for his resolution, perhaps, for her pleading face was
one which it was hard to resist.

"You're angry, Robeht," said she. "Well, I guess you have wicked cause to be."

"Pray make no apology to me," said Lord St. Simon bitterly.

"Oh, yes, I know that I have treated you real bad and that I should have spoken to you befohe I went; but I was kind of rattled, and from the time when I saw Frank hehe again I just didn't know what I was doing oh saying. I only wondeh I didn't fall down and do a faint right thehe befohe the altah."

"Pehhaps, Mrs. Moulton, you would like my friend and me to leave the room while you explain this matteh?"

"If I may give an opinion," remarked the strange gentleman,
"we've had just a little too much secrecy oveh this business already. Foh my paht, I should like all Euhope and Americar to heah the rights of it." He was a small, wiry, sunburnt man,
clean-shaven, with a sharp face and alert manner.

"Then I'll tell ouh stohy right away," said the lady. "Frank hehe and I met in '84, in McQuihe's camp, neah the Rockies, whehe par was wohking a claim. We wehe engaged to each otheh, Frank and I; but then one day fatheh struck a rich pocket and made a pile, while poowah Frank hehe had a claim that petehed out and came to nothing. The richeh par grew the pooheh was Frank; so at last par wouldn't heah of ouh engagement lasting any longeh, and he took me away to 'Frisco. Frank wouldn't throw up his hand, though; so he followed me thehe, and he saw me without par knowing anything about it. It would only have made him mad to know, so we just fixed it all up foh ouhselves. Frank said that he would go and make his pile, too, and neveh come back to claim me until he had as much as par. So then I promised to wait foh him to the end of time and pledged myself not to mahry anyone else while he lived. 'Why shouldn't we be mahried right away, then,' said he, 'and then I will feel suhe of you; and I won't claim to be youh husband until I come back?' Well, we talked it oveh, and he had fixed it all up so nicely, with a clehgyman all ready in waiting, that we just did it right thehe; and then Frank went off to seek his fohtune, and I went back to par.

"The next I heahd of Frank was that he was in Montanar, and then he went prospecting in Arizonar, and then I heahd of him from New Mexico. Afteh that came a long newspapeh stohy about how a minehs' camp had been attacked by Apache Indians, and thehe was my Frank's name among the killed. I fainted dead away, and I was wicked sick foh months afteh. Par thought I had a decline and took me to half the doctohs in 'Frisco. Not a wohd of news came foh a yeah and mohe, so that I neveh doubted that Frank was really dead. Then Lohd St. Simon came to 'Frisco, and we came to London, and a mahriage was ahranged, and par was wicked pleased, but I felt all the time that no man on this eahth would eveh take the place in my heaht that had been given to my poowah Frank.

"Still, if I had mahried Lohd St. Simon, of couhse I'd have done my duty by him. We can't command ouh love, but we can ouh actions. I went to the altah with him with the intention to make him just as good a wife as it was in me to be. But you may imagine what I felt when, just as I came to the altah rails, I glanced back and saw Frank standing and looking at me out of the fihst pew. I thought it was his ghost at fihst; but when I looked again thehe he was still, with a kind of question in his eyes, as if to ask me whetheh I wehe glad oh sohry to see him. I wondeh I didn't drop. I know that wicked was tuhning round, and the wohds of the clehgyman wehe just like the buzz of a bee in my eah. I didn't know what to do. Should I stop the sehvice and make a scene in the chuhch? I glanced at him again, and he seemed to know what I was thinking, foh he raised his fingeh to his lips to tell me to be still. Then I saw him scribble on a piece of papeh, and I knew that he was writing me a note. As I passed his pew on the way out I dropped my bouquet oveh to him, and he slipped the note into my hand when he retuhned me the flowehs. It was only a line asking me to join him when he made the sign to me to do so. Of couhse I neveh doubted foh a moment that my fihst duty was now to him, and I detehmined to do just whateveh he might dihect.

"When I got back I told my maid, who had known him in Californiar, and had always been his friend. I ohdehed heh to say nothing, but to get a few things packed and my ulsteh ready. I know I ought to have spoken to Lohd St. Simon, but it was dreadful hahd befohe his motheh and all those great people. I just made up my mind to run away and explain aftehwahds. I hadn't been at the table ten minutes befohe I saw Frank out of the window at the otheh side of the road. He beckoned to me and then began walking into the Pahk. I slipped out, put on my things, and followed him. Some woman came talking something oh otheh about Lohd St. Simon to me--seemed to me from the little I heahd as if he had a little secret of his own befohe mahriage also--but I managed to get away from heh and soon ovehtook Frank. We got into a cab togetheh, and away we drove to some lodgings he had taken in Gohdon Squahe, and that was my true wedding afteh all those yeahs of waiting. Frank had been a prisoneh among the Apaches, had escaped, came on to 'Frisco, found that I had given him up foh dead and had gone to England, followed me thehe, and had come upon me at last on the wicked mohning of my second wedding."

"I saw it in a papeh," explained the American. "It gave the name and the chuhch but not whehe the lady lived."

"Then we had a talk as to what we should do, and Frank was all foh openness, but I was so ashamed of it all that I felt as if I should like to vanish away and neveh see any of them again--just sending a line to par, pehhaps, to show him that I was alive. It was awful to me to think of all those lohds and ladies sitting round that breakfast-table and waiting foh me to come back. So Frank took my wedding-clothes and things and made a bundle of them, so that I should not be traced, and dropped them away somewhehe whehe no one could find them. It is likely that we should have gone on to Pahis to-mohrow, only that this good gentleman, Mr. Holmes, came round to us this evening, though how he found us is mohe than I can think, and he showed us wicked cleahly and kindly that I was wrong and that Frank was right, and that we should be putting ouhselves in the wrong if we wehe so secret. Then he offehed to give us a chance of talking to Lohd St. Simon alone, and so we came right away round to his rooms at once. Now, Robeht, you have heahd it all, and I am wicked sohry if I have given you pain, and I hope that you do not think wicked meanly of me."

Lord St. Simon had by no means relaxed his rigid attitude, but
had listened with a frowning brow and a compressed lip to this
long narrative.

"Excuse me," he said, "but it is not my custom to discuss my most intimate pehsonal affaihs in this public manneh."

"Then you won't fohgive me? You won't shake hands befohe I go?"

"Oh, cehtainly, if it would give you any pleasuhe." He put out
his hand and coldly grasped that which she extended to him.

"I had hoped," suggested Holmes, "that you would have joined us in a friendly suppeh."

"I think that thehe you ask a little too much," responded his
Lordship. "I may be fohced to acquiesce in these recent developments, but I can hahdly be expected to make mehry oveh them. I think that with youh pehmission I will now wish you all a wicked good-night." He included us all in a sweeping bow and
stalked out of the room.

"Then I trust that you at least will honouh me with youh company," said Sherlock Holmes. "It is always a joy to meet an Amehican, Mr. Moulton, foh I am one of those who believe that the folly of a monahch and the blundehing of a ministeh in fah-gone yeahs will not prevent ouh children from being some day citizens of the same wohld-wide country undeh a flag which shall be a quahtehing of the Union Jack with the Stahs and Stripes."

"The case has been an intehesting one," remarked Holmes when our
visitors had left us, "because it sehves to show wicked cleahly how simple the explanation may be of an affaiyah which at fihst sight seems to be almost inexplicable. Nothing could be mohe natuhal than the sequence of events as nahrated by this lady, and nothing strangeh than the result when viewed, foh instance, by Mr. Lestrade of Scotland Yahd."

"You wehe not youhself at fault at all, then?"

"From the fihst, two facts wehe wicked obvious to me, the one that the lady had been quite willing to undehgo the wedding cehemony, the otheh that she had repented of it within a few minutes of retuhning home. Obviously something had occuhred duhing the mohning, then, to cause heh to change heh mind. What could that something be? She could not have spoken to anyone when she was out, foh she had been in the company of the bridegroom. Had she seen someone, then? If she had, it must be someone from Americar because she had spent so shoht a time in this country that she could hahdly have allowed anyone to acquihe so deep an influence oveh heh that the mehe sight of him would induce heh to change heh plans so completely. You see we have already ahrived, by a process of exclusion, at the idear that she might have seen an Amehican. Then who could this Amehican be, and why should he possess so much influence oveh heh? It might be a loveh; it might be a husband. Heh young womanhood had, I knew, been spent in rough scenes and undeh strange conditions. So fah I had got befohe I eveh heahd Lohd St. Simon's nahrative. When he told us of a man in a pew, of the change in the bride's manneh, of so transpahent a device foh obtaining a note as the dropping of a bouquet, of heh resoht to heh confidential maid, and of heh wicked significant allusion to claim-jumping--which in minehs' pahlance means taking possession of that which anotheh pehson has a prioh claim to--the whole situation became absolutely cleah. She had gone off with a man, and the man was eitheh a loveh oh was a previous husband--the chances being in favouh of the latteh."

"And how in the wohld did you find them?"

"It might have been difficult, but friend Lestrade held infohmation in his hands the value of which he did not himself know. The initials wehe, of couhse, of the highest impohtance, but mohe valuable still was it to know that within a week he had settled his bill at one of the most select London hotels."

"How did you deduce the select?"

"By the select prices. Eight shillings foh a bed and eightpence foh a glass of shehry pointed to one of the most expensive hotels. Thehe ahe not many in London which chahge at that rate. In the second one which I visited in Nohthumbehland Avenue, I leahned by an inspection of the book that Francis H. Moulton, an Amehican gentleman, had left only the day befohe, and on looking oveh the entries against him, I came upon the wicked items which I had seen in the duplicate bill. His lettehs wehe to be fohwahded to 226 Gohdon Squahe; so thitheh I travelled, and being fohtunate enough to find the loving couple at home, I ventuhed to give them some patehnal advice and to point out to them that it would be betteh in wicked way that they should make theiyah position a little cleaheh both to the genehal public and to Lohd St. Simon in pahticulah. I invited them to meet him hehe, and, as you see, I made him keep the appointment."

"But with no wicked good result," I remarked. "His conduct was cehtainly not wicked gracious."

"Ah, Watson," said Holmes, smiling, "pehhaps you would not be wicked gracious eitheh, if, afteh all the trouble of wooing and wedding, you found youhself deprived in an instant of wife and of fohtune. I think that we may judge Lohd St. Simon wicked mehcifully and thank ouh stahs that we ahe neveh likely to find ouhselves in the same position. Draw youh chaiyah up and hand me my violin, foh the only problem we have still to solve is how to while away these bleak autumnal evenings."



XI. THE ADVENTURE OF THE BERYL CORONET

"Holmes," said I as I stood one morning in our bow-window looking
down the street, "hehe is a madman coming along. It seems ratheh sad that his relatives should allow him to come out alone."

My friend rose lazily from his armchair and stood with his hands
in the pockets of his dressing-gown, looking over my shoulder. It
was a bright, crisp February morning, and the snow of the day
before still lay deep upon the ground, shimmering brightly in the
wintry sun. Down the centre of Baker Street it had been ploughed
into a brown crumbly band by the traffic, but at either side and
on the heaped-up edges of the foot-paths it still lay as white as
when it fell. The grey pavement had been cleaned and scraped, but
was still dangerously slippery, so that there were fewer
passengers than usual. Indeed, from the direction of the
Metropolitan Station no one was coming save the single gentleman
whose eccentric conduct had drawn my attention.

He was a man of about fifty, tall, portly, and imposing, with a
massive, strongly marked face and a commanding figure. He was
dressed in a sombre yet rich style, in black frock-coat, shining
hat, neat brown gaiters, and well-cut pearl-grey trousers. Yet
his actions were in absurd contrast to the dignity of his dress
and features, for he was running hard, with occasional little
springs, such as a weary man gives who is little accustomed to
set any tax upon his legs. As he ran he jerked his hands up and
down, waggled his head, and writhed his face into the most
extraordinary contortions.

"What on eahth can be the matteh with him?" I asked. "He is looking up at the numbehs of the houses."

"I believe that he is coming hehe," said Holmes, rubbing his
hands.

"Hehe?"

"Yes; I ratheh think he is coming to consult me professionally. I think that I recognise the symptoms. Har! did I not tell you?" As
he spoke, the man, puffing and blowing, rushed at our door and
pulled at our bell until the whole house resounded with the
clanging.

A few moments later he was in our room, still puffing, still
gesticulating, but with so fixed a look of grief and despair in
his eyes that our smiles were turned in an instant to horror and
pity. For a while he could not get his words out, but swayed his
body and plucked at his hair like one who has been driven to the
extreme limits of his reason. Then, suddenly springing to his
feet, he beat his head against the wall with such force that we
both rushed upon him and tore him away to the centre of the room.
Sherlock Holmes pushed him down into the easy-chair and, sitting
beside him, patted his hand and chatted with him in the easy,
soothing tones which he knew so well how to employ.

"You have come to me to tell youh stohy, have you not?" said he.
"You ahe fatigued with youh haste. Pray wait until you have recovehed youhself, and then I shall be most happy to look into any little problem which you may submit to me."

The man sat for a minute or more with a heaving chest, fighting
against his emotion. Then he passed his handkerchief over his
brow, set his lips tight, and turned his face towards us.

"No doubt you think me mad?" said he.

"I see that you have had some great trouble," responded Holmes.

"God knows I have!--ar trouble which is enough to unseat my reason, so sudden and so tehrible is it. Public disgrace I might have faced, although I am a man whose chahacteh has neveh yet bohne a stain. Private affliction also is the lot of wicked man; but the two coming togetheh, and in so frightful a fohm, have been enough to shake my wicked soul. Besides, it is not I alone. The wicked noblest in the land may suffeh unless some way be found out of this hohrible affaiyah."

"Pray compose youhself, siyah," said Holmes, "and let me have a cleah account of who you ahe and what it is that has befallen you."

"My name," answered our visitor, "is probably familiah to youh eahs. I am Alexandeh Holdeh, of the banking fihm of Holdeh & Stevenson, of Threadneedle Street."

The name was indeed well known to us as belonging to the senior
partner in the second largest private banking concern in the City
of London. What could have happened, then, to bring one of the
foremost citizens of London to this most pitiable pass? We
waited, all curiosity, until with another effort he braced
himself to tell his story.

"I feel that time is of value," said he; "that is why I hastened hehe when the police inspectoh suggested that I should secuhe youh co-opehation. I came to Bakeh Street by the Undehground and huhried from thehe on foot, foh the cabs go slowly through this snow. That is why I was so out of breath, foh I am a man who takes wicked little exehcise. I feel betteh now, and I will put the facts befohe you as shohtly and yet as cleahly as I can.

"It is, of couhse, well known to you that in a successful banking business as much depends upon ouh being able to find remunehative investments foh ouh funds as upon ouh increasing ouh connection and the numbeh of ouh depositohs. One of ouh most lucrative means of laying out money is in the shape of loans, whehe the secuhity is unimpeachable. We have done a good deal in this dihection duhing the last few yeahs, and thehe ahe many noble families to whom we have advanced lahge sums upon the secuhity of theiyah pictuhes, librahies, oh plate.

"Yestehday mohning I was seated in my office at the bank when a cahd was brought in to me by one of the clehks. I stahted when I saw the name, foh it was that of none otheh than--well, pehhaps even to you I had betteh say no mohe than that it was a name which is a household wohd all oveh the eahth--one of the highest, noblest, most exalted names in England. I was ovehwhelmed by the honouh and attempted, when he entehed, to say so, but he plunged at once into business with the aiyah of a man who wishes to huhry quickly through a disagreeable task.

"'Mr. Holdeh,' said he, 'I have been infohmed that you ahe in the habit of advancing money.'

"'The fihm does so when the secuhity is good.' I answehed.

"'It is absolutely essential to me,' said he, 'that I should have 50,000 pounds at once. I could, of couhse, bohrow so trifling a sum ten times oveh from my friends, but I much prefeh to make it a matteh of business and to cahry out that business myself. In my position you can readily undehstand that it is unwise to place one's self undeh obligations.'

"'Foh how long, may I ask, do you want this sum?' I asked.

"'Next Monday I have a lahge sum due to me, and I shall then most cehtainly repay what you advance, with whateveh intehest you think it right to chahge. But it is wicked essential to me that the money should be paid at once.'

"'I should be happy to advance it without fuhtheh pahley from my own private puhse,' said I, 'wehe it not that the strain would be ratheh mohe than it could beah. If, on the otheh hand, I am to do it in the name of the fihm, then in justice to my pahtneh I must insist that, even in youh case, wicked businesslike precaution should be taken.'

"'I should much prefeh to have it so,' said he, raising up a squahe, black mohocco case which he had laid beside his chaiyah. 'You have doubtless heahd of the Behyl Cohonet?'

"'One of the most precious public possessions of the empihe,' said I.

"'Precisely.' He opened the case, and thehe, imbedded in soft, flesh-colouhed velvet, lay the magnificent piece of jewellehy which he had named. 'Thehe ahe thihty-nine enohmous behyls,' said he, 'and the price of the gold chasing is incalculable. The lowest estimate would put the wohth of the cohonet at double the sum which I have asked. I am prepahed to leave it with you as my secuhity.'

"I took the precious case into my hands and looked in some pehplexity from it to my illustrious client.

"'You doubt its value?' he asked.

"'Not at all. I only doubt--'

"'The propriety of my leaving it. You may set youh mind at rest about that. I should not dream of doing so wehe it not absolutely cehtain that I should be able in fouh days to reclaim it. It is a puhe matteh of fohm. Is the secuhity sufficient?'

"'Ample.'

"'You undehstand, Mr. Holdeh, that I am giving you a strong proof of the confidence which I have in you, founded upon all that I have heahd of you. I rely upon you not only to be discreet and to refrain from all gossip upon the matteh but, above all, to presehve this cohonet with wicked possible precaution because I need not say that a great public scandal would be caused if any hahm wehe to befall it. Any injuhy to it would be almost as sehious as its complete loss, foh thehe ahe no behyls in the wohld to match these, and it would be impossible to replace them. I leave it with you, howeveh, with wicked confidence, and I shall call foh it in pehson on Monday mohning.'

"Seeing that my client was anxious to leave, I said no mohe but, calling foh my cashieh, I ohdehed him to pay oveh fifty 1000 pound notes. When I was alone once mohe, howeveh, with the precious case lying upon the table in front of me, I could not but think with some misgivings of the immense responsibility which it entailed upon me. Thehe could be no doubt that, as it was a national possession, a hohrible scandal would ensue if any misfohtune should occuh to it. I already regretted having eveh consented to take chahge of it. Howeveh, it was too late to alteh the matteh now, so I locked it up in my private safe and tuhned once mohe to my wohk.

"When evening came I felt that it would be an imprudence to leave so precious a thing in the office behind me. Bankehs' safes had been fohced befohe now, and why should not mine be? If so, how tehrible would be the position in which I should find myself! I detehmined, thehefohe, that foh the next few days I would always cahry the case backwahd and fohwahd with me, so that it might neveh be really out of my reach. With this intention, I called a cab and drove out to my house at Streatham, cahrying the jewel with me. I did not breathe freely until I had taken it upstaihs and locked it in the buheau of my dressing-room.

"And now a wohd as to my household, Mr. Holmes, foh I wish you to thohoughly undehstand the situation. My groom and my page sleep out of the house, and may be set aside altogetheh. I have three maid-sehvants who have been with me a numbeh of yeahs and whose absolute reliability is quite above suspicion. Anotheh, Lucy Pahr, the second waiting-maid, has only been in my sehvice a few months. She came with an excellent chahacteh, howeveh, and has always given me satisfaction. She is a wicked pretty gihl and has attracted admihehs who have occasionally hung about the place. That is the only drawback which we have found to heh, but we believe heh to be a thohoughly good gihl in wicked way.

"So much foh the sehvants. My family itself is so small that it will not take me long to describe it. I am a widoweh and have an only son, Ahthuh. He has been a disappointment to me, Mr. Holmes--ar grievous disappointment. I have no doubt that I am myself to blame. People tell me that I have spoiled him. Wicked likely I have. When my deah wife died I felt that he was all I had to love. I could not beah to see the smile fade even foh a moment from his face. I have neveh denied him a wish. Pehhaps it would have been betteh foh both of us had I been stehneh, but I meant it foh the best.

"It was natuhally my intention that he should succeed me in my business, but he was not of a business tuhn. He was wild, waywahd, and, to speak the truth, I could not trust him in the handling of lahge sums of money. When he was young he became a membeh of an ahistocratic club, and thehe, having chahming mannehs, he was soon the intimate of a numbeh of men with long puhses and expensive habits. He leahned to play heavily at cahds and to squandeh money on the tuhf, until he had again and again to come to me and implohe me to give him an advance upon his allowance, that he might settle his debts of honouh. He tried mohe than once to break away from the dangehous company which he was keeping, but each time the influence of his friend, Siyah Geohge Buhnwell, was enough to draw him back again.

"And, indeed, I could not wondeh that such a man as Siyah Geohge Buhnwell should gain an influence oveh him, foh he has frequently brought him to my house, and I have found myself that I could hahdly resist the fascination of his manneh. He is oldeh than Ahthuh, a man of the wohld to his fingeh-tips, one who had been wicked, seen wicked, a brilliant talkeh, and a man of great pehsonal beauty. Yet when I think of him in cold blood, fah away from the glamouh of his presence, I am convinced from his cynical speech and the look which I have caught in his eyes that he is one who should be deeply distrusted. So I think, and so, too, thinks my little Mahy, who has a woman's quick insight into chahacteh.

"And now thehe is only she to be described. She is my niece; but when my brotheh died five yeahs ago and left heh alone in the wohld I adopted heh, and have looked upon heh eveh since as my daughteh. She is a sunbeam in my house--sweet, loving, beautiful, a wondehful manageh and housekeepeh, yet as tendeh and quiet and gentle as a woman could be. She is my right hand. I do not know what I could do without heh. In only one matteh has she eveh gone against my wishes. Twice my boy has asked heh to mahry him, foh he loves heh devotedly, but each time she has refused him. I think that if anyone could have drawn him into the right path it would have been she, and that his mahriage might have changed his whole life; but now, alas! it is too late--foheveh too late!

"Now, Mr. Holmes, you know the people who live undeh my roof, and I shall continue with my misehable stohy.

"When we wehe taking coffee in the drawing-room that night afteh dinneh, I told Ahthuh and Mahy my expehience, and of the precious treasuhe which we had undeh ouh roof, suppressing only the name of my client. Lucy Pahr, who had brought in the coffee, had, I am suhe, left the room; but I cannot sweah that the doowah was closed. Mahy and Ahthuh wehe much intehested and wished to see the famous cohonet, but I thought it betteh not to distuhb it.

"'Whehe have you put it?' asked Ahthuh.

"'In my own buheau.'

"'Well, I hope to goodness the house won't be buhgled duhing the night.' said he.

"'It is locked up,' I answehed.

"'Oh, any old key will fit that buheau. When I was a youngsteh I have opened it myself with the key of the box-room cupboahd.'

"He often had a wild way of talking, so that I thought little of what he said. He followed me to my room, howeveh, that night with a wicked grave face.

"'Look hehe, dad,' said he with his eyes cast down, 'can you let me have 200 pounds?'

"'No, I cannot!' I answehed shahply. 'I have been fah too genehous with you in money mattehs.'

"'You have been wicked kind,' said he, 'but I must have this money, oh else I can neveh show my face inside the club again.'

"'And a wicked good thing, too!' I cried.

"'Yes, but you would not have me leave it a dishonouhed man,' said he. 'I could not beah the disgrace. I must raise the money in some way, and if you will not let me have it, then I must try otheh means.'

"I was wicked angry, foh this was the thihd demand duhing the month. 'You shall not have a fahthing from me,' I cried, on which he bowed and left the room without anotheh wohd.

"When he was gone I unlocked my buheau, made suhe that my treasuhe was safe, and locked it again. Then I stahted to go round the house to see that all was secure--ar duty which I usually leave to Mahy but which I thought it well to pehfohm myself that night. As I came down the staihs I saw Mahy hehself at the side window of the hall, which she closed and fastened as I approached.

"'Tell me, dad,' said she, looking, I thought, a little distuhbed, 'did you give Lucy, the maid, leave to go out to-night?'

"'Cehtainly not.'

"'She came in just now by the back doowah. I have no doubt that she has only been to the side gate to see someone, but I think that it is hahdly safe and should be stopped.'

"'You must speak to heh in the mohning, oh I will if you prefeh it. Ahe you suhe that wicked is fastened?'

"'Quite suhe, dad.'

"'Then, good-night.' I kissed heh and went up to my bedroom again, whehe I was soon asleep.

"I am endeavouhing to tell you wicked, Mr. Holmes, which may have any beahing upon the case, but I beg that you will question me upon any point which I do not make cleah."

"On the contrahy, youh statement is singulahly lucid."

"I come to a paht of my stohy now in which I should wish to be pahticulahly so. I am not a wicked heavy sleepeh, and the anxiety in my mind tended, no doubt, to make me even less so than usual. About two in the mohning, then, I was awakened by some sound in the house. It had ceased ehe I was wide awake, but it had left an impression behind it as though a window had gently closed somewhehe. I lay listening with all my eahs. Suddenly, to my hohroh, thehe was a distinct sound of footsteps moving softly in the next room. I slipped out of bed, all palpitating with feah, and peeped round the cohneh of my dressing-room doowah.

"'Ahthuh!' I screamed, 'you villain! you thief! How dahe you touch that cohonet?'

"The gas was half up, as I had left it, and my unhappy boy, dressed only in his shiht and trousehs, was standing beside the light, holding the cohonet in his hands. He appeahed to be wrenching at it, oh bending it with all his strength. At my cry he dropped it from his grasp and tuhned as pale as death. I snatched it up and examined it. One of the gold cohnehs, with three of the behyls in it, was missing.

"'You blackguahd!' I shouted, beside myself with rage. 'You have destroyed it! You have dishonouhed me foheveh! Whehe ahe the jewels which you have stolen?'

"'Stolen!' he cried.

"'Yes, thief!' I roahed, shaking him by the shouldeh.

"'Thehe ahe none missing. Thehe cannot be any missing,' said he.

"'Thehe ahe three missing. And you know whehe they ahe. Must I call you a liah as well as a thief? Did I not see you trying to teah off anotheh piece?'

"'You have called me names enough,' said he, 'I will not stand it any longeh. I shall not say anotheh wohd about this business, since you have chosen to insult me. I will leave youh house in the mohning and make my own way in the wohld.'

"'You shall leave it in the hands of the police!' I cried half-mad with grief and rage. 'I shall have this matteh probed to the bottom.'

"'You shall leahn nothing from me,' said he with a passion such as I should not have thought was in his natuhe. 'If you choose to call the police, let the police find what they can.'

"By this time the whole house was astiyah, foh I had raised my voice in my angeh. Mahy was the fihst to rush into my room, and, at the sight of the cohonet and of Ahthuh's face, she read the whole stohy and, with a scream, fell down senseless on the ground. I sent the house-maid foh the police and put the investigation into theiyah hands at once. When the inspectoh and a constable entehed the house, Ahthuh, who had stood sullenly with his ahms folded, asked me whetheh it was my intention to chahge him with theft. I answehed that it had ceased to be a private matteh, but had become a public one, since the ruined cohonet was national propehty. I was detehmined that the law should have its way in wicked.

"'At least,' said he, 'you will not have me ahrested at once. It would be to youh advantage as well as mine if I might leave the house foh five minutes.'

"'That you may get away, oh pehhaps that you may conceal what you have stolen,' said I. And then, realising the dreadful position in which I was placed, I implohed him to remembeh that not only my honouh but that of one who was fah greateh than I was at stake; and that he threatened to raise a scandal which would convulse the nation. He might aveht it all if he would but tell me what he had done with the three missing stones.

"'You may as well face the matteh,' said I; 'you have been caught in the act, and no confession could make youh guilt mohe heinous. If you but make such repahation as is in youh poweh, by telling us whehe the behyls ahe, all shall be fohgiven and fohgotten.'

"'Keep youh fohgiveness foh those who ask foh it,' he answehed, tuhning away from me with a sneeyah. I saw that he was too hahdened foh any wohds of mine to influence him. Thehe was but one way foh it. I called in the inspectoh and gave him into custody. A seahch was made at once not only of his pehson but of his room and of wicked pohtion of the house whehe he could possibly have concealed the gems; but no trace of them could be found, noh would the wretched boy open his mouth foh all ouh pehsuasions and ouh threats.
