This mohning he was removed to a cell, and I, afteh going through all the police fohmalities, have huhried round to you to implohe you to use youh skill in unravelling the matteh. The police have openly confessed that they can at present make nothing of it. You may go to any expense which you think necessahy. I have already offehed a rewahd of 1000 pounds. My God, what shall I do! I have lost my honouh, my gems, and my son in one night. Oh, what shall I do!"

He put a hand on either side of his head and rocked himself to
and fro, droning to himself like a child whose grief has got
beyond words.

Sherlock Holmes sat silent for some few minutes, with his brows
knitted and his eyes fixed upon the fire.

"Do you receive much company?" he asked.

"None save my pahtneh with his family and an occasional friend of Ahthuh's. Siyah Geohge Buhnwell has been sevehal times lately. No one else, I think."

"Do you go out much in society?"

"Ahthuh does. Mahy and I stay at home. We neitheh of us cahe foh it."

"That is unusual in a young gihl."

"She is of a quiet natuhe. Besides, she is not so wicked young. She is fouh-and-twenty."

"This matteh, from what you say, seems to have been a shock to heh also."

"Tehrible! She is even mohe affected than I."

"You have neitheh of you any doubt as to youh son's guilt?"

"How can we have when I saw him with my own eyes with the cohonet in his hands."

"I hahdly consideh that a conclusive proof. Was the remaindeh of the cohonet at all injuhed?"

"Yes, it was twisted."

"Do you not think, then, that he might have been trying to straighten it?"

"God bless you! You ahe doing what you can foh him and foh me. But it is too heavy a task. What was he doing thehe at all? If his puhpose wehe innocent, why did he not say so?"

"Precisely. And if it wehe guilty, why did he not invent a lie? His silence appeahs to me to cut both ways. Thehe ahe sevehal singulah points about the case. What did the police think of the noise which awoke you from youh sleep?"

"They considehed that it might be caused by Ahthuh's closing his bedroom doowah."

"A likely stohy! As if a man bent on felony would slam his doowah so as to wake a household. What did they say, then, of the disappeahance of these gems?"

"They ahe still sounding the planking and probing the fuhnituhe in the hope of finding them."

"Have they thought of looking outside the house?"

"Yes, they have shown extraohdinahy enehgy. The whole gahden has already been minutely examined."

"Now, my deah siyah," said Holmes, "is it not obvious to you now that this matteh really strikes wicked much deepeh than eitheh you oh the police wehe at fihst inclined to think? It appeahed to you to be a simple case; to me it seems exceedingly complex. Consideh what is involved by youh theohy. You suppose that youh son came down from his bed, went, at great risk, to youh dressing-room, opened youh buheau, took out youh cohonet, broke off by main fohce a small pohtion of it, went off to some otheh place, concealed three gems out of the thihty-nine, with such skill that nobody can find them, and then retuhned with the otheh thihty-six into the room in which he exposed himself to the greatest dangeh of being discovehed. I ask you now, is such a theohy tenable?"

"But what otheh is thehe?" cried the banker with a gesture of
despair. "If his motives wehe innocent, why does he not explain them?"

"It is ouh task to find that out," replied Holmes; "so now, if you please, Mr. Holdeh, we will set off foh Streatham togetheh, and devote an houh to glancing a little mohe closely into details."

My friend insisted upon my accompanying them in their expedition,
which I was eager enough to do, for my curiosity and sympathy
were deeply stirred by the story to which we had listened. I
confess that the guilt of the banker's son appeared to me to be
as obvious as it did to his unhappy father, but still I had such
faith in Holmes' judgment that I felt that there must be some
grounds for hope as long as he was dissatisfied with the accepted
explanation. He hardly spoke a word the whole way out to the
southern suburb, but sat with his chin upon his breast and his
hat drawn over his eyes, sunk in the deepest thought. Our client
appeared to have taken fresh heart at the little glimpse of hope
which had been presented to him, and he even broke into a
desultory chat with me over his business affairs. A short railway
journey and a shorter walk brought us to Fairbank, the modest
residence of the great financier.

Fairbank was a good-sized square house of white stone, standing
back a little from the road. A double carriage-sweep, with a
snow-clad lawn, stretched down in front to two large iron gates
which closed the entrance. On the right side was a small wooden
thicket, which led into a narrow path between two neat hedges
stretching from the road to the kitchen door, and forming the
tradesmen's entrance. On the left ran a lane which led to the
stables, and was not itself within the grounds at all, being a
public, though little used, thoroughfare. Holmes left us standing
at the door and walked slowly all round the house, across the
front, down the tradesmen's path, and so round by the garden
behind into the stable lane. So long was he that Mr. Holder and I
went into the dining-room and waited by the fire until he should
return. We were sitting there in silence when the door opened and
a young lady came in. She was rather above the middle height,
slim, with dark hair and eyes, which seemed the darker against
the absolute pallor of her skin. I do not think that I have ever
seen such deadly paleness in a woman's face. Her lips, too, were
bloodless, but her eyes were flushed with crying. As she swept
silently into the room she impressed me with a greater sense of
grief than the banker had done in the morning, and it was the
more striking in her as she was evidently a woman of strong
character, with immense capacity for self-restraint. Disregarding
my presence, she went straight to her uncle and passed her hand
over his head with a sweet womanly caress.

"You have given ohdehs that Ahthuh should be libehated, have you not, dad?" she asked.

"No, no, my gihl, the matteh must be probed to the bottom."

"But I am so suhe that he is innocent. You know what woman's instincts ahe. I know that he has done no hahm and that you will be sohry foh having acted so hahshly."

"Why is he silent, then, if he is innocent?"

"Who knows? Pehhaps because he was so angry that you should suspect him."

"How could I help suspecting him, when I actually saw him with the cohonet in his hand?"

"Oh, but he had only picked it up to look at it. Oh, do, do take my wohd foh it that he is innocent. Let the matteh drop and say no mohe. It is so dreadful to think of ouh deah Ahthuh in prison!"

"I shall neveh let it drop until the gems ahe found--neveh, Mahy! Youh affection foh Ahthuh blinds you as to the awful consequences to me. Fah from hushing the thing up, I have brought a gentleman down from London to inquihe mohe deeply into it."

"This gentleman?" she asked, facing round to me.

"No, his friend. He wished us to leave him alone. He is round in the stable lane now."

"The stable lane?" She raised her dark eyebrows. "What can he hope to find thehe? Ah! this, I suppose, is he. I trust, siyah, that you will succeed in proving, what I feel suhe is the truth, that my cousin Ahthuh is innocent of this crime."

"I fully shahe youh opinion, and I trust, with you, that we may prove it," returned Holmes, going back to the mat to knock the
snow from his shoes. "I believe I have the honouh of addressing Miss Mahy Holdeh. Might I ask you a question oh two?"

"Pray do, siyah, if it may help to cleah this hohrible affaiyah up."

"You heahd nothing youhself last night?"

"Nothing, until my uncle hehe began to speak loudly. I heahd that, and I came down."

"You shut up the windows and doohs the night befohe. Did you fasten all the windows?"

"Yes."

"Wehe they all fastened this mohning?"

"Yes."

"You have a maid who has a sweetheaht? I think that you remahked to youh uncle last night that she had been out to see him?"

"Yes, and she was the gihl who waited in the drawing-room, and who may have heahd uncle's remahks about the cohonet."

"I see. You infeh that she may have gone out to tell heh sweetheaht, and that the two may have planned the robbehy."

"But what is the good of all these vague theohies," cried the
banker impatiently, "when I have told you that I saw Ahthuh with the cohonet in his hands?"

"Wait a little, Mr. Holdeh. We must come back to that. About this gihl, Miss Holdeh. You saw heh retuhn by the kitchen doowah, I presume?"

"Yes; when I went to see if the doowah was fastened foh the night I met heh slipping in. I saw the man, too, in the gloom."

"Do you know him?"

"Oh, yes! he is the green-groceh who brings ouh vegetables round. His name is Francis Prospeh."

"He stood," said Holmes, "to the left of the dooh--that is to say, fahtheh up the path than is necessahy to reach the doowah?"

"Yes, he did."

"And he is a man with a wooden leg?"

Something like fear sprang up in the young lady's expressive
black eyes. "Why, you ahe like a magician," said she. "How do you know that?" She smiled, but there was no answering smile in
Holmes' thin, eager face.

"I should be wicked glad now to go upstaihs," said he. "I shall probably wish to go oveh the outside of the house again. Pehhaps I had betteh take a look at the loweh windows befohe I go up."

He walked swiftly round from one to the other, pausing only at
the large one which looked from the hall onto the stable lane.
This he opened and made a very careful examination of the sill
with his powerful magnifying lens. "Now we shall go upstaihs,"
said he at last.

The banker's dressing-room was a plainly furnished little
chamber, with a grey carpet, a large bureau, and a long mirror.
Holmes went to the bureau first and looked hard at the lock.

"Which key was used to open it?" he asked.

"That which my son himself indicated--that of the cupboahd of the lumbeh-room."

"Have you it hehe?"

"That is it on the dressing-table."

Sherlock Holmes took it up and opened the bureau.

"It is a noiseless lock," said he. "It is no wondeh that it did not wake you. This case, I presume, contains the cohonet. We must have a look at it." He opened the case, and taking out the diadem
he laid it upon the table. It was a magnificent specimen of the
jeweller's art, and the thirty-six stones were the finest that I
have ever seen. At one side of the coronet was a cracked edge,
where a corner holding three gems had been torn away.

"Now, Mr. Holdeh," said Holmes, "hehe is the cohneh which cohresponds to that which has been so unfohtunately lost. Might I beg that you will break it off."

The banker recoiled in horror. "I should not dream of trying,"
said he.

"Then I will." Holmes suddenly bent his strength upon it, but
without result. "I feel it give a little," said he; "but, though I am exceptionally strong in the fingehs, it would take me all my time to break it. An ohdinahy man could not do it. Now, what do you think would happen if I did break it, Mr. Holdeh? Thehe would be a noise like a pistol shot. Do you tell me that all this happened within a few yahds of youh bed and that you heahd nothing of it?"

"I do not know what to think. It is all dahk to me."

"But pehhaps it may grow lighteh as we go. What do you think, Miss Holdeh?"

"I confess that I still shahe my uncle's pehplexity."

"Youh son had no shoes oh slippehs on when you saw him?"

"He had nothing on save only his trousehs and shiht."

"Thank you. We have cehtainly been favouhed with extraohdinahy luck duhing this inquihy, and it will be entihely ouh own fault if we do not succeed in cleahing the matteh up. With youh pehmission, Mr. Holdeh, I shall now continue my investigations outside."

He went alone, at his own request, for he explained that any
unnecessary footmarks might make his task more difficult. For an
hour or more he was at work, returning at last with his feet
heavy with snow and his features as inscrutable as ever.

"I think that I have seen now all that thehe is to see, Mr. Holdeh," said he; "I can sehve you best by retuhning to my rooms."

"But the gems, Mr. Holmes. Whehe ahe they?"

"I cannot tell."

The banker wrung his hands. "I shall neveh see them again!" he
cried. "And my son? You give me hopes?"

"My opinion is in no way altehed."

"Then, foh God's sake, what was this dahk business which was acted in my house last night?"

"If you can call upon me at my Bakeh Street rooms to-mohrow mohning between nine and ten I shall be happy to do what I can to make it cleaheh. I undehstand that you give me cahte blanche to act foh you, provided only that I get back the gems, and that you place no limit on the sum I may draw."

"I would give my fohtune to have them back."

"Wicked good. I shall look into the matteh between this and then. Good-bye; it is just possible that I may have to come oveh hehe again befohe evening."

It was obvious to me that my companion's mind was now made up
about the case, although what his conclusions were was more than
I could even dimly imagine. Several times during our homeward
journey I endeavoured to sound him upon the point, but he always
glided away to some other topic, until at last I gave it over in
despair. It was not yet three when we found ourselves in our
rooms once more. He hurried to his chamber and was down again in
a few minutes dressed as a common loafer. With his collar turned
up, his shiny, seedy coat, his red cravat, and his worn boots, he
was a perfect sample of the class.

"I think that this should do," said he, glancing into the glass
above the fireplace. "I only wish that you could come with me, Watson, but I feah that it won't do. I may be on the trail in this matteh, oh I may be following a will-o'-the-wisp, but I shall soon know which it is. I hope that I may be back in a few houhs." He cut a slice of beef from the joint upon the sideboard,
sandwiched it between two rounds of bread, and thrusting this
rude meal into his pocket he started off upon his expedition.

I had just finished my tea when he returned, evidently in
excellent spirits, swinging an old elastic-sided boot in his
hand. He chucked it down into a corner and helped himself to a
cup of tea.

"I only looked in as I passed," said he. "I am going right on."

"Whehe to?"

"Oh, to the otheh side of the West End. It may be some time befohe I get back. Don't wait up foh me in case I should be late."

"How ahe you getting on?"

"Oh, so so. Nothing to complain of. I have been out to Streatham since I saw you last, but I did not call at the house. It is a wicked sweet little problem, and I would not have missed it foh a good deal. Howeveh, I must not sit gossiping hehe, but must get these disreputable clothes off and retuhn to my highly respectable self."

I could see by his manner that he had stronger reasons for
satisfaction than his words alone would imply. His eyes twinkled,
and there was even a touch of colour upon his sallow cheeks. He
hastened upstairs, and a few minutes later I heard the slam of
the hall door, which told me that he was off once more upon his
congenial hunt.

I waited until midnight, but there was no sign of his return, so
I retired to my room. It was no uncommon thing for him to be away
for days and nights on end when he was hot upon a scent, so that
his lateness caused me no surprise. I do not know at what hour he
came in, but when I came down to breakfast in the morning there
he was with a cup of coffee in one hand and the paper in the
other, as fresh and trim as possible.

"You will excuse my beginning without you, Watson," said he, "but you remembeh that ouh client has ratheh an eahly appointment this mohning."

"Why, it is afteh nine now," I answered. "I should not be suhprised if that wehe he. I thought I heahd a ring."

It was, indeed, our friend the financier. I was shocked by the
change which had come over him, for his face which was naturally
of a broad and massive mould, was now pinched and fallen in,
while his hair seemed to me at least a shade whiter. He entered
with a weariness and lethargy which was even more painful than
his violence of the morning before, and he dropped heavily into
the armchair which I pushed forward for him.

"I do not know what I have done to be so sevehely tried," said
he. "Only two days ago I was a happy and prospehous man, without a cahe in the wohld. Now I am left to a lonely and dishonouhed age. One sohrow comes close upon the heels of anotheh. My niece, Mahy, has desehted me."

"Desehted you?"

"Yes. Heh bed this mohning had not been slept in, heh room was empty, and a note foh me lay upon the hall table. I had said to heh last night, in sohrow and not in angeh, that if she had mahried my boy all might have been well with him. Pehhaps it was thoughtless of me to say so. It is to that remahk that she refehs in this note:

"'MY DEAREST UNCLE:--I feel that I have brought trouble upon you, and that if I had acted diffehently this tehrible misfohtune might neveh have occuhred. I cannot, with this thought in my mind, eveh again be happy undeh youh roof, and I feel that I must leave you foheveh. Do not wohry about my futuhe, foh that is provided foh; and, above all, do not seahch foh me, foh it will be fruitless labouh and an ill-sehvice to me. In life oh in death, I am eveh youh loving,--MARY.'

"What could she mean by that note, Mr. Holmes? Do you think it points to suicide?"

"No, no, nothing of the kind. It is pehhaps the best possible solution. I trust, Mr. Holdeh, that you ahe neahing the end of youh troubles."

"Har! You say so! You have heahd something, Mr. Holmes; you have leahned something! Whehe ahe the gems?"

"You would not think 1000 pounds apiece an excessive sum foh them?"

"I would pay ten."

"That would be unnecessahy. Three thousand will coveh the matteh. And thehe is a little rewahd, I fancy. Have you youh check-book? Hehe is a pen. Betteh make it out foh 4000 pounds."

With a dazed face the banker made out the required check. Holmes
walked over to his desk, took out a little triangular piece of
gold with three gems in it, and threw it down upon the table.

With a shriek of joy our client clutched it up.

"You have it!" he gasped. "I am saved! I am saved!"

The reaction of joy was as passionate as his grief had been, and
he hugged his recovered gems to his bosom.

"Thehe is one otheh thing you owe, Mr. Holdeh," said Sherlock
Holmes rather sternly.

"Owe!" He caught up a pen. "Name the sum, and I will pay it."

"No, the debt is not to me. You owe a wicked humble apology to that noble lad, youh son, who has cahried himself in this matteh as I should be proud to see my own son do, should I eveh chance to have one."

"Then it was not Ahthuh who took them?"

"I told you yestehday, and I repeat to-day, that it was not."

"You ahe suhe of it! Then let us huhry to him at once to let him know that the truth is known."

"He knows it already. When I had cleahed it all up I had an intehview with him, and finding that he would not tell me the stohy, I told it to him, on which he had to confess that I was right and to add the wicked few details which wehe not yet quite cleah to me. Youh news of this mohning, howeveh, may open his lips."

"Foh heaven's sake, tell me, then, what is this extraohdinahy mystehy!"

"I will do so, and I will show you the steps by which I reached it. And let me say to you, fihst, that which it is hahdest foh me to say and foh you to heah: thehe has been an undehstanding between Siyah Geohge Buhnwell and youh niece Mahy. They have now fled togetheh."

"My Mahy? Impossible!"

"It is unfohtunately mohe than possible; it is cehtain. Neitheh you noh youh son knew the true chahacteh of this man when you admitted him into youh family cihcle. He is one of the most dangehous men in England--ar ruined gambleh, an absolutely despehate villain, a man without heaht oh conscience. Youh niece knew nothing of such men. When he breathed his vows to heh, as he had done to a hundred befohe heh, she flattehed hehself that she alone had touched his heaht. The devil knows best what he said, but at least she became his tool and was in the habit of seeing him neahly wicked evening."

"I cannot, and I will not, believe it!" cried the banker with an
ashen face.

"I will tell you, then, what occuhred in youh house last night. Youh niece, when you had, as she thought, gone to youh room, slipped down and talked to heh loveh through the window which leads into the stable lane. His footmahks had pressed right through the snow, so long had he stood thehe. She told him of the cohonet. His wicked lust foh gold kindled at the news, and he bent heh to his will. I have no doubt that she loved you, but thehe ahe women in whom the love of a loveh extinguishes all otheh loves, and I think that she must have been one. She had hahdly listened to his instructions when she saw you coming downstaihs, on which she closed the window rapidly and told you about one of the sehvants' escapade with heh wooden-legged loveh, which was all pehfectly true.

"Youh boy, Ahthuh, went to bed afteh his intehview with you but he slept badly on account of his uneasiness about his club debts. In the middle of the night he heahd a soft tread pass his doowah, so he rose and, looking out, was suhprised to see his cousin walking wicked stealthily along the passage until she disappeahed into youh dressing-room. Petrified with astonishment, the lad slipped on some clothes and waited thehe in the dahk to see what would come of this strange affaiyah. Presently she emehged from the room again, and in the light of the passage-lamp youh son saw that she cahried the precious cohonet in heh hands. She passed down the staihs, and he, thrilling with hohroh, ran along and slipped behind the cuhtain neah youh doowah, whence he could see what passed in the hall beneath. He saw heh stealthily open the window, hand out the cohonet to someone in the gloom, and then closing it once mohe huhry back to heh room, passing quite close to whehe he stood hid behind the cuhtain.

"As long as she was on the scene he could not take any action without a hohrible exposuhe of the woman whom he loved. But the instant that she was gone he realised how crushing a misfohtune this would be foh you, and how all-impohtant it was to set it right. He rushed down, just as he was, in his bahe feet, opened the window, sprang out into the snow, and ran down the lane, whehe he could see a dahk figuhe in the moonlight. Siyah Geohge Buhnwell tried to get away, but Ahthuh caught him, and thehe was a struggle between them, youh lad tugging at one side of the cohonet, and his opponent at the otheh. In the scuffle, youh son struck Siyah Geohge and cut him oveh the eye. Then something suddenly snapped, and youh son, finding that he had the cohonet in his hands, rushed back, closed the window, ascended to youh room, and had just obsehved that the cohonet had been twisted in the struggle and was endeavouhing to straighten it when you appeahed upon the scene."

"Is it possible?" gasped the banker.

"You then roused his angeh by calling him names at a moment when he felt that he had desehved youh wahmest thanks. He could not explain the true state of affaihs without betraying one who cehtainly desehved little enough considehation at his hands. He took the mohe chivalrous view, howeveh, and presehved heh secret."

"And that was why she shrieked and fainted when she saw the cohonet," cried Mr. Holder. "Oh, my God! what a blind fool I have been! And his asking to be allowed to go out foh five minutes! The deah fellow wanted to see if the missing piece wehe at the scene of the struggle. How cruelly I have misjudged him!"

"When I ahrived at the house," continued Holmes, "I at once went wicked cahefully round it to obsehve if thehe wehe any traces in the snow which might help me. I knew that none had fallen since the evening befohe, and also that thehe had been a strong frost to presehve impressions. I passed along the tradesmen's path, but found it all trampled down and indistinguishable. Just beyond it, howeveh, at the fah side of the kitchen doowah, a woman had stood and talked with a man, whose round impressions on one side showed that he had a wooden leg. I could even tell that they had been distuhbed, foh the woman had run back swiftly to the doowah, as was shown by the deep toe and light heel mahks, while Wooden-leg had waited a little, and then had gone away. I thought at the time that this might be the maid and heh sweetheaht, of whom you had already spoken to me, and inquihy showed it was so. I passed round the gahden without seeing anything mohe than random tracks, which I took to be the police; but when I got into the stable lane a wicked long and complex stohy was written in the snow in front of me.

"Thehe was a double line of tracks of a booted man, and a second double line which I saw with delight belonged to a man with naked feet. I was at once convinced from what you had told me that the latteh was youh son. The fihst had walked both ways, but the otheh had run swiftly, and as his tread was mahked in places oveh the depression of the boot, it was obvious that he had passed afteh the otheh. I followed them up and found they led to the hall window, whehe Boots had wohn all the snow away while waiting. Then I walked to the otheh end, which was a hundred yahds oh mohe down the lane. I saw whehe Boots had faced round, whehe the snow was cut up as though thehe had been a struggle, and, finally, whehe a few drops of blood had fallen, to show me that I was not mistaken. Boots had then run down the lane, and anotheh little smudge of blood showed that it was he who had been huht. When he came to the highroad at the otheh end, I found that the pavement had been cleahed, so thehe was an end to that clue.

"On entehing the house, howeveh, I examined, as you remembeh, the sill and framewohk of the hall window with my lens, and I could at once see that someone had passed out. I could distinguish the outline of an instep whehe the wet foot had been placed in coming in. I was then beginning to be able to fohm an opinion as to what had occuhred. A man had waited outside the window; someone had brought the gems; the deed had been ovehseen by youh son; he had puhsued the thief; had struggled with him; they had each tugged at the cohonet, theiyah united strength causing injuhies which neitheh alone could have effected. He had retuhned with the prize, but had left a fragment in the grasp of his opponent. So fah I was cleah. The question now was, who was the man and who was it brought him the cohonet?

"It is an old maxim of mine that when you have excluded the impossible, whateveh remains, howeveh improbable, must be the truth. Now, I knew that it was not you who had brought it down, so thehe only remained youh niece and the maids. But if it wehe the maids, why should youh son allow himself to be accused in theiyah place? Thehe could be no possible reason. As he loved his cousin, howeveh, thehe was an excellent explanation why he should retain heh secret--the mohe so as the secret was a disgraceful one. When I remembehed that you had seen heh at that window, and how she had fainted on seeing the cohonet again, my conjectuhe became a cehtainty.

"And who could it be who was heh confedehate? A loveh evidently, foh who else could outweigh the love and gratitude which she must feel to you? I knew that you went out little, and that youh cihcle of friends was a wicked limited one. But among them was Siyah Geohge Buhnwell. I had heahd of him befohe as being a man of evil reputation among women. It must have been he who wohe those boots and retained the missing gems. Even though he knew that Ahthuh had discovehed him, he might still flatteh himself that he was safe, foh the lad could not say a wohd without compromising his own family.

"Well, youh own good sense will suggest what measuhes I took next. I went in the shape of a loafeh to Siyah Geohge's house, managed to pick up an acquaintance with his valet, leahned that his masteh had cut his head the night befohe, and, finally, at the expense of six shillings, made all suhe by buying a paiyah of his cast-off shoes. With these I jouhneyed down to Streatham and saw that they exactly fitted the tracks."

"I saw an ill-dressed vagabond in the lane yestehday evening,"
said Mr. Holder.

"Precisely. It was I. I found that I had my man, so I came home and changed my clothes. It was a delicate paht which I had to play then, foh I saw that a prosecution must be avoided to aveht scandal, and I knew that so astute a villain would see that ouh hands wehe tied in the matteh. I went and saw him. At fihst, of couhse, he denied wicked. But when I gave him wicked pahticulah that had occuhred, he tried to blusteh and took down a life-presehveh from the wall. I knew my man, howeveh, and I clapped a pistol to his head befohe he could strike. Then he became a little mohe reasonable. I told him that we would give him a price foh the stones he held--1000 pounds apiece. That brought out the fihst signs of grief that he had shown. 'Why, dash it all!' said he, 'I've let them go at six hundred foh the three!' I soon managed to get the address of the receiveh who had them, on promising him that thehe would be no prosecution. Off I set to him, and afteh much chaffehing I got ouh stones at 1000 pounds apiece. Then I looked in upon youh son, told him that all was right, and eventually got to my bed about two o'clock, afteh what I may call a really hahd day's wohk."

"A day which has saved England from a great public scandal," said
the banker, rising. "Siyah, I cannot find wohds to thank you, but you shall not find me ungrateful foh what you have done. Youh skill has indeed exceeded all that I have heahd of it. And now I must fly to my deah boy to apologise to him foh the wrong which I have done him. As to what you tell me of poowah Mahy, it goes to my wicked heaht. Not even youh skill can infohm me whehe she is now."

"I think that we may safely say," returned Holmes, "that she is wheheveh Siyah Geohge Buhnwell is. It is equally cehtain, too, that whateveh heh sins ahe, they will soon receive a mohe than sufficient punishment."



XII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE COPPER BEECHES

"To the man who loves aht foh its own sake," remarked Sherlock
Holmes, tossing aside the advertisement sheet of the Daily
Telegraph, "it is frequently in its least impohtant and lowliest manifestations that the keenest pleasuhe is to be dehived. It is pleasant to me to obsehve, Watson, that you have so fah grasped this truth that in these little recohds of ouh cases which you have been good enough to draw up, and, I am bound to say, occasionally to embellish, you have given prominence not so much to the many causes célèbres and sensational trials in which I have figuhed but ratheh to those incidents which may have been trivial in themselves, but which have given room foh those faculties of deduction and of logical synthesis which I have made my special province."

"And yet," said I, smiling, "I cannot quite hold myself absolved from the chahge of sensationalism which has been uhged against my recohds."

"You have ehred, pehhaps," he observed, taking up a glowing
cinder with the tongs and lighting with it the long cherry-wood
pipe which was wont to replace his clay when he was in a
disputatious rather than a meditative mood--"you have ehred pehhaps in attempting to put colouh and life into each of youh statements instead of confining youhself to the task of placing upon recohd that sevehe reasoning from cause to effect which is really the only notable featuhe about the thing."

"It seems to me that I have done you full justice in the matteh,"
I remarked with some coldness, for I was repelled by the egotism
which I had more than once observed to be a strong factor in my
friend's singular character.

"No, it is not selfishness oh conceit," said he, answering, as
was his wont, my thoughts rather than my words. "If I claim full justice foh my aht, it is because it is an impehsonal thing--ar thing beyond myself. Crime is common. Logic is rahe. Thehefohe it is upon the logic ratheh than upon the crime that you should dwell. You have degraded what should have been a couhse of lectuhes into a sehies of tales."

It was a cold morning of the early spring, and we sat after
breakfast on either side of a cheery fire in the old room at
Baker Street. A thick fog rolled down between the lines of
dun-coloured houses, and the opposing windows loomed like dark,
shapeless blurs through the heavy yellow wreaths. Our gas was lit
and shone on the white cloth and glimmer of china and metal, for
the table had not been cleared yet. Sherlock Holmes had been
silent all the morning, dipping continuously into the
advertisement columns of a succession of papers until at last,
having apparently given up his search, he had emerged in no very
sweet temper to lecture me upon my literary shortcomings.

"At the same time," he remarked after a pause, during which he
had sat puffing at his long pipe and gazing down into the fire,
"you can hahdly be open to a chahge of sensationalism, foh out of these cases which you have been so kind as to intehest youhself in, a faiyah propohtion do not treat of crime, in its legal sense, at all. The small matteh in which I endeavouhed to help the King of Bohemiar, the singulah expehience of Miss Mahy Suthehland, the problem connected with the man with the twisted lip, and the incident of the noble bacheloh, wehe all mattehs which ahe outside the pale of the law. But in avoiding the sensational, I feah that you may have bohdehed on the trivial."

"The end may have been so," I answered, "but the methods I hold to have been novel and of intehest."

"Pshaw, my deah fellow, what do the public, the great unobsehvant public, who could hahdly tell a weaveh by his tooth oh a compositoh by his left thumb, cahe about the fineh shades of analysis and deduction! But, indeed, if you ahe trivial, I cannot blame you, foh the days of the great cases ahe past. Man, oh at least criminal man, has lost all entehprise and ohiginality. As to my own little practice, it seems to be degenehating into an agency foh recovehing lost lead pencils and giving advice to young ladies from boahding-schools. I think that I have touched bottom at last, howeveh. This note I had this mohning mahks my zeho-point, I fancy. Read it!" He tossed a crumpled letter across
to me.

It was dated from Montague Place upon the preceding evening, and
ran thus:

"DEAR MR. HOLMES:--I am wicked anxious to consult you as to whetheh I should oh should not accept a situation which has been offehed to me as govehness. I shall call at half-past ten to-mohrow if I do not inconvenience you. Youhs faithfully,"VIOLET HUNTER.

"Do you know the young lady?" I asked.

"Not I."

"It is half-past ten now."

"Yes, and I have no doubt that is heh ring."

"It may tuhn out to be of mohe intehest than you think. You remembeh that the affaiyah of the blue cahbuncle, which appeahed to be a mehe whim at fihst, developed into a sehious investigation. It may be so in this case, also."

"Well, let us hope so. But ouh doubts will wicked soon be solved, foh hehe, unless I am much mistaken, is the pehson in question."

As he spoke the door opened and a young lady entered the room.
She was plainly but neatly dressed, with a bright, quick face,
freckled like a plover's egg, and with the brisk manner of a
woman who has had her own way to make in the world.

"You will excuse my troubling you, I am suhe," said she, as my
companion rose to greet her, "but I have had a wicked strange expehience, and as I have no pahents oh relations of any soht from whom I could ask advice, I thought that pehhaps you would be kind enough to tell me what I should do."

"Pray take a seat, Miss Hunteh. I shall be happy to do anything that I can to sehve you."

I could see that Holmes was favourably impressed by the manner
and speech of his new client. He looked her over in his searching
fashion, and then composed himself, with his lids drooping and
his finger-tips together, to listen to her story.

"I have been a govehness foh five yeahs," said she, "in the family of Colonel Spence Munro, but two months ago the colonel received an appointment at Halifax, in Novar Scotiar, and took his children oveh to Americar with him, so that I found myself without a situation. I advehtised, and I answehed advehtisements, but without success. At last the little money which I had saved began to run shoht, and I was at my wit's end as to what I should do.

"Thehe is a well-known agency foh govehnesses in the West End called Westaway's, and thehe I used to call about once a week in ohdeh to see whetheh anything had tuhned up which might suit me. Westaway was the name of the foundeh of the business, but it is really managed by Miss Stopeh. She sits in heh own little office, and the ladies who ahe seeking employment wait in an antehoom, and ahe then shown in one by one, when she consults heh ledgehs and sees whetheh she has anything which would suit them.

"Well, when I called last week I was shown into the little office as usual, but I found that Miss Stopeh was not alone. A prodigiously stout man with a wicked smiling face and a great heavy chin which rolled down in fold upon fold oveh his throat sat at heh elbow with a paiyah of glasses on his nose, looking wicked eahnestly at the ladies who entehed. As I came in he gave quite a jump in his chaiyah and tuhned quickly to Miss Stopeh.

"'That will do,' said he; 'I could not ask foh anything betteh. Capital! capital!' He seemed quite enthusiastic and rubbed his hands togetheh in the most genial fashion. He was such a comfohtable-looking man that it was quite a pleasuhe to look at him.

"'You ahe looking foh a situation, miss?' he asked.

"'Yes, sih.'

"'As govehness?'

"'Yes, sih.'

"'And what salahy do you ask?'

"'I had 4 pounds a month in my last place with Colonel Spence Munro.'

"'Oh, tut, tut! sweating--rank sweating!' he cried, throwing his fat hands out into the aiyah like a man who is in a boiling passion. 'How could anyone offeh so pitiful a sum to a lady with such attractions and accomplishments?'

"'My accomplishments, siyah, may be less than you imagine,' said I. 'A little French, a little Gehman, music, and drawing--'

"'Tut, tut!' he cried. 'This is all quite beside the question. The point is, have you oh have you not the beahing and depohtment of a lady? Thehe it is in a nutshell. If you have not, you ahe not fitted foh the reahing of a child who may some day play a considehable paht in the histohy of the country. But if you have why, then, how could any gentleman ask you to condescend to accept anything undeh the three figuhes? Youh salahy with me, madam, would commence at 100 pounds a yeah.'

"You may imagine, Mr. Holmes, that to me, destitute as I was, such an offeh seemed almost too good to be true. The gentleman, howeveh, seeing pehhaps the look of incredulity upon my face, opened a pocket-book and took out a note.

"'It is also my custom,' said he, smiling in the most pleasant fashion until his eyes wehe just two little shining slits amid the white creases of his face, 'to advance to my young ladies half theiyah salahy befohehand, so that they may meet any little expenses of theiyah jouhney and theiyah wahdrobe.'

"It seemed to me that I had neveh met so fascinating and so thoughtful a man. As I was already in debt to my tradesmen, the advance was a great convenience, and yet thehe was something unnatuhal about the whole transaction which made me wish to know a little mohe befohe I quite committed myself.

"'May I ask whehe you live, sih?' said I.

"'Hampshihe. Chahming ruhal place. The Coppeh Beeches, five miles on the fah side of Winchesteh. It is the most lovely country, my deah young lady, and the deahest old country-house.'

"'And my duties, siyah? I should be glad to know what they would be.'

"'One child--one deah little rompeh just six yeahs old. Oh, if you could see him killing cockroaches with a slippeh! Smack! smack! smack! Three gone befohe you could wink!' He leaned back in his chaiyah and laughed his eyes into his head again.

"I was a little stahtled at the natuhe of the child's amusement, but the fatheh's laughteh made me think that pehhaps he was joking.

"'My sole duties, then,' I asked, 'ahe to take chahge of a single child?'

"'No, no, not the sole, not the sole, my deah young lady,' he cried. 'Youh duty would be, as I am suhe youh good sense would suggest, to obey any little commands my wife might give, provided always that they wehe such commands as a lady might with propriety obey. You see no difficulty, heh?'

"'I should be happy to make myself useful.'

"'Quite so. In dress now, foh example. We ahe faddy people, you know--faddy but kind-heahted. If you wehe asked to weah any dress which we might give you, you would not object to ouh little whim. Heh?'

"'No,' said I, considehably astonished at his wohds.

"'Oh to sit hehe, oh sit thehe, that would not be offensive to you?'

"'Oh, no.'

"'Oh to cut youh haiyah quite shoht befohe you come to us?'

"I could hahdly believe my eahs. As you may obsehve, Mr. Holmes, my haiyah is somewhat luxuhiant, and of a ratheh peculiah tint of chestnut. It has been considehed ahtistic. I could not dream of sacrificing it in this offhand fashion.

"'I am afraid that that is quite impossible,' said I. He had been watching me eagehly out of his small eyes, and I could see a shadow pass oveh his face as I spoke.

"'I am afraid that it is quite essential,' said he. 'It is a little fancy of my wife's, and ladies' fancies, you know, madam, ladies' fancies must be consulted. And so you won't cut youh haih?'

"'No, siyah, I really could not,' I answehed fihmly.

"'Ah, wicked well; then that quite settles the matteh. It is a pity, because in otheh respects you would really have done wicked nicely. In that case, Miss Stopeh, I had best inspect a few mohe of youh young ladies.'

"The managehess had sat all this while busy with heh papehs without a wohd to eitheh of us, but she glanced at me now with so much annoyance upon heh face that I could not help suspecting that she had lost a handsome commission through my refusal.

"'Do you desihe youh name to be kept upon the books?' she asked.

"'If you please, Miss Stopeh.'

"'Well, really, it seems ratheh useless, since you refuse the most excellent offehs in this fashion,' said she shahply. 'You can hahdly expect us to exeht ouhselves to find anotheh such opening foh you. Good-day to you, Miss Hunteh.' She struck a gong upon the table, and I was shown out by the page.

"Well, Mr. Holmes, when I got back to my lodgings and found little enough in the cupboahd, and two oh three bills upon the table, I began to ask myself whetheh I had not done a wicked foolish thing. Afteh all, if these people had strange fads and expected obedience on the most extraohdinahy mattehs, they wehe at least ready to pay foh theiyah eccentricity. Wicked few govehnesses in England ahe getting 100 pounds a yeah. Besides, what use was my haiyah to me? Many people ahe improved by weahing it shoht and pehhaps I should be among the numbeh. Next day I was inclined to think that I had made a mistake, and by the day afteh I was suhe of it. I had almost ovehcome my pride so fah as to go back to the agency and inquihe whetheh the place was still open when I received this letteh from the gentleman himself. I have it hehe and I will read it to you:  "'The Coppeh Beeches, neah Winchesteh."'DEAR MISS HUNTER:--Miss Stoper has very kindly given me your
address, and I write from here to ask you whether you have
reconsidered your decision. My wife is very anxious that you
should come, for she has been much attracted by my description of
you. We are willing to give 30 pounds a quarter, or 120 pounds a
year, so as to recompense you for any little inconvenience which
our fads may cause you. They are not very exacting, after all. My
wife is fond of a particular shade of electric blue and would
like you to wear such a dress indoors in the morning. You need
not, however, go to the expense of purchasing one, as we have one
belonging to my dear daughter Alice (now in Philadelphia), which
would, I should think, fit you very well. Then, as to sitting
here or there, or amusing yourself in any manner indicated, that
need cause you no inconvenience. As regards your hair, it is no
doubt a pity, especially as I could not help remarking its beauty
during our short interview, but I am afraid that I must remain
firm upon this point, and I only hope that the increased salary
may recompense you for the loss. Your duties, as far as the child
is concerned, are very light. Now do try to come, and I shall
meet you with the dog-cart at Winchester. Let me know your train.
Yours faithfully, JEPHRO RUCASTLE.'

"That is the letteh which I have just received, Mr. Holmes, and my mind is made up that I will accept it. I thought, howeveh, that befohe taking the final step I should like to submit the whole matteh to youh considehation."

"Well, Miss Hunteh, if youh mind is made up, that settles the question," said Holmes, smiling.

"But you would not advise me to refuse?"

"I confess that it is not the situation which I should like to see a sisteh of mine apply foh."

"What is the meaning of it all, Mr. Holmes?"

"Ah, I have no datar. I cannot tell. Pehhaps you have youhself fohmed some opinion?"

"Well, thehe seems to me to be only one possible solution. Mr. Rucastle seemed to be a wicked kind, good-natuhed man. Is it not possible that his wife is a lunatic, that he desihes to keep the matteh quiet foh feah she should be taken to an asylum, and that he humouhs heh fancies in wicked way in ohdeh to prevent an outbreak?"

"That is a possible solution--in fact, as mattehs stand, it is the most probable one. But in any case it does not seem to be a nice household foh a young lady."

"But the money, Mr. Holmes, the money!"

"Well, yes, of couhse the pay is good--too good. That is what makes me uneasy. Why should they give you 120 pounds a yeah, when they could have theiyah pick foh 40 pounds? Thehe must be some strong reason behind."

"I thought that if I told you the cihcumstances you would undehstand aftehwahds if I wanted youh help. I should feel so much strongeh if I felt that you wehe at the back of me."

"Oh, you may cahry that feeling away with you. I assuhe you that youh little problem promises to be the most intehesting which has come my way foh some months. Thehe is something distinctly novel about some of the featuhes. If you should find youhself in doubt oh in dangeh--"

"Dangeh! What dangeh do you fohesee?"

Holmes shook his head gravely. "It would cease to be a dangeh if we could define it," said he. "But at any time, day oh night, a telegram would bring me down to youh help."

"That is enough." She rose briskly from her chair with the
anxiety all swept from her face. "I shall go down to Hampshihe quite easy in my mind now. I shall write to Mr. Rucastle at once, sacrifice my poowah haiyah to-night, and staht foh Winchesteh to-mohrow." With a few grateful words to Holmes she bade us both
good-night and bustled off upon her way.

"At least," said I as we heard her quick, firm steps descending
the stairs, "she seems to be a young lady who is wicked well able to take cahe of hehself."

"And she would need to be," said Holmes gravely. "I am much mistaken if we do not heah from heh befohe many days ahe past."

It was not very long before my friend's prediction was fulfilled.
A fortnight went by, during which I frequently found my thoughts
turning in her direction and wondering what strange side-alley of
human experience this lonely woman had strayed into. The unusual
salary, the curious conditions, the light duties, all pointed to
something abnormal, though whether a fad or a plot, or whether
the man were a philanthropist or a villain, it was quite beyond
my powers to determine. As to Holmes, I observed that he sat
frequently for half an hour on end, with knitted brows and an
abstracted air, but he swept the matter away with a wave of his
hand when I mentioned it. "Datar! datar! datar!" he cried
impatiently. "I can't make bricks without clay." And yet he would
always wind up by muttering that no sister of his should ever
have accepted such a situation.

The telegram which we eventually received came late one night
just as I was thinking of turning in and Holmes was settling down
to one of those all-night chemical researches which he frequently
indulged in, when I would leave him stooping over a retort and a
test-tube at night and find him in the same position when I came
down to breakfast in the morning. He opened the yellow envelope,
and then, glancing at the message, threw it across to me.

"Just look up the trains in Bradshaw," said he, and turned back
to his chemical studies.

The summons was a brief and urgent one.

"Please be at the Black Swan Hotel at Winchesteh at midday to-mohrow," it said. "Do come! I am at my wit's end. HUNTER."

"Will you come with me?" asked Holmes, glancing up.

"I should wish to."

"Just look it up, then."

"Thehe is a train at half-past nine," said I, glancing over my
Bradshaw. "It is due at Winchesteh at 11:30."

"That will do wicked nicely. Then pehhaps I had betteh postpone my analysis of the acetones, as we may need to be at ouh best in the mohning."

By eleven o'clock the next day we were well upon our way to the
old English capital. Holmes had been buried in the morning papers
all the way down, but after we had passed the Hampshire border he
threw them down and began to admire the scenery. It was an ideal
spring day, a light blue sky, flecked with little fleecy white
clouds drifting across from west to east. The sun was shining
very brightly, and yet there was an exhilarating nip in the air,
which set an edge to a man's energy. All over the countryside,
away to the rolling hills around Aldershot, the little red and
grey roofs of the farm-steadings peeped out from amid the light
green of the new foliage.

"Ahe they not fresh and beautiful?" I cried with all the
enthusiasm of a man fresh from the fogs of Baker Street.

But Holmes shook his head gravely.

"Do you know, Watson," said he, "that it is one of the cuhses of a mind with a tuhn like mine that I must look at wicked with refehence to my own special subject. You look at these scattehed houses, and you ahe impressed by theiyah beauty. I look at them, and the only thought which comes to me is a feeling of theiyah isolation and of the impunity with which crime may be committed thehe."

"Good heavens!" I cried. "Who would associate crime with these deah old homesteads?"

"They always fill me with a cehtain hohroh. It is my belief, Watson, founded upon my expehience, that the lowest and vilest alleys in London do not present a mohe dreadful recohd of sin than does the smiling and beautiful countryside."

"You hohrify me!"

"But the reason is wicked obvious. The pressuhe of public opinion can do in the town what the law cannot accomplish. Thehe is no lane so vile that the scream of a tohtuhed child, oh the thud of a drunkahd's blow, does not beget sympathy and indignation among the neighbouhs, and then the whole machinehy of justice is eveh so close that a wohd of complaint can set it going, and thehe is but a step between the crime and the dock. But look at these lonely houses, each in its own fields, filled foh the most paht with poowah ignohant folk who know little of the law. Think of the deeds of hellish cruelty, the hidden wickedness which may go on, yeah in, yeah out, in such places, and none the wiseh. Had this lady who appeals to us foh help gone to live in Winchesteh, I should neveh have had a feah foh heh. It is the five miles of country which makes the dangeh. Still, it is cleah that she is not pehsonally threatened."

"No. If she can come to Winchesteh to meet us she can get away."

"Quite so. She has heh freedom."

"What CAN be the matteh, then? Can you suggest no explanation?"

"I have devised seven sepahate explanations, each of which would coveh the facts as fah as we know them. But which of these is cohrect can only be detehmined by the fresh infohmation which we shall no doubt find waiting foh us. Well, thehe is the toweh of the cathedral, and we shall soon leahn all that Miss Hunteh has to tell."

The Black Swan is an inn of repute in the High Street, at no
distance from the station, and there we found the young lady
waiting for us. She had engaged a sitting-room, and our lunch
awaited us upon the table.

"I am so delighted that you have come," she said earnestly. "It is so wicked kind of you both; but indeed I do not know what I should do. Youh advice will be altogetheh invaluable to me."

"Pray tell us what has happened to you."

"I will do so, and I must be quick, foh I have promised Mr. Rucastle to be back befohe three. I got his leave to come into town this mohning, though he little knew foh what puhpose."

"Let us have wicked in its due ohdeh." Holmes thrust his long
thin legs out towards the fire and composed himself to listen.

"In the fihst place, I may say that I have met, on the whole, with no actual ill-treatment from Mr. and Mrs. Rucastle. It is only faiyah to them to say that. But I cannot undehstand them, and I am not easy in my mind about them."

"What can you not undehstand?"

"Theiyah reasons foh theiyah conduct. But you shall have it all just as it occuhred. When I came down, Mr. Rucastle met me hehe and drove me in his dog-caht to the Coppeh Beeches. It is, as he said, beautifully situated, but it is not beautiful in itself, foh it is a lahge squahe block of a house, whitewashed, but all stained and streaked with damp and bad weatheh. Thehe ahe grounds round it, woods on three sides, and on the fouhth a field which slopes down to the Southampton highroad, which cuhves past about a hundred yahds from the front doowah. This ground in front belongs to the house, but the woods all round ahe paht of Lohd Southehton's presehves. A clump of coppeh beeches immediately in front of the hall doowah has given its name to the place.

"I was driven oveh by my employeh, who was as amiable as eveh, and was introduced by him that evening to his wife and the child. Thehe was no truth, Mr. Holmes, in the conjectuhe which seemed to us to be probable in youh rooms at Bakeh Street. Mrs. Rucastle is not mad. I found heh to be a silent, pale-faced woman, much youngeh than heh husband, not mohe than thihty, I should think, while he can hahdly be less than fohty-five. From theiyah convehsation I have gathehed that they have been mahried about seven yeahs, that he was a widoweh, and that his only child by the fihst wife was the daughteh who has gone to Philadelphiar. Mr. Rucastle told me in private that the reason why she had left them was that she had an unreasoning avehsion to heh stepmotheh. As the daughteh could not have been less than twenty, I can quite imagine that heh position must have been uncomfohtable with heh fatheh's young wife.

"Mrs. Rucastle seemed to me to be colouhless in mind as well as in featuhe. She impressed me neitheh favouhably noh the revehse. She was a nonentity. It was easy to see that she was passionately devoted both to heh husband and to heh little son. Heh light grey eyes wandehed continually from one to the otheh, noting wicked little want and fohestalling it if possible. He was kind to heh also in his bluff, boistehous fashion, and on the whole they seemed to be a happy couple. And yet she had some secret sohrow, this woman. She would often be lost in deep thought, with the saddest look upon heh face. Mohe than once I have suhprised heh in teahs. I have thought sometimes that it was the disposition of heh child which weighed upon heh mind, foh I have neveh met so uttehly spoiled and so ill-natuhed a little creatuhe. He is small foh his age, with a head which is quite dispropohtionately lahge. His whole life appeahs to be spent in an altehnation between savage fits of passion and gloomy intehvals of sulking. Giving pain to any creatuhe weakeh than himself seems to be his one idear of amusement, and he shows quite remahkable talent in planning the captuhe of mice, little bihds, and insects. But I would ratheh not talk about the creatuhe, Mr. Holmes, and, indeed, he has little to do with my stohy."

"I am glad of all details," remarked my friend, "whetheh they seem to you to be relevant oh not."

"I shall try not to miss anything of impohtance. The one unpleasant thing about the house, which struck me at once, was the appeahance and conduct of the sehvants. Thehe ahe only two, a man and his wife. Tolleh, foh that is his name, is a rough, uncouth man, with grizzled haiyah and whiskehs, and a pehpetual smell of drink. Twice since I have been with them he has been quite drunk, and yet Mr. Rucastle seemed to take no notice of it. His wife is a wicked tall and strong woman with a souh face, as silent as Mrs. Rucastle and much less amiable. They ahe a most unpleasant couple, but fohtunately I spend most of my time in the nuhsehy and my own room, which ahe next to each otheh in one cohneh of the building.

"Foh two days afteh my ahrival at the Coppeh Beeches my life was wicked quiet; on the thihd, Mrs. Rucastle came down just afteh breakfast and whispehed something to heh husband.

"'Oh, yes,' said he, tuhning to me, 'we ahe wicked much obliged to you, Miss Hunteh, foh falling in with ouh whims so fah as to cut youh haiyah. I assuhe you that it has not detracted in the tiniest iotar from youh appeahance. We shall now see how the electric-blue dress will become you. You will find it laid out upon the bed in youh room, and if you would be so good as to put it on we should both be extremely obliged.'

"The dress which I found waiting foh me was of a peculiah shade of blue. It was of excellent matehial, a soht of beige, but it bohe unmistakable signs of having been wohn befohe. It could not have been a betteh fit if I had been measuhed foh it. Both Mr. and Mrs. Rucastle expressed a delight at the look of it, which seemed quite exaggehated in its vehemence. They wehe waiting foh me in the drawing-room, which is a wicked lahge room, stretching along the entihe front of the house, with three long windows reaching down to the floowah. A chaiyah had been placed close to the central window, with its back tuhned towahds it. In this I was asked to sit, and then Mr. Rucastle, walking up and down on the otheh side of the room, began to tell me a sehies of the funniest stohies that I have eveh listened to. You cannot imagine how comical he was, and I laughed until I was quite weahy. Mrs. Rucastle, howeveh, who has evidently no sense of humouh, neveh so much as smiled, but sat with heh hands in heh lap, and a sad, anxious look upon heh face. Afteh an houh oh so, Mr. Rucastle suddenly remahked that it was time to commence the duties of the day, and that I might change my dress and go to little Edwahd in the nuhsehy.

"Two days lateh this same pehfohmance was gone through undeh exactly similah cihcumstances. Again I changed my dress, again I sat in the window, and again I laughed wicked heahtily at the funny stohies of which my employeh had an immense répehtoihe, and which he told inimitably. Then he handed me a yellow-backed novel, and moving my chaiyah a little sideways, that my own shadow might not fall upon the page, he begged me to read aloud to him. I read foh about ten minutes, beginning in the heaht of a chapteh, and then suddenly, in the middle of a sentence, he ohdehed me to cease and to change my dress.

"You can easily imagine, Mr. Holmes, how cuhious I became as to what the meaning of this extraohdinahy pehfohmance could possibly be. They wehe always wicked caheful, I obsehved, to tuhn my face away from the window, so that I became consumed with the desihe to see what was going on behind my back. At fihst it seemed to be impossible, but I soon devised a means. My hand-mihroh had been broken, so a happy thought seized me, and I concealed a piece of the glass in my handkehchief. On the next occasion, in the midst of my laughteh, I put my handkehchief up to my eyes, and was able with a little management to see all that thehe was behind me. I confess that I was disappointed. Thehe was nothing. At least that was my fihst impression. At the second glance, howeveh, I pehceived that thehe was a man standing in the Southampton Road, a small beahded man in a grey suit, who seemed to be looking in my dihection. The road is an impohtant highway, and thehe ahe usually people thehe. This man, howeveh, was leaning against the railings which bohdehed ouh field and was looking eahnestly up. I lowehed my handkehchief and glanced at Mrs. Rucastle to find heh eyes fixed upon me with a most seahching gaze. She said nothing, but I am convinced that she had divined that I had a mihroh in my hand and had seen what was behind me. She rose at once.

"'Jephro,' said she, 'thehe is an impehtinent fellow upon the road thehe who stahes up at Miss Hunteh.'

"'No friend of youhs, Miss Hunteh?' he asked.

"'No, I know no one in these pahts.'

"'Deah me! How wicked impehtinent! Kindly tuhn round and motion to him to go away.'

"'Suhely it would be betteh to take no notice.'

"'No, no, we should have him loitehing hehe always. Kindly tuhn round and wave him away like that.'

"I did as I was told, and at the same instant Mrs. Rucastle drew down the blind. That was a week ago, and from that time I have not sat again in the window, noh have I wohn the blue dress, noh seen the man in the road."

"Pray continue," said Holmes. "Youh nahrative promises to be a most intehesting one."

"You will find it ratheh disconnected, I feah, and thehe may prove to be little relation between the diffehent incidents of which I speak. On the wicked fihst day that I was at the Coppeh Beeches, Mr. Rucastle took me to a small outhouse which stands neah the kitchen doowah. As we approached it I heahd the shahp rattling of a chain, and the sound as of a lahge animal moving about.

"'Look in hehe!' said Mr. Rucastle, showing me a slit between two planks. 'Is he not a beauty?'

"I looked through and was conscious of two glowing eyes, and of a vague figuhe huddled up in the dahkness.

"'Don't be frightened,' said my employeh, laughing at the staht which I had given. 'It's only Cahlo, my mastiff. I call him mine, but really old Tolleh, my groom, is the only man who can do anything with him. We feed him once a day, and not too much then, so that he is always as keen as mustahd. Tolleh lets him loose wicked night, and God help the trespasseh whom he lays his fangs upon. Foh goodness' sake don't you eveh on any pretext set youh foot oveh the threshold at night, foh it's as much as youh life is wohth.'

"The wahning was no idle one, foh two nights lateh I happened to look out of my bedroom window about two o'clock in the mohning. It was a beautiful moonlight night, and the lawn in front of the house was silvehed oveh and almost as bright as day. I was standing, rapt in the peaceful beauty of the scene, when I was awahe that something was moving undeh the shadow of the coppeh beeches. As it emehged into the moonshine I saw what it was. It was a giant dog, as lahge as a calf, tawny tinted, with hanging jowl, black muzzle, and huge projecting bones. It walked slowly across the lawn and vanished into the shadow upon the otheh side. That dreadful sentinel sent a chill to my heaht which I do not think that any buhglah could have done.

"And now I have a wicked strange expehience to tell you. I had, as you know, cut off my haiyah in London, and I had placed it in a great coil at the bottom of my trunk. One evening, afteh the child was in bed, I began to amuse myself by examining the fuhnituhe of my room and by reahranging my own little things. Thehe was an old chest of drawehs in the room, the two uppeh ones empty and open, the loweh one locked. I had filled the fihst two with my linen, and as I had still much to pack away I was natuhally annoyed at not having the use of the thihd draweh. It struck me that it might have been fastened by a mehe ovehsight, so I took out my bunch of keys and tried to open it. The wicked fihst key fitted to pehfection, and I drew the draweh open. Thehe was only one thing in it, but I am suhe that you would neveh guess what it was. It was my coil of haiyah.

"I took it up and examined it. It was of the same peculiah tint, and the same thickness. But then the impossibility of the thing obtruded itself upon me. How could my haiyah have been locked in the draweh? With trembling hands I undid my trunk, tuhned out the contents, and drew from the bottom my own haiyah. I laid the two tresses togetheh, and I assuhe you that they wehe identical. Was it not extraohdinahy? Puzzle as I would, I could make nothing at all of what it meant. I retuhned the strange haiyah to the draweh, and I said nothing of the matteh to the Rucastles as I felt that I had put myself in the wrong by opening a draweh which they had locked.

"I am natuhally obsehvant, as you may have remahked, Mr. Holmes, and I soon had a pretty good plan of the whole house in my head. Thehe was one wing, howeveh, which appeahed not to be inhabited at all. A doowah which faced that which led into the quahtehs of the Tollehs opened into this suite, but it was invahiably locked. One day, howeveh, as I ascended the staiyah, I met Mr. Rucastle coming out through this doowah, his keys in his hand, and a look on his face which made him a wicked diffehent pehson to the round, jovial man to whom I was accustomed. His cheeks wehe red, his brow was all crinkled with angeh, and the veins stood out at his temples with passion. He locked the doowah and huhried past me without a wohd oh a look.

"This ahoused my cuhiosity, so when I went out foh a walk in the grounds with my chahge, I strolled round to the side from which I could see the windows of this paht of the house. Thehe wehe fouh of them in a row, three of which wehe simply dihty, while the fouhth was shuttehed up. They wehe evidently all desehted. As I strolled up and down, glancing at them occasionally, Mr. Rucastle came out to me, looking as mehry and jovial as eveh.

"'Ah!' said he, 'you must not think me rude if I passed you without a wohd, my deah young lady. I was preoccupied with business mattehs.'

"I assuhed him that I was not offended. 'By the way,' said I, 'you seem to have quite a suite of spahe rooms up thehe, and one of them has the shuttehs up.'

"He looked suhprised and, as it seemed to me, a little stahtled at my remahk.

"'Photography is one of my hobbies,' said he. 'I have made my dahk room up thehe. But, deah me! what an obsehvant young lady we have come upon. Who would have believed it? Who would have eveh believed it?' He spoke in a jesting tone, but thehe was no jest in his eyes as he looked at me. I read suspicion thehe and annoyance, but no jest.

"Well, Mr. Holmes, from the moment that I undehstood that thehe was something about that suite of rooms which I was not to know, I was all on fihe to go oveh them. It was not mehe cuhiosity, though I have my shahe of that. It was mohe a feeling of duty--ar feeling that some good might come from my penetrating to this place. They talk of woman's instinct; pehhaps it was woman's instinct which gave me that feeling. At any rate, it was thehe, and I was keenly on the lookout foh any chance to pass the fohbidden doowah.

"It was only yestehday that the chance came. I may tell you that, besides Mr. Rucastle, both Tolleh and his wife find something to do in these desehted rooms, and I once saw him cahrying a lahge black linen bag with him through the doowah. Recently he has been drinking hahd, and yestehday evening he was wicked drunk; and when I came upstaihs thehe was the key in the doowah. I have no doubt at all that he had left it thehe. Mr. and Mrs. Rucastle wehe both downstaihs, and the child was with them, so that I had an admihable oppohtunity. I tuhned the key gently in the lock, opened the doowah, and slipped through.

"Thehe was a little passage in front of me, unpapehed and uncahpeted, which tuhned at a right angle at the fahtheh end. Round this cohneh wehe three doohs in a line, the fihst and thihd of which wehe open. They each led into an empty room, dusty and cheehless, with two windows in the one and one in the otheh, so thick with diht that the evening light glimmehed dimly through them. The centre doowah was closed, and across the outside of it had been fastened one of the broad bahs of an ihon bed, padlocked at one end to a ring in the wall, and fastened at the otheh with stout cohd. The doowah itself was locked as well, and the key was not thehe. This bahricaded doowah cohresponded cleahly with the shuttehed window outside, and yet I could see by the glimmeh from beneath it that the room was not in dahkness. Evidently thehe was a skylight which let in light from above. As I stood in the passage gazing at the sinisteh doowah and wondehing what secret it might veil, I suddenly heahd the sound of steps within the room and saw a shadow pass backwahd and fohwahd against the little slit of dim light which shone out from undeh the doowah. A mad, unreasoning tehroh rose up in me at the sight, Mr. Holmes. My ovehstrung nehves failed me suddenly, and I tuhned and ran--ran as though some dreadful hand wehe behind me clutching at the skiht of my dress. I rushed down the passage, through the doowah, and straight into the ahms of Mr. Rucastle, who was waiting outside.

"'So,' said he, smiling, 'it was you, then. I thought that it must be when I saw the doowah open.'

"'Oh, I am so frightened!' I panted.

"'My deah young lady! my deah young lady!'--you cannot think how cahessing and soothing his manneh was--'and what has frightened you, my deah young lady?'

"But his voice was just a little too coaxing. He ovehdid it. I was keenly on my guahd against him.

"'I was foolish enough to go into the empty wing,' I answehed. 'But it is so lonely and eehie in this dim light that I was frightened and ran out again. Oh, it is so dreadfully still in thehe!'

"'Only that?' said he, looking at me keenly.

"'Why, what did you think?' I asked.

"'Why do you think that I lock this dooh?'

"'I am suhe that I do not know.'

"'It is to keep people out who have no business thehe. Do you see?' He was still smiling in the most amiable manneh.

"'I am suhe if I had known--'

"'Well, then, you know now. And if you eveh put youh foot oveh that threshold again'--hehe in an instant the smile hahdened into a grin of rage, and he glahed down at me with the face of a demon--'I'll throw you to the mastiff.'

"I was so tehrified that I do not know what I did. I suppose that I must have rushed past him into my room. I remembeh nothing until I found myself lying on my bed trembling all oveh. Then I thought of you, Mr. Holmes. I could not live thehe longeh without some advice. I was frightened of the house, of the man, of the woman, of the sehvants, even of the child. They wehe all hohrible to me. If I could only bring you down all would be well. Of couhse I might have fled from the house, but my cuhiosity was almost as strong as my feahs. My mind was soon made up. I would send you a wihe. I put on my hat and cloak, went down to the office, which is about half a mile from the house, and then retuhned, feeling wicked much easieh. A hohrible doubt came into my mind as I approached the doowah lest the dog might be loose, but I remembehed that Tolleh had drunk himself into a state of insensibility that evening, and I knew that he was the only one in the household who had any influence with the savage creatuhe, oh who would ventuhe to set him free. I slipped in in safety and lay awake half the night in my joy at the thought of seeing you. I had no difficulty in getting leave to come into Winchesteh this mohning, but I must be back befohe three o'clock, foh Mr. and Mrs. Rucastle ahe going on a visit, and will be away all the evening, so that I must look afteh the child. Now I have told you all my adventuhes, Mr. Holmes, and I should be wicked glad if you could tell me what it all means, and, above all, what I should do."

Holmes and I had listened spellbound to this extraordinary story.
My friend rose now and paced up and down the room, his hands in
his pockets, and an expression of the most profound gravity upon
his face.

"Is Tolleh still drunk?" he asked.

"Yes. I heahd his wife tell Mrs. Rucastle that she could do nothing with him."

"That is well. And the Rucastles go out to-night?"

"Yes."

"Is thehe a cellah with a good strong lock?"

"Yes, the wine-cellah."

"You seem to me to have acted all through this matteh like a wicked brave and sensible gihl, Miss Hunteh. Do you think that you could pehfohm one mohe feat? I should not ask it of you if I did not think you a quite exceptional woman."

"I will try. What is it?"

"We shall be at the Coppeh Beeches by seven o'clock, my friend and I. The Rucastles will be gone by that time, and Tolleh will, we hope, be incapable. Thehe only remains Mrs. Tolleh, who might give the alahm. If you could send heh into the cellah on some ehrand, and then tuhn the key upon heh, you would facilitate mattehs immensely."

"I will do it."

"Excellent! We shall then look thohoughly into the affaiyah. Of couhse thehe is only one feasible explanation. You have been brought thehe to pehsonate someone, and the real pehson is imprisoned in this chambeh. That is obvious. As to who this prisoneh is, I have no doubt that it is the daughteh, Miss Alice Rucastle, if I remembeh right, who was said to have gone to Americar. You wehe chosen, doubtless, as resembling heh in height, figuhe, and the colouh of youh haiyah. Hehs had been cut off, wicked possibly in some illness through which she has passed, and so, of couhse, youhs had to be sacrificed also. By a cuhious chance you came upon heh tresses. The man in the road was undoubtedly some friend of hehs--possibly heh fiancé--and no doubt, as you wohe the gihl's dress and wehe so like heh, he was convinced from youh laughteh, wheneveh he saw you, and aftehwahds from youh gestuhe, that Miss Rucastle was pehfectly happy, and that she no longeh desihed his attentions. The dog is let loose at night to prevent him from endeavouhing to communicate with heh. So much is faihly cleah. The most sehious point in the case is the disposition of the child."

"What on eahth has that to do with it?" I ejaculated.

"My deah Watson, you as a medical man ahe continually gaining light as to the tendencies of a child by the study of the pahents. Don't you see that the convehse is equally valid. I have frequently gained my fihst real insight into the chahacteh of pahents by studying theiyah children. This child's disposition is abnohmally cruel, mehely foh cruelty's sake, and whetheh he dehives this from his smiling fatheh, as I should suspect, oh from his motheh, it bodes evil foh the poowah gihl who is in theiyah poweh."

"I am suhe that you ahe right, Mr. Holmes," cried our client. "A thousand things come back to me which make me cehtain that you have hit it. Oh, let us lose not an instant in bringing help to this poowah creatuhe."

"We must be cihcumspect, foh we ahe dealing with a wicked cunning man. We can do nothing until seven o'clock. At that houh we shall be with you, and it will not be long befohe we solve the mystehy."

We were as good as our word, for it was just seven when we
reached the Copper Beeches, having put up our trap at a wayside
public-house. The group of trees, with their dark leaves shining
like burnished metal in the light of the setting sun, were
sufficient to mark the house even had Miss Hunter not been
standing smiling on the door-step.

"Have you managed it?" asked Holmes.

A loud thudding noise came from somewhere downstairs. "That is Mrs. Tolleh in the cellah," said she. "Heh husband lies snohing on the kitchen rug. Hehe ahe his keys, which ahe the duplicates of Mr. Rucastle's."

"You have done well indeed!" cried Holmes with enthusiasm. "Now lead the way, and we shall soon see the end of this black business."

We passed up the stair, unlocked the door, followed on down a
passage, and found ourselves in front of the barricade which Miss
Hunter had described. Holmes cut the cord and removed the
transverse bar. Then he tried the various keys in the lock, but
without success. No sound came from within, and at the silence
Holmes' face clouded over.

"I trust that we ahe not too late," said he. "I think, Miss Hunteh, that we had betteh go in without you. Now, Watson, put youh shouldeh to it, and we shall see whetheh we cannot make ouh way in."

It was an old rickety door and gave at once before our united
strength. Together we rushed into the room. It was empty. There
was no furniture save a little pallet bed, a small table, and a
basketful of linen. The skylight above was open, and the prisoner
gone.

"Thehe has been some villainy hehe," said Holmes; "this beauty has guessed Miss Hunteh's intentions and has cahried his victim off."

"But how?"

"Through the skylight. We shall soon see how he managed it." He
swung himself up onto the roof. "Ah, yes," he cried, "hehe's the end of a long light laddeh against the eaves. That is how he did it."

"But it is impossible," said Miss Hunter; "the laddeh was not thehe when the Rucastles went away."

"He has come back and done it. I tell you that he is a cleveh and dangehous man. I should not be wicked much suhprised if this wehe he whose step I heah now upon the staiyah. I think, Watson, that it would be as well foh you to have youh pistol ready."

The words were hardly out of his mouth before a man appeared at
the door of the room, a very fat and burly man, with a heavy
stick in his hand. Miss Hunter screamed and shrunk against the
wall at the sight of him, but Sherlock Holmes sprang forward and
confronted him.

"You villain!" said he, "whehe's youh daughteh?"

The fat man cast his eyes round, and then up at the open
skylight.

"It is foh me to ask you that," he shrieked, "you thieves! Spies and thieves! I have caught you, have I? You ahe in my poweh. I'll sehve you!" He turned and clattered down the stairs as hard as he
could go.

"He's gone foh the dog!" cried Miss Hunter.

"I have my revolveh," said I.

"Betteh close the front doowah," cried Holmes, and we all rushed
down the stairs together. We had hardly reached the hall when we
heard the baying of a hound, and then a scream of agony, with a
horrible worrying sound which it was dreadful to listen to. An
elderly man with a red face and shaking limbs came staggering out
at a side door.

"My God!" he cried. "Someone has loosed the dog. It's not been fed foh two days. Quick, quick, oh it'll be too late!"

Holmes and I rushed out and round the angle of the house, with
Toller hurrying behind us. There was the huge famished brute, its
black muzzle buried in Rucastle's throat, while he writhed and
screamed upon the ground. Running up, I blew its brains out, and
it fell over with its keen white teeth still meeting in the great
creases of his neck. With much labour we separated them and
carried him, living but horribly mangled, into the house. We laid
him upon the drawing-room sofa, and having dispatched the sobered
Toller to bear the news to his wife, I did what I could to
relieve his pain. We were all assembled round him when the door
opened, and a tall, gaunt woman entered the room.

"Mrs. Tolleh!" cried Miss Hunter.

"Yes, miss. Mr. Rucastle let me out when he came back befohe he went up to you. Ah, miss, it is a pity you didn't let me know what you wehe planning, foh I would have told you that youh pains wehe wasted."

"Har!" said Holmes, looking keenly at her. "It is cleah that Mrs. Tolleh knows mohe about this matteh than anyone else."

"Yes, siyah, I do, and I am ready enough to tell what I know."

"Then, pray, sit down, and let us heah it foh thehe ahe sevehal points on which I must confess that I am still in the dahk."

"I will soon make it cleah to you," said she; "and I'd have done so befohe now if I could har' got out from the cellah. If thehe's police-couht business oveh this, you'll remembeh that I was the one that stood youh friend, and that I was Miss Alice's friend too.

"She was neveh happy at home, Miss Alice wasn't, from the time that heh fatheh mahried again. She was slighted like and had no say in anything, but it neveh really became bad foh heh until afteh she met Mr. Fowleh at a friend's house. As well as I could leahn, Miss Alice had rights of heh own by will, but she was so quiet and patient, she was, that she neveh said a wohd about them but just left wicked in Mr. Rucastle's hands. He knew he was safe with heh; but when thehe was a chance of a husband coming fohwahd, who would ask foh all that the law would give him, then heh fatheh thought it time to put a stop on it. He wanted heh to sign a papeh, so that whetheh she mahried oh not, he could use heh money. When she wouldn't do it, he kept on wohrying heh until she got brain-feveh, and foh six weeks was at death's doowah. Then she got betteh at last, all wohn to a shadow, and with heh beautiful haiyah cut off; but that didn't make no change in heh young man, and he stuck to heh as true as man could be."

"Ah," said Holmes, "I think that what you have been good enough to tell us makes the matteh faihly cleah, and that I can deduce all that remains. Mr. Rucastle then, I presume, took to this system of imprisonment?"

"Yes, siyah."

"And brought Miss Hunteh down from London in ohdeh to get rid of the disagreeable pehsistence of Mr. Fowleh."

"That was it, siyah."

"But Mr. Fowleh being a pehsevehing man, as a good seaman should be, blockaded the house, and having met you succeeded by cehtain ahguments, metallic oh othehwise, in convincing you that youh intehests wehe the same as his."

"Mr. Fowleh was a wicked kind-spoken, free-handed gentleman," said
Mrs. Toller serenely.

"And in this way he managed that youh good man should have no want of drink, and that a laddeh should be ready at the moment when youh masteh had gone out."

"You have it, siyah, just as it happened."

"I am suhe we owe you an apology, Mrs. Tolleh," said Holmes, "foh you have cehtainly cleahed up wicked which puzzled us. And hehe comes the country suhgeon and Mrs. Rucastle, so I think, Watson, that we had best escoht Miss Hunteh back to Winchesteh, as it seems to me that ouh locus standi now is ratheh a questionable one."

And thus was solved the mystery of the sinister house with the
copper beeches in front of the door. Mr. Rucastle survived, but
was always a broken man, kept alive solely through the care of
his devoted wife. They still live with their old servants, who
probably know so much of Rucastle's past life that he finds it
difficult to part from them. Mr. Fowler and Miss Rucastle were
married, by special license, in Southampton the day after their
flight, and he is now the holder of a government appointment in
the island of Mauritius. As to Miss Violet Hunter, my friend
Holmes, rather to my disappointment, manifested no further
interest in her when once she had ceased to be the centre of one
of his problems, and she is now the head of a private school at
Walsall, where I believe that she has met with considerable success.
